The Long Orchard

Barış Kısır



Chapter 1: Graft Markers

The pear-rice trees were flowering out of season.

Sana Ilyen noticed before the orchard drones did.

That was not unusual. Drones watched in grids, by chlorophyll variance, transpiration, fruiting probability, pest shimmer, and branch-load stress. They were excellent at seeing the orchard as a problem of surfaces. Sana had been walking graft rows since she was old enough to reach a ladder rung, and she saw the orchard the way old fishers saw weather: through misbehavior that had not yet become data.

Three white petals lay on the black irrigation cloth between Row C-17 and Row C-18.

One petal would have been chance. A wind carry. A child with a flower in her pocket. A pollinator drone brushing the wrong canopy and dropping a soft accident. Three petals, all from the same spur cluster, all wax-edged and damp with a sugar sheen that belonged six weeks later in the season, meant the tree was either confused or lying.

Trees did not lie on purpose.

People did, often with trees as witnesses.

Sana crouched, touched the nearest petal with the back of her gloved finger, and watched the microfilm on her nail turn pale blue.

"You are not supposed to be awake," she said.

The tree said nothing. The orchard around it said everything else.

Morning rain ticked through the canopy in patient threads. Cascadia's northern recovery belt never rained like the old recordings said it had, not without towers and fog nets and regulated cloud seeding, but the restored slopes had learned enough wetness to make moss return to concrete. The air smelled of pear blossom, wet basalt, living mulch, and the faint mineral bite of nutrient channels. Far above the row, rain towers stood on the ridge like patient skeletons, catching fog from the coastal flow and persuading it inland drop by drop.

Below them stretched the Graft Nine preserve, a long terraced orchard stitched into what had once been a collapsed suburban logistics hill. Pear-rice grew there in paired verticals: woody trunks bred from old Asian pear, dwarf apple, and engineered flood-root stock; panicles of dryland rice emerging from graft platforms where fruiting branches would once have carried only sweetness. The crop was ugly to purists and beloved by ration clerks. It gave starch, sugars, shade, soil hold, pollinator work, and legal headaches in one organism.

Sana loved it for all those reasons.

She rose and tapped her wrist slate.

"Field note. Graft Nine preserve, Row C-17, spur cluster seven. Early anthesis. Petal sugar response positive. Request local dormancy audit."

The nearest orchard drone pivoted in midair, six black rotors adjusting with the annoyed grace of a mechanical insect disturbed during prayer.

Dormancy audit already scheduled, the drone printed on her slate.

"Move it forward."

No priority condition detected.

Sana looked at the three petals, then up at the tree. "Detected by whom?"

The drone did not answer because drones were mercifully not persons. Its silence was not contempt. It was only bad configuration.

"Manual override. Auditor authority Ilyen-Sana-443. Move dormancy audit to immediate."

Override requires anomaly classification.

"Category seven."

The drone paused.

Category seven requires living archive risk.

"Yes."

The word made the orchard seem to listen.

Two terraces below, a crew of apprentices in yellow rain shells moved along a nursery row, laughing as they clipped training ties from young branches. Their laughter rose, broke around the wet trunks, and thinned when they saw Sana standing still. In orchards, stillness was contagious. It meant someone had found rot, theft, disease, or a law.

Her slate pulsed green.

Manual override accepted.

The drone descended to the graft junction. A soft ring of light swept over bark, bud, and archive seal. Every tree in Graft Nine carried a living provenance chain: visible tags for humans, chemical markers for field tests, embedded ceramic flecks readable by drones, and a genetic ledger that could be sampled but not trusted alone. Earth had learned, after the Famine Patents and the Inland Hunger Marches, that whoever controlled the story of a seed could control who was allowed to plant it.

Sana waited while the drone read the seal.

Then she stopped waiting.

The seal was wrong before the result arrived.

It sat at the graft collar, a crescent of dull white ceramic no larger than a thumbnail. To most auditors it would look ordinary: archive mark, regional release code, orchard line, rootstock family, pollinator condition, field generation. Sana had read enough living seals under rain, ash, frost, and political panic to notice when the maker's pressure had been off by a fraction. The mark had been pressed into young bark by a left-handed clamp.

Graft Nine had used right-handed clamps the year this tree was planted.

The drone returned its report.

Provenance verified:

Line: PR-17 Long Orchard pear-rice

Rootstock: flood-dwarf 22

Pollen condition: corridor-dependent

Release authority: Cascadia Watershed Compact, North Rain Vault

Field generation: F4

Archive confidence: 0.992

"No," Sana said.

The apprentice laughter stopped completely.

She took a hand lens from her belt pouch and leaned close. Rain tapped the hood of her coat. Under magnification the ceramic mark revealed its shallow ridges, clean as a legal signature and just as capable of fraud. The release authority thread was North Rain Vault. The rootstock thread was flood-dwarf 22. The pollen condition belonged to corridor-dependent pear-rice, yes.

But the dormancy score hidden in the edge notch was not from PR-17.

It belonged to BR-9, a basin rice-pear line never approved for wet terraces because it woke under wrong heat and flowered when rain patterns shifted. BR-9 was a Dry Basin trial stock, drought-hard, politically sensitive, and legally restricted after the Reedfly Collapse.

Someone had put a Dry Basin dormancy notch into a North Rain line.

Or someone had wanted the tree to say they had.

"Lock this row," Sana said.

The drone's rotors clicked.

No disease condition detected.

"I didn't say disease."

The drone hovered.

Lock reason?

Sana felt the old anger rise, the one that came whenever a machine asked her to name a category before it would let her protect a living thing. Categories mattered. They also made wonderful doors for negligence.

"Provenance conflict. Living archive integrity. Manual quarantine, no destruction, no pruning, no sample removal without my chain."

The drone printed a red band on every slate within twenty meters. The apprentices looked down at once. One of them swore softly.

Their crew lead, Tem Arra, came up the terrace steps with a pruning saw clipped at his hip and worry already tightening his mouth. Tem had the broad hands of someone who trusted tools more than declarations. He was twenty years older than Sana and still called her Doctor only when angry.

"Tell me that is a drill," he said.

"It is not a drill."

"Tell me it is mites."

"It is not mites."

"I hate when you conserve words."

Sana handed him the lens and pointed.

Tem bent, looked, adjusted the lens, looked again. Rain collected in his gray beard. He did not swear this time. That was worse.

"Wrong notch," he said.

"Yes."

"Could be nursery error."

"Nursery error doesn't use a left-handed clamp from a tool series Graft Nine never bought."

He looked at her. "You remember clamp procurement?"

"I remember scandals."

"Healthy habit."

"No. Professionally useful damage."

Tem gave the lens back. "How many?"

"I have one visible conflict. I need the row scanned by human eye before drone confirmation."

"Because drones already passed it."

"Because drones already passed it."

He turned toward the apprentices. "No one cuts, cleans, tags, touches, samples, breathes dramatically on, or composes poetry near Row C until Auditor Ilyen says otherwise."

One apprentice raised a hand. "Can we breathe normally?"

"Under supervision."

Sana almost smiled. Almost.

Her slate vibrated against her wrist before she could log the next inspection. The caller ID carried the blue-green mark of the Cascadia Watershed Compact and the narrow black border used for succession alerts.

She stared at it.

Tem saw her face. "What now?"

The slate opened without her consent because emergency civic alerts were still permitted to be rude.

Compact Steward Elian Voss is dead.

Rain moved through the orchard.

For a moment, every drone in Graft Nine rose half a meter, receiving the same signal. Across the terraces, apprentices, pollinator techs, soil nurses, and graft crews looked down at their wrists. Some made small gestures, old religious signs with the religion worn thin. Some simply stood. Steward Voss had been eighty-one, stubborn, ceremonial, and better at delaying bad decisions than making good ones. In Cascadia, that counted as competence.

The alert continued.

Per Watershed Continuity Charter, food succession protocols are now active.

All seed archive release requests enter provisional review.

All disputed Long Orchard access keys enter protective lock pending Steward Council quorum.

All basin emergency claims must be refiled under succession authority within six hours.

Sana read the last line twice.

Six hours.

Across a compact where inland cooperatives were already waiting on drought grain, fungal cultures, and rootstock approvals. Across a compact where every city, basin, insurer, archive, and hunger council had spent years rehearsing fairness in public and priority in private.

Tem said, "That is bad timing."

Sana looked back at the wrong notch on the tree.

"No," she said. "That is timing."

Her slate vibrated again.

This call came from Ro Tamsin.

Sana nearly let it pass. Ro never called before writing unless the law had caught fire. Then again, the law had just caught fire.

She accepted.

Ro's face appeared in rain-broken resolution, angular and calm in the way only professional mediators and exhausted surgeons were calm. Behind them, the Columbia Estuary Courts moved in visible panic: people crossing, panes opening, blue charter lines scrolling along the walls.

"Sana," Ro said. "Tell me you are not in an orchard."

"I am in an orchard."

"Tell me it is not Graft Nine."

"It is Graft Nine."

Ro closed their eyes.

"That was not a real request for truth. That was a ritual plea."

"Then perform a better ritual."

"I need you at the estuary court."

"No."

Ro opened their eyes. "You have not heard why."

"A succession lock just froze disputed Long Orchard keys. Every council will file emergency claims. Someone will ask me to sit in a chamber while the living evidence is here in the rain."

"Someone altered the disputed access list before Voss died."

Sana stopped.

Tem watched her carefully.

Ro continued. "Three Dry Basin requests vanished from the provisional queue. Two Puget vertical claims were elevated. A North Rain Vault disease hold appeared on lines that were clear yesterday."

Sana turned slowly toward the quarantined row.

"Which lines?"

Ro's jaw tightened. "Pear-rice PR-17 among them."

Rain touched the three petals on the black cloth and flattened them into white scraps.

The tree above Sana trembled in the weather. Out of season. Out of ledger. Out of the comfortable order that let councils pretend archives were neutral because seeds did not speak.

"I found a graft marker conflict," she said.

Ro did not react visibly. That was how Sana knew they understood.

"Define conflict."

"North Rain release authority. Dry Basin dormancy notch. Wrong clamp signature. Drone confidence high."

"Meaning?"

"Meaning the ledger will say one thing, the tree another, and the machine will agree with the ledger until someone teaches it shame."

Ro glanced offscreen. "Can you certify tampering?"

"Not yet."

"Can you certify enough to stop destruction?"

"I already locked the row."

"Good. Do not let North Rain take custody."

Tem swore then.

Sana said, "Why?"

Ro's voice dropped. "Because the protective lock names North Rain Vault as temporary integrity holder for all PR lines."

Sana looked up the terrace toward the ridge, where the rain towers faded into low cloud. North Rain Vault sat beyond them in cold stone, trusted because cold was supposed to be impartial.

"They cannot hold a line under suspicion of false authority," she said.

"They can under succession emergency unless someone files living conflict before the key transfer completes."

"How long?"

"Forty-three minutes."

Sana laughed once, without humor.

Tem said, "That's not enough to scan the row."

"No," Sana said. "It is enough to keep them from saying no one objected."

Ro leaned closer to their camera. "Sana. Listen carefully. If you file living conflict, you become chain witness. Every claimant can compel your field record. Every council can challenge your authority. You will be accused of delaying emergency food release during succession."

"Are you advising me not to file?"

"I am advising you that process will try to make you responsible for its own delay."

"It always does."

"Yes."

Sana looked at the apprentices, at Tem, at the tree flowering under wrong rain, at the three petals stuck to irrigation cloth like small white flags. She thought of her brother Nilo at twelve, not in this orchard but in an inland settlement whose seed release had arrived nine days after the fever. She thought of adults saying verification takes time, and of time taking children because it had been offered them.

She would not confuse speed with care.

She would not confuse care with waiting, either.

"Tem," she said. "Human scan. Every graft collar in C row. Photograph, lens check, manual note. No samples unless I mark them."

He nodded once.

"Apprentices," she called.

They straightened as if she had become more official than she felt.

"Today you learn why archive marks matter. Not because the archive is sacred. Because hunger makes liars sound practical."

No one laughed.

Good.

She opened the emergency filing pane and selected:

Living archive conflict.

The pane demanded category.

She selected:

Provenance contradiction.

The pane demanded claimant.

She wrote:

The tree.

The pane rejected it.

Claimant must be legal person, council, archive, compact office, recognized guild, basin authority, insurer, or emergency steward.

Sana looked at the tree.

"Fine," she said. "We will translate."

She entered her own name as field auditor and chain witness, then attached the first petal image, the seal magnification, the wrong-notch note, the clamp discrepancy, the out-of-season anthesis record, and Ro's access-list warning as unverified legal context.

The pane asked for a summary in thirty words or fewer.

Sana typed:

Living graft record contradicts archive provenance during succession lock; custody transfer would place disputed evidence under possibly false authority.

Ro read it through the shared channel. "Good."

"It is twenty-six words."

"I meant legally good."

"Worse compliment."

She submitted.

For seven seconds nothing happened.

Then every drone in Graft Nine turned toward Row C.

Emergency living conflict filed.

Custody transfer paused.

Review authority pending.

North Rain Vault challenge expected.

Puget Verticals challenge expected.

Dry Basin Hunger Council challenge expected.

Food Security Insurance Cooperative challenge expected.

Unauthorized claimants expected.

Tem looked at the orchard drones. "The machines sound cheerful."

"They are not cheerful," Sana said. "They are relieved to have categories."

Her slate pulsed with new messages. North Rain Vault requested access. Puget Verticals requested access. The Dry Basin Hunger Council requested access. A Food Security Insurance Cooperative requested access and used words that made Sana want to drop the slate into the irrigation channel. Three news feeds asked whether she was obstructing succession food security. Someone had already leaked the filing title.

Ro said, "You need to leave Graft Nine."

"I just locked it."

"And now everyone knows where you are."

Sana looked down the row. Rain made silver threads of the air. The pear-rice tree above her held its impossible blossoms quietly, as if blooming at the wrong time had been an act of testimony and now the rest was human noise.

In the black cloth below, the three petals trembled under falling water.

Sana picked one up with sterile tweezers, sealed it in a field envelope, and wrote the chain mark by hand.

"I will leave when the row has human eyes on every collar."

"Sana."

"Ro."

The mediator exhaled. "You are impossible."

"No. I am an auditor."

Tem, already kneeling at the next tree, said, "Same thing."

Sana sealed the envelope and looked toward the ridge, where the North Rain Vault waited behind weather and law. Somewhere beyond it, basins were counting meals. Cities were counting days. Insurers were counting liability. Councils were counting votes. Everyone was counting seeds.

The tree had counted differently.

That was where the war would begin, if they let it.



Chapter 2: The Steward Dies in Rain

Elian Voss died with rain on his face and a spoon in his hand.

That was the first image the public received, because Cascadia had spent two centuries learning to distrust speeches that arrived without weather. The Steward's last address had been scheduled on the upper deck of Rain Tower Twelve, under open mist, so every watershed could see the same drops on the same old skin. It was supposed to be a reassurance: water was moving inland on schedule; the snowpack debt was real but manageable; the Long Orchard would release drought stock after standard review; no basin would be abandoned to models or panic.

Then the Steward lifted a tasting spoon of first-catch tower water, smiled as if the ritual still amused him after forty-nine years of office, and collapsed before he could drink.

The tower cameras did not cut fast enough.

Every compact channel showed the spoon falling. It struck the grate, bounced once, and vanished into the white noise of rain. Voss's body followed more slowly, held for a breath by the aides who had always hovered near his elbows but had never believed they would be asked to carry history. One aide screamed. Another said the Steward's name in the wrong tone, not public, not ceremonial, only frightened.

Then the channels cut to the Cascadia seal.

By the time Sana reached the east shelter at Graft Nine, the footage had already been clipped, slowed, interpreted, condemned, memorialized, and set to music by three grief feeds.

"Turn that off," she said.

No one did.

The shelter was a low concrete room built into the terrace, half tool locker, half storm refuge, with wet coats steaming against the wall and soil data panes flickering over seedling trays. Tem's apprentices crowded near the central screen despite pretending not to. Tem stood at the workbench, hands braced on either side of a sealed sample case. His jaw moved as if he were grinding words before permitting them into the room.

On the screen, Rain Tower Twelve replayed again.

Voss raised the spoon.

Voss smiled.

Voss fell.

Sana reached over the nearest apprentice and shut the pane down.

The apprentice, a tall girl named Liri, flinched. "My aunt is on tower crew."

Sana stopped with her hand still on the frame.

"Is she safe?"

"I don't know."

That changed the room. Public death had a way of becoming private without asking permission.

Sana turned the pane back on, but muted it and moved the feed to a corner. "Find the crew registry, not the replay."

Liri nodded quickly and began searching.

Tem looked at Sana. "You should have left."

"I am leaving after I file the human scan."

"You filed the human scan twelve minutes ago."

"Then I am leaving after I read it."

"You read it twice."

"Tem."

"Sana."

He had been letting her borrow stubbornness as if it were a tool. Now he was taking it back.

"There are six people outside the lower gate asking for you," he said. "Two say they are North Rain custody officers. One says she is Compact emergency audit. One says he represents Puget Verticals. Two did not say enough and are therefore either journalists or idiots."

"Those categories overlap."

"I noticed."

Sana opened the row scan. Her eyes tried to run ahead. She forced them to slow.

Row C contained sixty-four pear-rice trees. Forty-nine read clean by human collar inspection. Eight had weather damage obscuring at least one seal thread and needed controlled reinspection. Six had minor tool-pressure variation consistent with old bark swelling. One had the wrong notch.

One visible conflict.

That should have relieved her.

It did not.

Fraud rarely announced itself evenly. One wrong mark could be a planted seed of doubt, a test, a decoy, a mistake, or a door. The danger lay not only in what it changed, but in what it made institutions willing to do next.

She sealed the human scan under her field chain, copied it to Ro, copied it to the emergency living conflict file, and copied it to the local crew archive because field workers had learned, long before lawyers admitted it, that records vanished most often when only important people held them.

Liri made a small sound.

"My aunt is listed as injured," she said.

The shelter quieted.

"Name?" Tem asked.

"Maren So."

Tem entered it into his own slate. "Conscious?"

Liri swallowed. "Unknown."

Sana put a hand on the workbench, not on Liri's shoulder. Touch was a question, and the girl had enough unanswered.

"You can leave," Sana said. "Go to the tower family channel."

Liri shook her head. "If I leave, I will watch the clip."

Sana understood that too well.

"Then stay and do work that does not pretend not to be fear."

Liri nodded once, hard.

Tem said, "Inventory envelopes."

She moved immediately.

The muted pane filled with official text.

WATERSHED CONTINUITY CHARTER, SECTION 19:

Upon death, incapacitation, removal, or verified absence of the Compact Steward during active food-risk season, provisional succession authority shall preserve seed archive integrity, water allocation continuity, and basin ration stability until Steward Council quorum.

Protective locks shall apply to disputed access keys.

Emergency release requests shall be refiled under succession authority.

No regional claimant may alter living archive custody without quorum, except under disease, sabotage, or continuity breach.

Sana read the last exception.

Disease, sabotage, or continuity breach.

Someone had already used disease.

She had just filed continuity breach.

Sabotage would be next, if the pattern had any ambition.

Her slate chimed. Ro again.

She accepted audio only.

"I am still in the orchard," Sana said.

"I assumed you would be."

"Growth."

"Do not waste sarcasm. We have a problem."

"One?"

"A countable problem. I treasure those."

Ro's voice carried court noise behind it: overlapping declarations, emergency chimes, someone demanding standing in a tone that usually meant money had arrived.

"The succession clauses are active," Ro said. "Steward Council quorum requires seven of nine watershed signatories. Two are in transit, one is refusing remote certification, and one is claiming North Rain cannot sit while holding protective custody."

"That last claim is correct."

"Correct and weaponized are not opposites."

Sana closed her eyes briefly. "What do you need?"

"Your living conflict filing has paused the PR-17 transfer, but only that line and only pending authority review. Other disputed lines are still moving into protective locks. Dry Basin requests are being forced to refile from zero."

"That will kill people."

Tem looked over.

Ro did not soften the answer. "Yes."

Sana watched rain bead on the shelter threshold. Outside, drones hovered over Row C like uneasy punctuation.

"Who benefits from making Dry Basin start over?" she asked.

"Puget Verticals, if grain density claims move first. North Rain, if vault custody becomes default. Food insurers, if uncertainty raises backing fees. Any council that wants succession panic to centralize authority. Anyone who thinks hunger should be scheduled by population yield."

"Vale."

"He has not filed yet."

"That is not a no."

"No."

Dr. Mirek Vale was not an officeholder. That made him more dangerous in some rooms. He chaired the Food Security Actuarial Board, advised three watershed councils, designed half the ration-stability models people cited when they wanted cruelty to look literate, and never raised his voice. Sana had argued with him twice. Both times he had been polite enough that she distrusted herself for wanting to throw something.

"He will say the locks prevent panic," she said.

"He is already saying that on a closed advisory feed."

"You said he had not filed."

"He has not filed legally. He is shaping the room before law arrives."

Sana turned from the threshold. "Send me the feed."

"If I send you the feed, you will respond."

"Yes."

"From the orchard."

"Yes."

"While six people wait at the gate to turn your body into jurisdiction."

"Do not phrase things well when you want me to obey."

Ro sighed. "Sana, Voss died twelve minutes before your filing."

The shelter seemed to tilt.

"What?"

"The public alert was delayed while medical confirmation moved through charter protocol. Your graft conflict was found after he died but before the public knew. Whoever altered the access list likely knew before most councils."

Sana felt the wrong notch become colder in her memory.

"Internal timing."

"Yes."

"Tower medical, Steward aides, succession office, charter relay, maybe North Rain if they received preliminary lock notice."

"Yes."

"And whoever planted the disease hold."

"Yes."

Tem said, "Someone moved before the rain hit the public."

Sana had forgotten she was on speaker. Or maybe she had wanted the room to hear.

Ro said, "That is a concise way to terrify everyone. Thank you, Tem."

Tem grunted.

The shelter door opened and brought in colder rain.

A woman in a North Rain Vault field coat stood outside with two custody officers behind her. The coat was white-gray, beaded with water, marked with the vault's mountain line. She had silver hair braided tight against her head and a face arranged around institutional patience.

"Dr. Sana Ilyen," she said. "I am Keeper Iona Saye."

Tem straightened.

Even the apprentices knew the name. Iona Saye had kept the North Rain Vault since before most of them were born. She was said to know the founding seed lines by smell, which was nonsense, and the founding lies by tone, which might not be.

Sana ended Ro's call without saying goodbye.

"Keeper Saye."

Iona looked into the shelter, past Sana, to the sealed sample case, the apprentices, the muted death notice, the rain-soaked tools.

"I am not here to take your row."

Sana believed her exactly halfway.

"Why are you here?"

"To stop my officers from trying."

The two custody officers behind her looked unhappy enough to confirm this.

Iona stepped inside without waiting for invitation, then stopped just past the threshold, respecting the clean zone around the samples.

"Your filing has made North Rain look compromised."

"Is it?"

One officer made a sharp sound.

Iona lifted two fingers. The officer became silent.

"All archives are compromised by the fact that hungry people need them," Iona said. "If you mean did North Rain alter that graft marker, I do not know. If you mean did North Rain benefit from the succession lock naming us temporary holder, yes. If you mean will North Rain give up custody authority without a formal challenge, no."

Sana studied her. "That is a lot of honesty."

"I am old. I ration charm."

Tem muttered, "I like her."

Sana did not move. "What do you want?"

Iona looked at the muted pane, where Voss's name had become a banner under charter text.

"The Steward is dead. In three hours, half the compact will decide the other half is stealing food. In six, the refiling deadline will erase any basin without legal staff awake and connected. By night, someone will call that unfortunate but orderly."

"You still have not said what you want."

"I want your conflict filed broadly enough that the succession court cannot pretend this is one tree."

The shelter held itself still.

"You want me to accuse the archive system."

"I want you to state what the tree already did."

The line struck too close to Sana's own anger to trust immediately.

"Why not state it yourself?"

"Because I hold one of the institutions that must be challenged."

"Convenient integrity."

"Inconvenient integrity, Dr. Ilyen. Convenient integrity stays home and issues condolence statements."

Sana's slate chimed again, and this time the sound came from every slate in the shelter.

Emergency Advisory: Food Security Actuarial Board

Speaker: Dr. Mirek Vale

Topic: Succession Stability and Archive Continuity

The pane unmuted itself under public priority.

Mirek Vale appeared without rain.

That was the first thing Sana hated.

He stood in a dry chamber lined with living grain displays, each behind glass, each lit cleanly enough to look more like evidence than food. He was narrow, dark-skinned, elegant in a green-black collar, with the composed sorrow of a man who had prepared grief phrases before needing them.

"The death of Steward Voss is a loss to the compact," Vale said. "But the greatest dishonor we could do him is to let succession grief become food panic."

Iona closed her eyes.

Sana said, "There it is."

Vale continued.

"Protective locks are not theft. They are continuity. Emergency refiling is not denial. It is triage. The Long Orchard exists because previous generations accepted that seeds released in panic can destroy as surely as seeds withheld in fear. We must not confuse every local distress with compact-wide priority."

Amma Quill of the Dry Basin Hunger Council appeared in a side pane, face hard, connection unstable.

"Local distress," she said, "is what fed cities call hunger before it reaches them."

The advisory feed tried to lower her audio. Too late.

Vale inclined his head as if she had contributed usefully to his point.

"Hunger is real. That is why order matters."

Sana felt the room around her heat with human anger.

Vale's grain displays glowed behind him.

"I recommend immediate centralization of disputed release keys under provisional succession authority, actuarial prioritization of release schedules, and a temporary hold on non-verified living conflict claims until disease and sabotage risks are separated from political obstruction."

Tem said, "He means you."

"He means more than me," Sana said.

Vale looked directly into the camera.

"We cannot let grief, rumor, or isolated field anomalies decide who eats."

Sana reached for the pane.

Ro's message arrived before she could speak.

DO NOT RESPOND ON PUBLIC FEED FROM A TOOL SHELTER WITH WET HAIR.

Sana stared at the message.

Then another:

That was legal advice and aesthetic concern.

Despite herself, Sana laughed.

It saved her from answering badly.

She turned to Iona. "If I broaden the filing, North Rain challenges?"

"Yes."

"Puget challenges."

"Yes."

"Insurers challenge."

"Professionally."

"Dry Basin?"

Iona's mouth tightened. "They may support, if Amma trusts you enough to believe process is not another locked door."

Sana looked back at the pane. Vale was now showing charts: yield continuity, population density, protein replacement lag, corridor fragility, unrest probability. The charts were not false. That was their power. They were real numbers arranged around an absent question: who had been allowed to define survival before the crisis?

"I need to go to the estuary court," Sana said.

Tem threw both hands up. "Finally."

"After I finish the sample chain."

"Of course."

Iona said, "I will escort you."

"I do not need escort."

"No. You need a witness whose institution you distrust."

Sana looked at her for a long moment.

"That was also too well phrased."

"I am old," Iona said again. "I have had time."

Liri returned from the corner, face pale but steadier.

"My aunt is conscious," she said. "Broken wrist. Concussion watch. She says Steward Voss ruined her perfect tower shift."

The shelter exhaled.

Not relief exactly. Relief with bruises.

Sana nodded once. "Good. Inventory the petal envelopes with me."

Liri came to the bench.

Together they logged three petals, one seal magnification set, sixty-four collar inspection notes, drone audit output, and the handwritten discrepancy sheet. Sana made Liri read each item aloud, not because she doubted the girl but because voice made records harder to pretend away.

When the chain closed, Sana placed the sample case in a portable archive lock. The case recognized her palm, then Tem's, then Liri's as witness. Iona watched without interrupting. The North Rain officers watched as if each second cost them authority.

Outside, the rain had thickened. It blurred the orchard into bands of green, black, and white. The pear-rice trees held still under it, row after row of engineered patience.

Sana put on her field hood.

Ro called again.

"Are you moving?"

"Yes."

"To the court?"

"Yes."

"With whom?"

"Tem's fury, Liri's chain witness, Iona Saye, two unhappy custody officers, and a tree that objected."

Ro paused.

"I can work with that."

Sana stepped from the shelter into the rain.

At the lower gate, the six waiting claimants turned toward her at once. North Rain badges, Puget green, audit blue, feed lenses, insurer gray. Behind them the access road curved down toward the transit line, and beyond that the recovered valley opened: wet terraces, fog nets, nursery roofs, old concrete softened by moss, the long green argument of a planet trying to feed its children without repeating itself.

One of the journalists shouted, "Dr. Ilyen, are you obstructing emergency food release?"

Sana kept walking.

Another shouted, "Did Steward Voss know the archives were compromised?"

She stopped then.

Iona stopped beside her.

Tem muttered, "Bad idea."

Sana turned back to the lenses.

"Steward Voss died in rain telling the compact no basin would be abandoned to models or panic," she said. "I found a living record that does not match the archive. I am going to court so no one can use his death to make the seeds speak only for the powerful."

The lenses drank it.

Ro groaned audibly through the open slate. "Wet hair public feed. Of course."

Sana resumed walking.

Behind her, in Row C, drones held their quarantine line. Ahead, the estuary court waited with its clauses, claims, charts, grief, and hunger.

Rain fell on all of it with perfect indifference.

That was why people had to choose.



Chapter 3: Seed Lock

The North Rain Vault did not look like a weapon.

That had always been part of its strength.

It appeared on the court wall as a calm white cross-section cut into mountain stone: six cold chambers, two living nurseries, one fungal darkroom, one pollinator winter hall, three redundant water rights, nine legal seals, and a roofline buried under engineered snow. Its public icon showed a hand holding a seed beneath rain. Its children's tours described it as a pantry for the future. Its old founding oath said the vault kept no crown, only memory with roots.

At 11:07 Cascadia time, the pantry closed its hands.

Every Long Orchard access pane in the Columbia Estuary Court went red at once.

The sound that followed was not a shout. It was worse: a collective intake of breath from people who knew enough to understand that a lock could be quieter than violence and more efficient.

Sana stood near the wet entry rail with rain still dripping from her hood onto the polished floor. The court had been built in the shell of an old ferry terminal. Its western wall was glass, looking over a braided estuary where restored marsh, salmon channels, flood pylons, and drowned road signs shared the tide. Above the benches, legal panes hung in layered arcs, each one carrying a basin, city, guild, insurer, archive, or hunger council claim. On most days the room smelled of cedar polish, algae ink, and tired procedure.

Today it smelled of wet coats and fear.

Ro Tamsin met Sana halfway across the floor, took in her field case, Iona Saye, the two North Rain custody officers, Tem's remote witness feed, and the fact that Sana had indeed arrived with wet hair.

"I am choosing not to comment," Ro said.

"Growth."

"Do not reward me."

The court's central pane flashed again.

NORTH RAIN VAULT PROTECTIVE LOCK ACTIVE

Reason: Blight Flag, Multi-Line Integrity Risk

Affected Lines:

PR-17 pear-rice

DM-4 drought maize

FP-2 fungal protein starter

SR-11 smoke-resistant rootstock

LS-6 late-season legume

Release Status: Frozen pending disease separation review

Emergency Requests: Suspended

Estimated Review Window: 72 hours

The room broke.

Not into riot. Not yet. It broke into jurisdiction.

"Seventy-two hours?" shouted a woman wearing Dry Basin brown. "We filed five days ago."

"Disease review protects everyone," a North Rain legal officer answered.

"Everyone who has food for three days."

A Puget Verticals representative stood with both palms raised in the universal gesture of reasonable theft. "Population-density claims cannot wait behind politically motivated field objections."

Amma Quill's remote pane expanded without permission from anyone in the court, which told Sana the Dry Basin Hunger Council had found a sympathetic technician somewhere in the relay chain. Amma's face filled a rectangle above the central bench: dark, lined, furious, lit by the harsh interior light of a place that saved power by making everyone look unwell.

"Say politically motivated again," Amma said.

The Puget representative did not.

Food Security Insurance Cooperative counsel began speaking over both of them. "Backing instruments require integrity certainty before release. If the vault cannot certify line safety, distribution risk becomes uninsurable under succession emergency."

Ro's expression changed by half a degree.

Sana recognized that as contempt.

"Uninsurable hunger," Ro said softly. "They polished the phrase and still brought it outside."

At the high bench, Interim Adjudicator Pellan Marr struck the tone bar three times. Marr was small, severe, and famous for making men with inherited water rights cry without raising her voice. The tone bar cut through argument because the court's old architects had understood that law sometimes needed acoustics more than authority.

"All parties will stop performing urgency and provide it," Marr said.

The room quieted by fractions.

Marr looked down at the central pane. "Keeper Saye. Did North Rain initiate this lock?"

Iona stood beside Sana, white-gray coat darkening at the hem from rainwater. She did not approach the North Rain table.

"North Rain's automatic disease protocol initiated the lock after receiving a multi-line blight flag."

"That is not an answer."

"No. North Rain did not manually initiate."

"Did North Rain verify?"

"Not yet."

The North Rain legal officer snapped, "Keeper, the protocol carries provisional verification."

Iona turned her head. "The protocol carries provisional panic with grammar."

Someone in the gallery made a sound quickly swallowed.

Marr's mouth did not move, but Sana suspected she appreciated the sentence.

"Dr. Ilyen," Marr said. "You filed a living archive conflict on PR-17 before this multi-line lock."

"Yes."

"Do you assert the blight flag is false?"

"I assert that the PR-17 field evidence includes provenance contradiction, out-of-season anthesis, wrong clamp signature, and no observed disease condition at Graft Nine."

"That is narrow."

"Narrow is what I have."

"Do you assert connection between your conflict and the broader lock?"

Sana felt the room lean.

This was the door. If she said yes without proof, Vale and every insurer in the room would turn her into rumor wearing a raincoat. If she said no, the court would isolate the tree, and the larger lock would stand as weather.

"I assert timing sufficient for emergency inquiry," she said. "Not causation. Not yet."

Ro's shoulders loosened almost invisibly.

Marr nodded. "Good. The court prefers its accusations with bones."

Dr. Mirek Vale stood from the advisory table.

Of course he had a table already.

In person he looked less polished than on the feed and more dangerous for it. The chamber light showed a faint scar beside his left eye, a tremor in one hand controlled by pressure against the table edge, a man housed in a body that had also survived history. Sana disliked the sympathy this created in her. She disliked more that he probably knew it would.

"Adjudicator," Vale said, "with respect to Dr. Ilyen's caution, emergency food systems cannot operate on suggestive timing. A blight flag across five staple lines requires immediate freeze. Any release before separation review risks cross-basin contamination and a compact-wide collapse of trust."

Amma Quill laughed once from her pane.

"Trust collapsed when our request vanished."

Vale turned toward her image. "Your request will be reviewed under succession authority."

"We filed before succession."

"The Steward's death changes authority."

"It does not change hunger."

"No," Vale said. "It changes who is empowered to prevent hunger from becoming violence."

Sana heard the trap close in his language. Prevent hunger from becoming violence. Not prevent hunger. Prevent what hunger might do to order.

Ro stepped forward. "The Dry Basin requests should retain original filing time."

"Under normal continuity, perhaps," Vale said. "Under death of Steward during active food-risk season, Section 19 requires refiling."

"Section 19 requires ration stability," Ro said. "Erasing queue position destabilizes ration expectation."

The Food Security counsel smiled. "Expectation is not allocation."

Amma said, "Say that where my people can hear you."

"They are hearing me now."

"No," Amma said. "You are hearing yourself near people who eat lunch."

The pane flickered as someone tried again to lower her priority. Marr looked toward the clerk pit. "If the Dry Basin pane flickers once more without my order, the next technician will argue standing."

The pane steadied.

Sana set her field case on the evidence rail.

The sound was small but useful.

"I have live-chain evidence from Graft Nine," she said. "It does not prove blight. It proves the archive cannot be treated as clean while determining whether blight exists."

Vale looked at the case. "Field evidence from one orchard cannot override multi-line disease caution."

"I am not asking it to override. I am asking it to prevent custody consolidation."

"Disease review requires central control."

Iona said, "No. Disease review requires controlled access."

Vale's eyes moved to her. "Keeper Saye, North Rain wrote that distinction after the Red Spore Litigation and has invoked it twice in your tenure."

"Then I am pleased you read our work."

"I read failures carefully."

"Then continue."

Sana almost smiled. Iona rationed charm, but she did not ration knives.

Marr leaned forward. "Keeper Saye, can disease separation occur without freezing all emergency requests?"

The North Rain officer began, "Adjudicator, protocol--"

"I asked the Keeper."

Iona folded her hands. "Technically, yes. Legally, contested. Politically, inconvenient. Biologically, preferable."

"Explain."

"If all requests freeze, desperate claimants begin moving informal stock. That increases disease risk. If controlled micro-releases continue under field witness, soil match, isolation, and recipient tracking, risk is higher than zero but lower than black movement."

Kade Morrow had not entered the story yet, but Sana felt his outline in Iona's words: black movement, informal stock, the illegal routes everyone condemned publicly and survived privately.

Vale said, "That approach rewards pressure tactics."

Amma's pane snapped, "Eating is not a pressure tactic."

Marr struck the tone bar once. "Dr. Vale. Does the Actuarial Board have independent confirmation of blight across all five lines?"

Vale's pause was small.

Sana saw it.

Ro saw it.

Iona saw it.

"We have correlated archive flags," Vale said.

Marr's voice cooled. "That was not my question."

"No independent field confirmation yet."

"Has any affected field site reported active disease?"

"Not to my knowledge."

"Has the blight sample origin been verified?"

"North Rain protocol lists origin as pending."

Iona turned sharply toward her officers.

One would not meet her eyes.

Sana felt the room's floor move beneath the legal furniture.

Origin pending.

A disease flag without origin could still be real. Spores traveled before paperwork. But a multi-line lock with no field confirmation, no origin verification, and access-list edits before public death no longer looked like caution alone.

It looked like someone using caution's clothing.

Marr said, "The court orders the following immediate measures. One: the North Rain Vault lock remains in place for sealed bulk release but may not erase pre-succession filing priority. Two: emergency requests filed before Steward Voss's death retain timestamp pending quorum. Three: micro-release petitions may be heard under field witness and isolation protocol. Four: all blight flag origin data will be produced to this court within one hour."

The Food Security counsel rose. "Adjudicator, without insurer backing--"

"Five," Marr said, louder, "any insurer threatening to withdraw protein backing during a court-preserved emergency queue will file the threat in writing under its own name."

The counsel sat.

Sana liked Marr very much for three seconds.

Then Marr looked at her.

"Six: Dr. Ilyen's living conflict is broadened into an emergency integrity inquiry across the affected lines. Dr. Ilyen remains chain witness and will submit field methodology by end of day."

Sana stopped liking her.

"Adjudicator," Ro said, "Dr. Ilyen cannot be made sole hinge for a compact-wide inquiry."

"She will not be sole hinge. She will be first hinge. There is a difference."

"Hinges break."

Marr looked at Sana. "Do you refuse?"

The court went quiet again.

Refusal was possible. That mattered. It also made the answer heavier.

Sana thought of Liri reading sample labels aloud because fear needed work. Tem kneeling in rain. Iona stepping across her own institution's comfort. Amma holding a hunger council together over a flickering feed. Voss collapsing before he could drink. The tree flowering wrong because someone had placed a false season in its skin.

"No," Sana said. "I do not refuse."

Ro said nothing. Their silence had edges.

Marr nodded. "Then the record will show consent under objection to overload."

"Yes," Ro said immediately. "It will."

Marr allowed the correction with a blink.

Vale sat slowly.

He did not look defeated. That would have comforted Sana. He looked as if the board had changed shape and he had already begun recalculating. Competent antagonists did not rage when a door closed. They measured the hinges.

The central pane updated.

PRIORITY PRESERVED:

Dry Basin Hunger Council Request DB-44

Dry Basin Cooperative Request DB-47

Smoke Corridor Nursery Request SC-12

Puget Verticals Emergency Density Claim PV-3

North Rain Vault Integrity Hold NR-1

Food Security Insurance Backing Review FSIC-9

MICRO-RELEASE HEARING QUEUE OPEN

The room inhaled again, differently this time.

Not safe.

Never safe.

But not sealed.

Amma Quill's pane shrank by her own choice. Before it did, she looked directly at Sana.

"If this becomes another delay with better witnesses," Amma said, "I will name it."

"You should," Sana said.

"I will name you too."

"You should."

The pane contracted to a small brown square in the claim arc.

Ro came to Sana's side. "You understand what just happened?"

"Marr gave me work."

"Marr made you politically load-bearing."

"I prefer work."

"That is why people make you load-bearing."

Sana looked at the central pane. The North Rain Vault icon still glowed red. A child's pantry for the future, locked behind disease, law, succession, and whatever hand had moved before the public knew Voss was dead.

"Then help me distribute the load."

Ro's anger shifted into approval, which for Ro looked almost the same.

"That," they said, "I can do."

Iona approached with her two custody officers no longer standing quite so close behind her.

"North Rain will comply with the origin-data order," she said.

"Will North Rain comply before editing itself into innocence?"

The younger officer stiffened.

Iona did not. "That depends on whether I reach the vault before people who prefer innocence."

Sana understood the sentence one beat late.

"You are going back."

"I am Keeper."

"You said I needed a witness whose institution I distrust."

"You had one for the walk here. Now you need records."

"And if they lock you out?"

Iona smiled then, and it was not charming at all.

"Dr. Ilyen, I helped design the locks."

She left with both officers hurrying after her.

Ro watched her go. "That woman is either the best ally we have or the most dangerous archive politician in Cascadia."

"Both," Sana said.

"Your last book's word of the year?"

Sana looked at them.

Ro shrugged. "Mars legal digests. I read."

The reference passed strangely through the room: Mars, children, machine persons, pressure cities, other crises arriving as law before anyone had learned what to do with them. Earth had its own old emergencies. Seeds. Water. Hunger. Rain.

The court clerk approached with a tablet. "Dr. Ilyen, field methodology form."

Sana took it.

The form had thirty-one required fields, seven liability acknowledgments, and a box labeled Summary of Concern.

She almost laughed.

Instead she wrote:

The vault is locked. The hunger is not.

Ro read over her shoulder.

"Legally terrible," they said.

"I know."

"Keep it somewhere else."

Sana copied it to her private field notes and wrote the official version with more bones.

Outside the glass wall, the tide moved through the restored marsh. Salmon channels gleamed in the gray light. Flood pylons stood where old roads had drowned. Rain freckled the estuary until water and sky became one continuous surface, broken only by reeds, birds, and the court's reflection.

In that reflection, the legal panes looked like fruit hanging from a tree no one had remembered planting.

Red fruit. Locked fruit. Hungry fruit.

Sana thought of the three petals in their envelope.

The North Rain Vault had closed.

Across Cascadia, requests froze, queues trembled, councils sharpened their language, and families counted what remained in bins, tanks, trays, and cupboards.

The war had not begun.

Not yet.

But the seeds were behind locks now, and everyone knew locks had keys.

Chapter 4: Ro at the Water Table

The Columbia Estuary Courts had been built around a table no one owned.

That was the story told to schoolchildren, visiting delegations, basin interns, and nervous claimants waiting in the rain. It was half true, which made it useful. The original table had been a raft of cedar beams lashed together after the Third Retreat, when the old riverfront courthouse flooded so often that litigants stopped pretending walls had authority. Mediators, fish stewards, desal crews, farm towers, and river families had gathered on that raft under a patched canopy while the river rose around their boots. They had argued about salmon corridors, sewage rights, rain debt, salvage claims, and which neighborhoods would be lifted, abandoned, or turned into wetland. No chair had a back. No seat had a name. Anyone who came to the raft had to feel the river move beneath the boards.

The present table did not move.

It occupied the center of Hearing Chamber Seven, a circular slab of translucent mineral glass threaded with live water. Thin channels ran below the surface, fed by pumps that drew from sealed samples: Columbia estuary, upper Willamette, North Rain melt, Puget desal, Yakima basin groundwater, firebreak reservoir, dry inland cistern. Each sample flowed through its own narrow vein. When a case involved a watershed, that watershed lit. When a claim crossed basins, the channels braided into a temporary map.

At dawn, all of them were lit.

Sana stood beside the wall with her field bag against her hip and watched Ro Tamsin put both hands flat on the glass.

Ro had changed coats. During the night they had worn plain court gray, anonymous as rain. Now they wore mediator white with blue cuffs, a formal sash of braided river grass, and a narrow copper pin shaped like a spillway gate. The clothes made them look more official and less protected. Sana had never trusted ceremonial fabric. It told frightened people where to aim their anger.

"They will start with standing," Ro said quietly.

Sana looked at the chamber doors. "Who?"

"Everyone who does not want to discuss hunger yet."

The doors opened before she could answer.

They came in by delegations, because even panic had protocol. Puget Verticals sent Governor Hal Renn and three food-allocation advocates in dark green tower cloth that shimmered faintly with humidity control. The North Rain council sent two remote witnesses on wheeled screens because Iona was already gone and, according to the clerk, unavailable in transit. The Fire-Buffer towns sent a smoke orchard mayor with burned knuckles and ash braided into her hair. Columbia Estuary sent legal observers. The Dry Basin cooperatives were not physically present. Their packet had arrived late, compressed, and flagged as "emotional testimony, evidentiary status contested."

That phrase made Sana's teeth touch.

Food insurers occupied the second ring of benches. They always did. They wore no uniforms except the cultivated neutrality of people who had learned to price grief without appearing interested in it.

Dr. Mirek Vale entered last.

Sana knew him from committee recordings before she knew him in person. Food-security actuary. Succession model architect. Collapse-law historian. Calm voice. Accurate footnotes. A man often invited when other people had run out of courage and wanted arithmetic to wear the blame.

He was smaller than the recordings made him seem. Lean, black hair shaved close, skin the color of wet walnut, a mouth drawn in patient lines. His left hand held a slate. His right hand was bare except for a ring of old ceramic, white as bone. He paused at the threshold, looked once at the lit table, and then at Sana.

Recognition did not cross his face. Classification did.

Field botanist. Witness. Obstruction possible.

He inclined his head with perfect courtesy and took his seat.

Ro saw the motion. Their expression did not change, but the water under their palms brightened where Puget desal crossed dry cistern.

"This emergency mediation is convened under Compact succession article nine, archive access annex four, and Columbia Estuary procedural rule twelve," Ro said. Their voice carried without amplification. "We are here because the death of Steward Elian Voss has triggered contested authority over Long Orchard release pathways, and because the North Rain Vault has entered protective lock after a blight flag affecting emergency rootstock and grain requests."

Governor Renn leaned forward before Ro finished the sentence. "Puget Verticals contests the word contested."

Ro glanced at the clerk. "Noted."

"There is no lawful ambiguity in density protection triggers."

"There is enough ambiguity that you came to a mediation chamber."

One of Renn's advocates murmured into his ear. Renn ignored them.

"Mediator Tamsin, Puget currently feeds twenty-seven million permanent residents and approximately four million registered recovery migrants. Our fungal protein reserves depend on grain-stabilized substrate. Delay creates urban ration contraction in six days. Disorder in nine. Population flight in twelve. That is not rhetoric. That is warehouse math."

The channels under the table shifted. Puget desal pulsed brighter.

Ro nodded. "Filed as urgency claim one."

"Urgency claim?" Renn repeated. "This is not a claim. It is the central fact."

The smoke orchard mayor gave a short laugh from the opposite side. "Every central fact sits in the center until someone else starves around it."

Renn turned. "Mayor Odet, your towns receive firebreak insurance through Puget underwriting."

"And you receive pollinator continuity through our corridors. Threaten me after breakfast, Governor."

A few people inhaled. No one laughed. It was too early for humor to be safe.

Ro tapped the glass once. The water channels dimmed to equal brightness.

"The chamber will not rank fear by volume," they said.

"It will rank by lives," Renn said.

"It will hear by evidence."

Vale raised one finger.

Ro looked at him. "Dr. Vale."

"If I may clarify the actuarial frame. Lives and evidence are not separable in this matter. The Compact designed succession protocols because delays in food allocation create cascading mortality beyond the visible claimant populations. Density is not a moral preference. It is a network property."

His voice was mild. That made the sentence harder to hate.

Mayor Odet folded her burned hands. "And basin abandonment is what, Doctor? A weather pattern?"

"A risk category," Vale said.

The chamber went still.

Sana felt the words enter the room and look for owners.

Ro's gaze moved from face to face. "We are not adopting Dr. Vale's categories by hearing them."

"You will," Renn said, too softly.

Ro did not answer him. They turned to the clerk. "Enter Dry Basin packet."

The clerk hesitated.

Ro waited.

"Mediator," the clerk said, "evidentiary status is contested."

"By whom?"

"Puget Verticals, North Rain interim council, and Meridian Food Mutual."

One of the insurers leaned back as if the chair had accused him. Meridian Food Mutual. Sana marked the name. She had seen it in rootstock bond files, beside language about guaranteed continuity, deferred scarcity instruments, and basin resilience scoring.

Ro asked, "Grounds?"

The clerk read from the screen. "Nonstandard data collection. Emotional format. Absence of certified hunger measurements. Potential coercion within recorded testimony."

Sana looked at the empty Dry Basin seats. There were four of them, because the chamber had expected four people to travel three hundred kilometers through disrupted ration corridors to beg in person for evidence status.

"Who submitted?" she asked.

Ro did not look at her. "Dr. Ilyen, you are present as technical witness, not party."

"I am asking who sent the packet."

The clerk checked. "Amma Quill, Dry Basin hunger council, South Yakima cooperative chain, with attached family ledgers from seven settlements."

Ro's eyelids lowered a fraction. "Enter the packet provisionally."

Renn's advocate stood. "Objection."

"Recorded."

"Mediator Tamsin, provisional entry gives contested testimony public force."

"Yes," Ro said.

The advocate stopped, uncertain whether the answer was a mistake.

Ro lifted their hands from the table. The water inside the glass reshaped into a long interior basin, pale and thin, threaded by cistern lines like veins in an old wrist. A recording opened in the air above the empty chairs.

The woman who appeared was in her fifties, broad-shouldered, gray hair wrapped in a red cloth, standing under a roof patched with translucent irrigation film. Behind her, shelves held labeled jars: barley, bean, dry apple, mycelium cake, salt. Most were nearly empty. Children moved behind a hanging sheet, visible only as shadows and elbows.

"My name is Amma Quill," the woman said. "I am speaking for South Yakima cooperative chain, Dry Basin hunger council, and whoever is too tired to record twice."

The chamber's acoustics made her voice intimate. Not large. Not theatrical. A tired person choosing to spend strength on accuracy.

"We are told this packet has emotional format. That is true. Hunger is an emotional format. So is a warehouse. So is a city. So is a locked vault. We are told our measurements are uncertified. The certifiers came last season, took samples, delayed publication, and billed us for procedural travel. We measured anyway."

She lifted a ledger, paper, not slate.

"Household nine, elder with fungal allergy, protein substitution failed. Household twelve, twins under two, both below growth line. Household eighteen, orchard workers, four adults, one heat injury, one pregnancy. Household twenty, seed trial plot withheld pending rootstock release, soil ready for ten days. Household twenty-one, moved from old Moses Lake retreat zone, no legal orchard shares because the registry drowned before their grandmother was born."

Sana stopped breathing normally somewhere around household eighteen.

Amma Quill turned a page.

"We do not ask Puget children to eat less so ours can eat more. We ask why every emergency begins by proving we are small enough to wait. We ask why the archive can lock in one hour, but release requires councils, insurers, models, and signatures from people whose shelves are not being counted by spoon."

The recording flickered. Compression loss chewed the edge of her face. The children behind the sheet went still.

"If the seed lines are sick, show us. If they are scarce, show us. If we are being sacrificed, say our names before you do it."

The packet ended.

No one spoke.

Then Renn said, "Puget does not dispute hardship."

Mayor Odet swore under her breath.

Renn pressed on. "But anecdote cannot govern release policy."

"Neither can invisibility," Sana said.

Ro turned sharply. "Dr. Ilyen."

Sana knew the warning. She could lose witness status. She could be removed from the chamber and turned into a field note outside the room where people with cleaner clothing decided what her evidence meant.

She also saw Amma Quill's empty chairs.

"Anecdote is what certified data is called before someone funds the certificate," Sana said.

Vale watched her with interest now.

Renn's face hardened. "That is irresponsible."

"So is treating uncertified hunger as hypothetical."

Ro held up one hand. "Enough."

The word struck like a gate.

Sana stopped. Renn sat back with visible effort. The insurers resumed their neutral faces, which meant they had enjoyed the exchange.

Ro said, "The chamber will distinguish between release authority, biological risk, logistical capacity, and hunger testimony. It will not erase any category because another is easier to measure."

Vale placed his slate on the table. "Then it must also distinguish between distributive justice and system survival."

"Define system," Ro said.

"Food continuity at Compact scale."

"Define Compact."

For the first time, Vale looked faintly disappointed. "Mediator."

"Define Compact," Ro repeated.

Renn shifted. Mayor Odet looked at Ro as if she had just noticed a second blade in a tool handle.

Vale folded his hands. "The Compact is an interdependent watershed governance structure whose first duty is to prevent mass mortality through coordinated resource management."

"Not the cities?"

"The cities are primary mortality concentrations."

"Not the basins?"

"The basins are production and resilience zones with variable carrying capacity."

"Not the people?"

Vale's mouth tightened. "People are the reason mortality matters."

"But not the unit your model protects first."

"Models require units."

"So do graves," Amma Quill's recording said suddenly.

Everyone turned.

The clerk nearly dropped his tablet. "Apologies. The packet has a supplemental response keyed to anticipated model language."

For one absurd second, Sana wanted to laugh. Amma Quill had recorded answers to ghosts because she had known the living would say the same things.

The supplemental image opened. Amma was seated now, closer to the recorder.

"Someone will say models require units," she said. "We know. We use them. Calories. Seed weight. Cistern depth. Work hours lost to dizziness. Burial cloth. Here is our unit: a person who knows they were counted after the decision."

The recording ended again.

Ro let silence sit.

This was one of their skills, Sana realized. They did not fill silence to protect officials from what had entered it. They let the room hold the thing until someone revealed whether they could bear its weight.

Renn bore it badly.

"Mediator, with respect, if this hearing becomes moral theater, Puget will seek direct emergency authority from the succession court."

"You may," Ro said.

"And if the court grants it?"

"Then you will own what you request."

"I already own responsibility for twenty-seven million residents."

"No," Ro said. "You represent it. Ownership is exactly the disease in this room."

The second ring stirred. One insurer whispered to another. The North Rain screens flickered as remote witnesses conferred off-channel.

Sana looked at Ro with new concern. Mediators were not supposed to accuse the room this early. They were supposed to keep doors open, translate rage into procedure, make each side feel neither shamed nor triumphant. Ro knew that better than anyone.

Which meant they had chosen the accusation.

Vale also knew it. He leaned forward.

"Mediator Tamsin, I appreciate the philosophical point. But no governance system survives by refusing prioritization. The locked vault has created a decision vacuum. Into vacuums come panic, private force, and black-market release. If lawful authority does not centralize distribution within forty-eight hours, unauthorized pathways will appear."

Sana thought of Iona walking back toward the vault with two custody officers hurrying after her. She thought of lock designers and people who preferred innocence.

"Unauthorized pathways already exist," she said.

Vale's eyes moved to her field bag.

Sana regretted the sentence as soon as his attention landed there.

Ro did not rescue her. "Dr. Ilyen."

She could lie. She could say she meant historical leakage. She could protect Kade Morrow, whoever he was now, whatever he had been when he moved seeds under the law's blind side.

Instead she said, "The official ledger has gaps around drought rootstock movement in the old inland famine years. Some living markers in present graft stock suggest lines moved outside authorized release. If those lines survived, then the model's scarcity assumptions may be incomplete."

"Or contamination risk is greater than disclosed," Vale said.

"Yes."

That answer seemed to please him more than denial would have.

"Then you see why central control is necessary."

"I see why truth is necessary."

"Truth does not distribute food."

"Lies do," Sana said. "Badly."

The chamber shifted again. Not loud. Not dramatic. But attention moved toward her as if a current had changed.

Ro let it happen for three seconds, then reclaimed the room.

"Here is the mediation frame," they said. "No claimant receives exclusive release authority today. North Rain must submit origin data under court order. Puget may file density urgency calculations by noon, with assumptions exposed. Fire-Buffer towns will submit pollinator corridor dependency maps. Dry Basin testimony enters the record provisionally and will be certified at court expense, not claimant expense. Meridian Food Mutual and associated insurers will disclose all food-continuity instruments tied to delayed seed release."

The Meridian representative stood so fast his chair struck the bench behind him.

"Mediator, that exceeds the scope."

"No," Ro said. "It enters the scope you hoped to price outside the room."

"Those instruments are proprietary."

"So are locks, until people starve behind them."

Renn looked from the insurer to Vale. It was quick, but Sana saw it. So did Ro. So did Vale, which mattered most.

Vale's expression did not change, yet something closed behind it.

"Mediator," he said, "your orders may satisfy transparency values while worsening release delay."

Ro looked down at the water table. Under the glass, every basin still glowed.

"That is possible."

Sana heard the cost in the admission.

"Then why do it?" Renn demanded.

Ro looked up.

"Because every basin will read delay as violence," they said. "If delay is unavoidable, it must at least be visible, argued, witnessed, and accountable. Hidden delay becomes ownership. Visible delay can still become law."

No one liked the answer.

That was different from no one understanding it.

The North Rain screens brightened. A remote witness said, "We have just received a vault status update."

Ro turned. "From Keeper Saye?"

"No. From interim security."

Sana's neck tightened.

The witness read, "Keeper Saye has entered the outer archive without full escort. Internal access channels are partitioned. Security requests emergency authorization to suspend her keeper credentials pending review."

Renn said, "Do it."

Mayor Odet said, "Touch that old woman and see how many firebreak crews forget your pollinator shipments."

The insurer said nothing. Vale watched Sana.

Ro asked the witness, "Has Keeper Saye violated biological containment?"

"No confirmed violation."

"Has she removed seed stock?"

"No confirmed removal."

"Has she accessed origin data responsive to court order?"

A pause.

"Possibly."

Ro smiled without warmth.

"Then the keeper is complying creatively."

"Mediator, North Rain requests guidance."

Ro looked at the lit table, at Puget's bright vein, the dry cistern thread, the fire reservoir, the estuary current, the North Rain melt, all trapped under glass and moving because pumps told them how.

"Guidance is as follows," Ro said. "Do not suspend Keeper Saye without evidence of containment breach. Preserve all access logs. Transmit origin data to this chamber, the field auditor of record, and the provisional claimant file. If any record is edited, deleted, partitioned, or reclassified before transmission, I will treat that act as evidence of bad faith in emergency release proceedings."

The remote witness swallowed. On a screen, that looked like a stutter in the throat.

"Understood."

The screens dimmed.

For a moment, the room breathed with many lungs and no agreement.

Ro adjourned for two hours.

The delegations broke apart in controlled currents. Renn left surrounded by advocates and a silence that looked like strategy. Mayor Odet walked past Sana and murmured, "If your seeds need smoke hands, ask before noon." The insurers vanished into side corridors with the speed of people who preferred private math. Vale remained at the table, one finger resting lightly on the glass above the dry basin channel.

Sana should have left with Ro.

Instead she crossed to him.

"Dr. Vale."

He looked up. "Dr. Ilyen."

"You knew about the Dry Basin supplemental response."

"No."

"You did not seem surprised."

"People who are repeatedly categorized learn to answer categories."

It was not cruelty. That was the trouble with him. Sana had expected contempt and found comprehension sharpened into a weapon.

"Then why keep using them?"

"Because famine is not defeated by refusing ugly tools."

"Ugly tools cut the hand that uses them."

"Sometimes. Sometimes they cut bread."

Sana looked at the water under glass. "Bread from withheld seed."

Vale lifted his finger from the dry basin line. "You believe I want withholding."

"I believe you are comfortable deciding which withholding is rational."

"Comfort is irrelevant."

"No," Sana said. "It is the first audit point."

That reached him. Not deeply, but enough to interrupt the next prepared sentence.

Ro approached from behind her. "Sana."

Vale stepped back. "Mediator."

"Doctor," Ro said.

Vale gathered his slate. "You are buying legitimacy with time."

"And you are buying time with legitimacy."

He gave a small nod, almost appreciative. "Then we understand each other."

"Not yet," Ro said.

Vale left.

Sana watched him pass through the chamber doors. The hallway outside was already crowded with runners, clerks, claimants, and people pretending not to wait for permission to be afraid.

"You should not spar with him alone," Ro said.

"You did."

"I was on the record."

"Does that protect you?"

"No. It gives the wound an address."

Sana turned back to the table. With the hearing suspended, the basin lights had dimmed, but the water still moved below the surface. It made the glass look alive and trapped.

"I thought you believed law could slow violence," she said.

"I do."

"That hearing did not feel slow."

"It was not."

Ro touched the copper spillway pin at their collar, then let it go.

"The first job is to stop people from pretending delay is neutral. The second is to make sure no one uses that truth as an excuse to break every lock with a hungry crowd behind them."

"And the third?"

Ro looked toward the doors where Vale had gone.

"Find out who profits if both sides decide the other one started the war."

Sana thought of Meridian Food Mutual, of proprietary instruments, of Renn glancing toward Vale, of Iona alone inside a vault that wanted to call her breach instead of keeper.

Her field slate vibrated.

The sender ID was masked by old courier encryption, the kind retired after too many famine-era abuses and never entirely killed because hungry systems remembered useful sins.

The message contained no greeting.

Only a coordinate, a time, and nine words.

Official ledger lies. I have the wrong key. Come alone.

Ro read it over her shoulder.

"Do not," they said.

Sana looked at the empty Dry Basin chairs.

Then at the water table no one owned.

"I was not planning to come alone."

Chapter 5: Kade Morrow's Wrong Key

The coordinate led them under the city.

Columbia Estuary Courts had respectable exits for respectable people: glass bridges to the tram line, covered ramps to the delegation docks, quiet doors for judges who preferred not to be photographed beside hunger. Sana and Ro used none of them. Ro took her through a maintenance corridor behind Hearing Chamber Seven, past walls warm with server heat and ceiling pipes labeled in four languages and two obsolete legal codes. The corridor smelled of mineral damp, old insulation, and coffee someone had spilled during a night shift and not forgiven.

"You understand," Ro said, "that every correct answer to this message begins with calling security."

Sana kept walking. "And every useful answer?"

"Usually begins with a bad decision and paperwork later."

"That sounds like precedent."

"It is not precedent when I say it before doing it."

The corridor ended at a service lift with a cracked salmon decal on the door. Ro pressed their thumb to the panel. The lift refused them, requested second authorization, accepted a gesture from their copper spillway pin, and descended without asking where they wanted to go.

Sana looked at the pin. "Mediator jewelry has privileges."

"Mediator jewelry has liabilities."

"Does one of them include aiding an unauthorized meeting with a famine-era courier encryption source?"

"Several of them."

The lift opened into the substructure below the court, where the building stopped pretending it was transparent and remembered it was a machine sitting in wet ground. Pumps thudded behind reinforced panels. Condensate ran in thin silver threads down concrete. The old river had not been defeated here. It had been negotiated into channels, sumps, valves, and alarms.

The coordinate pulsed on Sana's slate.

LOWER INTAKE WALKWAY 3
FLOOD ACCESS NINE
NO COURT AUDIO

"No court audio," Ro read. "A phrase beloved by people who wish to die misunderstood."

"You can go back."

"No."

"That was quick."

"I am thinking of the report."

"What report?"

"The one I will have to write explaining why I allowed the field auditor of record to meet a possible archive criminal under the court intake pumps."

"Allowed?"

Ro looked at her.

Sana lifted both hands. "Fine. Accompanied."

"Better."

They passed a flood marker from 2198, a line of black ceramic tiles set shoulder high in the wall. Below it, someone had installed a small brass plaque: WATER WAS HERE BEFORE THE LAW. Below that, newer graffiti answered in blue marker: LAW LEARNED TO SWIM.

Sana slowed despite herself.

"Interns," Ro said.

"Good interns."

"Annoying interns."

The walkway narrowed. Water moved behind a grated barrier to their right, dark and fast, carrying the smell of silt, salt, algae, and cold metal. Above, old intake fans spun in slow protective cages. The court's beautiful water table depended on this uglier place: pumps, filters, sensors, people with boots, maintenance budgets, and corrosion schedules. Sana trusted it more for that.

Her slate vibrated again.

THIRTY STEPS. LIGHT OFF.

Ro stopped.

"No," they said.

Sana did not turn the light off.

Ahead, something clicked.

The walkway lights died anyway.

Darkness fell with mechanical completeness. Sana's body moved before thought finished. She stepped sideways, put her back to damp concrete, and pulled the field knife from her bag. It was not a weapon in law. It was for bark sampling, root cutting, tape slicing, emergency graft stripping, and the other peaceful acts that became sharp when held correctly.

Ro did not move. Or rather, they moved so quietly that Sana only knew their new position when their voice came from two meters left.

"Kade Morrow," Ro said into the dark. "If this becomes theatrical, I will be very disappointed."

A man's voice answered from beyond the grate.

"I would hate to damage your opinion of me before we meet."

"We have met."

"Professionally?"

"In charging documents."

"Those were aspirational."

Emergency guide strips woke along the floor, dim green lines that gave the world edges without faces. A figure stood behind the intake barrier where maintenance crews accessed the outer valve gallery. He was tall in the loose way of people who had spent years carrying weight and then stopped, leaving the body unsure what to do with the extra balance.
