His coat was patchwork waterproof, smoke gray over old orchard brown, repaired at the elbows with pollinator mesh. His hair was black, threaded with premature white, tied at the nape. He had a courier's stillness: not calm, exactly, but ready to move in any direction without warning.

Sana recognized him from no official file because the official files had tried too hard to make him into a type. Illegal seed runner. Provenance vandal. Hunger criminal. Cooperative rescuer. Archive breach suspect. The man himself looked tired, cold, and amused in the specific way of someone who knew amusement irritated frightened people.

"Dr. Ilyen," he said.

"Kade Morrow."

"Depending who asks."

"I am asking."

"Then yes."

Ro said, "You told her to come alone."

Kade looked at Sana. "She didn't."

"You are observant," Ro said.

"You are louder than mediators used to be."

"We evolved."

"So did locks."

Sana held up her slate. "You said the official ledger lies."

"It does."

"Everyone says that when the ledger disagrees with them."

"The difference is I brought the thing it says cannot exist."

He lifted his right hand slowly. Between thumb and first finger he held a seed key.

It looked disappointing.

Sana had seen archive keys that resembled ceremonial instruments: braided memory crystal, coded ceramic, sealed metal with lineage marks and embedded court signatures. This was smaller than the first joint of her thumb, a dull crescent of black-green composite set into a ring of old brass. Its surface was pitted. One edge had been cracked and repaired with resin so clumsily that any competent archive technician would have rejected it as contaminated hardware. It did not glow. It did not hum. It looked like something found in a drain.

Sana's disappointment made her more careful.

"That is not a North Rain key."

"No."

"Or a Compact key."

"Depends which Compact you mean."

Ro exhaled. "Do not do that."

Kade smiled. "Do what?"

"Say something historically loaded and then wait for someone to ask you to feel clever."

"I was going to say the first compact. Since you asked so warmly."

Sana stepped closer to the barrier. "The first Long Orchard compact did not use physical override keys. The founder archives used witness quorum seals and basin-matched release codes."

"That is the version taught after the insurers paid for curriculum updates."

"Careful," Ro said.

"I am careful. That is why I am alive."

"That is not evidence."

"No. This is."

Kade slid the crescent key through a gap in the barrier. It landed in Sana's palm with almost no weight.

Ro's hand moved. Sana closed her fingers before they could take it.

"Sana."

"I need to inspect it."

"You need gloves, a containment tray, chain-of-custody logging, and less dramatic lighting."

"I have three of those."

"Which one are you missing?"

The guide strips flickered.

"Lighting."

Ro made a sound that was not approval.

Sana opened her field kit and unfolded a sterile sample cloth over one palm. She set the key on it without touching the cracked resin again. Under magnification from her slate, the composite surface resolved into compressed layers: old seed ceramic, fungal memory lacquer, brass conductor, and something stranger tucked along the inner curve, a sliver of scarlet mineral glass etched with marks so small they looked organic.

Her mouth went dry.

"This has a living quorum scar."

Kade's amusement disappeared.

Ro leaned closer despite themselves. "Explain."

"Founder-era orchard controls were not supposed to accept single-person authority. Some release pathways required ecological witness: soil match, humidity proof, pollinator corridor confirmation, basin claimant consent. The quorum scars recorded which living systems had been verified."

"Recorded how?" Ro asked.

"Not AI. Not decision authority. A bounded biological sensor archive. You could lie to a court faster than you could fake a living corridor's chemistry, at least in theory."

"In theory," Kade said.

Sana looked at him. "Where did you get this?"

"From a dead woman who hated me."

"Name."

"Mara Venn."

Ro's head lifted. "The Rootfall courier."

Kade gave a little bow without pleasure. "Her least favorite apprentice."

Sana knew the name. Anyone who studied famine-era seed movement knew it. Mara Venn had been convicted of moving restricted drought barley and pear-rice rootstock through the old Yakima rail cuts during the Inland Hunger Marches. Officially, she had contaminated three provenance chains, undermined release authority, and forced two emergency trials to be destroyed. Unofficially, she had fed settlements whose names had appeared too late on certified maps.

"Mara Venn died in custody," Sana said.

"Mara Venn died in an infirmary bed with two guards arguing about whether water counted as contraband if I carried it." Kade's voice stayed light and became less human. "Before that, she gave me the wrong key."

"Why wrong?"

"Because it opens the path no one wants opened."

Ro said, "Stop speaking in riddles."

Kade looked past them toward the dark water. "The official ledger says there was no authorized movement from North Rain to South Yakima in 2319. It says drought rootstock remained sealed pending blight review. It says the cooperative failures that winter were due to soil exhaustion, fungal protein disruption, and unauthorized local substitution."

"And?"

"And every spring since, South Yakima has grown descendants of North Rain stock."

Sana felt the key change weight on the cloth.

"That could be illegal transfer."

"It was."

"That does not make the ledger false."

"It does if the transfer was authorized by a witness quorum before the authority was erased."

Ro went very still.

Sana looked from the key to Kade. "Erased by whom?"

"By people who needed the famine to have been unfortunate instead of managed."

The pumps thudded. Somewhere above them, respectable people were filing respectable motions while water held up the building from below.

Sana said, "Show me."

Kade reached into his coat.

Ro's hand went under their sash.

Kade froze. "Mediator, if I wanted you dead, I would have left you upstairs with the insurers."

"Hands slowly."

He withdrew a palm-sized reader wrapped in oilcloth. It was older than Sana expected, a courier field verifier from before the Compact standardized archive ports. Its casing was scarred, its screen replaced with a translucent patch, its battery pack tied on with orchard wire. It looked illegal because it looked repaired by necessity.

"This will not connect to court systems," Ro said.

"Correct."

"It could be loaded with forged records."

"Correct."

"You are not helping."

"I am telling you which parts to distrust."

Sana almost smiled. She did not want to.

Kade placed the reader on the maintenance shelf built into the barrier. Sana set the crescent key into the old slot. For a moment nothing happened. Then the reader clicked three times, each click softer than the last, like a seed coat splitting underground.

A projection opened between them.

Not a full ledger. A path.

North Rain Vault appeared as a blue node high in the map. South Yakima as a dry amber cluster. Between them ran no official corridor. The public records, faint gray overlays from Sana's own cached database, showed interruption, review, denial, delay. But under the gray, the old reader drew a living line: North Rain to Firebreak Seven, Firebreak Seven to Moss Corridor, Moss Corridor to an orchard monastery Sana did not recognize, monastery to South Yakima trial plots.

Each step bore witness marks.

Soil humidity. Pathogen screen. Pollinator viability. Hunger council receipt. Archive keeper dissent. Courier oath. Basin risk acceptance.

Sana leaned so close her breath fogged the projection.

"This is not a smuggling route."

Kade's laugh had no humor. "Tell my warrants."

"This is an emergency release pathway."

"It was."

Ro asked, "Why would the official ledger deny it?"

Sana read the marks again. One field appeared after courier oath and before basin risk acceptance, a field she did not recognize because it was not botanical.

Liability transfer refused.

She looked at Ro.

Ro's eyes had already found it.

"Insurers," they said.

Kade nodded. "Mara said the release was witnessed, lawful under the first compact, and later made impossible because no insurer would carry failure risk for a dry basin trial. If the crop failed, they wanted it unauthorized. If it succeeded, they wanted quiet access to the line."

"That is an allegation," Ro said.

"It is a pattern."

"Patterns need records."

Kade tapped the reader. "This is a record."

"A record you stole."

"A record I inherited from a woman the law called criminal because the alternative was calling a lot of clean people murderers."

The green floor lights flickered again. This time they did not settle.

Ro looked up.

"We have company."

Kade took the reader and key in one motion. Sana caught his wrist.

"No."

His eyes went flat.

Sana did not let go. "If you run with that, you remain a story people can dismiss. If you give it to me, it enters chain of evidence."

"If I give it to you, it disappears in a chain of custody."

"Not if Ro logs it."

"Mediator logs are breakable."

Ro said, "Not easily."

"Everything breaks if hungry enough."

Footsteps sounded above the pump noise. More than two people. Careful boots. Not maintenance.

Ro moved to the side of the walkway where a service ladder climbed toward an access hatch. "Decision."

Kade looked at Sana's hand on his wrist.

"You believe in the ledger," he said.

"I believe records rot unless someone keeps touching the living thing they describe."

"That is not a yes."

"It is the only yes I have."

For the first time, he looked uncertain.

Then he opened his hand.

The crescent key remained in his palm. The reader did not.

He pressed the old verifier into Sana's field bag, beneath the sample sleeves and graft tape.

"The key stays with me."

"Why?"

"Because it is wrong."

"Kade."

"The key opens the path. It also marks everyone who used it. If I hand it over, you will have proof and every dry basin family attached to the release becomes a defendant before noon."

Ro's face tightened. "He may be right."

Sana hated that.

The footsteps came closer.

Kade slipped the crescent key into his sleeve. "The reader has the path image, not the activation scar. Enough to prove denial. Not enough to burn the saved."

"And you?"

He smiled again, and now it looked like something he had learned from pain rather than chosen.

"I am already burned."

A voice called from the dark end of the walkway. "Court security. Stand visible."

Ro stepped into the green light with both hands raised just enough to insult the command.

"Mediator Tamsin," they said. "You are late."

Three security officers came into view. Not court gray. Compact field black. Sana recognized the difference and felt the day tilt. Court security protected procedure. Compact field security protected whoever had convinced them procedure had failed.

The officer in front looked from Ro to Sana to the barrier.

"We received report of unauthorized archive hardware."

Ro smiled politely. "From whom?"

"Operational source."

"How mysterious. I received a report of people misusing operational source to avoid warrants."

"Mediator, step aside."

"No."

The officer's jaw shifted. "This is an active succession emergency."

"Then file properly."

Behind the barrier, Kade moved.

The third officer saw him. "There."

Everything happened too fast and too slowly. The officer reached for a restraint launcher. Ro stepped into the line of fire. Sana threw her sample case, not at the officer's face but at the walkway lights above him. The case struck the protective cage, snapped open, and spilled sterile sleeves, tags, and reflective graft strips into the green glow.

The restraint charge fired.

It hit the cascade of reflective strips.

For one bright instant the dark intake gallery filled with ghost vines.

Kade vanished behind them.

Ro swore with legal precision.

Sana moved because Ro had moved, because the officer had shifted, because the field bag at her hip suddenly contained a record that could turn hunger testimony into evidence or evidence into indictments. She ducked under the officer's arm, slid on wet concrete, struck her shoulder against the barrier, and came up beside the service ladder.

"Up," Ro snapped.

"Kade?"

"Up."

Sana climbed.

Below, officers shouted. Ro did not shout. That was how Sana knew they were furious. They issued orders in a voice cold enough to make obedience feel like the safest available religion.

"This is now a procedural interference complaint. Badge numbers visible. Weapons down. I said down."

Sana reached the hatch and slammed her palm to the release. It requested authorization. She nearly laughed.

Behind her, Kade's voice came through the barrier, close and invisible.

"Use the wrong key next time, Dr. Ilyen."

Then the hatch opened from the other side.

A woman in maintenance orange looked down. She was elderly, broad, and chewing something green.

"Mediator sent you?" she asked.

"Not exactly."

"Good enough."

She hauled Sana through with astonishing strength.

Ro came up thirty seconds later, wet at one cuff and immaculate in expression. The hatch closed under them, cutting off the officers' voices.

The maintenance woman spat the green thing into a rag. "You people always make the pumps dramatic."

"Thank you, Jessa," Ro said.

"Don't thank me. Sign my deferred repair orders."

"Send them."

"I have. Since winter."

Ro closed their eyes briefly. "Send them again."

Jessa looked at Sana. "You bleeding?"

Sana checked. Her shoulder ached. Her hand had a thin cut across the knuckles. The field bag was still closed.

"No."

"That means yes but not enough." Jessa nodded down a narrow utility passage. "Go before someone remembers this corridor exists."

They went.

Only when the pump noise faded did Ro stop. They stood under a low ceiling where condensation gathered and fell in slow drops, one after another, onto the concrete between their shoes.

"Show me what he gave you," Ro said.

Sana opened the field bag.

The old verifier lay beneath the graft tape, wrapped in oilcloth. No key. No activation scar. A partial record. A path that should not exist.

Ro looked at it for a long time.

"This is enough to force disclosure," they said at last.

"And not enough to protect anyone."

"Often the same thing."

Sana sat on an overturned crate because her knees had remembered fear after the useful part of fear was done.

"The official ledger says no movement."

"Yes."

"The living path says movement."

"Yes."

"If the path was lawful under a suppressed first compact clause, then Kade's old crime may be evidence of a later cover-up."

"Yes."

"If we expose it badly, the Dry Basin families become proof of contamination, fraud, and unauthorized benefit."

"Yes."

Sana laughed once. It hurt. "You are using too many yeses."

"I am trying not to use the other word."

"Which one?"

Ro looked toward the hidden city above them.

"War."

Sana touched the oilcloth. The reader was warm from Kade's hand or from its own failing battery. She thought of Amma Quill's paper ledger, of Iona inside the vault, of Vale describing unauthorized pathways as if he had predicted them rather than helped make them necessary.

"Kade said the wrong key marks everyone who used it."

"That is why it is leverage."

"Against whom?"

"Everyone," Ro said. "That is the problem with old mercy hidden inside new law. It can save people twice or condemn them late."

The verifier's screen flickered once through the cloth.

Sana uncovered it.

A final line had appeared beneath the path map, delayed by the old reader's damaged memory.

FOUNDER SAFEGUARD: IF RELEASE DENIED AFTER QUORUM, PATH MAY BE WITNESSED BY HUNGER COUNCIL AND FIELD ECOLOGIST.

Below it, a second line blinked in weaker letters.

ECOLOGIST REQUIRED.

Ro read it.

Then they looked at Sana.

The pumps moved water through the court, through the table, through all the channels where respectable people would soon sit again and argue about urgency, risk, ownership, and delay.

Sana closed the verifier.

"Of course," she said.

Ro's voice was very soft. "This is why he came to you."

Above them, an alarm began to ring.

Chapter 6: Puget Wants Grain

The alarm did not sound like danger.

That was the first cruelty of it. No siren, no rising scream, no animal panic in the walls. The court used three low tones, evenly spaced, polite as a door chime in a house where no one had eaten. One tone for procedural suspension. Two for building hazard. Three for emergency assembly.

They heard three.

Ro's expression changed before the third note finished. Whatever private fear had been in their face vanished under mediator discipline, not hidden so much as folded into a smaller shape and put somewhere useful.

"That is not about us," Sana said.

"No. It is using us."

"Explain."

Ro took the oilcloth-wrapped verifier from her, turned it once, and put it back in her field bag as if returning a dangerous insect to a jar. "If Compact field security came below without proper court authority, someone expected either arrest or chase. If they failed, the next move is to move the room above us."

"Emergency assembly."

"A crowded one."

The third tone sounded again. The building lights shifted from maintenance amber to public blue.

Jessa's utility passage opened into a narrow stair that climbed between concrete ribs. They moved fast. Ro went first now, not because Sana needed protection but because every door that could legally resist them might hesitate for the mediator pin. Twice Ro palmed locks. Twice doors opened after a pause too long for comfort. On the third landing, a court runner nearly collided with them, saw Ro's sash, and stopped with both hands full of message tabs.

"Mediator Tamsin. Chamber Seven reconvened early."

"By whose order?"

"Succession court emergency clerk."

"Name."

The runner swallowed. "Clerk-Annotator Pell."

Ro's mouth thinned. "Pell does not wake before nine unless someone has already written the ruling."

"Governor Renn has filed a density-protection petition," the runner said. "Live public feed requested."

Sana felt the field bag at her hip become heavier.

"Requested by whom?" Ro asked.

"Puget Verticals."

"Granted?"

"Pending."

"Meaning yes, as soon as everyone important enters the room." Ro took one of the message tabs. "Go to Senior Clerk Omari. Tell her I am contesting improper reconvening and want the field-security access logs preserved before they become spiritual documents."

The runner blinked.

"Those exact words?" he asked.

"Those exact words."

He ran.

They climbed the last flight. By the time they reached the public corridor, the court had changed species. The morning's controlled currents had become a river in flood. Delegates moved in knots. Advocates dictated filings while walking. Remote witness screens rolled between bodies like sleepless ghosts. Food-policy aides carried sealed cups of nutrient broth and did not drink them. Two children in Puget school blue stood beside an allocation advocate, faces scrubbed, hair combed, eyes wide with rehearsed fear.

Sana saw them and understood the coming argument before anyone spoke it.

Puget would bring children.

Not because Puget children were not hungry. They were. Not because fear in the towers was fake. It was not. But someone had decided which fear was camera-ready and which fear would remain compressed in a contested packet from Amma Quill.

"Do not hate them for being afraid," Ro said quietly.

Sana looked at them.

"The children," Ro said. "Or the people who will use them. Hate wastes attention."

"What should I use instead?"

"Memory."

They entered Hearing Chamber Seven through a side door as the public feed lamps warmed overhead.

The water table had been brightened for broadcast. The channels under the glass glowed in vivid colors that made living basins look like clean design: Puget desal in electric blue, North Rain melt in white, Columbia estuary in green, dry cistern in amber, fire reservoir in ember red. It was beautiful in exactly the way Sana distrusted. Beauty made people forget pipes.

Governor Hal Renn stood at the center before his seat, hands clasped behind his back, jacket open enough to show he had not come armored. Behind him sat the two Puget children and an old woman in a tower-resident oxygen scarf. He had arranged human arithmetic by height.

Dr. Mirek Vale sat to one side with his slate dark. He did not look surprised to see Sana and Ro enter together, damp at the cuffs and carrying more silence than they had left with.

That frightened Sana more than surprise would have.

Mayor Odet was present again, ash braid newly pinned. The Meridian Food Mutual representative had moved closer to Renn. The North Rain screens flickered with a replacement witness, not Iona. Four Dry Basin seats remained empty.

Ro took their place at the table.

"This emergency assembly was reconvened without mediator consent," they said.

Clerk-Annotator Pell, a narrow man with silver eyelids and a voice trained to make theft sound like scheduling, looked up from his station. "Succession court emergency authority permits reconvening when delay presents material risk."

"Material risk to whom?"

Pell glanced toward Renn.

Ro smiled. "Thank you."

Renn stepped forward before Pell could repair the mistake. "To twenty-seven million permanent residents, four million registered recovery migrants, and the food workers currently maintaining Puget's vertical grain substrate on five days of stable reserves."

The feed lamps brightened.

There it was.

Five days, Sana thought. Six in the morning's hearing. One day had vanished in two hours because numbers knew how to dress for urgency.

Ro said, "Your petition will be entered."

"My petition must be heard."

"Those are not always the same act."

Renn turned slightly, enough to show the broadcast his profile. He was good at public grief. Not false. Good. That was worse. False grief could be punctured. Skilled grief enlisted real feeling and trained it to march.

"People in the towers woke this morning to ration uncertainty," he said. "They woke to feeds telling them seed archives are locked, dry basins are marching, insurers may withdraw backing, and mediators are debating evidentiary categories while household kitchens count substrate in grams. They do not see water tables and legal distinctions. They see breakfast becoming a vote."

One of the children behind him lowered her eyes at exactly the right moment.

Sana looked away so anger would not choose the wrong target.

Renn continued. "Puget Verticals is not asking to own the Long Orchard. We are asking for priority release of grain-stabilizing lines sufficient to prevent urban ration collapse while broader claims are reviewed. Population density is not arrogance. It is concentration of consequence."

Mayor Odet muttered, "And cameras."

Renn heard. "Fire-Buffer towns receive protection because Puget underwriting keeps burn corridors solvent."

"There it is again," Odet said. "The hand that feeds remembers the leash."

Ro tapped the table once. "Mayor."

Odet sat back, but her burned knuckles stayed white.

Vale raised his hand.

Ro looked at him. "Dr. Vale."

"Governor Renn's petition is not sufficient as written," Vale said.

Sana had expected support. So had Renn, judging by the small stillness in his shoulders.

Vale turned his slate on. The water table accepted his projection, perhaps too quickly. A model rose above the glass: tower stacks, substrate mills, fungal protein vats, desal inputs, grain stabilizer requirements, ration contraction curves. It was precise, legible, and merciless.

"Puget's five-day figure assumes full fungal mill compliance, no migrant redistribution, no hoarding acceleration, and stable worker attendance. Those assumptions are optimistic. If public fear crosses the sixteen percent panic threshold, hoarding behavior can consume two days of reserves in fourteen hours. If worker attendance drops below eighty-eight percent in substrate maintenance towers, losses compound. If insurers withdraw continuity guarantees, substrate shipments freeze not in five days but in forty-one hours."

The chamber absorbed the numbers like cold.

Renn's face recovered. "Then you agree priority release is necessary."

"I agree delay has a cost."

"Doctor."

Vale looked at him calmly. "I do not confuse a cost with a blank authorization."

For one dangerous second, Sana almost liked him.

Then he continued.

"The rational action is not Puget priority alone. It is temporary centralization of release authority under a Compact triage instrument, with Puget's grain-stabilizing lines ranked first because they support the greatest number of dependent calories per seed unit."

There it was too.

Not Renn's ownership. Vale's instrument.

Ro said, "You have not filed such an instrument."

"I can file within the hour."

"How convenient."

"Preparedness often is."

Sana felt the old verifier against her hip. The partial path record seemed to pulse in answer to Vale's word: preparedness. Someone had prepared the alarm, the field-security intrusion, the public feed, the children, the petition, the model. Everyone had arrived with paper except the people whose shelves were nearly empty.

Ro asked, "Does your model include Dry Basin hunger testimony entered earlier?"

"As uncertified qualitative pressure."

"That means no."

"It means I refuse to convert unverified household ledgers into allocation weights that may kill more people elsewhere."

Renn seized the opening. "And yet Puget's numbers are verified."

Sana said, "By whom?"

The chamber turned toward her. She had not meant to speak. Her body had become a bell someone else struck.

Ro's eyes warned her. Too late.

Renn gave her a look sharpened by broadcast. "Dr. Ilyen, are you questioning whether Puget has people?"

"I am questioning the substrate reserve assumptions. Dr. Vale just did too."

"You are a botanist."

"Grain is botanical before it becomes political."

A murmur moved through the room. The feed lamps adjusted, hungry for faces.

Renn said, "My city does not have the luxury of philosophical fieldwork."

"Neither does anyone else's hunger."

"Then support priority release before ration panic spreads."

"Priority release of what line?"

He paused.

Sana stepped closer to the table. "Which grain-stabilizing line are you requesting? Drought barley? Pear-rice? Fungal substrate wheat? North Rain old cold oats? The petition says grain-stabilizing lines. Plural. It does not name ecological fit, contamination risk, pollinator dependency, soil match, corridor burden, or replacement schedule."

Renn's advocate rose. "The technical annex contains line groups under sealed risk classification."

"Sealed by whom?"

The advocate looked to Renn, then to Vale, then to the Meridian representative.

Sana did not look at Ro. If she looked, she might stop.

"Sealed by whom?" she repeated.

The Meridian representative stood. "Several line identities are protected under continuity agreements to prevent hoarding, sabotage, or black-market targeting."

"Continuity agreements tied to delayed release instruments?" Ro asked.

Meridian's face closed.

Ro leaned forward. "The disclosure order from this morning was not decorative."

Pell interjected, "Mediator, this assembly concerns the Puget petition."

"Everything concerns the Puget petition if Puget's priority claim is entangled with proprietary instruments."

Renn pointed one controlled finger at Sana. "This is exactly the delay my residents fear. Every question becomes another corridor. Every corridor becomes another day."

"Then answer faster," Sana said.

The old woman behind Renn made a small sound. Not theatrical. Pain, perhaps, or breath.

Sana stopped.

Renn saw the stop and used it well.

"Her name is Lidia An," he said, softer. "She is eighty-nine. She lives on Tower Twelve, wet-side lower ring. Her ration plan depends on fungal protein cakes stabilized with grain substrate. Her granddaughter maintains algae scrubbers. Her grandson is one of the children behind me. Explain to her why a field auditor needs line names before breakfast."

The room waited.

Sana looked at Lidia An.

The old woman looked back with watery, intelligent eyes above the oxygen scarf. She did not look like a prop. That was the second cruelty. Props became people when looked at closely. People could still be used.

Sana said, "Because if the wrong line goes to the wrong system under fear, you can lose the next breakfast too."

Lidia An's eyes narrowed, not in anger. In appraisal.

Renn stepped in. "And if no line goes?"

"People suffer."

"People die."

"Yes."

The word landed. It did not help anyone.

Vale watched her as if adding a column to a table.

Ro spoke before Renn could. "Puget's petition is accepted for review. It is not granted."

Renn's head turned. "On what authority do you deny emergency protection?"

"I have not denied it. I am requiring that the line request be unsealed to the field auditor of record, that insurer instruments be disclosed under chamber seal, and that Dry Basin testimony be certified on the same emergency schedule as Puget density figures."

"That schedule kills time."

"So does litigation after bad release."

"Litigation?" Renn's voice rose for the first time. "Mediator, if ration contraction begins in Puget, litigation will be a luxury performed over barricades."

The child behind him flinched.

Sana saw Renn notice and hate himself for half a second before politics covered it.

That half second mattered. He was not a monster. He was a man choosing tools that made monster-shaped shadows.

The North Rain screen chimed.

Pell looked relieved to turn away. "Vault status update."

Ro said, "Proceed."

The replacement witness appeared pale and overlit. "North Rain interim security reports that Keeper Saye remains inside partitioned archive sectors. Origin data transmission has begun but is incomplete. Several historical release pathway files are locked under founder-era witness protocols."

Sana's hand tightened on her field bag.

Vale looked at her.

Too fast.

He knew, or guessed, or had been waiting to see who reacted to the phrase.

Ro asked, "Which protocols?"

"The system returns obsolete category names. Living quorum, hunger witness, field ecology countersignature."

The words entered the room like seeds dropped on stone.

Renn frowned. "What does that mean?"

No one answered immediately.

Sana could have. The verifier in her bag could have. Kade's wrong key could have opened the answer and marked everyone attached to it.

Vale said, "It means historical clutter."

Sana looked at him.

He did not look away.

"Founder-era systems retained redundant moral language," Vale continued. "Most such categories were superseded by modern liability frameworks."

"Conveniently," Sana said.

Ro's hand moved a fraction toward her. Warning, not restraint.

The North Rain witness said, "There is more. Keeper Saye attached a note to the first transmission."

Ro's voice lowered. "Read it."

The witness swallowed.

"To the chamber: If you are asking who owns the seeds, you are already asking the wrong question. Ask who must stand where the record touches soil."

The room seemed to tilt around Sana.

Renn said, "Poetry is not allocation."

Lidia An, behind him, said, "Sometimes it is instruction."

Everyone looked at her.

The old woman adjusted her oxygen scarf with one shaking hand. "I grew up before Tower Twelve had reliable substrate. My mother kept beans in the bathroom wall because official plans changed faster than dinner. When someone says wrong question, I listen."

Renn's face tightened. "Lidia."

"Don't Lidia me on public feed, Governor. You brought me."

The smoke orchard mayor barked one laugh before covering it as a cough.

Ro, to their credit, did not smile.

Vale did not either. His attention moved from Lidia An to the Puget children, then to Renn. The model above the table rotated slowly, ration curves gleaming.

"Instruction does not alter mortality curves," he said.

Lidia An looked at him. "No. But it tells you who you are willing to make into a curve."

For the first time, Vale had no immediate answer.

The victory lasted exactly four seconds.

Pell received another tab, read it, and went gray.

"Mediator," he said. "There are public gatherings forming outside Puget distribution towers. Feeds are carrying edited clips from this chamber."

Ro closed their eyes once. "Which clips?"

Pell did not want to say.

Renn did. "Dr. Ilyen saying people die if no line goes."

Sana felt the floor drop inside her.

Mayor Odet whispered, "Fast work."

Meridian's representative stared at the table.

Renn turned on him. "Did your people cut that?"

"Governor, we do not control public feeds."

"That is not an answer."

Ro stood. "Chamber recess denied. All parties remain visible. No one leaves to shape panic off-record."

"You cannot hold us," Renn said.

"I can make departure part of the record."

"People are gathering at my towers."

"Then speak to them here, on the same feed, with all claims visible."

Renn stared at Ro. "You want me to tell frightened residents they must wait."

Ro shook their head. "I want you to tell them what you are asking in their name."

The chamber fell silent.

This was the water table's old raft appearing under glass: no backs on chairs, no named seats, the river moving under every boot.

Renn looked at Lidia An. She looked back. The children watched both of them, learning something no curriculum would admit.

Finally Renn faced the feed lamps.

"Puget residents," he said, and now his voice was less trained. "I have asked this chamber for priority grain release because our density makes ration failure dangerous quickly. That fear is real. I will not soften it. But the release lines, the insurance agreements, and the archive records must be visible enough that we do not feed ourselves by making another basin disappear. I am not withdrawing the petition. I am saying you deserve the whole petition, not a slogan."

His advocates looked horrified.

Sana looked at Ro.

Ro watched Renn with cautious respect and no trust.

Outside the chamber, the court's public blue lights continued to burn.

Vale turned off his model.

The sudden absence left afterimages on the water table: towers, curves, lines, hunger arranged into architecture.

"Then file the central instrument," Ro said to him. "If that is your intended remedy, put it where everyone can see its teeth."

Vale gathered his slate. "Very well."

He looked at Sana then, not at her face but at the field bag.

"And Dr. Ilyen should disclose any archive material she has obtained outside authorized channels."

The chamber turned.

There was no gasp. Real danger rarely received one.

Sana felt the old verifier in the bag against her hip. No key. No activation scar. Enough to prove denial. Not enough to protect the saved.

Ro said, "If you are making an allegation, file it."

Vale's gaze remained on Sana. "I am making a procedural recommendation."

"File those too."

Renn's broadcast statement had bought a narrow strip of time. Vale had just spent half of it.

Sana put one hand on the field bag strap.

"I have field notes," she said. "Rootstock observations. Ledger inconsistencies. Historical questions."

"That is not an answer," Vale said.

"No," Sana said. "It is a line I can say without burning people who are not in this room."

Ro went still.

So did Vale.

The phrase had teeth she had not meant to show.

Not in this room.

The Dry Basin seats remained empty. Kade was gone under the court. Iona was inside a partitioned vault. Amma Quill's packet waited under contested evidence status. Lidia An breathed through her scarf behind a governor who had almost turned a slogan back into a responsibility.

Renn said, quietly, "Who burns if you answer?"

Sana looked at him.

For a moment the chamber held all its water.

"Everyone who ate because someone broke a rule before we were born," she said.

No one moved.

Then the North Rain screen chimed again.

The witness read the new line with visible confusion.

"Keeper Saye has transmitted a destination request. Founder pathway review requires presence of hunger council witness and field ecologist at initial soil contact. Suggested claimant witness: Amma Quill."

Amma's empty chair seemed to sharpen in the room.

Ro looked at Sana.

Sana thought of Kade's message, Lidia's beans hidden in a bathroom wall, Renn's children, Vale's curves, and the wrong key that could save people twice or condemn them late.

The chapter of delay had ended.

Now the record wanted soil.

Chapter 7: The Dry Basin Message

Amma Quill refused the court's first transport.

That was how the next hour began: not with a ruling, not with Vale's central instrument, not with Puget's unsealed grain annex, but with an old woman three hundred kilometers inland declining the fast craft sent to collect her.

The refusal arrived as a line of text on the chamber wall.

DRY BASIN HUNGER COUNCIL RESPONSE: AMMA QUILL WILL NOT TRAVEL ABOVE HER PEOPLE.

Pell read it twice as if repetition might turn it into something more administratively convenient.

Governor Renn looked toward the empty Dry Basin seats. "What does that mean?"

Mayor Odet answered before Ro could. "It means she will not ride over the road her neighbors have to walk."

Renn's face closed, then opened again with effort. He had learned something in the last ten minutes and did not yet know whether it would survive contact with his staff. "How far is she from the nearest court relay?"

Ro checked the chamber map. "South Yakima cooperative chain. If she travels by ground convoy with the hunger council witnesses, four hours to the lower basin relay, seven if road ration checks continue."

"Seven hours?" Renn said. "We do not have seven hours."

"No," Vale said. "We have less."

That was true and manipulative in equal measure. Sana felt the room bend toward him. The hunger council's dignity had become another delay in a chamber trained to punish delay.

She stepped to the water table. "Can the initial soil contact occur there?"

Ro looked at her. "At South Yakima?"

"The founder pathway request says presence of hunger council witness and field ecologist at initial soil contact. It does not say the soil has to come here."

Pell began shaking his head before she finished. "The court cannot validate a founder protocol outside controlled premises without a certified archive handler, chain-of-custody substrate container, contamination review, and jurisdictional consent from the basin registry."

"Then send them," Sana said.

"That would be irregular."

Mayor Odet snorted. "We have passed irregular and are halfway to feral."

Renn looked at Vale. Vale was watching Sana again, but not as if she were reckless. As if she had found a door he preferred remain described as a wall.

Ro placed both palms on the table. "Remote field validation is permitted under collapse-law continuity clause seven when travel to court produces claimant harm."

Pell blinked. "That clause applies to flood courts and contamination shelters."

"And hunger councils," Ro said.

"It does not say hunger councils."

"It says claimant harm. Hunger qualifies."

Pell's silver eyelids flickered. "Mediator, if this is appealed-"

"Everything here will be appealed. Put it in the record with clean grammar."

Renn's advocate leaned toward him, whispering urgently. Renn listened, then held up one hand to stop her.

"If Dr. Ilyen goes to the Dry Basin," he said, "Puget wants a witness present."

Odet said, "Of course you do."

Renn ignored her. "Not to obstruct. To see. If the record touches soil there, my residents need to know what is being weighed against them."

It was not a generous statement. It was, however, less small than it could have been.

Ro said, "Approved. One Puget observer, no release authority."

"I will send Lidia An," Renn said.

The old woman behind him looked up sharply. "You will ask Lidia An."

Renn turned. For a moment the governor vanished and a much younger man looked at an elder who had known him before he wore public grief well.

"Will you go?" he asked.

Lidia An adjusted the oxygen scarf. "Can the craft fly low?"

Ro said, "Yes."

"Then I will see the road."

Vale's mouth tightened. "This risks turning a technical validation into theater."

Amma Quill's empty chair seemed to answer him by remaining empty.

Sana said, "It already is theater. We are deciding who gets to stand onstage."

Vale looked at her. "And you think that improves truth?"

"No. I think hiding the stage worsens lies."

Ro cut in before the exchange could sharpen. "Field party: Dr. Sana Ilyen, field ecologist of record; Amma Quill, Dry Basin hunger council witness; one Puget observer, Lidia An; one court relay officer; one archive handler if North Rain can provide one remotely; and myself."

"You?" Pell said.

"Yes."

"Mediator, the chamber-"

"Will remain convened under Senior Clerk Omari. Dr. Vale may file his central instrument where everyone can see its teeth."

Vale inclined his head. "I will."

Sana did not like the calm of that promise.

The court craft waited on the lower dock, not one of the sleek delegation fliers but a basin relay skimmer built for bad weather, low altitude, and landing where no one had polished the ground. Its belly bore old scratches from reed beds and gravel. Its side carried the Columbia Estuary mark: a salmon, a rain line, a scale.

Sana boarded with the field bag pressed to her ribs.

Ro sat opposite her, already reading three filings on a slate while fastening the harness one-handed. Lidia An climbed aboard with help from no one, though she accepted a hand from the pilot when the step proved higher than politics. She had changed nothing except adding a knitted cap under her oxygen line. It was green and blue, tower colors, hand-mended.

"You are staring," she told Sana.

"Sorry."

"No you are not. You are cataloging."

Sana looked down. "Maybe."

"Botanists."

Ro did not look up. "She does it to people too."

"Mediators do not?"

"We pretend it is listening."

Lidia made a dry sound that might have been a laugh if her lungs had granted the luxury.

The skimmer lifted.

At first Cascadia looked like the recovery posters promised: wetland geometry, salmon channels shining under broken cloud, vertical farms rising in green stacks along the estuary, roofs furred with moss and solar skin. Then the craft turned inland and the repair became less complete. Fire scars crossed hillsides in black combs. Reservoirs sat below their old mineral rings. Orchard corridors narrowed. The color changed from deep green to gray green to the tired yellow of land that had been asked to forgive too much.

Lidia watched through the window.

"I have not left Puget in twelve years," she said.

Ro lowered the slate. "Why now?"

"Because Hal Renn brought me to frighten people and accidentally reminded me I am old enough to disobey."

Sana smiled despite herself.

Lidia caught it. "Do not make me charming in your notes."

"I do not write charming notes."

"Good. Write this. Puget is afraid because density is a stomach with many mouths and few cupboards. That is true. Also write that towers teach people to think height is innocence. We stack ourselves and call the ground someone else's problem."

Sana opened her field notebook.

"You meant that literally?" Lidia asked.

"Yes."

"Good."

Ro's slate chimed. They read, frowned, and angled it so Sana could see without Lidia needing to pretend not to read upside down.

VALE CENTRAL TRIAGE INSTRUMENT FILED.

Attached below were four pages of emergency authority language, risk categories, calorie preservation tables, and abandonment without the word abandonment. Vale had not hidden the teeth. He had filed a jaw.

"He is moving fast," Sana said.

"He has been moving for months," Ro answered. "We are just now seeing the motion."

Lidia leaned back. "Does his model say what happens to people who are not worth their seed?"

Ro was quiet.

"It uses different words," Sana said.

"They always do."

The skimmer crossed the firebreak towns. Smoke-resistant orchards formed deliberate bands across the slopes, rows of low trees with silver leaves and black trunks bred to char without dying. Crew yards flashed below: water bladders, drone nests, tool sheds, community kitchens. People looked up as the court craft passed. Some waved. Some did not.

Beyond the last firebreak, the Dry Basin opened.

It was not dry in the way people from wet cities imagined dryness. Not empty. Not dead. It held work everywhere: cistern fields covered with shade mesh, narrow orchards protected by wind walls, algae trenches shining dark behind cracked berms, grain trial plots no wider than rooms, solar stills, salvage barns, family gardens planted in half-buried containers. Every green thing looked chosen. Every shadow looked budgeted.

The road beneath them was lined with people walking.

Not a march yet. Not quite. Families, carts, bicycles, two wheeled medical chairs, a herd of goats, teenagers carrying tool rolls, elders under reflective cloth. They moved in clusters along the road toward the lower basin relay. Some carried signs. Most carried water.

Lidia put one hand to the window.

"Low enough," she said.

The pilot glanced back. Ro nodded.

The craft descended until the road filled the windows. Dust lifted beneath them. People shielded their eyes and looked up. Sana saw faces change as they read the court mark. Hope, anger, calculation, exhaustion, and the flat refusal to be impressed.

They landed in a cooperative yard beside a cistern painted with a red handprint and the words COUNT US BEFORE YOU CUT US.

Amma Quill was waiting.

In person she seemed both smaller and more difficult to move than her recording. The red cloth was tied around her gray hair. Her boots were cracked white with alkali dust. She carried the paper ledger under one arm and a metal lunch tin under the other.

She looked first at Sana, then Ro, then Lidia.

"Puget sent a grandmother," Amma said.

Lidia climbed down carefully. "Puget sent a warning and got me instead."

Amma considered her. "Can you stand?"

"Long enough."

"Good. We are tired of speaking to chairs."

Ro stepped forward. "Amma Quill, I am Ro Tamsin, Columbia Estuary mediator. This field validation is convened under-"

Amma lifted one hand.

"No."

Ro stopped.

"You can say all that after we show you the shelves," Amma said. "If law must be present, it can walk hungry first."

Pell would have objected from three hundred kilometers away if objection could travel faster than shame.

Ro said, "Lead."

They walked.

The cooperative yard opened into a row of storage houses built from old rail containers insulated with straw panels and ceramic paint. Amma opened the first. Cool air drifted out, smelling of dust, beans, and metal. Inside, shelves had labels as precise as any archive: household number, allergy restrictions, work capacity, infants, elders, seed obligation, ration status. The jars Sana had seen in the recording were here multiplied by hundreds. Most were not empty. That was another truth. Hunger did not always look like absence. Sometimes it looked like measured presence that would not last.

Amma pointed to the first shelf. "Household nine. Fungal allergy. Puget asks grain for fungal substrate. We ask what happens when the substitute is the thing that closes a throat."

Lidia moved closer. Her oxygen scarf whispered.

Amma pointed again. "Household twelve. Twins below growth line. They are not dying today, which is how courts decide they can wait. Household eighteen. Heat injury and pregnancy. If rootstock is delayed, their trial plot misses soil window by six days. The model will call that deferred production. We call it next winter."

Sana wrote until her hand hurt.

Amma turned on her. "Do not just write."

Sana lowered the pen.

"Tell me what your field eyes see."

Sana looked.

At first she saw ration shelves. Then seed obligation tags. Then dust seals not properly seated because hands had opened them too often. Then a pattern of substitution: bean flour replacing fungal cake in allergic households, dried apple rations thinned with mineral water, seed jars marked not food, not yet, not unless vote. She saw a ledger of restraint. People had not eaten what they might need to plant. Hunger had been made to hold still for future hunger.

"You are preserving seed at the cost of current calories," she said.

"Yes."

"Because if you eat the trial stock, you survive a week and lose a season."

"Yes."

"Your certified hunger measurements miss that."

"Yes."

Amma's face did not soften, but something in the room did. Lidia leaned on a shelf and closed her eyes.

Ro said quietly, "This enters the field record."

Amma looked at them. "Everything enters records. That is not the same as mattering."

"No," Ro said. "It is not."

The lunch tin under Amma's arm clicked as she shifted it.

Sana noticed. "What is in the tin?"

Amma did not answer immediately. She led them out of the storage house and across the yard to a plot no larger than a hearing chamber. Wind walls made from old solar frames surrounded it. The soil inside was dark, almost black, protected beneath a thin cover of chopped straw. Stakes marked rows that had not yet been planted.

Three children stood at the far edge with an adult who might have been their teacher. They were not polished for broadcast. One had dust on their cheek. One held a broken drone wing. One was chewing a strip of dried kelp with solemn concentration.

"This is claimant soil," Amma said. "South Yakima cooperative trial plot A-seventeen. It has been ready twelve days."

Sana knelt.

She forgot the court for three breaths.

The soil was not good in any simple way. It was engineered patience: composted orchard waste, fungal binder, mineral amendments, char, old river silt transported at absurd cost, microbiome cultures coaxed through heat waves, and dry basin grit that would never become valley loam no matter how many committees wanted metaphors. She took a sample between gloved fingers. It held shape, then broke cleanly.

"Moisture?"

Amma nodded to the teacher. The child with the drone wing ran to a shade box and brought back a sensor.

Sana inserted it. "Eleven point four."

"Need?"

"For pear-rice rootstock? Ten to twelve for initial contact if wind stays below threshold."

Amma looked at Ro. "Wind?"

Ro checked the relay. "Within threshold for thirty hours."

"And after?"

"Maybe not."

Amma opened the lunch tin.

Inside lay three sealed envelopes, each with a red handprint and a household number. Not seeds. Statements.

"Hunger council witness," Amma said. "You asked for me. I brought those who cannot leave storage duty, water duty, fever duty."

Ro activated the field relay. A thin column of court light rose above the plot, flickered, and steadied. Hearing Chamber Seven appeared in miniature: Renn, Vale, Odet, the North Rain screen, empty seats now filled by the image of dry soil. The public feed remained active unless someone had found a legal way to blink.

Amma faced the relay.

"My name is Amma Quill. I stand on South Yakima soil. I am not emotional format. I am not uncertified pressure. I am the person you named because the old record remembered hunger has a witness."

No one in the projected chamber interrupted.

Amma opened the first envelope.

"Household nine says: do not send us protein that closes Elder Ren's throat and call it equal calories. If grain goes first to fungal substrate, name the allergic before you count them fed."

She opened the second.

"Household twelve says: twins do not wait in percentages. Growth lines do not pause while models argue scale."

She opened the third.

Her hand shook once. Only once.

"Household eighteen says: we saved seed from our own mouths because you told us planting was survival. If the release misses this soil window, do not call us low yield. Call us delayed by name."

The relay light buzzed in the wind.

Sana felt the field bag at her side. The old verifier seemed heavier beside the living soil. Kade had brought a path image. Iona had sent a destination request. Amma had brought names.

Ro said, "Dr. Ilyen, field assessment."

Sana looked at the plot, the shelves, the road beyond the yard where people still moved toward the relay because they had not yet heard whether anyone had listened.

"Soil is ready for initial pear-rice rootstock contact," she said. "Moisture within threshold. Wind within threshold for approximately thirty hours. Delay beyond that risks loss of planting window. Substitution with grain-stabilized fungal protein does not meet household-specific needs where fungal allergy exists. Certified hunger metrics presented to the chamber are incomplete because they fail to count preserved seed restraint, soil-window expiration, and allergy-limited substitution."

Vale's miniature image spoke. "Does the field ecologist recommend release of the contested line?"

There it was. The trap shaped like urgency.

Sana could say yes and make herself into the door Vale wanted: release under pressure, then centralize because everything had become too dangerous. She could say no and turn Amma's testimony into another beautiful delay. She could say technical words until the soil window closed politely.

Amma looked at her but did not plead.

Lidia An stood beside the plot, breathing through her scarf, Puget blue cap bright against alkali dust.

Sana said, "I recommend immediate disclosure of line identity, ecological fit data, and liability instruments for any release proposed to this soil. I recommend provisional preparation for initial contact under hunger council witness. I do not recommend blind release under sealed terms."

Vale's expression did not change.

Renn's did. Frustration, then comprehension, then the beginning of a headache that might become conscience if fed correctly.

"How long to unseal?" Renn asked.

Ro looked at him through the relay. "If no one obstructs? Minutes."

Every face turned toward Meridian Food Mutual.

The representative looked as if the dry basin had reached through the light and put dust on his shoes.

Amma closed the lunch tin.

"You hear that?" she asked the projected chamber. "Minutes. Remember that word when someone tells you we waited years because hunger is complicated."

The child with the broken drone wing whispered to the teacher, too loudly, "Are they going to send seeds?"

No adult answered quickly enough.

Lidia An did.

"They are going to stop hiding the question first."

The child considered this and found it inadequate. "That is not seeds."

"No," Lidia said. "But sometimes it is the lock before seeds."

Sana looked at Ro.

Ro's eyes were on the road.

At the edge of the cooperative yard, a second group of walkers had stopped. They were listening to portable speakers carrying the public feed. Beyond them more people came, not rushing, not yet, but gathering around the possibility that the court had finally been made to look at ground.

Then the field relay chimed.

North Rain's screen cut through the chamber projection, unstable with distance and archive interference.

Iona Saye appeared for the first time since leaving the court. Her hair had come loose. One cheek was scratched. Behind her, frost fogged metal racks and red archive lights turned the vault into a winter wound.

"Dr. Ilyen," she said. "Do you stand with hunger witness on claimant soil?"

Sana stood.

"Yes."

"Does the soil meet initial contact conditions?"

"Yes."

"Does the chamber hear names?"

Sana looked at Amma.

Amma stepped forward. "It does if it wants to keep pretending it is a chamber."

Iona smiled then, fierce and brief.

"Good. North Rain will transmit the first sealed line identity under founder safeguard."

Vale stood in the chamber projection. "Keeper Saye, you do not have authority to bypass modern liability review."

Iona looked at him as if he were a document placed in the wrong folder.

"Doctor, I am not bypassing it. I am making it stand where the record touches soil."

The transmission fractured.

For a heartbeat, the relay filled with snow.

Then a line of text appeared over the dry plot.

PEAR-RICE ROOTSTOCK LINE NR-77A / HISTORICAL PATHWAY FLAG / LIABILITY TRANSFER REFUSED / HUNGER WITNESS REQUIRED / FIELD ECOLOGIST REQUIRED / RELEASE STATUS: WITHHELD PENDING HUMAN SIGNATURE.

Human signature.

Sana laughed once, not because it was funny but because the archive had reduced all their law to the one thing no one could automate without lying.

Amma held out the lunch tin. "Use the lid."

"For what?"

"People sign better on things that carried food."

Ro, from beside the relay, said, "No signature yet. We need the liability instruments unsealed."

Amma did not lower the tin. "Then make them hurry."

Back in the projected chamber, Renn turned to Meridian.

"Unseal them."

The representative opened his mouth.

Renn's voice hardened. "Puget wants grain. Puget also wants to know whether it has been made into your shield. Unseal them."

Mayor Odet leaned back with a look of grim satisfaction.

Vale watched the dry soil.

Sana could not read his face now. Perhaps the model was recalculating. Perhaps the man was.

The field relay recorded everything: the dust, the shelves, the old woman from Puget, the hunger council ledger, the field ecologist's gloved hands, the seed line still withheld, the human signature not yet given.

For the first time since the vault locked, delay had a place, a clock, and witnesses who could name what it cost.

Chapter 8: Pollinators Down

The liability instruments did not unseal quickly.

They unsealed like a door opened by someone counting witnesses in the hall: first the index, then the harmless definitions, then a page of exceptions so dense with cross-reference that Ro laughed once without humor and said, over the relay, "Meridian has mistaken fog for strategy."

The food insurer's representative objected from the projected chamber. Ro ignored him with the full force of formal attention. Governor Renn stayed standing beside the water table, hands clasped so tightly behind his back that Sana wondered whether Lidia An had taught him restraint by leaving the tower. Vale read every line as it appeared, face calm, eyes moving fast.

In the cooperative yard, wind worried the straw cover on plot A-seventeen.

Amma Quill kept the lunch tin in both hands.

"Minutes," she said.

No one answered.

The first meaningful page finally arrived under seal: continuity agreement NR-77A, Meridian Food Mutual, Puget Verticals, North Rain archive escrow, historic release exception suspended pending risk assignment. Sana scanned the language and felt the shape of it before she understood each legal tooth. If the line failed in Dry Basin soil, no insurer carried yield liability. If it succeeded, derivative access could be claimed for high-density substrate systems under emergency continuity. If it moved without liability transfer, the receiving basin accepted contamination, failure, and downstream claims.

"It makes success rentable and failure local," she said.

Ro looked at her. "That is a clean field translation."

Amma's face did not change. "Say it less clean."

Sana looked at the shelves behind them, the road beyond, the plot ready for seed contact.

"If the crop fails here, you own the loss. If it works here, others can claim the benefit. If it moves before they bless it, they can call you reckless."

Amma nodded as if confirming weather.

Lidia An said, "That is not insurance. That is a hook."

"Yes," Ro said.

From the chamber projection, Meridian's representative said, "The agreement reflects extraordinary actuarial exposure under contested ecological conditions."

Amma lifted the lunch tin lid. "Put that on a ration shelf and see if it feeds anyone."

The representative's mouth closed.

Sana might have signed then anyway. That frightened her later. The soil window was real. The household statements were real. The old record had placed her where the decision touched ground, and every part of her trained body wanted to move the living thing before the law killed it by waiting. She reached for the field stylus.

The sky above the cooperative yard clicked.

Once. Twice. Then a stutter of small rotors.

Everyone looked up.

Pollinator drones came over the wind wall in a broken flock.

At first Sana thought they were birds, because fear made old categories first. Then the machines resolved: palm-sized hex-wing drones with pollen baskets, orchard-yellow bodies, and black guidance eyes. They should have flown in a low patterned drift, each unit holding distance from the next to mimic bee scatter without crowding living pollinators. Instead they lurched. Two collided above the plot and fell spinning into the straw. Another struck the wind wall, corrected too late, and scraped along the frame in a shriek of plastic and metal. A fourth dropped straight down, rotors still beating, and buried itself half a finger deep in the prepared soil.

The child with the broken drone wing shouted, "Down!"

The teacher dragged the children back. Amma closed the lunch tin with a snap and stood in front of the plot as if her body could shelter soil from machinery.

Sana was already moving.

"Do not touch the embedded unit," she said. "Power field?"

"Low," the child said before any adult answered. "They are orchard class, not spray class."

Sana looked at him.

He held up the broken wing. "I fix them."

Another drone came over the wall, wobbling toward Lidia. Ro caught the old woman by the elbow and pulled her aside. The drone struck the ground where her foot had been, bounced, and lay twitching in the dust.

From beyond the wind wall came a voice, young and furious.

"Cut local beacon! Cut it now!"

A girl vaulted the frame without waiting for the gate.

She landed hard, rolled, came up with a tool loop already in one hand. She was perhaps nineteen or twenty, narrow-shouldered, brown-skinned, hair shaved on one side and braided tight on the other with copper wire and tiny blue feathers. Smoke dust marked her jacket. A patch on the sleeve showed a stylized moth over crossed firebreak tools.

Tali Moss, Sana thought before anyone said the name. The outline of the crisis had been waiting for her: fire-buffer mechanic, pollinator systems, generation raised inside recovery rather than collapse. In person she looked less like a role and more like a thrown tool.

"Beacon!" Tali shouted again.

The child with the broken wing sprinted to the shade box and slapped a switch. The damaged drones dropped as if their strings had been cut. Three hit the soil. One fell into Amma's shoulder and bounced off the tin. Silence came after them in pieces, rotor whine dying one motor at a time.

Tali saw the field relay, the projected chamber, Ro's sash, Lidia's oxygen scarf, and Sana kneeling beside a drone.

"Oh," she said. "Of course the court is watching the one time they fall on important people."

Ro said, "Identify yourself for the record."

"Tali Moss, Firebreak Seven pollinator mechanic, temporary contractor to South Yakima cooperative chain, unpaid since winter, which is also for the record."

Mayor Odet's projected image straightened in the chamber. "Tali."

"Mayor," Tali said without looking away from the drones. "Your corridor is being cut."

The chamber shifted.

Sana held still.

Tali dropped beside the drone embedded in the plot. "Did anyone touch this?"

"No," Sana said.

"Good. Soil contact?"

"Tip of left basket and lower rotor housing."

"Contamination?"

"Unknown. Orchard class?"

"Supposed to be. Unless someone swapped reservoirs. You have a field sniffer?"

Sana opened her kit and handed it over.

Tali took it with the ease of someone who believed tools belonged to whoever could use them fastest. She scanned the broken drone's pollen basket, rotor housing, and cracked guidance eye. The sniffer gave three green lights, then one amber.

"No spray toxin. Trace fungal attractant. Not enough to matter unless someone wants a false flag later."

Vale's projected voice entered. "A false flag of what kind?"

Tali finally looked at the chamber image. "Oh good. A calm man."

Ro said, "Answer technically."

"Fine. If you find fungal trace in a pollinator crash on a pear-rice trial plot after a blight lock, you can say uncontrolled cross-contact, suspend soil validation, and delay release until everyone forgets who was hungry first."

Sana felt cold rise from the prepared soil.

Amma said, "Was this accident?"

Tali did not answer quickly. She collected two drones, removed their guidance chips with a thumb tool, and held the chips up to the light. Then she whistled once through her teeth, not surprise, recognition.

"No."

The single word changed the yard.

Ro stepped closer. "Basis."

"These units are from Firebreak Seven corridor swarm. They should not be here during this wind window. Their pollination route is plum-ash buffer, upper slope, then Moss Corridor handoff. South Yakima trial plot is manual witness zone today. No autonomous pollinator traffic within fifty meters until field ecologist clears contact."

"Could wind have pushed them?"

"Wind can push a drone into a wall. It does not rewrite its route table."

Tali plugged one chip into her wrist reader. The device projected a jagged line of coordinates above her hand. She pulled up another. Then another. Five lines appeared. Each began in a different safe route and then bent sharply toward the same narrow band.

Sana leaned in. "That band is not this plot."

"No." Tali expanded the map. "This plot is where the broken units landed after losing correction. The target is here."

She marked a corridor running southwest to northeast, a thin living strand through the basin: wind walls, orchard strips, insect refuge boxes, drone charging posts, and a road of blooming cover crop that connected Firebreak Seven to South Yakima and then onward toward the Moss Corridor.

"Pollinator corridor," Sana said.

"The pollinator corridor," Tali said. "For NR-77A compatibility. Pear-rice can self-set poorly if stressed, but viable release needs corridor support in weeks two through five. If this strand is marked unstable, line release to this basin becomes ecologically irresponsible."

Ro looked toward the projected chamber. "And if release is ecologically irresponsible?"

Vale answered before anyone else. "The line would be redirected to a controlled substrate environment."

"Puget," Amma said.

Renn's face tightened. "Not by my order."

No one had accused him. That made the denial worse and more honest.

Tali laughed once, sharp. "Orders are for people who want signatures. This is route math."

Sana asked, "Show me the failure pattern."

Tali's eyes flicked to her. "You are the field ecologist?"

"Yes."

"Then keep up."

She walked fast out of the plot and through the cooperative yard, taking the broken drone wing from the child as she passed. "Borrowing."

"It is mine," he protested.

"Then invoice me when grown people stop breaking everything."

The child seemed satisfied by this as a category of promise.

They followed Tali beyond the cistern and storage houses to the corridor edge. Up close, the pollinator strand was not a neat line of flowers. It was infrastructure disguised as habitat: native shrubs, pear-rice trial blossoms in protected pockets, insect hotels made from drilled orchard wood, drone perches, soil moisture nodes, tiny weather vanes, ceramic bowls of mineral water, and mesh tunnels that let small living pollinators cross open dust without becoming bird food. It smelled faintly of nectar, hot plastic, and dry leaves.

Three more drones lay dead among the shrubs.

Tali crouched beside the first. "Guidance eye burned. Not power failure." She moved to the second. "Route table overwritten." Third. "Beacon spoof."

Sana knelt beside a refuge box. Living bees moved inside, slow from heat but alive. She let out a breath she had not realized she held.

"Living pollinators unaffected so far."

"So far," Tali said. "But if drones crash into refuge boxes, living pollinators get blamed for pathogen movement or corridor instability. Then someone says close the strand for everyone's safety."

Ro said, "Who can spoof the beacon?"

Tali gave them a look. "Anyone with corridor credentials, a stolen maintenance seed, or a purchased override from someone who should be deeply ashamed."

"Narrow it."

"I am trying."

She opened a charging post. Inside, wires were labeled in a hand so neat it had to be hers. She touched two contacts with a tester. The post chirped. Her face went very still.

"This was updated remotely."

Sana looked down the corridor, toward the road where people still listened to the feed.

"From where?"

"That is the rude part."

Tali connected her wrist reader to the post. The first result appeared as a string of authorization marks. Firebreak Seven. Moss Corridor. Meridian continuity maintenance. Puget substrate emergency routing. A token nested inside a token nested inside a token.

Ro swore softly.

Tali pointed at the nested token. "This one issued the route correction."

Renn's projected image, still visible in the field relay Tali had dragged along by accident or design, stepped forward. "That is a Puget emergency routing token."

"Yes," Tali said.

"Puget did not authorize sabotage."

"Then someone borrowed your fear with a very official cup."

Renn looked at Vale.

Vale did not return the look. He was reading the token chain.

Sana watched him instead of the map and saw, for the first time, not calculation but anger. Controlled, buried, quickly translated into thought, but anger. Either the sabotage was not his, or he was a better actor than she wanted him to be.

Tali pulled the connector free. "If I publish this raw, Puget residents will think Dry Basin mechanics are accusing them. Dry Basin people will think Puget tried to starve them. Firebreak crews will lock their corridor tools. Meridian will say token theft. Vale's people will say centralization would have prevented credential misuse. Everyone gets exactly the fight someone wants."

Ro said, "You have practiced that speech."

"I have lived that speech. Every time a drone falls, someone far away discovers a reason to own the route."

Amma had followed more slowly with Lidia. She reached the charging post as Tali closed it.

"Can the corridor work?"

Tali looked at her, and the sharpness left her face just enough to show the young woman underneath. "If we quarantine the bad route tables, switch to hand-confirmed beacon pulses, and keep living refuge boxes open, yes. But I need crews and I need no one to call it contaminated because a drone kissed the dirt wrong."

"How many crews?"

"Four for the strand today. Twelve for the full Moss handoff by tomorrow. More if someone keeps playing games."

Mayor Odet's projected voice came through the relay. "You have six from Firebreak Seven."

Tali turned. "Mayor, if you had six, why am I unpaid since winter?"

Odet grimaced. "You have six and back pay."

"Witnessed," Ro said.

Tali pointed at the projection. "I want that in writing before the old people start being noble with my labor again."

Lidia An said, "Good girl."

Tali looked offended. Then uncertain. Then too busy to decide.

Sana touched one of the damaged drones with a probe. "This fungal attractant trace. Can it affect the blight flag?"

"Not biologically. Politically, absolutely."

"If someone samples this crash and connects it to NR-77A?"

"They can argue corridor contamination. Not prove it. Argue. Sometimes that is cheaper."

Sana stood.

The field relay carried the corridor map, the token chain, the dead drones, the living refuge boxes, and Tali Moss's dirty hands to the chamber. The water table there must have been showing all of it now: not hunger as a curve, not density as a slogan, not seeds as property, but a corridor thin enough that a few corrupted drones could turn ecology into accusation.

Vale spoke at last.

"Mechanic Moss, preserve the token chain. Do not transmit raw credentials over public feed."

Tali gave him a flat look. "I know."

"Send sealed copies to the mediator, the field ecologist, and-" He paused. "And Governor Renn."

Renn's head moved slightly. Surprise, this time real.

Ro noticed too. "Not to your office, Dr. Vale?"

"My office can subpoena it. It should not receive it first."

Sana did not know what to do with that.

Amma did. "A man can move one stone out of a road and still own the quarry."

Vale inclined his head. "Yes."

The answer was too honest to be comfortable.

Tali was already sending sealed packets. "Done. Now I need authority to ground all autonomous pollinator traffic from Firebreak Seven through Moss Corridor until route tables are hand-checked."

Ro looked to Odet.

Odet said, "Granted."

Renn said, "Puget will not contest."

Meridian's representative began, "Continuity agreements-"

Renn turned. "Will adapt."

The representative stopped.

Sana looked back toward plot A-seventeen. The soil waited under straw. The seed line waited under human signature. Now the corridor waited too.

Every living thing in the Long Orchard seemed to require not one permission but a chain of people willing to stand where their part touched the next.

Tali followed her gaze. "If you sign without corridor support, NR-77A limps."

"If I do not sign before wind shifts, it misses."

"Yes."

"How long to quarantine?"

Tali looked at the corridor, the dead drones, the refuge boxes, and the road of waiting people.

"Give me two hours."

Amma said, "You have one."

Tali's jaw set.

"Then stop talking to me."

She ran.

Not away from the court, not away from blame, but down the corridor with the broken wing in one hand and the route table in the other, shouting names of crew members before anyone had officially assigned them. The child with the drone wing ran after her until the teacher caught his shirt. Firebreak workers on the relay began calling in. A woman by the cistern opened a tool shed. Two teenagers abandoned the road line and sprinted toward the charging posts.

The corridor, which had looked like habitat, revealed itself as community under repair.

Sana returned to the plot.

The lunch tin lid waited in Amma's hand.

"Not yet," Amma said before Sana could speak. "I heard."

Lidia An stood beside her. Dust had settled on the Puget blue cap. "In towers, we think food comes from systems. Out here systems keep turning into people."

"They always were people," Amma said.

"Yes," Lidia said. "We designed the elevators to forget."

Above the field relay, the chamber projection flickered. Renn spoke quietly to someone off feed. Vale read the token chain again. Ro stood between worlds, one hand near the relay controls, the other near the soil as if mediation had become a physical posture.

Sana looked at the prepared rows and imagined roots entering them, then blossoms needing drones, bees, refuge boxes, corridors, crews, honest beacons, unsealed agreements, and names that did not disappear when translated into risk.

The seed was not a thing.

It was a demand for a world competent enough to receive it.

At the far end of the pollinator strand, Tali's voice carried back on the wind.

"Beacon one clean! Beacon two lying! Who installed this trash?"

No one laughed.

But several people moved faster.

Chapter 9: False Blight

The first clean beacon took twenty-three minutes.

Tali announced it by climbing halfway up a wind wall, bracing one boot against an old solar frame, and shouting down the corridor, "If anyone touches beacon one before I say so, I will teach your tools to hate you."

No one touched beacon one.

Sana used those twenty-three minutes to make the blight flag smaller.

Not less dangerous. Smaller. That was the discipline. Panic made a thing expand until it filled every category that might justify fear: blight became contamination, contamination became corridor failure, corridor failure became irresponsible release, irresponsible release became centralization, centralization became the only adult voice in a room crowded with hungry people. Sana knelt beside plot A-seventeen with her field kit spread on a sterile cloth and forced the chain backward, link by link, until the word blight had to return to biology.

The North Rain transmission had sent the flag packet under archive seal. It was not complete, and the missing parts had teeth. But the portion Sana could see contained enough to begin: pathogen class, sampling time, archive sector, host line, containment reason, sensor chain, and provenance context.

She read the host line three times.

NR-77A PEAR-RICE ROOTSTOCK / COLD MOUNTAIN RESERVE / PRE-RELEASE PATHOGEN HOLD.

Then she read the pathogen class.

FUSARIUM-LIKE WILT COMPLEX / HEAT-BASIN VARIANT / VIABILITY LOW.

"That is wrong," she said.

Ro looked over from the relay. "Useful wrong or ordinary wrong?"

"Useful."

Amma crouched opposite her. "Speak basin."

Sana turned the slate so Amma could see the packet. "The blight flag says NR-77A carries a wilt variant associated with heat-basin collapse fields. But NR-77A was stored in cold mountain reserve context. Pear-rice rootstock can carry fungal companions, but this variant prefers stressed heat soil and damaged irrigation tissue. It is not impossible. It is just not where I would expect it first."

Lidia An stood nearby, one hand on her oxygen scarf. "Could it have traveled?"

"Yes. Pathogens travel. People travel. Tools travel. That is why suspicion is not proof."

Tali shouted from the corridor, "Beacon two is still lying!"

Sana did not look up. "But context matters. A real pathogen in the wrong context can trigger the same lock as a real outbreak."

Ro's face sharpened. "A placed sample."

"Maybe."
