Vale's projected image spoke from the relay. "Dr. Ilyen, be precise. A false context is not the same as a false pathogen."

"I know."

"Then say that for the record."

Sana looked at him. The request was correct. That made it annoying.

"For the record," she said, "I am not claiming the pathogen sample is fake. I am claiming the archive context may be false or incomplete. The sample could be real and still be misused."

Vale nodded once.

Renn said, "Does that mean the vault lock was wrong?"

"It means the lock may have done exactly what it was designed to do after being given the wrong story."

Amma gave a low sound. "Like courts."

Ro said, "Noted."

"I was not filing," Amma said.

"Still noted."

Sana enlarged the sensor chain. Three sample events appeared. The first was old: a heat-basin wilt reference from a collapse-era archive, recovered from a drowned corporate agronomy server. The second was recent: trace match in North Rain quarantine sector. The third was the trigger: automated association between the trace match and NR-77A pre-release hold.

The shape was too neat.

"I need the physical sample metadata," she said.

Iona's image flickered into the relay, weaker than before. "Working on it."

The old keeper looked worse. Frost had gathered on one sleeve. Behind her, red archive lights strobed at long intervals.

Ro leaned toward the projection. "Keeper Saye, status."

"Annoyed."

"Containment status."

"Also annoyed. No breach. Security has learned three new ways to fail at politely detaining me."

Lidia smiled despite the oxygen scarf.

Iona's eyes moved to Sana. "I can transmit sample metadata, but not full chain-of-custody. It has been partitioned under emergency insurer review."

Meridian's representative said, "Those partitions protect liability integrity."

Iona did not look at him. "A phrase no seed has ever spoken."

Ro said, "Transmit what you can."

The relay filled with a new table. Sana pulled it into her slate and read fast.

SAMPLE NR-Q-4431
PHYSICAL FORM: ROOT TISSUE SCRAPE
HOST CLAIM: NR-77A RESERVE GRAFT
COLLECTION SITE: NORTH RAIN QUARANTINE BAY 4
COLLECTOR: AUTOMATED ARM NR-Q4-C
WITNESS: PENDING
TRANSFER PATH: INTERNAL
ASSOCIATED REFERENCE: HB-WILT-2319-C

Sana stopped.

"What?" Ro asked.

"The reference."

"HB-wilt," Ro read. "Heat-basin wilt?"

"Twenty-three nineteen."

Amma's hand tightened on the lunch tin.

Kade's wrong key had shown a North Rain to South Yakima emergency release pathway in 2319. The official ledger denied it. The reference pathogen tied to the current lock was from 2319. A year could be coincidence. In archives, coincidence was often a drawer someone had labeled later.

Sana opened her own field notes from the verifier image Kade had given her. She did not transmit it. Not yet. She looked only at the path map, the witness marks, the liability transfer refused field.

Firebreak Seven. Moss Corridor. Orchard monastery. South Yakima trial plots.

She turned back to the sample metadata. Heat-basin wilt reference. Drowned corporate agronomy server. Collection site: North Rain quarantine bay 4. Host claim: NR-77A reserve graft.

"I need a tissue morphology image," she said.

Iona answered, "Sending."

The image arrived in fragments, then resolved: root tissue under magnification, pale vascular strands, fungal staining, cell collapse patterns.

Sana forgot the relay, the court, the road, the waiting signature.

The tissue was wrong.

Not diseased wrong. Identity wrong.

"This is not NR-77A rootstock."

The chamber projection went silent.

Vale's voice came carefully. "Basis."

"Vascular ring spacing. NR-77A pear-rice rootstock has a tighter alternating ring pattern from the cold reserve adaptation. This tissue shows wide heat-stress lignification. Also the root hair density is wrong. This is from a basin trial descendant or a heat-adapted reference line, not cold mountain reserve graft."

Renn said, "Could storage conditions change that?"

"Not enough."

Meridian's representative said, "Dr. Ilyen is making a visual assessment under field conditions."

Sana looked at Ro. "I need comparison images from North Rain NR-77A reserve and South Yakima descendant stock."

Iona said, "Reserve image available. South Yakima?"

Amma stood. "We have root scrapings."

Sana turned. "From what?"

"Old illegal children," Amma said.

No one needed translation. The descendants of the 2319 pathway, the plants the official ledger said could not exist, had roots in South Yakima soil.

Amma led Sana to a shade cabinet beside the trial plot. Inside were sample sleeves, old labels, local seed records, and small packets of dried root tissue. Not archive-clean. Not court-approved. But kept with reverence and use.

"Kade's people brought some," Amma said. "Mara Venn's people. Our people. Depends who was making the warrant."

Sana selected a sleeve marked PEAR-RICE / SOUTH YAKIMA / OLD NORTH LINE / DO NOT EAT SEED STOCK. She prepared a quick mount with shaking hands.

Tali ran past the plot carrying a beacon core. "Beacon two lies because someone taught it Puget time! Also I need wire!"

"Tool shed!" Amma shouted back.

"The good wire!"

"Then say good wire!"

The exchange steadied Sana more than calm would have. People yelling about wire meant the world still contained tasks smaller than war.

She placed the South Yakima root sample under the field scope.

The image bloomed.

Wide heat-stress lignification. Similar root hair density. Similar vascular spacing. Not identical, because years of basin adaptation had written their own history, but close enough to make the North Rain sample's host claim look like a lie standing in borrowed clothes.

Sana split the relay feed: North Rain alleged NR-77A infected tissue on one side, verified NR-77A reserve comparison from Iona on the second, South Yakima old north descendant on the third.

"There," she said.

Ro leaned in. "Say it."

"The blight sample that locked North Rain is a real pathogen-bearing tissue sample, but it does not match NR-77A cold reserve rootstock. It matches heat-basin adapted descendant morphology. It was placed or misclassified into NR-77A quarantine context. The pathogen is real. The claimed host context is false."

Vale's face had gone very still.

Renn whispered, "So the lock was triggered by a sample from people the ledger says never received the line."

Sana looked at him. "Likely."

Amma said, "Say it without likely."

"I cannot."

"Then say what you can."

Sana nodded. "The sample is consistent with a descendant line connected to the denied 2319 pathway. It is not consistent with the reserve line named in the lock."

The court projection erupted.

Not in sound, at first, but in motion. Advocates turned to clients. Pell reached for three tabs at once. Meridian's representative began speaking to someone off feed. Mayor Odet stood. Renn went pale in a way that had nothing to do with performance. Lidia An sat down on an overturned crate as if her legs had decided truth was heavy.

Ro's voice cut through. "All parties remain visible."

Meridian's representative said, "This is unverified field speculation."

Iona's image sharpened as if anger improved transmission. "North Rain confirms reserve comparison. Dr. Ilyen's morphology assessment is consistent with archive tissue records."

"Keeper Saye is under review," Meridian snapped.

"I have been under review since before your grandparents insured weather," Iona said.

Tali shouted from the corridor, "Beacon three clean!"

No one reacted except the crews.

Sana kept her eyes on the tissue images. "I need chain-of-custody."

Ro looked to the chamber. "Meridian will unseal the partitioned chain."

"We cannot," the representative said.

Renn turned on him. "Cannot or will not?"

"Governor, liability partitions exist to protect-"

"If Puget's emergency token was used to bend pollinator routes and a blight sample was placed in the wrong context, you do not get to hide behind protect."

Vale spoke. "Unseal the chain."

The representative stared at him. "Doctor, that may expose unrelated continuity instruments."

"Then they should not have been stored next to a lock trigger."

Sana heard something in Vale's voice then: not mercy, not alliance, but fury at incompetence. He could tolerate cruelty if it was structured. He did not forgive messy manipulation that made his model look like an accomplice.

Ro said, "Unseal."

The chain-of-custody appeared in pieces.

Collection: automated arm NR-Q4-C.
Maintenance window: twenty minutes prior to lock.
Manual bay access: contractor token.
Contractor token issuer: Meridian continuity maintenance.
Subtoken authority: emergency routing pool.
Cross-reference: Puget substrate emergency routing.

The same family of credentials as the pollinator spoof.

Tali appeared in the relay, breathless, hair coming loose from its copper braid. "Say that again."

Ro sent her the chain.

Tali read it and became quiet in a way more alarming than her shouting.

"Same architecture," she said. "Not same token. Same nesting style. Someone is using emergency routing pools like laundry."

"Can you trace issuer?" Ro asked.

"Eventually. Not in one hour while also saving your seed sex life."

Amma blinked.

Lidia An coughed.

Ro said, "Mechanic Moss."

"Pollination. Fine. Saving your pollination."

Sana almost laughed. Then she looked at the tissue again and could not.

The false blight had not been invented from nothing. That would have been easier to dismiss and easier to punish. Someone had taken a real wound from history, a real pathogen from a real basin line, and placed it where it could lock the archive against the very people whose survival had created the evidence.

It was elegant in the way traps were elegant.

Amma understood before Sana said it.

"They used our old seed to prove new seed could not come."

Sana closed her eyes once.

"Yes."

The word did not feel technical enough to survive.

Vale said, "The central triage instrument is withdrawn pending credential audit."

The chamber froze.

Renn stared at him. "Withdrawn?"

"Pending credential audit," Vale repeated. "A triage model cannot rely on compromised scarcity inputs."

Mayor Odet said, "How principled."

Vale looked at her. "How necessary."

Ro said, "The withdrawal is entered. It does not resolve Puget's petition, Dry Basin release, North Rain lock, pollinator sabotage, or Meridian credential exposure."

"No," Vale said. "It prevents those from being hidden under my instrument."

Sana wished that made him less dangerous. It did not. It made him dangerous in a more complicated way.

The North Rain lock status changed while they were still staring at the tissue images.

PROTECTIVE LOCK PARTIAL REVIEW: BLIGHT HOST CONTEXT DISPUTED.
AUTOMATIC FULL RELEASE: DENIED.
FOUNDER SAFEGUARD REVIEW: ACTIVE.
FIELD ECOLOGIST COUNTERSIGNATURE: AVAILABLE.
HUNGER WITNESS: PRESENT.
POLLINATOR CORRIDOR STATUS: UNDER MANUAL QUARANTINE.

Under it, a new timer appeared.

WIND WINDOW REMAINING: 01:07.

Tali's voice came from the corridor. "I hate clocks!"

Amma held out the lunch tin lid again.

Sana looked at Ro.

Ro's face was pale but steady. "We still need corridor clearance."

"Beacon four clean!" Tali shouted. "Beacon five lying, but badly!"

Lidia An leaned toward Amma. "Is badly good?"

"It means she can insult it," Amma said. "So maybe."

The road beyond the yard had gone quieter. People were listening. Not just to the court now. To the corridor crews, the mechanic, the old women, the field ecologist, the proof that had made blight smaller without making danger vanish.

Sana updated her field assessment.

False context blight flag. Real pathogen sample. Host mismatch. Credential overlap with pollinator route spoof. Founder safeguard still active. Corridor support not yet cleared. Human signature pending.

She sent it to Ro, Iona, the chamber record, and sealed copies to Renn and Amma.

Then she added one sentence to her private field notes:

The archive did not lie. Someone taught it the wrong grief.

Ro read it over her shoulder.

"Legally terrible," they said.

"I know."

"Keep it somewhere else."

"I did."

For one moment, despite the timer, despite the poisoned credentials, despite the waiting soil, they both remembered the court wall, the first note, the vault locked and hunger not.

Then Tali yelled, "Beacon five clean if nobody breathes on it!"

Amma said, "Nobody breathe on beacon five."

The order traveled down the corridor faster than any court ruling had moved all day.

Chapter 10: Succession Triage

Beacon five survived because nobody breathed on it.

That was not technically true, as Tali explained loudly to anyone within range and several people who were not. The beacon survived because she isolated the spoofed route table, rewrote the local timing handshake by hand, cut the compromised emergency routing pool out of the corridor, and made three teenagers hold reflective shade cloth between the post and the wind until the sensor stopped interpreting dust flicker as swarm traffic. But by the time the relay carried the update back to the chamber, Amma Quill's version had already moved down the road.

Nobody breathe on beacon five.

People repeated it as instruction, joke, warning, prayer. It was the first thing in hours that everyone understood without a clerk.

Forty-one minutes remained in the wind window when Tali declared the pollinator strand provisionally clean.

"Provisionally," she said, face close to the relay so the chamber could not miss the dirt on her cheek. "That means if someone later says I promised perfection, I will come to their office and teach their chairs to collapse."

Ro said, "Mechanic Moss."

"Fine. Field-safe for initial contact, manual beacon confirmation required, autonomous traffic grounded, living refuge boxes open, Firebreak Seven crew assuming corridor watch until Moss handoff."

"Entered," Ro said.

Amma held out the lunch tin lid again.

This time Ro did not say no.

Sana took the lid. It was dented, clean, and warm from Amma's hands. Someone had scratched an old household number into the underside. Someone else had once used the edge to pry open a stubborn jar; the metal bore the mark. It had carried food. It had carried statements. Now it would carry the kind of signature that made people blame hands instead of systems.

Sana set the lid on a field case.

"Before I sign," she said, "the record needs one more thing."

Amma's eyes narrowed. "There is always one more thing."

"Yes."

Ro looked at her. "What?"

Sana looked at the projected chamber. "Dr. Vale withdrew the central triage instrument pending credential audit. I want him to present the model's assumptions without the compromised inputs. Not as an active remedy. As record."

The chamber went very quiet.

Tali, still kneeling by the beacon relay, muttered, "Why would you invite the knife back in?"

Vale's projection did not move for several seconds. Then he set his slate on the water table.

"Dr. Ilyen," he said, "this is not necessary for initial contact."

"No. It is necessary before the first signature becomes the story of the crisis."

Renn frowned. "What does that mean?"

Sana kept her eyes on Vale. "If we sign, people will say the problem was a locked seed line and a brave exception. If we do not understand the triage model, the next emergency will rebuild the same lock with cleaner credentials."

Ro's face gave away nothing, but their hand moved once near the relay controls. Approval, maybe. Or concern.

Vale studied Sana with an expression close to respect and far too close to interest.

"You want the model visible because you think visibility weakens it."

"I want the model visible because hidden arithmetic becomes law without witnesses."

"And witnessed arithmetic?"

"Can still be cruel. But at least it has to speak."

Vale looked to Ro. "Mediator?"

Ro said, "The field ecologist's request is relevant to consent context. Present assumptions only. No authority request attaches."

Pell's distant voice objected, "Mediator, this creates procedural confusion."

"Then label the tab clearly."

Vale opened the model.

It rose over the water table in the projected chamber and over the dry plot as a ghost of light: basins, towers, corridors, vaults, fungal mills, grain lines, water rights, ration curves, work attendance, migration pressure, panic thresholds, mortality estimates, reseeding intervals, and something called continuity yield. The model was beautiful in the way a cut gem was beautiful: every face made from pressure, every reflection hiding the labor of extraction.

Sana felt the yard lean toward it despite itself.

Vale spoke without amplification, yet every relay carried him cleanly.

"Succession triage begins from an ugly premise: scarcity cannot be made moral by refusing sequence. When simultaneous claims exceed available release capacity, any distribution order produces harm. A governance system under food stress must therefore prioritize actions that preserve the highest future survival capacity per unit of present release."

"Continuity yield," Ro said.

"Yes. Calories preserved now, production capacity maintained, labor systems stabilized, ecological recovery not irreversibly damaged, conflict probability reduced."

Amma said, "People turned into a braid."

Vale looked at her. "People already live braided. The model only admits it."

"Models admit what their makers can stand to count."

"Correct."

The answer unsettled the yard more than denial would have.

Vale moved the projection. Puget Verticals brightened: dense towers, fungal substrate mills, algae scrubbers, ration networks. A line moved from grain release to stabilized substrate to calorie preservation to worker attendance to reduced riot probability.

"A grain unit sent to Puget substrate systems preserves a larger immediate calorie network than the same unit sent to a small basin trial," Vale said. "That does not make Puget morally superior. It makes the consequence field larger."

Renn's jaw tightened. He did not look pleased to be turned into an equation that helped him.

Vale shifted the model. South Yakima lit: trial plots, seed restraint, soil window, corridor dependency, future drought adaptation, political legitimacy, historical injury. The line moved differently, slower, branching into seasons rather than days.

"A rootstock unit sent to South Yakima preserves lower immediate calories but higher adaptive value under heat-basin futures, repairs legitimacy damage, and reduces long-term abandonment pressure. Its continuity yield increases if corridor viability is confirmed and if release prevents claimant radicalization."

Amma laughed once, sharp. "There it is. Feed us before we become inconvenient enough to count."

Vale did not flinch. "Yes."

The road beyond the yard stirred.

Ro said, "You are not helping yourself."

"I am not trying to help myself."

"Then who?"

Vale paused.

It was a small pause. Sana might have missed it before this day.

"The people who will be governed by worse versions of this if we pretend no one ranks harm."

Lidia An adjusted her oxygen scarf. "Doctor, who ranks you?"

Vale turned toward her projection. "Courts. Councils. Evidence."

"No," Lidia said. "Those rank your paperwork. Who ranks the part of you that can say yes to abandonment and still sleep?"

For the first time, Vale looked tired.

"I do not sleep well."

Amma's face changed, not softened, but reclassified. "That is not restitution."

"No."

The model continued to turn.

Sana saw now why Vale was dangerous beyond the obvious cruelty. His model was not stupid. It counted real constraints: grain moved through systems, systems fed people, corridors failed, worker attendance mattered, panic killed, soil windows closed, and symbolic legitimacy sometimes kept roads from becoming battle lines. A foolish model could be defeated by facts. This one ate facts and arranged them into permission.

"Show abandonment," Sana said.

Vale's eyes moved to her.

"It is not a model category."

"Then show where the model stops investing."

Renn looked up sharply.

Ro said nothing.

Vale touched the slate.

Several regions dimmed.

Not only Dry Basin settlements. Small coastal retreats with salt intrusion. Two firebreak hamlets where corridor maintenance costs exceeded modeled output. A migrant barge cluster with unstable water rights. A highland fungal co-op whose culture library was redundant if one ignored local diets. Neighborhoods, not named. People as low-return nodes.

The yard became still.

Amma walked closer to the relay until her face filled the field pickup.

"What happens to dimmed places?" she asked.

Vale answered slowly. "Emergency investments are deferred. Populations receive relocation support, ration maintenance where feasible, and no new seed release unless strategic conditions change."

"Say abandoned."

"Managed retreat."

"Say abandoned."

Vale's mouth tightened.

"The model does not use that word."

Amma turned to Sana. "Does soil care what word he uses?"

"No."

Amma turned back. "Then say abandoned."

Vale looked at the dimmed map. His face had gone hard in a way that was not refusal alone. It was the face of a man gripping a tool by the blade because he would not let anyone else hold it.

"Abandoned," he said.

No one moved.

Then the road outside the yard began to make sound. Not shouting. Not yet. A low murmur moving through bodies as portable speakers carried the word down the line.

Abandoned.

The model had spoken.

Sana felt no triumph. The word did not free anyone. It only removed one layer of insulation.

Ro said, "Entered."

Pell's voice came thinly from the chamber. "Mediator, the public feed-"

"Remains active."

Renn looked at the dimmed regions. "Puget benefits from those deferrals."

Vale said, "In some scenarios."

"Do not soften it now."

"Yes. Puget benefits in some scenarios."

Renn closed his eyes once. When he opened them, he looked older and less broadcast-ready. "And later?"

"Later Puget inherits instability, migration, moral debt, and ecological fragility. The model discounts those because immediate mortality dominates the horizon."

Ro asked, "Who set the horizon?"

Vale did not answer quickly enough.

Sana said, "You did."

"The horizon is based on food panic intervals and reserve depletion."

"You chose when the future became too far away to count."

Vale's fingers rested on the slate. "All models choose horizons."

"Yes," Sana said. "And then pretend the horizon is weather."

Tali's voice came from the corridor, breathless. "Beacon six clean. Also I can hear the abandonment math from here and it is making my tools mean."

Ro said, "Mechanic Moss, status only."

"Status: angry and technically successful."

"Corridor?"

"Manual safe through Moss handoff if no one breathes on anything important."

Amma looked at Sana.

The wind moved the straw on plot A-seventeen.

WIND WINDOW REMAINING: 00:29.

The succession triage model still hovered above the soil, showing bright places, dim places, choices dressed as necessity. Sana understood then that Vale had not presented the model to win this moment. He had presented it because he believed someone would have to use it eventually, and he wanted its cruelty made accurate before panic made it stupid.

That did not absolve him.

It made the fight harder.

"Dr. Vale," Sana said, "does your model assign value to public proof of falsified scarcity inputs?"

"Yes."

"Where?"

He highlighted a category: LEGITIMACY RESTORATION / VIOLENCE AVOIDANCE.

"Weight?"

"Variable."

"Set it here."

Vale's eyes narrowed. "Field ecologist is not a model governor."

"No. Hunger witness is."

Amma looked at Sana, then at Vale. "Set it high."

Vale glanced at Ro.

Ro said, "This is assumptions record, not binding computation."

"Everything becomes binding if repeated often enough," Vale said.

"Then repeat carefully."

Vale adjusted the weight.

The dimmed regions changed. Not saved. Not restored. But some shifted from abandonment to contested support. South Yakima's continuity yield rose, not because the soil had changed, but because truth in public reduced modeled violence and increased cooperation probability.

Tali shouted, "Did you just make honesty a crop input?"

Sana did laugh then. Once. Small and involuntary.

Vale looked almost offended. "It always was."

Amma held the lunch tin lid out again.

"Then sign before honesty misses the wind."

Ro looked at the corridor status, the lock status, the unsealed liability hook, the false blight proof, the model assumptions record, and the public feed that had turned technical language into something people could shout on a road.

"Field ecologist," they said.

Sana took the stylus.

The lid's metal gave slightly under her hand.

She did not sign release ownership. She signed field countersignature for founder safeguard initial soil contact, conditional on hunger witness, manual corridor watch, public provenance record, liability dispute reserved, and no transfer of seed ownership. Ro had drafted the language while everyone argued; of course they had. Amma read every word before nodding. Lidia An read it too, lips moving.

"This does not give us enough," Amma said.

"No," Sana said.

"It gives us a beginning."

"Yes."

Amma signed beneath her, not as supplicant, not as recipient, but as witness.

The field relay accepted both signatures.

North Rain answered with a single line.

FOUNDER SAFEGUARD INITIAL CONTACT AUTHORIZED.

No one cheered.

The road had not received food. Puget had not received grain. The dimmed regions had not brightened into justice. The model still hovered. The liability hook still existed. The credential audit had barely begun.

But in plot A-seventeen, under witness, with corridor crews shouting down the strand and an old lunch tin lid still warm from human hands, the first sealed packet of NR-77A rootstock opened.

Sana took the seedling tissue as if accepting an argument from the future.

Amma whispered, "Soil is ready."

Sana looked once at Vale's model, then at the ground.

"The model can wait," she said.

For the first time that day, the seed touched soil.

Chapter 11: The First Threat of Bread

The seed did not look important once it was in the ground.

That was the first mercy of it. No light rose from plot A-seventeen. No archive bell rang. No model adjusted itself into humility. Sana pressed the seedling tissue into prepared soil, covered it with a measured pinch of dark compost, and held her gloved palm there for three breaths while the field relay confirmed contact. The living thing accepted none of their drama. It entered darkness and began, or failed, according to conditions more intimate than politics.

Amma Quill watched without expression.

Lidia An cried silently.

Tali Moss, halfway down the corridor, shouted, "If anyone cheers before I finish the handoff, I will revoke your optimism."

No one cheered.

The road stayed quiet, but not peaceful. Quiet like a room after a match has been lit. People had seen the seed touch soil. They had also seen that one seedling tissue did not feed a child, restock a shelf, or answer what Puget would eat if substrate shipments stalled. The public feed did what public feeds always did: carried proof faster than understanding and fear faster than both.

Five minutes after initial contact, Meridian Food Mutual filed its response.

It arrived as a red legal band across the relay, the chamber projection, and every official tablet in the field party.

CONTINUITY BACKING REVIEW INITIATED.

Ro read the full notice once, then again.

Their face did not change, but Sana saw the posture shift: mediator to flood engineer, someone feeling pressure through a wall before anyone else heard the crack.

"What is it?" Renn asked from the projected chamber.

Ro did not answer him first. They looked at Lidia An.

The old woman saw the look and sat down on the edge of a soil crate.

"Say it," she said.

Ro turned to the relay. "Meridian Food Mutual is initiating review of fungal protein backing agreements across Puget Verticals, Firebreak Seven, and South Yakima cooperative chain pending determination of unauthorized seed release exposure."

Amma's head lifted. "Backing agreements?"

Sana felt cold move through the yard more sharply than the morning wind.

Renn understood before Amma did. His face went gray. "They cannot do that."

Meridian's representative appeared in full projection now, no longer seated behind anyone. He was a tall man with polished black hair, narrow shoulders, and the exhausted elegance of people who slept in clothes designed to hide sweat. His name band identified him as Soren Il, Continuity Counsel.

"Governor Renn," he said, "the review is automatic under exposure clauses triggered by unapproved release contact involving insured biological infrastructure."

"The contact was authorized under founder safeguard," Ro said.

"The founder safeguard is under dispute."

Amma stepped toward the relay. "What is fungal protein backing?"

The question struck harder than accusation.

Renn looked ashamed that she had to ask. Vale closed his eyes once, as if the model had just become too literal.

Sana answered because no one else did quickly enough.

"Fungal protein mills run on risk guarantees. Grain substrate, culture lines, power priority, contamination insurance, transport claims. Backing means if a batch fails, if contamination is alleged, if shipments are disrupted, the insurer pays enough for mills to keep operating and suppliers to keep sending inputs."

Amma listened. "And if backing is reviewed?"

Ro said, "Shipments slow. Some freeze."

"Bread stops."

"Protein cakes," Renn said automatically.

Lidia An looked at him.

He swallowed. "Bread."

There it was: the first threat of bread. Not a soldier at a vault door, not a governor demanding priority, not a model dimming regions. A legal notice that could thin breakfasts in towers, firebreak towns, and dry basin storage houses by making everyone upstream afraid to touch a shipment.

Soren Il adjusted his cuffs. "No one is threatening bread. Meridian is fulfilling continuity obligations by assessing exposure."

Tali's voice came from the corridor. "I would like to assess exposure by throwing that sentence into a turbine."

Ro said, "Mechanic Moss, status."

"Still angry. Handoff holding. Also he is threatening bread."

"Noted."

Soren Il's mouth tightened. "This is precisely the issue. Public theatricality around contested release increases actuarial volatility. Centralized authority would reduce exposure ambiguity."

Vale opened his eyes.

"Do not attach my withdrawn instrument to your notice," he said.

"Dr. Vale, Meridian is not invoking your instrument. We are noting that fragmented release authority creates unacceptable backing risk."

Renn stepped toward the water table. "You are saying unless seed release is centralized, Puget's fungal protein backing may be suspended."

"Reviewed."

"That word will not feed anyone either."

Soren Il looked mildly pained. "Governor, you understand the scale of exposure. If unauthorized or disputed biological releases produce contamination claims, food insurers cannot guarantee every dependent substrate chain while claimants improvise governance in fields."

Amma raised the lunch tin lid. The signatures still shone wet in the relay light.

"Improvised governance?" she said. "This is older than your contract."

"Then the contract must review whether it can cover it."

The road murmured.

Not loudly. Loud would have been easier. This was the sound of people translating policy into shelves.

Sana looked toward the storage houses. Household nine. Fungal allergy. Household twelve. Twins below growth line. Household eighteen. Heat injury and pregnancy. Fungal protein was not equal food for everyone, but it was still part of the ration web. Pull one guarantee and every household had to recalculate hunger in private.

Ro asked, "What exactly is frozen during review?"

Soren Il looked relieved by procedural language. "No freeze has been declared."

"What slows?"

"New substrate shipment guarantees may require manual approval. Culture replacement coverage enters cautionary review. Cross-basin transport claims are subject to exposure hold."

"In days."

"Mediator?"

"Translate it into days."

Soren Il hesitated.

Vale answered instead. "Puget mills feel uncertainty within thirty hours. Firebreak kitchens within two days. Dry Basin cooperative protein reserves within three to five, depending on local stores and allergy substitutions."

Renn turned on Meridian's counsel. "You filed a notice that creates the panic risk you price."

"We filed a notice required by your agreements."

"Agreements you wrote."

"Agreements your city signed."

Lidia An's voice came from the soil crate, thin but precise. "Because towers fear empty shelves more than ugly hooks."

Renn closed his mouth.

Amma looked at the old woman. "Basins too."

Lidia nodded. "Yes."

For a moment they were not Puget and Dry Basin. They were two old women who had both hidden food from plans.

Sana held onto that moment because the relay immediately tried to kill it.

Pell's voice cut in from the chamber. "Multiple council observers are requesting clarification whether founder safeguard contact constitutes release."

Ro's answer came fast. "Initial soil contact under witness does not constitute bulk release, transfer of ownership, or distribution authority."

Soren Il said, "But it creates biological exposure."

Tali shouted, "So does breathing near beacon five!"

"Mechanic Moss," Ro said.

"Status: still true."

Vale touched his slate. A smaller model appeared beside the larger, not the full succession triage but a backing cascade: guarantees, shipments, mills, ration kitchens, public confidence, hoarding. He pushed it into the public record without asking.

"This is the effect of Meridian's notice," he said. "Not certainty. Risk propagation."

Soren Il looked sharply at him. "You do not have access to Meridian's internal exposure calculations."

"No. I have your public agreements, Puget substrate reserves, Firebreak kitchen dependence, and Dry Basin ration ledgers entered today. Enough."

The cascade lit.

Puget's towers flashed first, thousands of kitchens connected by substrate deliveries. Then Firebreak Seven, where crews maintaining the pollinator corridor received protein cakes during burn season because orchards could not be tended by people fainting in smoke. Then South Yakima, where fungal protein was less central but still protected households that could not eat certain substitutes. The threat did not move like a sword. It moved like cold through plumbing.

The road saw it.

Someone outside the yard shouted, "They can stop food because seed touched dirt?"

Another voice answered, "They can stop promise."

That was worse.

Ro said, "Soren Il, does Meridian intend to suspend backing unless release authority is centralized?"

"Meridian intends to reduce ambiguity."

"Answer."

"Centralized release authority would materially reduce ambiguity."

"That is not an answer."

Soren Il's polished control thinned. "Yes. Meridian's review will strongly prefer a single accountable release authority."

The word prefer landed like a closed fist.

Renn said, "Prefer is doing a lot of violence in that sentence."

Amma lifted the tin lid higher. "We have accountable authority. Soil, witness, ecologist, corridor, record."

"That is not singular," Soren Il said.

"Exactly."

Vale looked at Amma with something like reluctant approval. "That is the conflict."

Sana turned to him. "And your model would solve it by making singular look merciful."

"My model would centralize only under audited inputs and visible abandonment."

"Listen to yourself."

"I do."

"That is not the same as hearing us."

Vale did not answer.

Ro stepped between them with their voice. "Meridian's notice is entered as coercive pressure toward centralization."

Soren Il stiffened. "Mediator, that characterization is prejudicial."

"Yes."

"You cannot-"

"I can characterize a public notice that threatens backing across dependent food systems immediately after witnessed seed contact as pressure. If you prefer a softer word, file one that tells the truth."

The chamber projection showed Mayor Odet leaning forward, eyes bright with anger. "Firebreak Seven rejects backing review. We will not have corridor crews held hostage."

Soren Il said, "Mayor, your burn-season protein reserves are insured under the same continuity family."

"I know what hook is in my mouth."

Renn looked at Odet. "Puget can cover some Firebreak protein draw for seventy-two hours."

His advocates began speaking at once. He cut them off.

"Seventy-two hours," he repeated. "No ownership claim, no corridor leverage. Emergency food support."

Odet stared at him. "Why?"

Renn's face tightened. "Because if your crews fall, the corridor fails, the Dry Basin release fails, and Puget gets to be the villain even if we did not order the sabotage. Also because it is right. Choose whichever reason lets you accept."

Odet nodded once. "Accepted under protest of your personality."

"Noted," Ro said.

Amma said, "Dry Basin can send bean flour to Firebreak kitchens for allergy-safe households."

Several people turned toward her.

She shrugged. "Not enough. Some. We are not helpless just because we are hungry."

Lidia An said, "Puget tower residents can reduce fungal cake draw if told why without slogans."

Renn looked at her, then at the feed. "We will ask for voluntary ration shaping."

Soren Il's expression had shifted from threat to alarm. "Uncoordinated ration shaping may worsen panic."

Ro said, "Coordinated by whom?"

No answer.

There was the opening. Not victory. Openings were smaller than victory and more useful.

Sana looked at the field relay, at the road, at the model, at the soil where NR-77A had disappeared beneath compost.

"Make it visible," she said.

Ro glanced at her.

"Everything," Sana said. "Backing review, Puget seventy-two-hour support, Firebreak crew needs, Dry Basin bean flour, tower voluntary reduction, allergy limits, corridor status, seed contact, liability dispute. Put it on one table. Not a central authority. A public obligation table."

Vale's eyes narrowed. "That is not governance."

"It is what governance looks like before it hardens."

Amma said, "Put names on it."

Lidia An said, "And shelves."

Tali yelled, "And wire! Good wire!"

Ro was already building the table.

They did it on the relay, not the chamber system. That mattered. The table appeared over the dry soil first, then propagated to the water table, then to public feed. Columns: need, source, time, witness, constraint, expiration, dispute. Rows began to fill.

Puget: seventy-two-hour Firebreak protein draw support.
Firebreak Seven: six corridor crews plus back pay confirmed; manual pollinator watch.
Dry Basin: bean flour reserve partial support; allergy-safe households named by consent.
North Rain: NR-77A initial contact authorized; bulk release pending.
Meridian: backing review notice contested as coercive pressure.
Tali Moss: good wire required by sunset.

"Do not put my name next to wire," Tali shouted.

Ro did not look up. "Too late."

The road laughed then.

Not all of it. Not enough. But laughter moved through the waiting people like a small repair. Even Soren Il had to wait for it to pass before objecting, and by then the table was public.

Vale studied it. "This will not scale."

"No," Sana said. "Not yet."

"It is vulnerable to dishonesty."

"So was your model."

"It requires constant witness."

Amma said, "Now you are getting it."

Soren Il recovered. "Meridian cannot accept obligation tables as substitutes for accountable authority."

Ro looked at him. "Meridian does not have to accept it. Meridian has to decide whether it wants to be recorded refusing named food support while demanding singular control as the price of not disrupting bread."

Soren Il went silent.

The field relay marked that too.

Sana looked down at the soil. Nothing visible had changed. The seed remained hidden. But around it, the first fragile architecture of obligation had appeared: ugly, partial, underpowered, legally contested, and alive because everyone could see where it might fail.

Then the North Rain relay chimed.

Iona appeared again. She looked exhausted, and triumphant in a way that made Sana nervous.

"Dr. Ilyen," she said, "initial contact accepted. Founder safeguard has opened the next pathway condition."

Ro said, "Read it."

Iona's eyes moved to the public feed, to Meridian's red notice, to Vale's model, to the obligation table trembling over dry soil.

"Condition two: release may continue only if no claimant converts emergency access into exclusive ownership by food, force, contract, or delay."

The words moved through the yard slowly.

Food. Force. Contract. Delay.

Amma looked at Soren Il's projection.

Lidia An looked at Renn.

Tali looked at the corridor.

Sana looked at Vale.

Vale looked at the model.

Ro looked at the table they had just built and said, very quietly, "Well."

For once, no one had a procedural phrase ready.

Chapter 12: Leaving the Green Zone

The procedural phrase arrived seventeen seconds later.

Pell found it, of course. "The chamber proposes a temporary interpretive stay pending clarification of Condition Two."

Ro looked at the relay as if it had personally disappointed them. "No."

"Mediator, the condition's language is broad."

"That is because the ways to steal food are broad."

"A stay would preserve legal posture."

"A stay would convert delay into ownership, which the condition explicitly names. Denied."

The road heard that and moved the words outward. Delay into ownership. Sana watched people pass the phrase from one mouth to the next, testing whether it held. Some nodded. Some looked angrier. A few looked afraid because a phrase that explained injury also made injury harder to endure politely.

Soren Il's projection vanished without requesting leave.

The relay marked that too.

"He left," Amma said.

Ro made a note. "He chose to be recorded leaving."

"That is not the same as stopping him."

"No."

The honesty landed with more weight than comfort. There would be no single moment when the insurers, governors, models, councils, and archives all agreed to behave. Condition Two had not solved coercion. It had named the four hands by which coercion would reach for the seed: food, force, contract, delay. Naming gave them a map. It did not move their feet.

Tali came back from the corridor with wire over one shoulder, a dead drone in each hand, and dust up to her knees.

"Bad news," she said.

Everyone looked at her.

"I found more good wire than expected, which means someone has been hiding it from me."

Mayor Odet's projected face pinched. "Tali."

"Fine. Other bad news. The route spoof and the quarantine-bay token share maintenance architecture, but not issuer identity. Whoever did this knows how to borrow official urgency without leaving the same fingerprints twice. Also beacon seven is clean and I hate it because it should not be."

Sana rubbed her forehead with the back of one wrist. "Why hate clean?"

"Because clean means the attack was not trying to break the whole corridor. It was trying to make one section look unreliable while leaving enough function for a redirect."

"Redirect where?" Ro asked.

Tali pointed with a drone wing toward the northeast. "Moss Corridor handoff if you are honest. Puget substrate route if you are a coward. Old rail seed road if you are doing something illegal and nostalgic."

The last phrase entered the yard like a person with a hood up.

Sana said, "Old rail seed road."

Tali looked at her. "You know it?"

"Kade showed me a path image. North Rain, Firebreak Seven, Moss Corridor, orchard monastery, South Yakima. There were old rail cuts in the famine years."

Amma's face closed around memory. "Mara Venn's road."

Lidia An whispered, "Bathroom beans."

No one understood except perhaps Sana, and maybe that was enough.

Ro said, "If the physical seed route was altered, the archive record will not be enough. We have to inspect the route."

"We?" Renn asked from the projection.

Ro's eyes moved to him. "You may send no one with authority."

"I was going to say you need someone who understands Puget substrate routes."

Tali raised one hand without looking at him. "I understand enough to insult them accurately."

Renn continued as if she had not spoken. "My observer remains Lidia An if she consents. Puget's official authority stays in chamber. I will not put a city badge on your road."

Lidia An adjusted her cap. "I consent to less sitting."

Amma said, "You just stood."

"I am eighty-nine. Sitting can be an occupation."

Tali gave her an approving look. "She can come."

Ro said, "This is not your crew."

"Everything becomes my crew when drones fall on it."

The relay chimed before Ro could answer.

This time the sender was not North Rain, not the chamber, not Meridian.

Sana's field slate pulsed with old courier encryption.

Official ledger still lies, the message read. Wrong key opens rail road. If you go, go before they count walkers as crowd risk.

No signature.

Sana did not have to say the name. Ro saw her face.

"Kade," they said.

Amma took one step closer. "Kade Morrow?"

Sana nodded.

The hunger council leader held out her hand. "Show me."

Sana hesitated. Not because she distrusted Amma. Because every copy of Kade's message turned him from rumor into target.

Amma saw the hesitation and did not soften it. "Mara Venn's road runs under our dead. If Kade is walking it, he does not get to be only your secret."

Sana handed her the slate.

Amma read. Once. Twice. Then she looked toward the road, where families still stood near the cooperative yard, listening, waiting, beginning to understand that the seed touching soil was not the end of anything.

"He is right," Amma said. "They will call us crowd risk soon."

Pell, apparently unable to resist proving her point, spoke from the chamber. "Mediator Tamsin, field reports indicate gatherings along unauthorized roadways. Crowd management protocols may be needed to preserve safe passage."

Amma looked at the relay. "There it is."

Ro said, "No crowd protocol will be invoked against claimant witnesses without chamber review."

Pell cleared his throat. "Chamber review may be expedited."

"By whom?"

Silence.

Renn's voice came low. "Not Puget."

Mayor Odet said, "Not Firebreak."

Vale said, "Not my office."

That left enough unnamed power in the air to make the yard feel smaller.

Sana looked at the soil where NR-77A lay hidden. "If we stay, the argument comes to us. Food, force, contract, delay."

Ro followed her gaze. "If we go, we leave the seed."

Amma said, "The seed is not alone."

She raised her voice. "Household eighteen!"

A woman near the storage houses turned. She was broad, pregnant, one arm in a heat-injury brace. Beside her stood a man with a water ledger and two teenagers carrying shade cloth.

"Plot watch," Amma said.

The woman nodded. "Already assigned."

"Household nine?"

An elder lifted two fingers from the shade beside a cistern.

"Allergy record with Lidia An."

Lidia blinked. "With me?"

Amma handed her a folded packet. "You wanted shelves."

Lidia held it as if it weighed more than paper.

"Household twelve?"

The teacher with the children raised a hand. "Growth line updates every two hours."

Amma looked back at Sana. "The seed is not alone."

Sana believed her.

That was new and unsettling. She trusted soil, root, graft, witness marks, scars on bark. Trusting a human arrangement felt more fragile. But the arrangement was visible. It could be criticized, repaired, embarrassed, reinforced. It could fail in public rather than succeed in secret.

"We inspect the rail road," Sana said.

Ro nodded once. "Field party?"

"Me. You. Tali."

"Lidia," the old woman said.

Ro looked pained. "Lidia An."

"Kade if he stops being dramatic," Tali said.

Amma said, "And one hunger council runner."

"No," Ro said. "Too many."

Amma pointed to the road. "Too few is how people disappear."

Ro's jaw tightened.

Sana said, "One runner."

"I was not negotiating with you," Ro said.

"I know."

That almost made them smile. Almost.

Amma called the runner forward. He was sixteen or seventeen, lean as a bean pole, with dust in his hair and a sling bag repaired with three colors of thread.

"Name," Ro said.

"Jori Quill."

"Relation?"

"Everyone asks that," he said.

Amma said, "Nephew. Fast feet. Good memory. Bad at cards."

Jori looked offended only by the last item.

Ro entered him into the field record. "You follow instructions."

"Whose?"

Ro paused.

Tali grinned. "Good question."

"Mine for safety," Ro said. "Dr. Ilyen's for field evidence. Mechanic Moss's for drones and beacons. Lidia An's if she says she cannot breathe. Your own if the road knows something we do not."

Jori considered this and nodded. "That is a lot of instructions."

"Yes."

"Better than one bad one."

Amma touched his shoulder once and let go.

They left the cooperative yard by the north service gate.

That was the official version. In truth, they left by walking through a gap in the wind wall that everyone used and no registry map admitted. The gap opened onto a service track lined with cover crop and repair flags. Behind them, the field relay remained over plot A-seventeen, carrying the obligation table, the seed status, the contested backing notice, and Amma's witnesses to the chamber and the public feed. Ahead, the land rose in long dry folds toward the old rail cuts.

Sana had spent her career walking ground. Restored marsh, orchard corridor, seed trial, flood terrace, fungal grove. This felt different. The green zone was not simply a place of wet plants and polished courts. It was the area where systems still pretended their own maintenance was invisible. Leaving it meant entering ground where every pipe, wire, seed packet, ration claim, family debt, and old illegal mercy showed.

The first kilometer passed between low wind walls and heat-stressed shrubs. Tali walked ahead, one wrist reader open, scanning for spoofed beacons and muttering insults at each clean signal. Jori moved beside her, matching her stride with the flexible patience of someone used to carrying messages between adults who thought urgency belonged to them. Ro stayed near Lidia without making it obvious. Sana walked behind, recording soil condition, corridor markers, old rail fragments, and the way waiting people stepped aside without leaving.

They were not alone.

Families followed at a distance. Not a crowd, not yet, not in any language Pell could use without lying. Witnesses moving at walking speed. Some carried water. Some carried tool rolls. One child carried a paper sign folded so only the back showed.

Ro noticed. "This will complicate passage."

Lidia said, "Everything honest does."

The old rail cut appeared as a dark line in the slope.

It had once carried freight: grain, timber, machine parts, people leaving places after the heat made them temporary. During the famine years, it had carried seed in violation of maps. Now it was half-buried, rails removed or stolen, ties replaced by drought grass and dust. Along one side ran a maintenance channel lined with ceramic tiles. Some were cracked. Some had red handprints.

Amma's people had been marking the road for a long time.

Tali crouched beside the first tile. "Beacon shadow."

Sana knelt. "Meaning?"

"No active beacon, but old routing memory. Drones use shadows when maps fail. Like people using footpaths after roads lose funding."

Jori said, "This one goes to the monastery."

Everyone looked at him.

He shrugged. "Bad at cards. Good memory."

Ro said, "Which monastery?"

"Orchard Sisters of Saint No-One. That is what Amma calls them. Their sign says House of Perennial Witness."

Sana remembered Kade's path image. Orchard monastery between Moss Corridor and South Yakima.

"How far?"

"Walking? Before dark if Lidia An can breathe and Tali stops insulting every post."

Tali looked up. "I can walk and insult."

The field slate pulsed again.

Old courier encryption.

This time the message held coordinates overlaid on the rail cut and a single instruction.

Do not trust the clean tiles.

Tali leaned over Sana's shoulder.

"I hate him," she said.

Ro asked, "Why?"

"Because I was about to trust the clean tiles."

Sana scanned the nearest ceramic tile. It was one of the few uncracked pieces, white under dust, its old routing mark still legible.

At first it showed nothing.

Then she changed the field scope to heat memory.

A handprint appeared.

Not red pigment. Oil. Recent. Someone had cleaned the tile after touching it, but not deeply enough. The hand had pressed over the old routing mark, then dragged downward as if opening a hidden panel.

Tali slid a thin tool into the tile seam and lifted.

The tile came free.

Behind it lay a seed capsule socket.

Empty.

Jori whispered something under his breath.

Ro said, "Record."

Sana was already recording. The socket was old founder architecture, not modern Compact equipment. Brass conductor, fungal lacquer, scarlet mineral glass scar. The wrong key's family.

Tali's wrist reader chirped.

"This socket was opened recently."

"How recently?" Ro asked.

"Hours. Maybe less."

Sana looked up the rail cut.

At the bend, a figure stepped from the shadow of a broken bridge pier.

Kade Morrow wore the same patchwork coat, but now he carried a pack across one shoulder and a long wrapped tube under one arm. He looked at the empty socket, then at the people behind Sana, then at Lidia An, then at Tali's tools.

"You brought a parade," he said.

Ro folded their arms. "You sent coordinates to a field auditor during a public food crisis."

"I said don't come alone."

"You said come alone."

"I was testing her judgment."

Tali pointed a tool at him. "If you make one more charming illegal courier joke, I will put you in a beacon socket."

Kade looked at Sana. "I like her."

"No," Sana said. "You need her."

That landed. His smile faded.

Sana pointed to the empty socket. "What was here?"

Kade's eyes went to the rail cut behind him.

"A witness capsule."

"For NR-77A?"

"For the route that moved its ancestor."

Ro said, "Where is it?"

Kade shifted the wrapped tube under his arm.

"Not with me."

Tali made a disbelieving sound.

He ignored her. "I got here too late. Someone opened the clean tiles before me. Took three capsules. Left the dirty ones."

Sana felt the old pattern assemble: false blight, pollinator spoof, backing notice, model pressure, now physical route evidence removed from the parts of the path that looked most official.

"Why leave dirty ones?" Jori asked.

Kade looked at him with sudden interest. "Good question."

Jori straightened a little.

Kade crouched and brushed dust from a cracked tile marked with a red handprint. "Because people who clean records trust clean records. They miss the ones everyone has touched."

He lifted the cracked tile with his knife.

Behind it, in a socket packed with dust and seed husk fragments, lay a capsule the size of a thumb bone.

Kade did not pick it up.

He looked at Sana.

"Field ecologist," he said. "Your route now."

The green zone was behind them.

Ahead, the old seed road opened into heat, dust, and evidence that had learned to survive by looking unimportant.

Chapter 13: Firebreak Supper

The witness capsule was heavier than it looked.

Sana lifted it from the socket with sterile tongs while Ro recorded, Tali muttered about people who hid useful things in places full of spiders, and Kade stood far enough away to make clear he was not touching evidence. Jori held the shade cloth over Sana's hands. Lidia An sat on a broken rail tie, breathing carefully through the oxygen scarf, watching the capsule as if it might answer for every locked pantry she had ever known.

The capsule was thumb-bone small, brass-black beneath dust, sealed with fungal lacquer that had cracked but not failed. A red handprint had once marked the tile above it. A living system had hidden under touch, not polish.

"Field condition," Ro said.

"Dry socket, dust packed, seed husk fragments, no visible water intrusion," Sana answered. "Founder architecture. Scarlet witness scar intact. Capsule seal intact but aged."

"Chain?"

"Recovered by field ecologist from cracked hand-marked tile on old rail seed road. Witnessed by mediator, hunger council runner, Puget observer, Firebreak mechanic, and Kade Morrow."

Ro looked up. "Do you want his name in recovery?"

Kade's face gave away nothing.

Sana said, "He stood here."

"That is not an answer."

"It is the one the route gives."

Ro entered it.

Kade looked down the rail cut. "If the clean capsules are gone, this one may be all that says the road was witnessed."

"What does it contain?" Jori asked.

"Usually a route hash, witness sequence, soil proof, courier oath, and sometimes a mercy clause if the founders were feeling guilty enough."

Tali squinted at him. "You say that like you have opened one."

"I have been accused of opening many things."

"Not an answer."

"No."

Ro said, "The capsule stays sealed until we reach a controlled field station."

Tali pointed toward the darkening ridge. "Then we go to Firebreak Seven before the road gets clever in the dark."

The old rail cut climbed toward smoke country.

They walked until the dry basin behind them softened into copper evening and the restored green of the coast was no longer visible even as memory. The land changed by degrees. The shrubs grew lower and tougher. The wind smelled of char before the first burned trunks appeared. Underfoot, dust mixed with black flakes from managed burns. At intervals, ceramic posts marked old fire lines: dates, crew names, wind directions, casualty counts, orchard survival rates. Sana read them the way other people read grave markers.

Kade walked ahead of her but behind Tali, never quite leading, never quite following. He carried the wrapped tube without shifting it to his other arm. Ro stayed close to Lidia. Jori ranged between them and the families still following at a respectful distance, carrying messages back with hand signs Amma must have taught him before they left.

"You should tell them to return," Ro said after the third time Jori ran back.

Jori looked at the line of walkers. "They would hear you."

"And?"

"They would not return."

Lidia said, "An efficient report."

Tali stopped at a ridge sensor and slapped it with the heel of her hand. A small green light woke. Then another. Down the slope ahead, a line of lamps appeared one by one, low to the ground, amber under smoke haze.

"Firebreak Seven," she said.

It was not a town in the old sense. It was a deliberate interruption.

The settlement spread along a managed burn corridor where two restored forest bands had been kept apart by orchards bred to char and live, gravel lanes, water cisterns, drone sheds, tool yards, and long communal kitchens with roofs that could vent smoke in six directions. Houses sat low and wide, plastered in pale clay, with shutters of blackened composite. Between them grew trees that looked dead until the wind turned their leaves silver: smoke plum, ash pear, ember olive, charbark apple. Their trunks were black by design. Their roots held soil when fire passed over them. Their fruit grew small, dense, and stubborn.

People were waiting at the first cistern.

Mayor Odet stood among them with her ash braid unpinned, sleeves rolled, burned knuckles bare. The relay projection had made her look severe. In person she looked tired enough to be honest and angry enough to stand.

"You brought half a basin," she said to Ro.

"They brought themselves."

"That is always what half a basin says."

Tali held up the wire coil. "I found the good wire."

Odet closed her eyes. "Of course you did."

"And back pay?"

"In public, Tali?"

Tali gestured at the old road, the walkers, the field recorder, and the sky. "Where else do we live now?"

Odet sighed. "Back pay entered."

"With interest."

"Do not become a food insurer."

"Then pay on time."

Someone in the waiting group laughed. It broke the stiffness. People moved. Water was passed first to Lidia, then to Jori, then to the others. A girl younger than Jori brought Sana a cloth to wipe dust from her face. An old man took one look at Kade and said, "No smoking near the west sheds."

Kade lifted both hands. "I am reformed in that one category."

"Doubtful," the old man said, and walked away.

They ate in the long kitchen because Firebreak Seven did not ask crisis guests whether they were hungry before feeding them.

Supper came from the smoke-resistant orchards: charbark apple sliced thin and sour, ash pear roasted until its skin split black and its center turned honey-soft, bean cakes griddled with ember olive oil, bitter greens from shaded trenches, a small bowl of fungal protein stew stretched with barley, and hard flatbread baked against the side of a cooling fire wall. Nothing was abundant. Everything had been counted before it reached the table. But it was food placed before them without being made into leverage, and that made Sana's throat ache.

Lidia An held the flatbread in both hands.

"It smells like tower festival bread," she said.

Odet sat across from her. "Puget stole the recipe from firebreak refugees two generations ago."

Renn's distant voice would probably have objected if he had been present. He was not. Lidia only nodded.

"We probably called it integration."

"Probably."

Amma's walkers ate outside in the yard, seated on tool crates and cistern steps. Firebreak crews brought them water, not as charity but as logistics. Tali ate standing, one hand in a drone opened on the table, the other tearing bread. Jori watched her repair the drone with unconcealed worship.

"Do not apprentice yourself without permission," Amma's voice called from outside, though she was not supposed to be close enough to hear.

Jori straightened. "I was just looking."

"Looking is how they get you."

Tali grinned. "She is not wrong."

Ro set the sealed witness capsule in the center of the table on a folded cloth. No one ate over it. No one touched it. The small object gathered attention the way a coal gathered heat.

Odet looked at it. "Founder road?"

Sana nodded. "Recovered from a hand-marked tile. Clean tile sockets were emptied."

Odet's face hardened. "How many?"

"Kade says three."

All eyes moved to Kade.

He had taken a place near the kitchen door, back to the wall, bowl untouched.

"Three clean sockets opened," he said. "One at lower rail bend, one at ridge gate, one before monastery turn. Whoever took them knew which tiles official teams would inspect first."

"How do you know all three were opened?" Ro asked.

Kade tapped the wrapped tube beside him. "Because Mara Venn left a map that makes people who trust official maps look underdressed."

Tali stopped chewing. "That tube is a map?"

"Partly."

"What is the other part?"

"A bad idea that survived."

Ro said, "Unwrap it."

Kade shook his head. "Not in a room with open vents, active relays, and half of Firebreak Seven pretending not to listen."

The kitchen went briefly too quiet.

Odet said, "My people listen when food is at stake."

"Good," Kade said. "Then they should know some records can burn the people who kept them."

Sana watched him. "You are deciding that alone again."

"I am delaying harm."

Ro's expression sharpened. "Careful."

Kade heard the warning. Delay had become one of the four named hands of ownership. He looked away first.

Odet leaned forward. "While you decide what burns whom, I have a missing shipment."

Sana turned. "Graft shipments?"

The mayor nodded to a crew chief at the end of the table. The woman brought a slate and three paper tags, each singed at one corner.

"Firebreak Seven maintains smoke-resistant orchard buffer for Moss Corridor handoff," Odet said. "Three weeks ago we were scheduled to receive graft stock: charbark apple root repair, ash pear pollinator support, and ember olive windbreak replacements. Not glamorous. Not priority. Without them, corridor still functions, but weakly."

Tali said, "I filed six complaints."

"You filed nine, three of which contained threats."

"Efficient complaints."

Odet ignored her and slid the slate to Sana. "Shipment ledger says delivered."

Sana read the record. Delivered: Firebreak Seven West Graft Shed. Witness: automated depot scan. Condition: accepted. Carrier: Cascadia Recovery Logistics subcontract under Meridian continuity guarantee.

She looked at the paper tags.

They were physical graft labels. Charbark apple. Ash pear. Ember olive. Firebreak Seven's mark had been overwritten by a temporary reroute stamp.

PU-VAULT SUBSTRATE SUPPORT / EMERGENCY HOLD.

"Puget," Lidia said quietly.

Odet did not look at her. "That is what the stamp wants us to say."

Tali wiped oil from her fingers. "The grafts never reached Puget."

Sana looked up.

Tali pointed at the tags. "Wrong burn on the corners. Puget vault transfer seals heat-crimp clean. These were smoke-shed burned. Someone stamped Puget on tags after the tags came through a firebreak shed."

Odet nodded. "Our shed. Not West Graft. Old east shed, decommissioned after the 2418 burn."

Ro said, "Why use a decommissioned shed?"

Kade spoke from the door. "Because it still has a rail spur under it."

The room turned.

He finally picked up his bowl, looked into it as if surprised food had arrived there, and set it down again.

"Mara's road runs under your east shed," he said. "If someone is moving graft stock along old seed routes, they can make it look delivered, rerouted, or burned depending which record you trust."

Sana felt the day fold again. Missing witness capsules. Pollinator route spoof. False blight. Backing review. Now missing graft shipments that should have strengthened the very corridor someone wanted declared unstable.

"Why steal graft stock?" Jori asked from beside Tali.

Sana answered slowly. "To weaken the corridor later without making it fail today."

Tali's eyes lit with grim recognition. "Same as the drones."

"Enough function for redirect," Ro said. "Enough weakness for argument."

Odet's burned hands closed around each other. "We fed you from orchards that are being thinned on paper."

Lidia An looked at the flatbread in her hands.

"Bread again," she said.

No one corrected her.

Ro stood. "We inspect the east shed."

Odet stood too. "After supper."

"Mayor-"

"After supper," Odet repeated. "You are not walking into an old burn shed hungry, in the dark, with a sealed founder capsule and an illegal courier, while my crews watch you ignore food they counted for you. That is how people become symbols instead of useful."

Ro opened their mouth.

Sana touched their sleeve. "She is right."

Ro sat down.

The meal resumed, but not the ease. Conversation moved in small practical pieces: water, wire, who would watch the capsule, which crew knew the east shed floor, whether Lidia could manage another kilometer, whether Jori's sling bag could carry sample sleeves without contaminating them. Kade finally ate. Tali pretended not to watch him. Sana ate the ash pear last and tasted smoke, sugar, and a bitterness that stayed.

When supper ended, Odet brought a small clay bowl to the center of the table. Inside were charred twigs from the last managed burn.

"Firebreak rule," she said. "Guests who go into old burn ground take ash."

She marked her own forehead first. Then Tali's, Jori's, Lidia's oxygen scarf ring, Ro's copper pin, Sana's glove, and finally the cloth under the witness capsule. She paused before Kade.

"Do couriers take ash?" she asked.

Kade's answer was quiet. "The ones who come back."

Odet marked him.

Outside, the sun had gone red behind smoke haze. The old east shed waited beyond the orchard rows, where trees bred to survive fire held their black trunks against the darkening sky.

Sana lifted the witness capsule.

For the first time since the vault locked, she felt the story pulling them not toward a hearing but into the infrastructure itself: roads under sheds, grafts under stamps, bread under contracts, seeds under ash.

The Long Orchard was no longer an archive.

It was a trail.

Chapter 14: The Courier's Confession

The old east shed had not been abandoned.

It had been officially abandoned, which was different. The registry map called it decommissioned after the 2418 burn. The maintenance budget marked it inactive. The western sheds received inspections, drone sweeps, inventory scans, and polite language about safety. The old east shed received dust, rumor, and the kind of use people preferred not to itemize.

It stood beyond the last row of ember olives, a long low structure with a bowed roof and walls blackened by fire old enough to have become weather. Smoke-resistant vines climbed one side in stubborn green fingers. The sliding doors had been sealed with two modern locks and three older habits: a chain, a wedge, and a mark of ash across the threshold.

Odet touched the ash mark first.

"No one enters burn ground without naming purpose," she said.

Ro looked as if they wanted to object and decided not to become foolish in a local ritual.

"Evidence recovery," they said.

Tali said, "Wire theft investigation."

"Graft shipment trace," Sana said.

Jori swallowed. "Memory."

Lidia An breathed through the scarf for three careful counts. "Shelves."

Kade was last.

The ash on his forehead had smeared where sweat reached it. He looked at the sealed doors, then at the ground.

"Return," he said.

Odet studied him. "Return what?"

"Whatever I can."

She accepted that with a small nod and cut the chain.

The shed opened with the sound of a throat unused to speech.

Inside, the air smelled of char, old oil, dry graft wax, and something green trying to live without permission. Their lights found rows of empty racks, a collapsed sorting table, plastic crates with melted corners, and a floor scarred by drag marks. Most of the roof had held. A narrow section near the east wall had burned through and been patched with smoke-glass panels. Beneath the panels, seedlings grew in a line where rain had found cracks.

Tali stepped in first, scanner raised. "No active drone traffic. Two dead beacons. One illegal repeater trying to look like a rat nest."

"A what?" Lidia asked.

"A small machine pretending to be trash. Common personality type."

Odet pointed to the back. "Rail spur is under the sorting pit."

They crossed the shed slowly. Ro recorded continuously. Sana walked with the sealed witness capsule in a hard case against her chest, feeling absurdly protective of a thing that had survived longer than most laws. Kade stayed near the wall. Every few steps, his eyes paused on details before anyone else noticed them: a notch in a beam, a stain near the floor, a hook bent open, a patch of dust too smooth.

Sana saw him seeing.

"You have been here," she said.

"Yes."

"When?"

"Which time?"

The question moved through the shed.

Tali stopped beside the illegal repeater she had found under a crate. "That sounds like confession smell."

Kade looked at her. "Is everything mechanical to you?"

"No. Some things are obviously guilt."

Ro said, "Kade."

He took a breath. The old courier mask slid over his face, charm aligning itself for use. Then he looked at Jori, who was standing beside a rack of burned graft ties with a sling bag full of sample sleeves, and the charm failed.

"The first time was 2319," Kade said.

No one spoke.

Outside, wind moved through the ember olives. The shed creaked.

"I was seventeen," he continued. "Mara Venn said I was old enough to carry a box and young enough that no one believed I knew what was in it. She was right about the first part."

Sana set the hard case on the sorting table.

Kade looked at it but did not touch it.

"The official story says South Yakima was not authorized for North Rain rootstock because of blight review. That part is not entirely false. There was a review. There was a pathogen concern. There was also a council delay, an insurer refusal, and a warehouse full of people using careful words while the cooperative ration line started cutting seed porridge with mineral water."

Amma was not there. She had stayed with the plot. But her household ledgers seemed to enter the shed anyway.

Lidia whispered, "Mineral water."

Kade nodded. "It keeps the spoon moving. That is all."

Ro's voice was low. "What did Mara ask you to carry?"

"Pear-rice rootstock. Restricted. Witnessed under founder quorum, if you believe the capsule records. Not valid under the modern ledger, if you believe the people who later erased them."

"How much?"

"Three cold packs. Enough for trial plots, not enough for a miracle. Nothing is ever enough for a miracle."

Sana heard the phrase settle beside one of the book's oldest warnings: not a miracle seed.

Kade walked to the sorting pit. The floor there was metal, warped by heat and covered by a rubber mat stiff with age. Tali and Odet pulled the mat aside. Under it lay a rectangular hatch with a rusted wheel.

"Mara said the Long Orchard had two kinds of law," Kade said. "The law written so people could agree while fed, and the law hidden so people could disobey before starving. I thought that was heroic. I was seventeen. I thought all useful crimes made you clean."

Tali crouched by the hatch. "This wheel is jammed."

Kade reached past her to a small notch under the rim and pressed. The wheel loosened with a click.

She stared at him. "I hate that you know that."

"I earned it badly."

Together they turned the wheel.

The hatch opened onto darkness and the smell of old rail dust.

Sana's light found steps descending to a narrow tunnel. The rails were gone, but the grooves remained. Ceramic seed sockets lined one wall. Some were empty. Some had cracked covers. Some bore red handprints. Along the other wall, newer marks showed recent passage: scraped dust, a boot print with modern tread, a strand of packing fiber caught on a bolt.

"Graft shipments came through here," Sana said.

Odet's face hardened. "Recently."

Tali held up the fiber. "Cold-graft insulation. Same weave as the missing shipment."

Ro recorded. "Kade, continue."

He stood at the top of the steps, looking down as if the tunnel were not below the shed but inside his own ribs.

"We moved at night. Mara, me, a woman named Sef from the monastery, and two South Yakima runners. I carried the smallest pack because I was smallest and because if patrol caught us Mara wanted me to look pathetic."

"Did it work?" Jori asked.

Kade looked at him. "Once."

"What happened the other times?"

"We ran."

Jori nodded, accepting this as useful detail.

They descended.

The tunnel was cooler than the shed. Their lights caught old labels painted above branching paths: WEST GRAFT, MONASTERY SPUR, RIVER RELAY, BASIN SOUTH. Some had been scratched out. Some had been updated by hand. Sana saw more founder architecture here than in the court's beautiful table: sockets, scars, witness marks, biological residue wells, courier oath plates. The first Long Orchard had not been a single vault. It had been a nervous system buried under practical places.

Kade stopped at a wall plate blackened by smoke.

"Here," he said.

The plate read: COURIER OATH / EMERGENCY PATH / NO OWNERSHIP TRANSFER.

His fingers hovered near it.

"Mara made me put my hand there and say the words. I thought it was theater."

Ro asked, "What words?"

Kade swallowed.

"I carry life, not property. I accept witness, not title. I will name what I move, where I move it, who receives it, and who is harmed if I lie."

No one spoke for several seconds.

Lidia An said, "Not bad theater."

"No," Kade said. "It was better than I deserved."

Sana stepped closer. "Did you name it?"

"At first."

There it was. The edge.

Ro's voice became precise. "Explain."

Kade looked down the tunnel toward the basin south marking.

"We reached South Yakima late. People were not waiting like grateful victims in a story. They were angry. They were sick. They were organized. They had soil ready and not enough calories to keep arguing with officials. Mara handed over the rootstock under witness. The cooperative signed receipt. Soil contact happened before dawn."

"And then?"

"Then one of the cold packs had a breach flag."

Sana's body tightened. "Pathogen?"

"Maybe. Maybe condensation. Maybe a sensor damaged when I fell near the ridge gate. I do not know. I did know that if we reported the breach as written, the whole route would lock, the trial plots would be destroyed, and the people who had already planted would be called contaminated thieves."

The tunnel seemed to narrow.

Tali said, more quietly than usual, "What did you do?"

Kade looked at her, then at Sana.

"I cut the provenance chain."

The confession did not echo. It sank.

Ro said, "How?"

"I removed one witness capsule from the clean route and moved it to a dirty socket after soil contact. I thought if the official chain broke in the right place, no one could trace the planted line without also admitting the hunger witness record. I thought I was protecting them."

Sana closed her eyes once.

"You made the line deniable."

"Yes."

"You also made it vulnerable to being called illegal later."

"Yes."

"And vulnerable to false context."

His face flinched then, only slightly.

"I did not know that part."

"No," Sana said. "But you built the gap someone used."

Kade nodded.

The honesty did not cleanse him. It made him more visible. Sana found that she preferred it to charm and hated that preference because it asked her to do something with the truth besides judge it.

Jori asked, "Did it save them?"

Everyone looked at him.

He looked back, stubborn. "That matters too."

Kade's mouth tightened.

"Yes."

"How many?"

"I do not know."

"Guess."

Ro said, "Jori-"
