Kade lifted one hand. "No. Let him ask."

He leaned against the tunnel wall, suddenly looking older than he had in the shed.

"The cooperative had two hundred and forty people that winter. Nearby settlements came for seed later, though they were not supposed to. Maybe six hundred ate because the trial worked. Maybe more over the years. Maybe fewer if you subtract what my broken chain cost later. I do not know how to do the math without lying."

Lidia An whispered, "No one does."

Tali looked at the wall plate. "So you saved them and damaged the record."

"Yes."

"And now someone else is using the damage."

"Yes."

"That is awful."

"Yes."

"Useful confession," Tali said.

Kade almost smiled. It failed. "Thank you."

Sana opened the witness capsule case.

Ro looked sharply at her. "We said controlled field station."

"We are in the route."

"That is not a station."

"No. It is the context."

Ro held her gaze. The old argument between procedure and ground returned, but it had changed. Procedure had followed them into the tunnel. Ground had learned to speak in records.

"We open only the outer scan," Ro said. "No seal breach."

Tali was already beside Sana with a reader. "I can scan through fungal lacquer if no one breathes on it."

Lidia sighed. "That instruction is spreading."

The scan took four minutes. Kade stood apart. Odet watched the tunnel entrances. Jori held the light steady. Sana watched the reader build a ghost image of the capsule contents: route hash, witness sequence, soil proof, courier oath, and one damaged segment flagged by age and deliberate relocation.

Then a name appeared.

KADE MORROW / COURIER WITNESS / EXCEPTION RECORDED.

Under it, another.

MARA VENN / ARCHIVE DISSENT / RELEASE NECESSARY.

Under that, in weaker letters:

BREACH FLAG DISPUTED / DO NOT DESTROY PLANTED LINE WITHOUT HUNGER COUNCIL REVIEW.

Kade made a sound Sana had never heard from him before.

Not grief exactly. Not relief. Something with both roots.

"She knew," he said.

Sana looked at the text. "Mara?"

"She knew I cut the chain. She recorded exception."

Ro leaned closer. "Then the official ledger omitted not only the release, but the dispute over the breach."

"And the instruction not to destroy the planted line," Sana said.

Tali's eyes narrowed. "Meaning someone did not just inherit your gap. They buried the patch."

Kade put one hand on the wall plate as if the oath had become a railing.

"I thought I carried the guilt alone."

Lidia An's voice was gentle and unsparing. "People like carrying guilt alone. It lets them own the shape of the story."

Kade nodded once as if struck.

Ro saved the scan in three sealed copies. "This enters the record."

Kade did not object.

Odet called from the next bend. "You need to see this."

They moved down the tunnel to where the east shed spur met a newer maintenance alcove. There, stacked behind a false panel, lay graft crates marked with Firebreak Seven's missing labels. Some were empty. Some held dead graft stock, dried past saving. Three still held viable bundles wrapped in cold-graft insulation, weak but not lost.

Tali cursed with such precision that Ro did not even try to stop her.

Sana knelt by the viable bundles. "Charbark apple. Ash pear. Ember olive."

Odet's breath caught.

"Can they be saved?"

"Some."

Tali was already opening her tool roll. "If we move now."

Ro looked down the tunnel toward the old road, then back toward the shed.

"We have confession, capsule scan, missing graft stock, recent route use, and evidence of deliberate record manipulation."

Sana lifted one viable ash pear graft. Its cambium was pale but alive.

"And work," she said.

They carried the living grafts out first.

Kade took one bundle without being asked. No one thanked him. No one stopped him. At the courier oath plate, he paused long enough to press his free hand to the words again.

This time he said nothing.

Maybe the road had already heard enough.


Chapter 15: Rain Tower Court

The rain tower court sat halfway up a column of captured weather.

Sana had seen the towers from orchard ridges all her life, thin white frames standing in rows above the green zones, each one strung with fog mesh, condenser ribs, pressure ladders, and the quiet machinery that persuaded the coast to give inland people a share of cloud. From below they looked skeletal and patient. From inside, with wind pushing at the glass and water ticking through a thousand channels around the walls, the tower felt less patient than hungry.

It drank the sky.

The court chamber had been built where the tower's middle reservoir ring widened into a circular platform. Rain ran down the outer panes in wavering sheets. Beyond them Cascadia fell away in layers: black fir, restored terraces, firebreak orchards, buried roads with alder growing through them, then the pale smear of Puget light under cloud. The North Rain Vault was not visible from here. That was deliberate. No one in a water court was supposed to argue from the sight of a locked door.

They argued from records, standing with wet cuffs and hard mouths while the tower pulled water out of the air above them.

Ro Tamsin stood at the petitioner rail with no notes in her hands.

That worried Sana more than notes would have.

Ro usually carried procedure the way Sana carried pruning shears: not because she expected to cut, but because she believed a good tool should be within reach before panic began. Here she had left her slate on the rail, dark except for the docket mark pulsing in one corner. Her palms rested flat beside it. Her face had the stillness she wore when she had already counted the rules and disliked the sum.

Behind her, Sana sat with Tali, Odet, Kade, and two tower clerks who smelled faintly of ozone from climbing through condenser galleries. The rescued graft bundles waited in a cold cradle beside Sana's boots, wrapped in new gel and tagged as evidence. Three living bundles did not look like much in court. They looked like sticks someone had loved too late.

Sana knew better. Charbark apple, ash pear, ember olive. Three lines from Firebreak Seven, taken off route, hidden, nearly killed, and still alive enough to accuse everyone in the room.

Kade had not sat down. He stood behind the witness rail with his hands loosely folded, as if keeping them visible was a form of apology. Since the tunnel he had spoken only when asked. The confession had not made him smaller. It had removed some false surface from him, and what remained was harder to dismiss.

Tali whispered, "I hate courts in infrastructure."

Sana kept her eyes on Ro. "You hate most courts."

"Yes, but this one sweats."

Odet leaned closer from Sana's other side. Her rain shell was still marked with tunnel dust and graft gel. "Does that help us?"

"Sweating?"

"The place. Water people hearing a seed release."

Sana looked around the chamber. The adjudicators sat above the petitioner rail on three suspended benches, each bench anchored by tension cables to the reservoir ring. They were not judges in the old national sense. Cascadia had spent two centuries breaking authority into smaller pieces and then tying those pieces together with emergency knots. Water courts decided allocation disputes, rain tower access, basin priority, and compact compliance whenever the weather, the pumps, or the food system made delay more dangerous than error.

Emergency seed unlocks crossed their jurisdiction only when a locked archive threatened water stability or food riots in a watershed zone.

"It should," Sana said. "That is the problem."

The center adjudicator, Merrow Pell, tapped the sound plate with two fingers. She was old enough to remember the Second Inland Rationing as a child and young enough to have built her career after the Compact had decided memory was not evidence unless sealed. Her hair was silver, braided close to her scalp. A rain tattoo climbed from her wrist into the sleeve of her court jacket, each line marking a tower she had served before promotion.

"Petition W-Tower-Seven-Forty-Three," Pell said. "Temporary unlock request for North Rain Vault seed channels, limited to drought pulse relief and corridor stabilization pending succession review. Petitioner: Ro Tamsin, mediator recognized by Basin Compact and Coastal Stewardship Table. Interested parties: Cascadia Watershed Compact; Puget Vertical Authority; Dry Basin Hunger Councils; Long Orchard Archive Office; insurer underwriters currently filed under seal."

At the underwriter phrase, Tali made a small sound that meant she was storing violence for later use.

On the opposing rail, Governor Hal Renn had not come in person. He appeared as a chest-high projection in a clean black coat, his background calibrated to show a vertical farm atrium bright with lettuce and schoolchildren. It was a good background. It said density, innocence, mouths. Beside the projection stood a live counsel from the Puget Vertical Authority, a woman named Cass Brindle whose calm had edges. Dr. Mirek Vale sat at a separate rail as technical respondent for the succession model, hands folded over a slate, expression grave enough to look almost kind.

Almost was doing too much work.

Ro inclined her head to the bench. "I request immediate provisional unlock of channels N-RV-12 through N-RV-18 for supervised release to Dry Basin corridors and firebreak cooperatives, subject to revocation after full review. The request is narrow. We are not asking the court to decide succession. We are asking the court to prevent the succession freeze from becoming a food decision disguised as caution."

Pell glanced to the left bench, where Adjudicator Sene was already reading the emergency matrix. The right bench, Adjudicator Ko, watched Kade.

"Grounds?" Pell asked.

Ro lifted her slate. "New evidence of misfiled blight flag, targeted pollinator interruption, missing graft stock, and deliberate manipulation of courier witness records. The North Rain Vault lock rests on a risk picture that is materially false. Maintaining the lock therefore creates the danger it claims to avoid."

Cass Brindle said, "Objection to characterization. The vault lock rests on verified archive status, not on petitioner's narrative of motive."

"Noted," Pell said. "Petitioner may establish."

Ro moved her hand once. The chamber wall behind her filled with a clean sequence: Graft Nine false provenance marks; the misplaced pathogen sample; Tali's map of pollinator drone failures along one corridor; Firebreak Seven missing shipment logs; the tunnel scan of the courier capsule; the cold cradle beside Sana's boots.

Sana had seen each piece separately. In the tower court they looked smaller and sharper, like pruning cuts. Anyone could claim a cut was minor until enough of them showed the tree had been shaped against its nature.

Ro did not raise her voice.

"The Compact froze emergency seed channels after Steward Voss's death because the archive returned a blight conflict tied to North Rain Vault holdings. Field audit has shown the pathogen was real but contextually displaced. It was not found in the threatened line. It was used to create an archive contradiction. Pollinator failures then struck the same corridor whose release requests would have been discredited by that contradiction."

Brindle touched her rail. "The petitioner says 'used' without proof of actor."

"The actor is not required for temporary unlock," Ro said. "Only material falsehood in the risk basis."

Pell looked at Ro over the rain shimmer. "Continue."

"Firebreak Seven reported missing graft stock two days ago. We recovered some of it from a concealed maintenance alcove along a restricted old courier route. The stock includes viable lineages needed for firebreak corridor stabilization. We also scanned a witness capsule naming Kade Morrow as courier witness to an exception release suppressed from the official ledger. That capsule includes archive dissent by Mara Venn and an instruction that the planted line not be destroyed without Hunger Council review."

Kade's eyes lowered at Mara's name.

Ro let the silence last just long enough to make everyone hear it.

"The official record omitted the exception, the dissent, and the instruction. The court is therefore facing a pattern: living material removed, records narrowed, release requests frozen, and dependent regions told to wait for a succession process whose evidentiary foundation is compromised."

Dr. Vale spoke for the first time. "May I clarify the model's position?"

Pell nodded.

Vale stood. He had chosen a plain field coat instead of formal black, an ethical costume Sana resented because it worked. "The triage model does not choose power. It estimates continuity under uncertainty. If uncertainty rises because the petitioner has discovered possible manipulation, the model does not automatically favor unlock. It may favor a narrower freeze until contamination is bounded."

"People cannot eat bounded contamination," Tali muttered.

Sana touched her wrist, a warning and agreement together.

Ro said, "A narrower freeze is still a freeze where there is hunger."

Vale's eyes moved to her. "And a premature release could spread corrupted or patent-encumbered lines into public corridors, creating legal and biological exposure across the watershed."

"Patent-encumbered?" Sana said before she meant to.

Every head in the chamber turned.

Pell's expression did not change. "Field Auditor Ilyen, you are not at the rail."

Sana stood anyway. "No patent encumbrance has been listed in the North Rain Vault lock notice."

"Auditor," Ro said quietly.

But Vale had already seen the opening. "Because the encumbrance question is historical and unresolved. It is one reason caution is warranted."

Sana felt the chamber tilt around a word she had not expected to matter yet. Patent. Old corporate claims were supposed to be dead in public seed lines, burned out by famine law, indemnified by the Compact, buried under living-use doctrine. Supposed to be dead did not mean dead. Seeds outlived governments. So did lawyers, if they became software.

Pell tapped the plate. "Auditor Ilyen will sit unless called."

Sana sat, heat in her face.

Ro did not look back. That was mercy.

"The respondent has introduced a new basis for freeze," Ro said. "If patent encumbrance is at issue, then the lock notice is incomplete and affected parties have been denied the chance to answer."

Brindle stepped in before Vale could. "The Puget Vertical Authority does not concede patent encumbrance. We do, however, agree that unresolved title risk supports procedural restraint."

"Title risk," Odet whispered, incredulous. "They are talking about title while basins ration breakfast."

Sana looked at the graft bundles. Viable cambium in gel. A court hearing the word title over living hunger.

Ro pressed. "Procedural restraint cannot be infinite. Under Water Court emergency authority, where delay causes irreversible food harm, the court may order supervised release."

Pell leaned back. Rain made moving shadows across her face. "May. Not must."

"Yes."

"And only where the court has competent evidence sufficient to determine that the emergency action is less dangerous than inaction."

"We have competent evidence."

Ko, on the right bench, finally spoke. "The courier capsule scan was conducted in the field without full seal breach, correct?"

"Outer scan only," Ro said. "To preserve the capsule."

"Not certified by an archive examiner."

"It can be certified."

"But is not certified now."

Ro's jaw shifted once. "No."

Sene said, "The recovered graft stock is marked from Firebreak Seven, but chain of custody from removal to recovery remains unknown."

"Because someone concealed it."

"Agreed. Unknown because concealed."

Ro inhaled slowly.

Pell said, "Kade Morrow's confession concerns an illegal transfer more than forty years old."

Kade lifted his head. "It concerns the suppressed exception."

"Mr. Morrow, you are not recognized."

He closed his mouth.

Pell's voice softened by no measurable amount. "The court understands the moral weight. We are discussing admissibility and authority."

Ro said, "That division is the danger."

The chamber went quiet enough that the water in the walls became loud.

Pell watched her. "Choose your next sentence carefully, Mediator."

Ro did. Sana could see her choose it. Not because she became cautious, but because she refused to waste the truth on a sentence the court could punish instead of hear.

"The Compact was built after people learned that lawful delay can kill as thoroughly as unlawful theft," Ro said. "The emergency provisions exist because our predecessors understood that records fail under pressure. They did not ask us to abandon evidence. They asked us to prevent procedure from becoming a locked gate with hungry people outside."

Hal Renn's projection flickered as he leaned forward somewhere in Puget. "And the people inside my towers? Are they props to this court? Nine million depend on vertical yield. If compromised lines enter shared water or pollinator routes, density pays first. The petitioner speaks as if rural hunger is the only hunger with moral force."

Ro turned toward him. "No. I speak as if your density has microphones."

Brindle said, "Objection."

Pell said, "Sustained. Mediator Tamsin."

Ro bowed her head a fraction. "Withdrawn."

But it had landed. Even the tower clerks looked down at their slates with the expression of people trying not to have faces.

Renn's projection recovered. "Puget is willing to share verified stock after full succession review. What we cannot accept is activist release based on a tunnel scan, stolen grafts, and a self-confessed courier criminal whose past actions may be the origin of the present contamination."

Kade flinched as if the words had physical weight.

Sana wanted to stand again. She did not. Ro had the rail. Ro was doing what Ro had come to do, and every interruption gave the court another way to make this about temperament instead of seeds.

Ro said, "If Mr. Morrow's past action created a ledger gap, the capsule shows an archive dissent correcting that gap. The later omission is the relevant act."

Vale answered with sorrow-shaped precision. "Or the capsule shows exactly why unilateral mercy is dangerous. One exception becomes one gap. One gap becomes a route for later misuse. That is not an argument for more exception. It is an argument for containment."

Tali's fingers dug into her own knee.

Pell conferred with Sene and Ko in a low channel. The rain continued its patient extraction from the sky. Sana looked through the glass at the slopes below and tried to imagine Amma Quill listening to this from a dry basin hall where breakfast had become a negotiation with children. She imagined Amma's face when the word containment arrived. Not surprise. That was worse. Some communities had been contained so often they recognized the shape from the first polite phrase.

The adjudicators separated.

Pell tapped the plate.

"The court finds that the petitioner has raised substantial concern regarding the integrity of the North Rain Vault risk basis. The court further finds that the evidence presented is not yet competent to support emergency unlock under Water Court authority. The field scan of the witness capsule requires archive certification. The recovered graft stock requires chain-of-custody reconstruction and biological screening. The blight-context claim requires independent confirmation. The question of historical title or patent encumbrance, once raised, requires notice to affected parties."

Sana felt Odet go very still beside her.

Ro did not move.

Pell continued, "Temporary unlock request is denied without prejudice. The court orders expedited evidentiary review within twelve days."

"Twelve days?" Odet said.

Pell looked toward her. "Citizen Odet Marr, you are cautioned."

Odet stood. "Firebreak Seven has three days before the south corridor loses leaf."

"Sit," Ro said, still facing the bench.

Odet sat because Ro's voice had changed. Not louder. Lower. The voice of someone holding a bridge while people crossed.

"The court orders preservation of all recovered graft stock under joint custody," Pell said. "The court orders the Long Orchard Archive Office to accept the capsule for immediate certification. The court orders parties to file any known patent, title, insurer, or legacy claim affecting North Rain Vault lines within forty-eight hours under penalty of sanction."

Brindle's calm sharpened. Vale's hands stilled.

There it was, Sana thought. Not a door opened. A crack in a wall they had not known was there.

Ro heard it too. "Does the preservation order include the planted line referenced in the capsule?"

Pell paused. "If identifiable, yes. No destruction or alteration pending review."

"And the court recognizes Hunger Council interest?"

Sene answered, "For the limited purpose of notice and testimony."

Limited. The word had become a fence. Still, fences marked where to push.

Pell struck the plate once. "Denied without prejudice. Orders to issue before tower close."

The hearing ended the way rain ends on metal: not all at once, but with a thinning of insistence. Projections collapsed. Brindle bent over her slate. Vale looked at Sana, and for the first time she saw irritation under his sorrow. Not fear. Not yet. Irritation, because someone had spoken a word he had intended to introduce later and now it belonged to the room.

Patent.

Ro stepped back from the rail. Her face was composed. Her hands shook when she picked up her slate.

Sana rose. "Ro."

"Not here."

They collected the graft cradle under clerk supervision and followed a spiral service ramp down from the court chamber. Water moved in pipes around them. Every few meters, a transparent section showed rain being filtered, mineral balanced, and measured into allocation channels. Numbers moved beside the flow: tower yield, basin claim, coastal draw, emergency reserve, evaporation loss. The tower never stopped counting.

Halfway down, Ro stopped in a maintenance alcove between two condensation drums and pressed her palms against the wall.

For a moment no one spoke.

Then Tali said, "I have several illegal suggestions."

Ro laughed once. It was not amusement. It was pressure escaping through a crack.

"No," she said.

"I did not say them yet."

"You arranged them alphabetically in your head."

"By tool type, actually."

Odet looked from one to the other, angry and lost. "We lost."

"Yes," Ro said.

Sana said, "We also forced patent claims into notice."

"That does not feed anyone today."

"No."

Odet's eyes shone. "Then why does it feel like everyone is waiting for us to be grateful for losing politely?"

Kade answered before Ro could. "Because polite loss is how systems teach hungry people to thank the lock for explaining itself."

No one contradicted him.

Ro straightened. "I am not grateful. I am inventorying."

"Inventorying what?" Odet asked.

"The shape of the obstruction."

She turned her slate toward them. During the hearing she had marked three phrases: patent encumbrance, insurer underwriters under seal, title risk. Each one had a time stamp tied to the speaker.

"Vale introduced patent risk before anyone filed it. Brindle adopted it as a procedural reason without conceding it. Pell ordered claims filed within forty-eight hours. If old claims exist, they must surface or hide harder. Either way, they move."

Tali's anger shifted, becoming technical. "And moving systems leave logs."

"Yes."

Sana looked at the rain channel beside them. A thin stream ran clear through glass, measured so precisely it seemed less like water than permission. "Where do old seed patent claims live after two hundred years?"

Kade looked up.

"Drowned places," he said.

They all turned to him.

He rubbed one hand over his mouth. "Not the public archive. Not if someone wanted them useful but deniable. Before the Compact absorbed the coastal seed companies, most claims were mirrored in corporate escrow servers along the old waterfront. After the sea walls failed, a lot of that became salvage jurisdiction. People assumed the water took it."

Tali stared. "People are idiots. Water preserves all kinds of inconvenient things."

Ro's eyes narrowed. "Do you know a site?"

"I know a route to one." Kade hesitated. "I never entered. Couriers carried around it. The maps called it Patent House, though that may have been a joke."

Sana thought of the next ring of the problem: not a vault door, but the ghost of ownership submerged under brackish water, still able to reach through law and close a seed line.

Odet said, "Firebreak Seven cannot wait twelve days."

"Then we do two things," Ro said. "We send Amma the order so Hunger Councils prepare testimony. We put the recovered grafts under joint custody, which keeps them alive and visible. And we find the claim before the claim finds us."

"That is three things," Tali said.

"I am expanding under pressure."

This time the laugh was real enough to hurt.

Sana looked back up the service ramp toward the court chamber. The tower was still drinking sky. Somewhere above them, adjudicators had denied the unlock because evidence had not yet learned the proper posture. Somewhere below, people would hear that the court had acted quickly and done nothing fast enough.

She took the cold cradle from the clerk. The ash pear bundle inside had begun to show a faint green line at one bud, a color so small it would have been easy to miss.

Easy, but not forgivable.

"We go to the drowned server ruin," Sana said.

Kade nodded.

Ro wiped rain condensation from her slate and opened a route map.

The tower counted water around them, drop by drop, as if counting could keep the world from owing more than it had.


Chapter 16: Drowned Patent House

The old waterfront had not drowned cleanly.

It had gone under by argument, meter by meter, lawsuit by lawsuit, temporary wall by temporary wall, every retreat named as resilience until the tide learned the names did not matter. The ruins south of the Puget verticals still showed the sequence. First came the elevated transit spine, severed in three places and threaded now with gull nests and emergency antennae. Beneath it stood the old financial towers, their lower floors sealed behind algae-dark flood glass, their upper windows broken into glittering teeth. Farther out, where streets had become canals and canals had become warnings, the corporate escrow district rose from brackish water like a set of black knuckles.

Patent House was the smallest of them.

That made Sana trust it less.

The building squatted on a drowned corner between two collapsed skybridges, six stories of basalt composite and old smartglass, its logo removed so thoroughly that the wall where it had been looked wounded. Salt weed grew from the second-floor balconies. Wind drove gray water through the lobby doors and back out again with the slow breath of a sleeping animal. The salvage markers on the nearest mooring posts were faded from yellow to bone.

"Not abandoned," Tali said.

She crouched in the bow of the skiff while the autopilot eased them between floating hazard buoys. Rain tapped her hood. A cable drone sat folded beside her knee, its legs magnetized, its camera eye dark. Since the court she had become intensely cheerful in the way engineers became cheerful when given a problem that could not talk back.

Ro looked up from the route map. "The registry says inactive salvage hold, no active custodian, hazard grade orange."

"That is a legal way of saying abandoned." Tali pointed to the third floor. "I mean the building disagrees."

Sana followed her finger. A drain slit above the third-floor flood line pulsed once with faint blue light.

Kade saw it too. "Old security heartbeat."

"Old?" Odet asked.

"The rhythm is old. The power is not."

Odet sat on the cold cradle with one hand on the latch, as if the grafts might try to escape if no one reminded them to stay alive. After the court, Ro had argued with two clerks, one archive liaison, and a water officer until the joint custody order allowed the viable bundles to travel under Sana's care as long as the cradle broadcast its seal every five minutes. The result was a living evidence box that complained whenever the skiff rocked too hard.

It complained now.

Sana braced it with her boot. "Sorry."

The cradle blinked amber, unimpressed.

Ro said, "We are not entering to retrieve property. We are entering to verify whether a known claim source is active in relation to an emergency food proceeding."

Tali looked back. "Are you telling us or rehearsing for prosecution?"

"Both."

"Efficient."

Kade had been quiet since they left the tower. He sat near the stern with a coil of line over one shoulder, watching the water move through the broken street grid. The old route had not been on public maps. He had drawn it from memory on Ro's slate, then corrected himself twice with visible discomfort, as if touching the memory too firmly might bruise someone dead.

Sana sat beside him as the skiff drifted toward the mooring teeth.

"You said couriers carried around this place," she said.

"Around, under, never through." He nodded toward the black glass. "Companies kept escrow mirrors there. Seed claims, licensing keys, indemnity triggers, contract ghosts. After the Famine Patents, public law stripped most of the claims. But stripping a claim from law and stripping it from every machine that remembers being paid to guard it are different tasks."

"Machines are not owed loyalty to dead owners."

"They are owed maintenance. Sometimes maintenance looks like loyalty."

The skiff bumped a rubberized piling. Tali caught a rail and hopped onto the mooring platform before the vessel settled. The platform flexed under her weight but held. She scanned the metal with a palm reader, then held up three fingers: live current, low load, no obvious trap.

"No obvious trap is not my favorite category," Ro said.

"It is in my top five."

They disembarked one by one. Salt wind pushed at Sana's rain shell. The water below slapped against old steps that vanished into brown-green depth. Something pale moved under the surface and resolved into a plastic chair turning slowly in the tide.

The entrance lobby had once been designed to intimidate people with money. The ceiling rose three stories, ribbed with black carbon beams. A reception island stood in the center, half submerged, its polished stone split by freeze cracks and barnacle seams. Old display walls showed nothing except their own damaged pixels. The floor under the water was invisible.

Tali released the cable drone. It unfolded, climbed the wall with soft magnetic taps, and sent a map to her wrist.

"Dry path above the lobby via maintenance ladder," she said. "Main stairs gone. Elevator shafts flooded. Server core likely behind rear vault wall, floors four through six."

Ro looked at Kade. "Likely?"

"Patent houses were built around cores," he said. "Offices outside, lawyers above, machines in the middle where no one could throw a brick through a window."

"That was a design criterion?"

"You would be surprised how much architecture is fear with better lighting."

They climbed.

The maintenance ladder ran behind a service panel that had swollen with salt and fungus. Tali cut the hinges with a narrow heat line, muttering at every corporate fastener as if each one had personally wronged her. The ladder smelled of rust, wet insulation, and old electrical fires. Sana climbed with the cold cradle strapped to her back, feeling the grafts' weight through the harness. Evidence, food, accusation, future. Too many categories for one small box.

On the third-floor landing, water noise receded. The corridor ahead was dry except for condensation running down walls printed with motivational slogans no one had bothered to remove.

OWN THE SEED OF TOMORROW.

Someone had scratched through OWN long ago and written FEED above it in black marker. The marker had faded less than the slogan.

Odet touched the wall. "Who wrote that?"

"Someone who knew the difference," Sana said.

Kade did not look at it.

The first security door recognized none of them and objected in a voice so polite it felt obscene.

Access unavailable. Custodial continuity pending.

Ro stepped forward. "Water Court emergency evidence inquiry. Recognize order Pell-W-Seven-Forty-Three."

The door paused.

Order not found in local archive.

"It was issued two hours ago."

Local archive last synchronized: one hundred seventeen years, four months, nine days ago.

Tali grinned. "Finally, a system with boundaries."

Sana said, "Can you open it?"

"I can ask badly."

Tali placed a contact pad on the lock plate. The cable drone descended from the ceiling and plugged a filament into a maintenance seam. Light crawled over the door in thin blue veins.

Kade stepped back.

Sana noticed. "What?"

"Old escrow locks ran legal simulations before physical release. They liked to make entrants agree to things."

Ro's eyes sharpened. "What things?"

"Liability. Noninterference. Recognition of custodial rights."

The door chimed.

By proceeding, entrant acknowledges provisional custodial standing of Ventrix Agronomic Holdings and agrees not to alter, copy, damage, export, disclose, or materially interpret protected claim instruments without authorized counsel present.

Tali stared at the door. "Materially interpret?"

"They tried to patent understanding," Kade said.

Ro smiled without warmth. "Decline."

Access denied.

"Override under emergency public necessity."

Public necessity not recognized by Ventrix Agronomic Holdings active charter.

"Ventrix no longer exists."

Corporate continuity pending.

Odet made a strangled sound. "It has been pending for a century."

Sana looked at the door, then at the slogan behind them. Own the seed of tomorrow. Feed the seed of tomorrow. The building had kept the first command longer than the city had kept the street.

Tali said, "I can bypass the lock without accepting terms, but it will know I bypassed it."

"How loudly?" Ro asked.

"Depends who is listening."

Before anyone answered, the door opened three centimeters.

Not enough for a person. Enough for breath.

A strip of interior light touched the corridor floor.

Sana's throat tightened.

Tali lifted both hands. "That was not me."

The door spoke again, but the voice had changed. The corporate courtesy remained, stretched over a second signal like skin over a wire frame.

Courier route credential detected.

Everyone looked at Kade.

He had gone gray.

"I did not send anything," he said.

The door widened to a handspan.

Morrow key lineage recognized. Exception chain unresolved. Counsel absent. Archive dissent pending.

Ro whispered, "Mara."

Kade's face moved once, pain crossing it too quickly to name.

"Or the thing that stored her dissent," Sana said.

The door opened the rest of the way.

They entered because the alternative was to stand outside a truth and ask whether it had filled the proper forms.

Inside, Patent House had kept its climate better than its dignity. The server core corridor was cold and dry. Transparent floor panels showed cable bundles running below them in sealed troughs. The walls were lined with claim cabinets: black drawers with embedded legal seals, each marked by crop family, region, release date, and dispute class. Most seals were dark. Some pulsed faintly.

Too many pulsed.

Tali's cheer vanished. "This is not dormant."

Ro lifted her slate. "Record everything."

"Already recording."

Kade walked like a man in a house where he had once wronged someone but could not remember which room. The lights ahead brightened as he moved, not for the rest of them. Sana stayed close enough to see his hands.

At the central node, the corridor opened into a vertical chamber. Server stacks climbed three floors around an empty shaft, their black faces beaded with condensation. In the center hung an old legal engine: a cube of cloudy glass suspended by carbon struts, full of slow amber light. Around it rotated thousands of thin projection tiles, each holding a fragment of text too small to read until summoned.

The engine woke.

Ventrix Agronomic Holdings escrow mirror active.

Ro said, "State current charter authority."

Charter authority: contested. Continuity action filed. Resolution pending.

"Filed by whom?"

The amber light shifted.

Insurer consortium proxy. Puget Vertical Authority notice agent. Long Orchard Archive compatibility counsel. Additional sealed parties.

Sana felt cold move through her that had nothing to do with the room. "Compatibility counsel?"

Ro's mouth flattened. "That sounds internal."

Tali's fingers flew over her wrist slate. "It is pinging. Not outward wideband. Tightbeam relay through tower infrastructure."

"Stop it."

"Trying."

The engine continued as if no one had spoken.

Recent query detected: North Rain Vault line cluster N-RV-12 through N-RV-18. Claim instruments identified. Patent survival uncertain. Use restrictions asserted pending adjudication.

Ro stepped closer. "Who made the recent query?"

Identity shielded by counsel privilege.

"Counsel for whom?"

Privilege shield active.

Tali cursed. "It is wrapping the query in legal encryption."

Odet pointed at one projection tile that had enlarged beside the cube. "That says Firebreak."

Sana moved toward it.

The tile sharpened.

FIREBREAK SEVEN / ASH PEAR DERIVATIVE / VENTRIX DROUGHT-CARBON ROOT PLATFORM / PUBLIC RELEASE CONTESTED / RESTRICTION MAY ATTACH TO DESCENDANT LINE IF EMERGENCY PROPAGATION EXCEEDS LICENSED BASIN.

Odet whispered, "Licensed basin?"

"They cannot do that," Sana said.

The engine answered.

Statement interpretive. Legal conclusion unavailable.

"I was not asking you."

More tiles unfolded. Charbark apple. Ember olive. Pear-rice platform. Dry basin millet grafts. Fungal protein starter cultures. Old claims, dormant claims, claims marked void, claims marked uncertain, claims marked revived for review after emergency notice.

Revived.

That word turned Sana's stomach.

Ro saw it too. "Can you export the query log?"

Export prohibited pending counsel review.

"Water Court order."

Order not found in local archive.

"Then we certify by observation."

Ro began dictating into her slate, voice level, each word a stake driven into wet ground. "Observed active escrow mirror within submerged corporate server ruin known as Patent House. Observed recent query for North Rain Vault cluster N-RV-12 through N-RV-18. Observed assertion of use restrictions over Firebreak Seven derivative lines, including ash pear, charbark apple, ember olive. Observed parties listed as insurer consortium proxy, Puget Vertical Authority notice agent, Long Orchard Archive compatibility counsel, and sealed parties."

At the phrase Long Orchard Archive compatibility counsel, the engine dimmed.

Tali looked up sharply. "It did not like that."

Kade said, "It is not alone."

They heard the sound then: metal on metal below them, not the drone, not the building settling. A hatch closing.

Ro stopped dictating.

Tali sent the cable drone down the shaft. Its feed flickered onto her wrist. For half a second Sana saw a lower access gantry, water stains, a figure in a gray hazard coat, and a hand pulling a relay cartridge from a wall.

Then the feed went black.

"Someone is here," Tali said.

Odet pulled the cold cradle closer, as if she could shield the grafts from a person three floors below.

Sana moved to the shaft rail. "Identify yourself."

Her voice fell into the server chamber and came back broken by machines.

No answer.

Kade leaned over the rail. "That exit floods at high tide."

Tali said, "Helpful if they are patient enough to drown."

"There is another way out through the counsel floors."

"You said you never entered."

"Couriers listen."

Ro said, "Can they erase the log?"

Tali was already moving. "They can sever the relay and claim the mirror never answered. They cannot erase what we recorded unless they take us too."

"Then we leave with what we have."

The engine spoke again.

Unauthorized material interpretation detected. Injunctive preservation recommended.

The corridor door behind them began to close.

Tali threw a jammer the size of a plum. It struck the floor, bounced once, and emitted a brutal white pulse. The door froze halfway.

"Now," she said.

They ran.

The server corridor seemed longer on the way out. Claim cabinets flashed as they passed, each one waking to name another old ownership. Sana heard fragments like accusations: drought platform, pollen vector, root carbon exchange, basin-limited license, famine exception contested. The past had not stayed buried. It had been waiting for hunger to make its terms valuable again.

At the security door, Kade stopped.

"Move," Ro said.

He put one hand against the frame. The gap shuddered, held by Tali's jammer.

"Kade."

"Mara's dissent is in here."

Sana grabbed his sleeve. "So is whoever came to erase the relay."

"If we leave it, they bury her again."

Odet said, "If we stay, they bury us with her."

That reached him. Or perhaps Mara did. He pulled away from the frame and ran.

They descended the maintenance ladder too fast. Twice Sana's boot slipped on wet rungs. The cold cradle banged against her back and protested in sharp amber flashes. Below, the lobby had grown louder. Tide was entering through the doors in choppy waves, carrying leaves, plastic, and a slick of oil that rainbowed over the water.

Tali's drone did not return.

At the mooring platform, the skiff was still tied, but another vessel's wake slapped the pilings. A narrow service boat was already moving away through the ruined street, its running lights off.

Ro lifted her slate and captured its outline.

"Can you tag it?" Sana asked.

"Done."

Tali looked at the signal and shook her head. "Spoofed municipal salvage mark."

Kade watched the boat vanish behind a collapsed skybridge. "Not salvage."

"No," Ro said. "Counsel."

They shoved off as the water rose around the platform. The Patent House receded behind them, black glass reflecting a sky the color of old metal. On the third floor, the blue security heartbeat pulsed once, twice, then went dark.

For a minute no one spoke. The skiff's motor kept low, threading them between drowned traffic lights and kelp-wrapped signposts. Puget's vertical farms glowed to the north, stacked green rectangles bright enough to pretend the world had been solved.

Sana opened the cold cradle. The ash pear bud still showed its faint line of green. It had survived the tower, the court, and the drowned house. It did not care who claimed its ancestor. It cared about cambium, moisture, light, and whether human beings could stop making hunger into paperwork long enough to plant.

Ro sent the recorded packet to three addresses: Water Court emergency docket, Amma Quill's hunger council channel, and a private archive contact whose name Sana did not know.

"Will that be enough?" Odet asked.

Ro watched the send marks turn sealed. "No."

"Then why send it?"

"Because not enough is different from nothing."

Tali stared at the blank place where her drone feed had been. "They saw us see it."

"Good," Sana said.

The others looked at her.

She closed the cradle. "Now they know the buried claims are not invisible. If they move to protect them, they make more tracks. If they stop moving, the claims stay exposed."

Kade's voice was hoarse. "And Mara?"

Sana thought of the door opening to his old key, of a dead archivist's dissent stored in a machine built to defend ownership. "We go back for her record."

Ro looked south, toward the black district sinking by inches and refusing to die. "First we find out why Long Orchard counsel was in that mirror."

Salt spray struck Sana's face. It tasted like old ocean, old law, and something newly broken open.

Behind them, Patent House stood in the tide with its name erased and its claims awake.


Chapter 17: Salt in the Archives

The private archive contact answered from a greenhouse full of knives.

At least that was how it looked at first.

The call opened on ranks of silver leaves, each blade long as Sana's forearm and edged with tiny monitoring teeth. The plants grew in suspended trays under violet light, root systems exposed in clear nutrient mist. Every few seconds the leaves flexed together, not in wind but in response to some invisible pulse, and the greenhouse made a sound like a room of people quietly sharpening scissors.

Then a woman stepped between the rows and the illusion became botany again.

"Ro Tamsin," the woman said. "Your emergency packets have become less boring."

Ro had taken over a rented analysis berth in a harbor archive annex, three blocks inland from the drowned district and high enough that the old tide marks stopped at the basement stairs. The berth contained two benches, a sealed sample hood, a wall reader certified for court evidence, and a window facing a rain gutter full of salt grass. Sana had spread the evidence around her with the care of someone setting out surgical tools: graft cradle, field notes, Patent House recording, pollinator failure maps, tunnel capsule scan, and the court order that had refused to save anyone quickly but had forced old claims to move.

Kade sat near the door. Odet slept badly in a chair with one hand still on the graft cradle. Tali was building a substitute cable drone from parts bought from three harbor vendors and one person who had asked no questions because Tali had asked hers first.

Ro rubbed one hand over her face. "Nin Saye, this is Sana Ilyen, field auditor. Sana, Nin maintains pre-Compact archive recovery protocols for the Monastery of Living Indexes."

Nin Saye's eyes sharpened through the call. She was narrow-faced, brown-skinned, with close-cropped gray hair and a work apron patterned in old burn marks. "Iona's niece," Sana said.

"Depending on whether I owe her money, yes." Nin looked past Ro, taking in the room. "You went into Patent House."

"Briefly," Ro said.

"That is how people usually survive it."

Sana leaned into the call. "You received the recording?"

"Enough of it. Your signal was dirty with salt scatter and legal noise. I can confirm the mirror is active. I can confirm the claim language is not generic. I can also confirm you have made several institutions unhappy."

Tali called from the floor, "Do any of them repair drones?"

"Not voluntarily."

"Then my sympathy is limited."

Nin's mouth twitched. "Send me the Firebreak derivative frame."

Ro pushed the relevant packet. The wall reader repeated the Patent House tile in court-certified text:

FIREBREAK SEVEN / ASH PEAR DERIVATIVE / VENTRIX DROUGHT-CARBON ROOT PLATFORM / PUBLIC RELEASE CONTESTED / RESTRICTION MAY ATTACH TO DESCENDANT LINE IF EMERGENCY PROPAGATION EXCEEDS LICENSED BASIN.

Nin read it once. Her expression did not change, which told Sana it was worse than surprise.

"That phrasing is old," Nin said.

"How old?" Ro asked.

"Corporate famine era. Early claim survival language. Most of it was voided after the Inland Hunger Marches, but the machines kept dormant interpretations in case a later court became more friendly."

"Courts do not become friendly by accident," Ro said.

"No. They are cultivated." Nin lifted a silver leaf aside and clipped something from a stem. "Sana, do your recovered grafts carry ceramic provenance flecks?"

"Yes."

"Read one under salt contrast."

Sana frowned. "Salt contrast is for marine contamination."

"It is also good for old corporate watermarking. Public auditors stopped using it when the Compact standardized living-use tests. That was convenient for everyone who preferred the old marks stay politely invisible."

Sana looked at the cold cradle.

Odet opened her eyes. "What does that mean?"

"It means," Sana said slowly, "that we are about to ask a plant a question I should have known to ask earlier."

She lifted the ash pear bundle into the sample hood. The graft looked frail under the berth lights, no thicker than two fingers, its cut end sealed with rescue gel, its small bud green enough to hurt. She removed a sterile bark flake from beside the old archive seal and placed it on the reader stage.

The standard provenance scan returned what she expected: Firebreak Seven cooperative line, public corridor release, drought-carbon root platform, Compact indemnified, living-use open.

Open.

The word sat on the screen like a promise.

"Now salt contrast," Nin said.

Sana changed the reader mode. The machine warned that salt contrast could degrade organic trace quality if repeated. She acknowledged once. A fine mist of tagged saline crossed the sample. The ceramic flecks in the bark caught light differently, not the clean white Sana had seen a thousand times, but a hidden violet shimmer under the public mark.

The reader hesitated.

Secondary mark detected.

Odet stood.

Tali stopped building.

Sana felt her pulse move into her hands. "Display."

The screen unfolded a second provenance layer beneath the first:

VENTRIX DCRP-4 / BASIN-LIMITED PLATFORM / DESCENDANT CLAIM RETAINED IN ESCROW / PUBLIC RELEASE SUBJECT TO COMPATIBILITY REVIEW.

For a moment the room had no sound except the mist cycling in the hood.

Ro said, very softly, "Salt in the archive."

Nin nodded through the call. "Hidden under the public fleck. Not active enough to trigger standard field audit. Active enough for an escrow mirror to recognize when someone asks the right old question."

Sana stared at the screen until the letters blurred.

She thought of every row she had walked in Graft Nine, every living seal she had trusted because the Compact had taught auditors that public release meant clean release. She thought of apprentices laughing in yellow rain shells while trees carried two histories in their bark: one for the commons, one for a dead company's machine.

"How many lines?" she asked.

Nin's silence answered first.

"We do not know."

"Guess."

"If Firebreak Seven carries it, then any corridor descended from Ventrix drought-carbon root platforms may carry it. Pear-rice. Ash pear. Some dry millet grafts. Possibly flood bean supports if the same platform was used in the southern trials."

Odet said, "That is not one patent ghost."

"No," Sana said. "It is a graveyard under an orchard."

Kade made a small sound near the door. When Sana looked at him, he was watching the screen as if it had accused him personally.

"This predates you," she said.

"Not all guilt has to be earned to be inherited."

Ro turned to Nin. "Could Long Orchard Archive compatibility counsel know this?"

Nin's face changed at the exact phrase. Only slightly. Enough.

"That office exists to prevent conflicts between living-use doctrine and archived obligations," she said.

"Archived obligations," Tali repeated. "That is an elegant way to say dead people still billing the hungry."

Nin did not disagree. "The office was supposed to identify old claims and extinguish them quietly before they could interfere with public release."

"Supposed to," Ro said.

"Yes."

Sana removed the bark flake and sealed it in an evidence slide. Her hands wanted to shake. She did not let them.

"If someone in compatibility counsel queried Patent House, they knew a court fight was coming."

"Or they helped create one," Ro said.

Nin looked away from the call. The silver leaves flexed behind her in a coordinated ripple. "Be careful with that conclusion."

"Because it might be wrong?"

"Because it might be right without being complete. Compatibility counsel may have been trying to map exposure. They may have been trying to suppress it. They may be split. Institutions are rarely one liar. They are usually arguments with doors."

Kade said, "Doors can still lock."

Nin's gaze moved to him. "Kade Morrow."

He stiffened.

"Iona said you were dead twice."

"I disappointed several expectations."

"Good. Stay inconvenient." She turned back to Sana. "You need a broader sample. Not just Firebreak. Test a supposedly clean public line from Graft Nine and a Dry Basin hunger line if you can get one."

Ro said, "I can request samples under the court preservation order."

"Requesting will warn people."

"Not requesting will make the evidence vulnerable."

"Both are true."

Tali lifted a half-built drone leg. "I would like to note that evidence is already vulnerable to people in gray coats stealing relays."

Odet wrapped her arms around herself. "If these marks are in the grafts, can the court stop us from planting?"

Ro did not answer quickly enough.

That was an answer.

Sana closed the sample hood. "A court can stop a release. It cannot stop a tree from needing ground."

"That sounds meaningful," Odet said, "but I need to know whether Firebreak Seven gets leaves."

Sana met her eyes. Odet had followed them from supper table to tunnel to tower to drowned server ruin because she had three living bundles and a town with three days of leaf. The scale of the conspiracy did not make her problem smaller. If anything, it made every abstraction more cruel.

"I am going to keep those grafts alive," Sana said. "And I am going to find a lawful way to plant them where they can hold the corridor."

"Lawful," Tali said. "There is that troublesome word."

Ro said, "Lawful gives the planting a defense."

"Sometimes defense arrives after the damage."

"Then we make the defense arrive faster."

Nin said, "There may be another way. Living-use doctrine has an emergency salt exception."

Ro looked up sharply. "No it does not."

"Not in current training."

"Where?"

"Early Compact commentary. Written after the first coastal archive salvage, when auditors found seawater had altered stored seed records and corporate marks could not be distinguished from contamination. The exception says that where salt-damaged archive claims interfere with famine prevention, living trustees may treat the claim as evidentiary noise until a public tribunal proves otherwise."

Sana felt the shape of it before the words settled. "Salt-damaged claims."

"Yes."

"But these marks are not damaged. They are hidden."

Nin smiled thinly. "A distinction beloved by counsel. The commentary speaks of claims made unreliable by salt exposure, archival displacement, or broken custody. Patent House is drowned. Its local archive is one hundred seventeen years out of sync. Its recent relay was tampered with by an unidentified actor. Its claim instruments are not clean."

Ro was already searching her slate. "I have never seen this exception cited."

"Because if you cite a tool that lets people plant before ownership finishes speaking, ownership learns to take the tool away."

Tali pointed at Nin with the drone leg. "I like her."

"I am not always likeable," Nin said.

"That has never been my criterion."

Ro found nothing on her slate and looked frustrated in a way Sana recognized: a procedural mind discovering that procedure had been pruned.

"Send it," Ro said.

"I cannot send what is not digitized in public channels. I can give you a location."

Sana knew before Nin said it.

"The Monastery," she said.

Nin nodded. "Iona has the early commentary in paper, fungal copy, and one very stubborn memory. She will deny the memory if you ask rudely."

Ro exhaled. "That is inland."

"Everything important is inconveniently located."

Odet said, "Firebreak Seven does not have time for a monastery trip."

Nin's eyes softened. "No. But a citation can travel faster than people if someone reads it aloud under seal."

"Iona," Sana said.

"Iona," Nin agreed. "She owes me no money today, so she may answer."

Ro sent a sealed call request. It returned pending.

While they waited, Sana tested the charbark apple and ember olive. Both carried the public mark. Both carried the hidden violet shimmer. The exact claim strings differed, but the pattern was the same: public release on the surface, escrow-retained descendant claim beneath, compatibility review as the hinge.

Three living bundles. Three ghosts.

Then Sana tested a control sample from her own field kit, a dried reference fleck from Graft Nine pear-rice used in training audits. It should have been clean. It had been certified seven times, by three offices, across nine years.

Salt contrast lit violet.

Sana stepped back from the reader.

Ro saw her face. "What?"

Sana turned the screen.

GRAFT NINE / PEAR-RICE PLATFORM / PUBLIC RELEASE INDEMNIFIED / VENTRIX DCRP-2 DERIVATIVE / COMPATIBILITY REVIEW RETAINED.

No one spoke.

The room seemed to shrink around the reader. The harbor annex, the salt grass in the gutter, the tired window, the sleeping city behind rain. Sana had thought the corruption was aimed at vulnerable corridors. Now she saw the older cruelty: vulnerability had been prepared everywhere and activated where hunger made resistance weakest.

"My orchard," she said.

Kade stood. "Sana."

"My orchard carries it."

Ro said, "A training fleck is not necessarily current stock."

"It is from Row C-17's release family."

The same row where petals had fallen early. The same row where the first wrong seal had started everything. Sana had been walking under ghosts and calling them provenance.

Her slate chimed.

The sealed request to Iona Saye had connected without image.

An old woman's voice filled the berth, dry as stored beans. "If this is about the salt exception, read fast. I am in the middle of arguing with a plum tree."

Ro closed her eyes for one grateful second.

Sana leaned toward the speaker. "Iona, we need the exact language."

"Of course you do. Everyone needs exact language after ignoring useful language for two centuries." Paper rustled. "Early Compact Commentary, Living-Use Doctrine, Coastal Salvage Addendum. Clause seven. 'Where an archive claim has been exposed to salt intrusion, broken custody, displaced storage, or machine interpretation not synchronized with public famine law, and where delay in release creates probable hunger harm, living trustees may proceed under provisional planting obligation. Such action shall preserve evidence, notify affected councils, and submit to public tribunal, but no private or dormant claim shall suspend necessary propagation absent proof of present biological danger.'"

Ro was writing before the second sentence ended.

Iona continued, "Clause eight is better, but no one likes it."

"Read it," Sana said.

"'A seed line cannot be made guilty of the paperwork that survived beside it.'"

Odet made a sound like a sob and a laugh refusing to choose.

Ro said, "Can you seal that reading?"

"Already did. I may be old, not theatrical."

Tali whispered, "I definitely like her."

Iona's voice sharpened. "Who said that?"

"No one important," Tali said.

"Then become important quietly. Sana, are you there?"

"Yes."

"You sound like someone who found salt where she expected law."

Sana looked at the violet marks on the reader. "I found it in my own orchard."

The line was silent for a moment.

"Good," Iona said.

Sana almost laughed from the shock of it. "Good?"

"Painful, not good. Useful. People defend distant victims with speeches. They defend their own compromised ground with teeth."

Sana looked at Odet, at Kade, at Ro's moving hands, at Tali's half-built drone, at the grafts under the hood. Her anger had been wide before. Now it had roots.

Ro sealed the commentary citation with the Patent House recording, the salt contrast scans, and the court order. "This gives us an emergency planting argument."

"An argument," Iona said. "Not permission."

"I know."

"Then stop waiting for permission to become the only moral shape of action."

The call ended.

Rain struck the window. Somewhere in the harbor a warning horn sounded twice. Tali returned to her drone with a new intensity. Kade stood by the door, face shadowed, as if he were listening for footsteps in the hall. Odet had both hands on the cold cradle now, not guarding it from escape but holding on.

Sana saved each scan in three sealed copies.

Then she opened a new field order under her own auditor authority.

Ro watched her. "What are you doing?"

"Provisional planting obligation."

"For Firebreak Seven?"

"For Firebreak Seven first." Sana's fingers moved steadily. "And for Graft Nine review. And for every line where salt claims have been allowed to sleep under public bark."

Ro did not stop her.

Odet whispered, "Does that mean we can plant?"

Sana sent the order to the Water Court docket, Amma Quill, Firebreak Seven, and the Long Orchard Archive Office. The last address took one extra second to accept, as if surprised to be accused by a form it had once taught her to use.

"It means," Sana said, "that if they want to stop us, they have to prove the tree is dangerous instead of merely claimed."

For the first time since the court, Odet breathed like the air had reached all the way into her.

Ro's slate chimed with an incoming notice.

She read it once.

Her expression hardened.

"Puget media just published a story," she said. "Dry Basin militants accused of attempting to steal disputed seed stock from a protected archive."

Tali looked up. "We were in a drowned corporate ruin."

"They have images of the skiff leaving Patent House."

Kade shut his eyes.

Sana felt the old machinery turn: ownership wounded, hunger blamed, proof made suspect before it could become public. The salt had entered more than archives. It was in the story now, preserving what should have rotted.

Ro turned the slate toward them. The headline filled the wall reader.

BASIN AGITATORS TARGET SEED CLAIM SITE AMID SUCCESSION CRISIS.

Below it was a blurred image of the skiff. Odet's face was visible beside the graft cradle.

Odet stared. "They will come to Firebreak."

Sana closed the field order and stood.

"Then we get there first."

Outside, rain washed the harbor windows clean for a moment. Through the glass, the city looked almost honest.


Chapter 18: A City That Eats First

Puget ate in vertical light.

From the east approach, the city rose in stacked rectangles of green and white, each farm tower bright enough to stain the low clouds. Lettuce floors glowed over mushroom vaults. Bean ladders climbed through transparent shafts. Pollinator galleries shimmered behind mesh with the disciplined movement of insects bred to forget weather. At night, when the old waterfront became a dark mouth behind them, the Puget verticals looked like a promise Earth had made to itself: no more empty shelves, no more ration chalk, no more children measuring hunger by the sound of adults not eating.

Promises became dangerous when they learned to point.

The road to Firebreak Seven passed under three public media spans. Each span carried the same story in different colors.

BASIN AGITATORS TARGET SEED CLAIM SITE.

DRY CORRIDOR DEPENDENTS DEMAND RISKY RELEASE.

PUCKET FEEDS MILLIONS WHILE LOW-YIELD REGIONS THREATEN COMMON SUPPLY.

The third span misspelled Puget for six seconds before correcting itself. Tali noticed.

"Good," she said from the back of the transport. "Let their propaganda be incompetent."

Ro did not look up from the legal packet she was rebuilding for the fourth time. "Incompetent propaganda still arrives."

"Yes, but I resent clean malice."

Odet sat between them with the cold cradle strapped across her knees. The public story had changed her posture more than the court had. In the tower she had been angry with people who would not move fast enough. Now she seemed to understand that strangers were being invited to hate her before she arrived home.

Kade drove. He had chosen the oldest ground route instead of a tower shuttle because the media story included their skiff image, because tower transit would log the cradle, and because couriers did not survive by taking the route their enemies had time to imagine. The transport smelled of wet canvas, graft gel, old battery heat, and the bitter tea Tali had spilled while swearing at a broadcast.

Sana watched the city pass.

Every Puget tower displayed yield figures along its lower skin: calories per liter, protein index, school ration stability, fungal reserve days, infant supplement coverage. The numbers were not lies. That was what made them effective. Puget did feed millions. Its density was not a trick. Its children were not less real because politicians placed them behind lettuce in carefully framed atriums.

But numbers could be arranged like walls.

The media span shifted to a governor's address. Hal Renn appeared thirty meters tall, standing inside a vertical farm with a child on either side of him. His face carried the practiced exhaustion of a man willing to be seen bearing responsibility.

"Puget will always share," he said, voice filling the road. "But sharing cannot mean surrendering verified food security to unvetted actors who enter protected claim sites, remove disputed seed material, and demand emergency privilege over the millions who depend on stable production. Compassion without verification is not justice. It is negligence."

Odet made a low sound.

Sana said, "Do not answer him in your head."

"I am answering him with several things I cannot say on public roads."

"Good. Save them for someone who can be made to hear."

Ro looked up. "He is using our evidence language."

"What?"

"Verified. Protected. Disputed. Emergency privilege. He took the court vocabulary and put it around his accusation."

Tali leaned forward. "Can we sue him for theft of procedural furniture?"

"No."

"Shame."

Kade kept his eyes on the road. "He does not need people to believe every word. He needs them to feel that hunger elsewhere is a hand reaching into their child's bowl."

The governor's image changed to a diagram: Puget's towers as green pillars, Dry Basin corridors as brown channels, arrows showing proposed seed movement toward "low-yield dependents." The phrase appeared in soft gray, as if cruelty became technical when colored politely.

Sana's hands curled.

"They are not low-yield," she said. "They are low-water, high-variance, corridor-stabilizing regions. Their orchards prevent fire jumps. Their millet lines keep soil from leaving. Their fungal trades backstop Puget protein when towers fail."

Ro's mouth tightened. "Say that later."

"To whom?"

"Everyone."

The transport left the last Puget span and entered the broken belt between city and fire country. The light changed first. Puget's vertical glow faded behind them, replaced by rain-dim afternoon and the dull red of burned hills recovering under sown moss. Salvage lots gave way to buffer orchards. Then the road narrowed, running between windbreaks of smoke pine and ash pear. Signs appeared on fence posts:

FIREBREAK SEVEN COOPERATIVE.

NO PRIVATE CLAIM ENFORCEMENT WITHOUT COUNCIL WITNESS.

WATER IS A VISITOR. FEED IT SHADE.

At the last sign, Odet pressed one hand to the cradle as if answering a door.

Then they saw the checkpoint.

Three Compact security vehicles blocked the road before the cooperative gate. Not Puget police. Not private insurer guards. Compact security, which was worse because it meant someone had remembered how to make force look neutral. A dozen officers stood in rain shells the color of wet stone. Two carried archive seal cases. One held a portable biological hazard hood. A media drone hovered above them at legal distance, pretending not to be part of the scene.

Firebreak Seven residents had gathered on the other side of the gate: Lidia An, Jori, the supper hall cooks, orchard hands, two children on a fence rail, old people in smoke shawls. They stood quietly, which made the officers look louder.

Odet unbuckled before the transport stopped.

Ro caught her sleeve. "Wait."

"That is my gate."

"And that is a camera."

Odet looked up at the media drone. Its lens turned slightly, hungry as any animal.

Sana took the cold cradle. "I will carry it."

"They will say you stole it."

"They are already saying that."

Kade parked at an angle that blocked the media drone's clean view of Odet's face. Tali stepped out first, looking at the drone with such focused intent that it rose three meters.

"Coward," she told it.

Ro exited with her slate already open. Sana followed with the cradle held against her chest. The living grafts were inside, cold, tagged, broadcasting their court seal every five minutes. Evidence and food. The distinction mattered to the law. It did not matter to the buds.

The lead officer approached. "Sana Ilyen?"

"Yes."

"I am Captain Arlen Vos, Compact Security. We have an emergency preservation request from the Long Orchard Archive Office and a hazard concern filed by Puget Vertical Authority notice counsel. You are instructed to surrender disputed graft material pending review."

Odet said, "No."

The media drone dipped.

Ro stepped half a pace forward. "Captain Vos, the grafts are under Water Court joint custody and field auditor care. They are also subject to a provisional planting obligation filed under living-use doctrine."

Vos looked tired in a way Sana distrusted. Tired people could do harm while believing they were only finishing paperwork.

"I have seen your filing," he said. "It is contested."

"By whom?"

"Archive Office, Puget notice counsel, insurer proxy, and two sealed parties."

Tali said, "All the people whose ghosts we annoyed."

Vos ignored her. "The concern is biological and public order risk."

Sana said, "Name the biological danger."

"Unverified derivative lines with disputed claim status may expose public corridors to-"

"That is legal danger. Name the biological danger."

Vos blinked.

Ro's expression did not change, but Sana felt her attention sharpen beside her.

"Auditor Ilyen," Vos said, "I am not here to litigate."

"Then do not use litigation as biology."

The Firebreak side of the gate made a sound, not quite a cheer. Lidia An lifted one hand and the sound stopped. She had the authority of a woman who could silence a town by reminding it not to waste breath.

Vos lowered his voice. "You are on a public road with a charged crowd, an active media narrative, and disputed plant material. Help me keep this from becoming something no one controls."

"Then let us through."

"I cannot."

"You can. You choose not to."

His mouth tightened. "I have orders."

Kade said from behind Sana, "Orders are where people hide when they still want to think of themselves as movable."

Vos looked at him. Recognition flickered. "Kade Morrow."

"Unfortunately durable."

"There is also a witness warrant pending for you."

Ro said, "No warrant has been served."

"Pending," Vos repeated.

The media drone dropped another meter.

Sana understood the shape then. Not arrest, not yet. Pressure. Make them argue at the gate. Put Odet's frightened face in the broadcast. Make Firebreak look like a crowd around stolen food. Make Sana choose between surrendering the grafts and appearing reckless. A city that ate first did not need to crush the basin. It only needed the basin to look like a threat.

She turned away from Vos and faced the media drone.

Ro hissed, "Sana."

Sana lifted the cold cradle high enough for the lens to see the court seal.

"This material is alive," she said.

The drone's record light brightened.

"It is not contraband. It is not a weapon. It is three damaged graft lines recovered from concealment after Firebreak Seven reported missing stock. They are under Water Court joint custody. They have been salt-contrast tested. The only danger identified so far is not biological. It is an old private claim hidden under a public mark."

Vos said, "Auditor-"

She kept speaking.

"Puget feeds millions. That is true. Firebreak Seven protects the corridors that keep fire from taking the orchards that feed other regions. That is also true. Dry Basin families are not dependents reaching into a city's bowl. They are part of the system that keeps the bowl from breaking."

The Firebreak residents were utterly still.

"If anyone wants to stop these grafts from being planted under provisional obligation, show present biological danger. Show blight. Show contamination. Show harm that belongs to the plant, not paperwork that survived beside it."

The phrase moved through the crowd. Paperwork that survived beside it. Iona's language, carried on Sana's anger.

Vos stepped closer. "You are escalating."

"No," Sana said. "I am distinguishing."

Ro was suddenly beside her, no longer trying to stop her. "Captain Vos, Water Court docket has received sealed evidence and early Compact commentary supporting provisional planting where salt-damaged or displaced archive claims interfere with famine prevention. Your seizure request cites disputed claim status, not biological hazard. Unless you can state the hazard, any seizure risks violating the preservation order by allowing living material to die."

Vos turned to one of the officers with the archive seal case. The officer checked a slate, uneasy.

The media drone drifted sideways, trying for Odet again. Tali's half-built replacement drone rose from behind the transport with three exposed legs and one naked stabilizer fan. It looked like a device assembled by someone who believed casing was for cowards. It placed itself between the media drone and Odet, buzzing unevenly.

Tali smiled. "Legal distance works both ways."

The media drone rose.

At the gate, Lidia An spoke.

"Captain."

Her voice carried without amplification.

Vos looked past Sana. "Councilor An."

"We have a planting trench ready in South Corridor Shade Row. Soil temperature nine degrees. Moisture acceptable. Fire interval risk rising. If the grafts remain in that cradle until tomorrow, survival drops. If they go into ground today, survival improves."

"The material is disputed."

"The weather is not waiting for title."

Jori, standing beside her with a shovel taller than his shoulder, said, "We dug three trenches."

Lidia glanced down at him. "That was not your line."

"It is true."

The crowd breathed around a restrained laugh. The sound changed the scene. It made Firebreak not a mob, but a town with a boy and a shovel and old people who knew when to be quiet.

Sana saw Vos hear that change.

His orders had expected anger. They had not expected competence.

Ro stepped in. "Captain, you can preserve public order by escorting the grafts to the planting site under joint observation. Your officers witness the placement. The media drone records biological care instead of confrontation. The Archive Office retains objection rights. The court receives immediate notice. Everyone keeps their legal arguments alive, and the plants remain alive too."

Vos stared at her.

Tali murmured, "She just offered him a ladder."

Kade said, "Let us see if he prefers holes."

Vos did not smile. But something in his shoulders shifted.

"If I escort," he said, "the grafts remain under dispute."

"Everything is under dispute," Ro said. "That is not a reason to kill it."

He looked at Sana. "You will document no propagation beyond the three recovered bundles."

"I will document exactly what we plant."

"You will submit samples."

"I already did."

"You will not represent this as court-approved release."

"I will represent it as provisional planting obligation under living-use doctrine, because that is what it is."

Vos held her gaze. Then he stepped aside.

Odet made a sound and covered her mouth.

The gate opened.

They walked through slowly: Sana with the cradle, Ro with the docket slate, Odet close enough to touch the latch, Kade watching the officers, Tali's ugly little drone wobbling overhead like a bad idea that had learned balance. Compact security followed. The media drone followed them all. Firebreak residents parted without cheering. Silence was wiser. Cheering could be edited.

South Corridor Shade Row lay beyond the supper hall, where the cooperative had planted smoke pine in staggered bands to slow fire winds. The missing grafts' parent trees stood along the inner row, leaf thin after the recent stress. Three trenches waited at their feet, dark soil opened like careful questions.

Lidia knelt despite her age and touched the first trench. "We kept it moist."

Sana set the cradle down.

Her hands were steady until she opened it.

The grafts lay in their gel wraps, small and absurdly vulnerable. The ash pear bud still showed green. The charbark apple looked less certain. The ember olive had a dark seam near the cut that Sana did not like.

"I need sterile ties, shade cloth, mycorrhizal slurry, and no one breathing directly into the graft faces."

Tali said, "That instruction has become portable."

"Good."

They worked under cameras and rain.

Sana forgot the cameras as soon as the first graft touched rootstock. That was one mercy plants offered: they punished divided attention. She trimmed the cut, matched cambium, wrapped, tied, sealed. Tali handed tools. Odet held shade cloth. Lidia mixed slurry. Jori stood ready with water and did not spill any. Kade held the media drone's line of sight by standing exactly where his body made it choose between his old guilt and Sana's current work.

The first ash pear took.

Not in the dramatic way stories liked. No sudden leaf, no glow, no proof the court could understand from a distance. Only the correct fit of living surfaces and the absence of immediate failure. Sana breathed once and moved to the next.

The charbark apple was harder. Its cambium had dried along one edge. She cut deeper than she wanted, losing length to find life. Odet watched every millimeter as if Sana were cutting a relative.

"Will it hold?" Odet whispered.

"If it chooses to."

"Do trees choose?"

"Not like we do."

"Better?"

Sana pressed the graft into place. "Sometimes."

By the ember olive, her back hurt and the rain had found the seam at her neck. The media drone had lost interest in the close work and moved toward the security officers, who looked less impressive standing in mud. Sana took satisfaction in that because she was tired enough to take satisfaction where she could.

When the third graft was wrapped, she sealed the planting record with her thumb.

"Three recovered bundles placed under provisional planting obligation," she dictated. "Firebreak Seven South Corridor Shade Row. Compact Security witness. Water Court notice. Biological condition guarded. No additional propagation performed."
