Ro sealed the legal copy. Vos sealed the security witness. Lidia sealed the cooperative witness by pressing her thumb to the slate with soil still under the nail.

The media drone sent its packet.

Thirty seconds later, Puget's public feed changed.

Tali saw it first. "Oh, that is disgusting."

Ro opened the feed on her slate.

The headline had softened but not surrendered:

DISPUTED SEED STOCK PLANTED UNDER PROTEST AS BASIN CROWD DEFIES REVIEW.

The image showed Sana kneeling in mud with Odet behind her. The caption called the trench "unauthorized expansion." It did not show Vos's seal. It did not show the court docket. It did not show the dead graft crates from the tunnel. It did not show Jori not spilling water.

Odet stared at the feed, then at the planted row. "They can watch it happen and still lie."

Kade said, "Watching only gives them better material."

Sana wiped mud from her hands. "Then we need records they cannot crop."

Ro looked at her. "Amma."

Sana nodded. "Her hunger ledger. If Puget is framing the basins as dependents, we need the hand-kept records that show what the basins give, lose, store, trade, and prevent. Not yield per liter. System value."

Lidia An folded her soil-marked hands. "Amma will show you. She has been waiting for someone to ask the right question."

Vos approached, helmet under one arm. Without it, he looked younger and more tired.

"Auditor Ilyen," he said. "I need to inform you that a formal challenge to your provisional order has already been filed."

"By whom?"

"Puget Vertical Authority, insurer proxy, and Long Orchard Archive Office."

"Compatibility counsel?"

He hesitated. "The filing is signed by Deputy Counsel Mara Vale."

Ro's head came up. "Vale?"

Kade looked at her sharply.

Sana felt the name settle between them like another hidden mark.

"Relation to Dr. Mirek Vale?" Ro asked.

Vos shook his head. "Not stated."

Of course not, Sana thought.

Rain ran down the new graft wraps. Under the ties, living surfaces held against living surfaces, doing quiet work no headline could understand.

Odet touched the air above the ash pear without touching the graft. "At least they are in ground."

"Yes," Sana said.

For now.

Beyond the row, Puget's distant glow brightened as evening fell. A city that ate first shone beautifully in the rain, and all along the corridors that helped keep it fed, people were being taught to look like thieves.

Sana sealed the last planting image and sent it to Amma Quill.

The reply came almost immediately.

COME BEFORE DAWN. BRING RO. BRING THE LIES.

Sana showed it to Ro.

Ro looked toward the dry basin road, then back at the planted grafts.

"We leave tonight," she said.


Chapter 19: Amma's Ledger

The dry basin road began where the rain gave up.

Not all at once. Nothing in Cascadia changed honestly anymore. The wet forest thinned into smoke pine, the smoke pine into gray scrub, the gray scrub into old orchard windbreaks holding the last moisture like a secret. The transport climbed through night, past water towers that no longer filled, past terraces where root cages held soil against slopes that wanted to become dust, past houses with cistern lights dimmed to the legal minimum. By the time dawn put a copper line along the eastern ridge, the windshield carried no new drops. The road ahead looked scraped clean.

Sana had slept for eleven minutes and woken with her hand closed around nothing.

Ro sat beside her, reading every challenge filed against the provisional planting order. Tali slept in the back with one hand inside the unfinished drone's open body, as if repairing it in dreams. Kade drove again. Odet had stayed at Firebreak Seven because someone needed to watch the grafts and because leaving home twice in one day had been more than anyone should ask of a person whose town had been made into a headline.

The message from Amma Quill had remained open on Sana's slate all night.

COME BEFORE DAWN. BRING RO. BRING THE LIES.

They arrived after dawn because roads built around scarcity did not care about urgency.

Amma's settlement was called Mouth-of-Ash, though the ash field that named it had greened over fifty years before Sana was born. It sat in a shallow basin where three seasonal washes met, ringed by wind walls of woven cane and old turbine ribs. Nothing there looked dramatic enough for Puget media. No starving crowd. No collapsed granary. No theater. Only low houses half buried for heat control, shade nets patched with different decades of fabric, cistern domes painted white, and gardens arranged not by beauty but by argument with wind.

People were already working.

Children carried graywater buckets under the eye of an old man who tapped a rhythm with two fingers when they tilted too much. Two women checked fungal shade trays in a trench roofed with reed panels. A group of teenagers turned compost with the solemnity of priests and the expressions of people who would rather be asleep. At the edge of the settlement, millet stood short and stubborn in rows broken by stone mulch.

"Low-yield dependents," Ro said quietly.

The phrase sounded obscene here.

Amma Quill waited under a shade roof outside the council house, a narrow woman with braided white hair and a face made of weather and decisions. Sana had heard her testimony before seeing her: a voice cutting through technical language to say hunger had names and calendars. In person, Amma looked smaller than the force of her recorded words, which made the words more impressive.

She did not greet them.

She held out her hand. "The lies."

Ro gave her the slate with Puget's media clips, the challenge filings, the official Compact risk map, and Sana's sealed planting record. Amma did not look at the planting record first. She opened the media story and watched Odet's face appear beside the graft cradle.

Her mouth tightened.

"They used the girl."

"Yes," Sana said.

"Then we use the truth with her consent, or not at all."

Sana liked her immediately and was too tired to hide it. "Agreed."

Amma turned and walked into the council house. "Come."

Inside, the room was cool and dim. The floor had been tamped clay once, then sealed with transparent resin over layers of embedded paper. Names, dates, rainfall totals, seed loans, births, funerals, harvest failures, and repair tallies lay beneath their feet, preserved under the walking surface. Shelves lined the walls. Some held slates. Most held books.

Actual books.

Not ceremonial codices in archive cases. Not printed art. Working ledgers, spines worn, corners tied with cloth, pages swollen from handling.

Tali woke fully at the sight. "Oh."

Amma looked back. "You expected dust?"

"I expected data."

"This is what data looks like when people cannot afford for it to vanish."

She led them to a central table where six ledgers waited. Each was the size of a serving tray, bound in patched canvas and labeled by hand:

GRAIN RECEIVED.

WATER GIVEN.

SHADE MAINTAINED.

FIRE PREVENTED.

HUNGER DAYS.

DEBTS NO ONE SHOULD OWE.

Ro read the last title twice.

Amma noticed. "That one is not for court unless court grows a soul."

"Court rarely does," Ro said.

"Then it can wait."

Sana rested both hands on the table. "We need to contradict the official risk map."

"No," Amma said. "You need to show what it was built not to see."

She opened HUNGER DAYS. The pages were ruled in careful columns: date, household, mouths, missed meals, substituted food, labor lost, illness, cause, witness. No abstraction. No smoothing. A child's fever stood beside a broken pump. A grandmother's skipped dinner beside pollinator failure. A whole week of half rations marked after fungal protein backing was withdrawn by an insurer.

Sana felt each line as a weight.

"The Compact map says this basin is stable," Ro said.

"The Compact map counts death and riot," Amma replied. "We count the day before a child stops growing."

Kade stood near the door, arms folded, looking at the floor ledger beneath his boots as if afraid to step on a name. "How long have you kept these?"

"Since official memory became too expensive."

"That is not a year."

"No. It is a condition."

Amma opened WATER GIVEN. These columns ran outward instead of inward: date, source, amount, recipient corridor, purpose, return obligation, loss accepted. Sana saw Firebreak Seven listed six times in the last two seasons. Water sent not because the basin had surplus, but because a shaded fire corridor upwind would save more moisture later than the basin could hoard now.

"Puget's map labels this water export as basin inefficiency," Ro said.

"Of course it does."

"It is corridor investment."

"Of course it is."

Tali leaned over the page. "You gave water to preserve someone else's shade so your own wind would not burn."

"And theirs. And yours. Wind is promiscuous."

Sana almost smiled.

Amma opened SHADE MAINTAINED. The ledger tracked kilometers of living windbreak, canopy density, soil temperature differences, pollinator rest points, road dust reduction, and estimated water saved downwind. The math was simple enough to audit by hand and stubborn enough to make a model ashamed of itself.

Ro was photographing with court seals, page by page.

"This is system value," Sana said.

"This is survival value," Amma corrected. "System value is what people call it when survival must impress someone with a title."

They moved through FIRE PREVENTED. Each entry recorded a day when dry wind, heat, and fuel load should have carried flame but did not because a corridor held. Cross references pointed to orchard yields in regions that had never thanked the basin because no one thanks a wall for not burning.

Then GRAIN RECEIVED.

Here the story reversed. The basin did take. It took fungus meal, pear-rice starch, bean flour, emergency oil. Amma had not softened that. Every receipt was written in dark ink.

"They will use this against you," Ro said.

"Only if we hide it first."

"They will say dependency."

Amma's finger moved to the return columns. Labor crews sent to rebuild flood terraces. Pollinator larvae raised and traded. Drought data contributed to seed trials. Stone mulch teams dispatched after mudslides. Firewatch weeks. Basin millet seed sent during a Puget tower blight two years earlier.

"Eating from a network is not dependency," Amma said. "Refusing to name what you give back is theft."

Sana thought of Puget's glowing yield numbers. Calories per liter. Protein index. School ration stability. The numbers were true and incomplete in exactly the way a locked gate was a true description of metal and a false description of justice.

Ro opened the official Compact risk map beside Amma's ledgers. The basin appeared pale amber: low productivity, moderate demand, high release risk, low continuity priority. Firebreak Seven appeared as a small corridor with limited direct yield. Puget appeared green and dense, critical infrastructure.

Amma tapped the map. "This is not a map. It is an appetite wearing geography."

Tali looked up from scanning. "I am stealing that sentence."

"Use it well."

Sana asked, "Can we model these ledgers quickly enough for court?"

"You can model anything badly if you are in a hurry," Amma said.

Ro said, "We need enough to challenge the map before the provisional order hearing."

Amma opened a drawer beneath the table and removed a slim slate wrapped in cloth. "Then use the ledger engine."

Tali froze. "You have a ledger engine?"

"No. We have a stubborn arithmetic machine that refuses to call itself intelligent."

She unwrapped the slate. It was old, thick, and patched with three different kinds of casing. Its screen came alive in black and green. No cloud mark. No archive seal. No insurer certification.

Ro looked wary. "Is it admissible?"

"It is explainable," Amma said. "Admissibility is your problem."

Tali took the slate with reverence. "What does it do?"

"Reads our ledgers. Converts them into the categories official maps omit. Fire prevented. Water saved downwind. Soil retained. Hunger deferred. Labor returned. Ecological debt unpaid. It does not predict. It accounts."

"Who built it?"

"A child who left and a grandmother who stayed."

"Which were you?"

Amma looked at her.

Tali nodded. "Right. Not my question."

The ledger engine accepted the first book with a soft click. It did not scan like an archive reader. It listened to the page through pressure, ink density, fiber swelling, and human correction marks. Lines became tables. Tables became relationships. Relationships became a map that did not glow green for density or amber for inconvenience, but wove the basin into every place its work had prevented loss.

Firebreak Seven brightened first.

Not as a needy point. As a hinge.

Then the smoke pine corridors. Then the pollinator rest lines. Then the fungal protein routes Puget depended on during tower sanitation cycles. Then the dry basin millet trials that had contributed traits to supposedly Puget-certified grain ladders. Then water loans that looked irrational until paired with later fire suppression savings.

The official map had shown the basin as a mouth.

Amma's ledger showed it as lungs.

Sana sat down because standing had become too difficult.

"How many people know this exists?"

"Everyone here," Amma said. "No one important."

Ro's eyes remained on the growing map. "That changes now."

"Does it?" Amma asked.

Ro looked at her.

Amma closed HUNGER DAYS with one hand. "You think showing a truer map makes people kinder. Sometimes it only teaches them where to cut."

Kade said, "Then why show us?"

"Because the knife is already out."

The room went quiet.

Outside, a bucket rang against stone. A child laughed once, was hushed, then laughed again more softly. Morning had fully entered the basin now, hard and gold through the shade nets.

Sana looked at the ledger engine's map. "Puget's story says the basins threaten common supply. Your records show the basins stabilize it."

"Yes."

"The Compact risk map undervalues them because it counts extracted food and ignores prevented loss."

"Yes."

"And if the North Rain Vault releases are denied using that map, the denial is based on false continuity math."

Amma's gaze sharpened. "There is the question."

Ro was already writing. "We can file a motion to challenge the model basis."

"A motion takes time," Tali said.

"Everything takes time. Some things buy it."

Kade looked toward the open door. "We may not have much."

Two riders approached the council house on dust bikes, scarves pulled over their mouths. One carried a white signal cloth. Not surrender. Courier urgency. The bikes stopped outside, and a young woman came in with a sealed tube.

"From Firebreak Seven," she said. "Sent on secondary road."

Sana stood before she realized she had moved.

Ro broke the seal.

Her face changed.

"Read it," Amma said.

Ro read aloud. "Compact Security withdrew from Firebreak gate. Media drones remain. Unknown parties marked South Corridor Shade Row with private claim notices during night watch shift. No physical damage yet. Odet requests instruction."

Sana's chair scraped backward.

Tali swore. Kade moved toward the door. Ro caught Sana's wrist.

"If you run back now, they keep you moving between fires."

"They marked the grafts."

"They marked the row. That is provocation."

"It is trespass."

Amma stood. "It is also admission."

Sana turned on her. "Admission?"

"Private claim notices placed after your provisional order, after your planting record, after the media story. They are no longer pretending the claims are abstract. They touched ground."

Ro's grip loosened, not because the danger was smaller, but because the shape of evidence had changed.

Amma pointed to the ledger engine. "Bring the row into the map."

Tali understood first. She bent over the slate. "If we overlay Firebreak's corridor value with the claim notice location-"

"Then the private claim is not just against a graft," Ro said. "It is against a corridor function the official map omitted."

Sana forced herself to breathe.

The claim notices had been meant to frighten Firebreak. They did frighten Firebreak. They frightened her. But fear could be evidence if someone held it still long enough to read.

"Odet needs instruction," Kade said.

Sana took Ro's slate and recorded carefully. "Do not remove notices. Do not touch them without witness. Photograph placement, distance to graft, seal language, footprints, tool marks, and direction of approach. Keep watch in pairs. No confrontation. Tell Jori his shovel is not a legal instrument."

Kade's mouth moved as if he might smile and had forgotten how.

Ro added, "Send copies to Water Court docket, Amma's council, and Auditor Ilyen."

Amma added, "And feed the people standing watch."

Sana looked at her.

"People make bad evidence when hungry," Amma said.

The message went out.

The ledger engine finished its first pass. The wall filled with a new map: not green and amber, but threads of obligation, shade, water, labor, hunger, and prevented fire. Firebreak Seven glowed where the planted grafts held a corridor edge. Puget glowed too, not as villain, not as savior, but as a city fed by more hands than it named.

Sana felt the exhaustion in her body like stone, but under it something steadier formed.

"We file this," she said.

Ro nodded. "We file it, and we make the court ask why the official map cannot see what hand records can."

Amma closed DEBTS NO ONE SHOULD OWE without opening it.

"Not that one?" Tali asked.

"Not yet."

"What is in it?"

Amma looked toward the basin, where morning had turned every shade net into a held breath.

"The part that wins only after everyone admits they have already lost something."

No one asked again.

Ro began assembling the filing. Tali coaxed the ledger engine into exporting a human-readable chain. Kade went outside to check the road. Sana stayed at the table with Amma's books and the map Puget had never wanted to draw.

For the first time since the petals in Graft Nine, she understood the shape of the fight not as a locked vault, not as a stolen graft, not even as a buried patent, but as a question every system had tried to avoid:

Who gets counted before hunger counts them?


Chapter 20: The Orchard Ledger Speaks Narrowly

The Orchard Ledger did not have a voice.

That was the first thing the Archive Office representative said, as if Ro had accused the system of singing.

"The Ledger produces certified responses," Deputy Counsel Mara Vale said. "It does not speak."

She sat at the far end of the remote hearing table, rendered in court light with impossible clarity. The name had already done damage before her image arrived. Mara Vale was younger than Mirek Vale by enough years to make sibling possible and child unlikely, though family trees in Compact service had always found ways to grow sideways. She had the same stillness around the mouth, the same careful economy of expression, but where Mirek wore sorrow like a field coat, Mara wore precision like glass.

Sana watched her from Amma's council house, where the hearing had been convened under emergency docket because no one trusted the roads, the media, or the weather to hold still. Ro had turned one end of the ledger table into a court station. Amma's books lay wrapped in protective cloth. The ledger engine sat beside them, plugged into three adapters and one battery Tali had called "morally temporary." Kade stood near the door again. Tali crouched beside the wall projection, eyes moving between the signal quality and the officers listed in the hearing channel.

On the wall, the remote court assembled itself in rectangles: Merrow Pell at the center bench; Sene and Ko beside her; Captain Vos as security witness from Firebreak Seven; Odet and Lidia An under a shade row camera; Governor Hal Renn's counsel Cass Brindle in Puget's atrium; Dr. Mirek Vale in a technical chamber; Deputy Counsel Mara Vale in the Long Orchard Archive Office; Amma Quill in person at Sana's left, refusing to sit.

The Orchard Ledger appeared as a blank green seal.

Not a face. Not even a symbol of a tree. Just certification geometry: root, branch, checksum, custody, response.

Pell looked tired. "Let the record show emergency session on petitioner motion challenging Compact risk map basis and provisional planting challenge. Mediator Tamsin, you may proceed."

Ro rose. She had slept less than Sana, which meant almost not at all, but fatigue had clarified rather than dulled her. "The official risk map used to deny North Rain Vault emergency releases understates Dry Basin and Firebreak corridor continuity value. It counts extracted food yield and direct demand while omitting prevented fire loss, soil retention, water savings downwind, pollinator refuge support, labor returns, and hunger deferred. We submit Amma Quill's hand-kept ledgers, the Mouth-of-Ash ledger engine transformation, Firebreak Seven claim notice evidence, and field auditor Sana Ilyen's provisional planting record."

Brindle said, "Puget objects to treating unaudited local ledgers as equivalent to Compact model data."

Amma's mouth moved.

Sana whispered, "No."

Amma whispered back, "I did not say it aloud."

Pell said, "Objection noted. The court will first determine whether the submitted records can be verified for integrity. Interpretation follows, if at all."

If at all.

Sana heard the fence being built.

Ro sent the packet.

For a few seconds nothing happened. The wall seal held green and motionless. Then geometric branches unfolded from it, each one a verification path. Amma's ledgers became document hashes. The ledger engine's transformation became reproducible code steps. Firebreak claim notices became timestamped images with soil disturbance markers. Sana's planting order became court-sealed field action. Vos's witness seal clicked into place. Odet's photographs arrived from Firebreak: white private claim notices tied to smoke pine stakes near the new grafts, each printed with a Ventrix descendant-claim reference and a compatibility review warning.

The Orchard Ledger processed them without drama.

Certified response pending.

Tali mouthed, I hate it.

Ro did not smile.

Mara Vale spoke. "Before certification, the Archive Office requests exclusion of the local ledger engine transformation. The device lacks current audit registration, chain-of-software custody, and recognized model status."

Tali stood so quickly one of her adapters fell out. "It is not a model."

Pell's eyes shifted. "State your name for the record."

"Tali Moss, field systems mechanic and apparently the only person here who knows when a counting machine is not pretending to be prophecy."

"Ms. Moss."

Ro closed her eyes.

Tali continued anyway. "The Mouth-of-Ash engine is deterministic. It reads handwritten columns and converts them into declared categories. Fire prevented remains fire prevented. Water given remains water given. It does not predict future compliance, assign moral weight, or decide who eats. That is already better behavior than half the certified systems in this room."

Ko leaned forward. "Can the transformation be reproduced manually?"

"Yes."

"How long?"

"For all ledgers? Weeks. For the sampled entries used in the emergency motion? Hours."

Amma said, "We have done the hours."

Everyone looked at her.

She lifted a stack of copied pages. "Before sending for them, we hand-checked the engine against three months of entries. Children can do arithmetic. They complain, but they can do it."

Pell's gaze returned to the green seal. "The court will allow the engine output for limited integrity review, subject to later challenge."

Mara Vale's expression did not change.

The Ledger finished.

CERTIFIED: SOURCE RECORDS INTERNALLY CONSISTENT.

CERTIFIED: TRANSFORMATION STEPS REPRODUCIBLE WITHIN DECLARED PARAMETERS.

CERTIFIED: FIREBREAK SEVEN CLAIM NOTICE IMAGES MATCH FIELD TIMESTAMP AND COURT-SEAL CHAIN.

CERTIFIED: PROVISIONAL PLANTING RECORD MATCHES WATER COURT DOCKET.

The room should have felt relieved.

Instead, everyone waited for the next line.

Ro said, "Request inference: official risk map materially omits continuity value shown in certified records."

The green seal shifted.

Inference outside certified scope.

Ro's hands tightened on the table. "Request comparison between official risk map categories and submitted ledger categories."

Comparison available.

A chart opened. The official map categories appeared in a clean column: production yield, population demand, release risk, archive conflict, continuity priority. Amma's categories appeared beside them: fire prevented, shade maintained, water saved, hunger deferred, labor returned, soil retained, pollinator refuge, ecological debt.

Most cells between the columns remained blank.

Ro said, "Identify omitted categories."

Categories not represented in official model: fire prevented; shade maintained; water saved downwind; hunger deferred below riot threshold; labor returned; soil retained; pollinator refuge; ecological debt.

Sana felt Amma inhale beside her.

Ro said, "Does omission affect continuity priority?"

The Ledger paused.

Cannot determine. Continuity priority is model-defined output. Submitted categories are outside model-defined inputs.

"If a model excludes relevant continuity inputs, can its continuity output be complete?"

Cannot determine relevance. Relevance is governance-defined.

Amma laughed once.

It was not a happy sound.

Pell looked toward her. "Amma Quill?"

"The machine is honest," Amma said. "Narrow, but honest."

Mara Vale said, "The Archive Office agrees. The Ledger has certified integrity without endorsing petitioner's policy interpretation."

"Policy interpretation," Sana said before Ro could stop her.

Pell said, "Auditor Ilyen, you may speak if you address the question before the court."

Sana stood. "Then I will. The question is whether emergency seed release decisions are being made from a risk map that cannot see the work keeping the system alive."

Brindle said, "The question is whether petitioners can replace certified continuity modeling with sympathetic local accounting."

"No," Sana said. "That is your question because it is easier."

Ro whispered, "Careful."

Sana was careful. She was tired of being polite in places where precision had been mistaken for surrender.

"The Orchard Ledger has certified that Amma's records are internally consistent. It has certified that the transformation is reproducible. It has identified categories missing from the official model. It refuses to infer relevance because relevance is governance-defined. That means the refusal is not technical. It is ours."

Mirek Vale spoke for the first time. "That is an eloquent overreach."

Sana turned toward his rectangle. "Then name the biological or mathematical reason Firebreak's prevented fire loss should not count toward continuity."

He folded his hands. "Continuity models must avoid double-counting indirect benefits."

"So count them once."

"Indirect benefits are uncertain."

"So is hunger."

"Hunger is measured."

Amma said, "Badly."

Mirek's eyes moved to her. "With respect, local records often lack standardization."

Amma stepped closer to the projection. "With respect, standardization often lacks shame."

The court went still.

Pell let the sentence stand.

Mara Vale entered the silence with surgical calm. "No party disputes that Dry Basin communities perform valuable ecological work. The issue is whether emergency unlock of disputed North Rain Vault lines is justified before archive conflicts are resolved. The Ledger has not found that it is."

Ro said, "The Ledger cannot find that. It is not empowered to weigh hunger harm."

"Correct."

"Then its silence cannot be used as evidence against release."

Mara's eyes flicked toward her. "Nor can its integrity certification be used as evidence for release beyond the certified facts."

"Those facts show the official map is incomplete."

"They show additional categories exist."

"Categories directly tied to system continuity."

"A governance interpretation."

Ro smiled then, a small dangerous thing. "Good. Then let governance answer publicly."

Pell leaned back. Rain tower court, water court, archive court; Sana had begun to learn the posture of people who knew their decisions would be read by frightened regions before sunset.

"The court will not order North Rain Vault unlock on this record alone," Pell said.

Odet's face in the Firebreak rectangle went pale.

Pell continued before anyone could speak. "The court does find that the official risk map omits certified categories potentially material to continuity priority. The court orders an emergency governance review of model scope within seventy-two hours. The court further orders that no private claim notices placed after the provisional planting order may be enforced, removed, or expanded pending review. The Firebreak grafts remain under provisional planting obligation. Security witness to continue by remote check rather than physical roadblock."

Ro exhaled.

Not victory. Not enough.

But space.

Brindle objected. Mara Vale reserved Archive Office rights. Mirek Vale requested technical participation in model review. Amma snorted at that. Pell ignored the snort with the restraint of an experienced adjudicator.

Then Ko spoke. "The court also requests that the Orchard Ledger answer one further integrity question."

Mara Vale's stillness sharpened.

Ko said, "Does the Long Orchard Archive Office have internal records of compatibility counsel queries to Patent House or related escrow mirrors regarding North Rain Vault cluster N-RV-12 through N-RV-18?"

The green seal held.

Mara said, "Archive Office objects. Internal counsel query records are privileged."

Ko said, "The question is existence, not content."

Mara said, "Existence may reveal privileged strategy."

Pell looked at the seal. "The court requests only whether such records exist and whether their timestamps precede the Water Court hearing in which patent encumbrance was first raised."

The Ledger processed.

Sana felt Kade stop breathing near the door.

CERTIFIED: INTERNAL QUERY LOGS EXIST.

CERTIFIED: QUERY TIMESTAMPS PRECEDE WATER COURT HEARING BY THIRTY-SIX HOURS.

CERTIFIED: QUERY AUTHORIZATION: DEPUTY COUNSEL MARA VALE.

For the first time, Mara Vale's face changed.

Only a fraction. A small tightening at the eyes.

But in a room where every person had learned to survive on partial signals, it was enough.

Mirek Vale looked down.

That was worse.

Ro said, "The Archive Office knew of the Patent House issue before Dr. Vale introduced patent risk in court."

Mara said, "The existence of preliminary legal inquiry does not establish wrongdoing."

"No," Ro said. "It establishes sequence."

Amma murmured, "Sequence is where lies begin to limp."

Pell tapped the docket plate. "The court orders preservation of all internal query logs, authorization records, communications between Archive Office compatibility counsel and any party to this proceeding, and related escrow mirror contacts. Content privilege claims may be briefed. Destruction or alteration will be sanctionable."

Mara Vale bowed her head once. "Understood."

Sana did not believe her.

The green seal returned to blank geometry.

The Orchard Ledger had spoken narrowly. It had refused justice, refused mercy, refused hunger, refused common sense, and in doing so had left a clean trail of what could no longer be denied: the records were real, the map was incomplete, the claims had touched ground, and the Archive Office had known before anyone admitted knowing.

The hearing ended in fragments. Rectangles disappeared. Odet held up one hand from Firebreak before her feed cut, palm muddy, alive. Captain Vos looked relieved not to be standing at a roadblock. Brindle vanished into Puget lettuce light. Mirek Vale's rectangle went dark last, but not before Sana saw him look toward some offscreen place with the expression of a man calculating whether honesty had become less dangerous than loyalty.

When the wall cleared, Amma sat down at last.

"Well," Tali said. "The machine declined to have a conscience but did leave fingerprints."

Ro rubbed her eyes. "That may be enough for the next seventy-two hours."

"I dislike how often enough means not dead yet," Tali said.

Kade said, "It often does."

Sana looked at Amma's unopened ledger: DEBTS NO ONE SHOULD OWE.

Amma saw her looking. "Not yet."

"When?"

"When they make hunger testify against itself."

Sana did not ask what that meant. She suspected the answer was already walking toward them.

Her slate chimed with a new alert.

Not from court. Not from Firebreak.

From the North Rain Vault.

AUTOMATED NOTICE: POLLINATOR CORRIDOR BREACH DETECTED. LIVING ROUTE C-17 TO FIREBREAK SOUTH INTERRUPTED. PROVISIONAL PLANTING VIABILITY AT RISK.

Tali was on her feet before the second line appeared.

Ro read the notice and went very still. "They cannot enforce the claim notices, so something cut the corridor."

Kade opened the door and looked out toward the road, as if he could see the route from there.

Sana's first thought was not law, or maps, or court orders.

It was the ash pear bud, small and green under rain.

Then the second line of the alert arrived.

DRONE ACTIVITY DETECTED. ORIGIN MASKED.

Tali's face changed.

"They touched my corridor," she said.

Amma stood again, slower this time. "Then stop arguing with ledgers."

Sana grabbed her field kit.

Outside, the dry basin morning had turned white with heat. Behind them, the Orchard Ledger's certified response remained sealed in the court file, narrow and useful and nowhere near enough.

Ahead, something living had been cut.


Chapter 21: Night Graft

They reached the cut after sunset because urgency had to share the road with heat, dust, and fear.

The corridor lay between Mouth-of-Ash and Firebreak Seven, not belonging entirely to either place and therefore belonging to everyone who needed it not to fail. On maps it was Route C-17, a thin green line with pollinator rest nodes, shade grafts, moisture stones, and fungal stepping beds. On the ground it was stranger and more fragile: a braid of smoke pine, ash pear, charbark apple, dry millet platforms, insect wells, and half-buried clay jars that released night moisture through porous throats. In daylight it would have looked like a poor orchard trying to be a road. In the dark, under headlamps and a moon blurred by heat haze, it looked like an argument the land had nearly won.

The saboteurs had cut the argument in three places.

Sana saw the first wound before the transport stopped.

The pollinator rest mast was down, its reed lattice snapped and twisted, its sugar wick ripped open and drying in the dust. A cloud of displaced moths circled the broken mast in confused spirals, landing on nothing, lifting again, spending strength they could not spare. Nearby, two ash pear shade limbs had been severed at the graft collars with a tool too clean to be anger and too sharp to be accident.

Tali jumped out before Kade had fully braked.

"Lights low," she snapped. "Do not flood the insect wells."

No one argued. Even Ro, whose first instinct around danger was to make a record, dimmed her slate until it glowed red.

Sana opened her field kit on the hood. "Odet?"

Ro had her on a live channel from Firebreak Seven. Odet's face appeared small and grainy, lit by watch lanterns. "I am here."

"Are the planted grafts still sealed?"

"Yes. The notices are still up. We photographed everything. Jori did not use the shovel as a legal instrument."

"Good."

Jori's distant voice said, "It is still available."

Sana almost laughed and could not afford the breath. "Keep shade cloth on the ash pear. If pollinators do not move by dawn, mist the row manually. No sweet feed unless Tali says so."

Tali, already crawling under the broken mast, called, "No sweet feed. It will pull them to the wrong bloom."

Odet nodded. "Understood."

The feed closed. Sana turned to the first severed limb.

The cut was new. Sap still stood at the edge, dark in the headlamp. The graft collar had been sliced just above the living union, not random branch vandalism but targeted interruption. Whoever had done it understood enough botany to hurt the corridor without killing everything at once. That made the act colder.

Kade stood watch on the road with Amma's old pulse lantern in one hand and a courier knife in the other. Amma had come despite Sana telling her not to. She sat on a stone mulch wall thirty meters back, organizing volunteers from both settlements with a voice that turned panic into lists.

"You," she told a basin teenager, "count moth clusters. Not guesses. Clusters. You two, water stones. Half measure. You, stop breathing like that near the fungal bed."

Tali called, "I approve that instruction."

Amma did not look up. "I did not request approval."

Ro moved from wound to wound, recording distances, tool marks, drone scuffs, pollen disturbance, and the thin lines where landing feet had pressed dust into the bark. The media drones were absent for once. That worried Sana more than their presence would have.

"No public feed," Ro said, reading the same worry. "Whoever did this does not want images yet."

"Yet," Kade said.

Sana cleaned the first cut. "They want the grafts to fail before the story arrives."

"Then the story becomes: unauthorized planting dies after basin interference."

"Or: dangerous lines prove unstable."

Ro's jaw tightened. "Yes."

Sana matched the severed limb to the collar. The cambium still held moisture. Possible. Not easy. She cut a narrow diagonal, exposing living green. "Tali, I need mycorrhizal bridge."

"In your left tray."

"Fresh."

"I am under a crime scene."

"And yet."

Tali slid backward from the mast, one cheek streaked with dust, and threw a sealed vial without looking. Sana caught it against her chest.

"Show-off," Ro said.

"Competence is not showing off if everyone is suffering," Tali said, and disappeared under the mast again.

They worked by red light.

Night grafting was never ideal. Trees did not care about drama, but they did care about temperature, moisture, light, and the cleanliness of human hands. Sana had done emergency grafts after windfall, frost split, deer damage, and once after a drunk apprentice had backed a cart through a nursery row. This was different. The cuts had intention in them. Every repair felt like answering a sentence written by a knife.

She bound the first limb with bridge gel and living tape. The tape tightened as it warmed, pulling surfaces into contact. Not enough to heal, not yet. Enough to keep the possibility of healing from drying out.

"One," she said.

Ro marked it.

At the mast, Tali found the first drone sign.

"Here."

They gathered, careful of the insect wells. Tali pointed to the underside of a broken reed strut. A smear of black polymer clung to the splinter.

"Rotor guard," she said. "Small craft. Agricultural body, modified quiet. It clipped the mast after the cutting tool did the branch work."

Kade crouched. "Can you trace?"

"If it was stupid."

"Was it?"

Tali smiled without pleasure. "It wanted to look smart."

She scraped the polymer into a sample tube. "This is not Puget municipal. Not Compact. The quieting resin is insurer logistics grade. Same family as the pollinator failure corridor commands."

Ro's head came up. "You can support that?"

"After I run it, yes."

"How long?"

"If people stop breaking my world, an hour."

Another volunteer called from the second cut. "Auditor!"

Sana ran.

The second wound was worse. A charbark apple support had been cut below the graft collar and peeled down in a strip, exposing cambium to dry air. The severed piece lay ten meters away, not tossed but placed across a moisture stone, as if someone had wanted it to be found too late.

Sana felt anger rise hot and useless.

Amma appeared beside her, quiet as a shadow. "Can it be saved?"

"The limb, maybe. The collar, maybe. The lineage, if I take scions now."

"Take them."

"That weakens it."

"Dead is weaker."

Sana looked at her. Amma's face in the red light was all planes and old patience.

Sana cut six scions from the living portion, each no longer than her hand, each with viable buds. She wrapped them in damp cloth and sealed them in a cold sleeve. The mother limb she pared, bridged, and bound. It might live. It might not. The scions were insurance against the corridor being forced to testify with a corpse.

"Two," she said, less steadily.

Ro marked it and touched Sana's shoulder once, brief enough not to interrupt.

The third cut waited near the fungal stepping bed.

There the saboteurs had been crueler.

They had not only cut. They had salted.

At first Sana thought the pale scatter was dust. Then her headlamp caught the crystals. Fine, controlled, laid in a crescent around the ember olive support and the fungal bed's intake seam. Not enough to poison a field. Enough to disrupt the fungal bridge, confuse moisture readings, and make a later scan show "salt stress" around the very corridor now tied to salt-damaged archive claims.

Ro swore softly.

Kade said, "They are writing evidence."

"No," Amma said. "They are planting lies."

Sana knelt outside the crescent. "No one steps inside. Tali."

"I see it."

Tali's voice had changed. The technical anger was gone. What remained was focus so cold it felt dangerous.

"They want the system to record salt exposure after our filing," Tali said. "Then they argue our own salt exception admits contamination."

Ro's face went pale in the red light. "And they can say the provisional planting created biological uncertainty."

"If we let the record stand alone," Sana said.

She opened a contamination kit. "We document the pattern before mitigation. Grain size, distribution, wind direction, absence beyond crescent, tool marks, drone residue if present."

Amma called two volunteers closer. "You heard her. Slow and exact."

Kade moved to the road. "Vehicle."

Everyone froze.

Not an engine. A low electric whine, distant but approaching. Tali killed her drone's running lights. Ro dimmed the court slate. The volunteers crouched among shade trunks.

The whine passed on the old service road above the corridor. Sana saw no headlights, only a momentary blackness moving against stars. A drone carrier, or a silent bike, or fear giving shape to sound.

Tali waited until it faded. "That was not a farm vehicle."

Kade said, "It slowed when it reached us."

Amma stood. "Then it saw work, not panic."

"It may come back."

"Then we should be doing something worth interrupting."

Sana returned to the salt crescent. Her hands no longer shook. Anger had burned down into sequence.

Photograph. Sample. Seal. Map. Mitigate.

They lifted the top layer of salt with adhesive sheets, marking each sheet by position. Tali found two more polymer flecks near the fungal intake and a microscopic nick in the clay lip where a delivery nozzle had touched. Ro sealed every step. Amma's volunteers spread clean buffer clay outside the documented line. Sana flushed the intake with a measured rinse, then introduced a fungal counterculture from her kit, praying quietly to no one that the resident web would accept help rather than interpret it as invasion.

The ember olive support had one cut, shallow but placed to interrupt sugar flow. Sana bridged it with living tape and splinted it to a smoke pine stake.

"Three," she said.

No one answered at first.

Then Tali said from the mast, "Mast bypass is ugly but alive."

She had built a temporary pollinator guide from salvage wire, spare drone ribs, reed splints, and three sugar wicks cut thinner than regulation but correctly spaced. Moths had already begun finding it. Not many. Enough to form a moving question in the dark.

Sana stood slowly. Her back hurt. Her knees were damp. Her gloves smelled of sap, salt, and adhesive. The corridor around them was still injured, but not silent. Insects moved. Moisture jars breathed. Fungal beds held. Three wounds were bound. Scions slept in cold sleeves.

Enough lineage preserved.

Not enough corridor secured.

Ro sent the sealed emergency packet to the Water Court, the Orchard Ledger docket, Firebreak Seven, Amma's council, and Captain Vos. "Filed."

Tali lifted the polymer sample. "Give me twenty minutes and a flat surface."

Amma pointed to the transport hood. "There."

Tali spread her tools. Sana walked the repaired line once more, counting. Kade kept watch. Ro argued quietly with a court clerk who did not want to wake an adjudicator. Amma's volunteers fed the moth counters dates and water.

When Tali's scan finished, she did not curse. That was how Sana knew it was bad.

"Logistics resin match," Tali said. "High confidence. Same supplier family as the pollinator failures near Graft Nine. Not enough to name a company from polymer alone. But the drone firmware residue includes a routing stub."

Ro ended her clerk call mid-sentence. "Routing to where?"

Tali turned the slate.

The stub was short, half corrupted, but one identifier remained:

PV-AUX / NOTICE-COMPLIANCE RELAY.

Ro stared. "Puget Vertical auxiliary."

"Or someone using their relay."

"Can you distinguish?"

"Not tonight."

Kade said, "Tonight may be enough to keep them from calling this basin vandalism."

Sana looked toward the dark road where the silent vehicle had passed. "They will say spoofed."

"Maybe," Ro said. "But now we have wound pattern, salt placement, drone residue, routing stub, and repaired biological condition. They tried to write evidence. We wrote over them."

Amma shook her head. "No. You wrote beside them. Never write over a lie. Leave it visible."

Ro accepted the correction with a tired nod.

The court clerk called back. Pell had accepted the emergency packet for morning review. Captain Vos sent a terse acknowledgment and promised remote watch until dawn. Odet sent a photo of the Firebreak grafts under mist cloth. The ash pear bud was still green.

Sana sat on the transport step and let that small green fact hold her upright.

Tali sat beside her, leaving a careful gap so their dirty sleeves would not stick together.

"They touched my corridor," Tali said again, softer now.

Sana nodded. "You kept it moving."

"Barely."

"Barely is a form of alive."

Tali looked at the temporary mast. Moths moved around it in uncertain loops. "I hate that this keeps being true."

"So do I."

Kade approached from the road. "We have company from Firebreak. Friendly."

Lanterns appeared in the west: Odet, Jori, Lidia, and six others walking with water packs and tool rolls. Odet reached Sana first and looked at the repaired line, the salt sheets, the bound grafts, the ugly mast.

"Is it saved?"

Sana could have said no. She could have said maybe. She could have given the precise answer: viability retained under stress, next twelve hours critical, lineage preserved but corridor function reduced.

Instead she chose the truth that could be carried.

"Enough to keep fighting."

Odet nodded once, as if that was the only answer she had expected.

Jori held up his shovel. "Not legal."

"Still useful," Sana said.

They worked until the eastern sky paled. The night grafts held. The moths found the bypass. The fungal bed did not collapse. The salt sheets lay sealed in evidence sleeves like captured frost.

At dawn, the corridor was not healed.

But it was not dead.

And somewhere beyond the ridge, whoever had cut it would learn that living things did not always fail on schedule.


Chapter 22: Kade Taken

Kade was arrested beside a temporary pollinator mast that looked too poorly made to be historic.

Later, Sana would remember that detail with unreasonable clarity: the mast's salvage wire bent in three directions, the sugar wicks hanging at uneven intervals, moths already treating the whole ugly structure as if it had always been there. She would remember Tali asleep sitting up against a smoke pine, one hand still closed around a polymer sample tube. She would remember Odet washing mud from a graft knife in a cup of rationed water, and Jori trying to make his shovel stand upright in the dust by packing stones around the blade.

She would remember all of that because the arrest itself happened with terrible neatness.

No shouting. No roadblock. No media drone visible in the sky.

Just two Compact security transports coming down the service road at first light, silent until they were close enough that everyone looked up at once.

Captain Vos stepped out first.

That gave Sana half a second of hope.

Then she saw the second officer carrying a warrant tube with a black archive band.

Ro saw it too. She crossed the corridor before Vos reached the mast. "No."

Vos stopped. He had shaved badly, or not at all. Red dust clung to the hem of his rain shell. "Mediator Tamsin."

"No."

"I have a warrant."

"You have a pending witness notice, unless someone converted it in the last three hours while we were saving the corridor."

Vos's face told her enough.

Ro held out her hand. "Show me."

He gave her the tube.

Kade stood by the road with his hands loose at his sides. He had not moved toward escape. He had not moved toward them. That made Sana angrier than flight would have. He looked like a man who had expected the road to send a bill eventually and disliked only the timing.

Sana walked to him. "Do not make this easy for them."

"I am considering several unhelpful options."

"Good. Choose one."

He almost smiled. "You are getting harsher."

"You are getting arrested."

"That can sour a personality."

Ro broke the seal and read. Her mouth tightened line by line.

"This is for historical unlawful transfer of restricted rootstock, breach of courier oath, alteration of seed chain, and suspected relation to present North Rain Vault contamination."

Tali was awake now. "Suspected relation? He helped us find the contamination."

Vos said, "The warrant was requested by Dr. Mirek Vale as technical respondent and endorsed by Long Orchard Archive compatibility counsel."

No one needed to ask which counsel.

Sana looked toward Kade. He did not flinch at Mirek's name. He did at compatibility counsel.

Ro said, "The Water Court just ordered preservation of Archive Office communications."

"The warrant cites risk of witness flight and evidence contamination."

"He has been standing in every record we filed."

"I know."

Vos said it quietly enough that Sana believed he did know and hated that knowledge did not help.

Amma approached from the repaired fungal bed, her shawl dusty, eyes sharp. "Captain, you were here when he stood watch over the same evidence he is accused of contaminating."

"I was not here. I received remote packets."

"Then your packets omitted useful facts."

"Often," Vos said.

Ro looked up from the warrant. "This is not only an arrest. It includes transfer authority to North Rain Vault holding."

Kade's hands changed.

Sana noticed because she was watching him too closely. His fingers curled once, then opened.

"No," she said.

Vos turned to her. "Auditor Ilyen-"

"North Rain Vault is not neutral. That is the contested archive site."

"The warrant states specialized seed-chain detention required."

"He is not a seed packet."

"No," Vos said. "He is a courier witness tied to a disputed seed chain."

The distinction had the coldness of a tool made for this exact day.

Kade said, "Sana."

"Do not."

"Listen."

"No."

He took one step closer. The officers shifted. Vos lifted a hand, and they stopped.

Kade spoke low. "If I run, they get to say old guilt is current guilt. If I fight, they get to say the same thing louder. If I go, they still say it, but you have the record of how they needed me silent."

"That is not a reason to let them take you."

"It may be the only useful one."

Tali came up beside them with dust in her hair and fury in every line of her body. "I can disable both transports."

Ro said, "No."

"You did not ask how quietly."

"No."

"One of them has a rear axle begging for instruction."

Kade looked at her. "Thank you."

"Do not thank me. I am not doing it, apparently."

"Still."

Odet pushed through the Firebreak volunteers. "They cannot take him for saving people."

Kade's face softened. "That is one of the ways people get taken."

Jori said, "We have shovels."

Lidia An put a hand on his shoulder. "And sense."

"Some."

"Use that part."

Sana turned to Vos. "Delay transfer. Hold him at Firebreak under local witness until Ro can challenge jurisdiction."

"I cannot."

"You can."

He looked at her, tired beyond paperwork now. "I cannot and still keep command of the arrest. If I refuse the transfer authority, they send archive enforcement or Puget security liaison. If I serve it, I can keep him visible."

Ro's eyes narrowed. "You are offering custody conditions?"

"I am stating what I can control."

"State it on record."

Vos opened his slate. "Captain Arlen Vos, Compact Security, serving warrant on Kade Morrow. Subject will be transported under visible custody, no private interrogation, no archive-only handling, medical check at transfer, counsel notification to Mediator Ro Tamsin and Water Court docket, continuous location seal, and no biological sampling without separate court order."

Mara Vale would hate that, Sana thought.

Not enough.

But hate was sometimes a measuring tool.

Ro said, "Add: subject is a material witness in Water Court emergency docket, Orchard Ledger docket, and Firebreak corridor sabotage filing."

Vos added it.

"Add: arrest occurs after subject assisted in preserving evidence and repairing living corridor."

He added it.

"Add: field auditor objects to North Rain Vault transfer as prejudicial."

Vos looked at Sana.

She said, "Yes."

He added it.

Kade watched the slate as if the words were weather. "You have become a difficult group."

Amma said, "You selected well."

"I did not select. I was dragged."

Sana said, "You walked."

He looked at her, and for a moment the corridor, the officers, the warrant, the broken mast all receded. "Eventually."

The second officer stepped forward with soft restraints.

Tali made a sound.

Kade lifted both wrists before the officer could ask.

Sana hated him for that. Only for a second. Then she hated the world that made it look wise.

Ro said, "Kade Morrow, do you authorize me to act as counsel contact and docket representative pending formal counsel?"

"Yes."

"Do you waive anything?"

"No."

"Do you consent to transfer?"

"No."

"Do you intend flight?"

He looked at the officers, then at the repaired corridor. "No."

"Do you intend silence?"

Kade's eyes moved to Sana.

"No," he said.

Vos nodded to the officer. The restraints closed with a sound too small for what it did.

Odet started crying silently. Jori looked at the ground as if trying to memorize where anger should be planted. Lidia held him in place. Amma watched with the expression of someone adding a new debt to the ledger she had not opened.

Sana stepped close to Kade before the officers led him away.

"Tell me what they will use," she said.

He understood immediately.

"Mara's exception. My courier key. The restricted rootstock. The cooperative I saved. Any missing seal they can connect to the North Rain Vault cluster."

"What did you never tell us?"

Ro glanced at her, sharp.

Kade did not pretend to misunderstand. "The cooperative was not on any legal settlement list."

Sana's stomach tightened. "Why?"

"Migrant salvage families. Burn-zone returnees. Children born between jurisdictions. People the Compact later counted after it became convenient to have saved them."

Amma said, very softly, "Debts no one should owe."

Kade looked at her. "Yes."

"Names?" Sana asked.

"Some."

"Where?"

He hesitated.

The officers began to move.

Kade said, "Mara kept a hunger appendix. Not in the capsule. Too dangerous. She sent it inland. I thought to Iona, but I never knew."

Amma's eyes narrowed.

Ro said, "Iona would have mentioned it."

Amma shook her head. "Not if it was in the debt ledger."

The unopened book seemed to grow heavier from thirty meters away.

Vos said, "We need to go."

Sana did not move. "Kade."

He met her eyes.

"You are not useful dead," she said.

"I will make a note."

"This is not charm."

"No." His voice changed. "I know."

The officers led him to the transport.

No media drone appeared. That was the second wrong thing. The first had been the neatness. If they had wanted public spectacle, they would have brought cameras. If they wanted quiet, they wanted the story shaped somewhere else before anyone saw the taking.

Ro saw the same thing. "We need to send our record first."

Tali was already moving. "I can push the custody conditions to every docket and three public mirrors."

"Do it."

"Four mirrors if I borrow Amma's uplink."

Amma handed her a key without asking what an uplink was, which meant she knew exactly.

The first transport turned around. Kade sat in the rear, visible through the side window because Vos had made that part of the record. He did not look noble. He looked tired, angry, and older than he had before the restraints. Good, Sana thought bitterly. Let no one edit this into martyrdom. Let it remain a man in a vehicle because old mercy had been made prosecutable again.

The transport moved east.

Sana watched until dust swallowed it.

Then she turned to Amma. "Open the debt ledger."

Amma did not answer at once.

Ro said, "If Kade's old cooperative is in it, the warrant's theory may expose why the illegal transfer happened."

"Not only why," Amma said. "Who was allowed to remain unnamed."

She walked back toward the transport hood where Tali was forcing the custody packet into the world. The volunteers made way for her. At the stone wall, she opened her satchel and withdrew the book she had refused to show them.

DEBTS NO ONE SHOULD OWE.

The cover was softer than the others, not from age but handling. Amma set it on the hood with both hands.

"This ledger is not for winning arguments," she said.

Sana stood opposite her. "What is it for?"

"Remembering what survival cost when no one wanted receipts."

She opened it.

The first pages were not columns. They were names.

Families without settlement codes. Children listed by first name because no one had agreed which jurisdiction could register them. Salvage crews who had cleared ash-choked culverts in exchange for seed that no office later admitted issuing. Hunger witnesses who had given testimony under numbers because names would have made them deportable, arrestable, deniable. Beside some names were small seed marks. Ash pear. Charbark apple. Dry bean. Millet. Root platform. Restricted.

Sana found she had to sit.

Amma turned pages until she reached a section marked WINTER RELEASE / COURIER EXCEPTION / MORROW-VENN.

There was Kade's name.

There was Mara Venn's.

There were two hundred and forty cooperative names, some written cleanly, some with question marks, some later corrected in a different hand. There were ration counts, illness marks, frost dates, seed weights, rootstock sources, and a note at the bottom:

OFFICIAL REQUEST DENIED DUE TO STATUS ABSENCE. RELEASE MADE UNDER HUNGER NECESSITY. IF PROSECUTED, ASK WHO BENEFITS FROM THE PEOPLE HAVING BEEN ILLEGAL BEFORE THEY WERE HUNGRY.

Ro read it twice.

"This is why they want him at North Rain Vault," she said.

Sana looked at the seed marks. "Because the illegal transfer proves the model once excluded people who later became official."

"And because the same rootstock saved them."

Amma touched the page. "Because mercy made a record."

Tali's slate chimed. "Custody packet posted. Public mirrors accepted. Also, someone is trying to throttle the third mirror, which means it is irritating the correct people."

Ro said, "Send the debt ledger page under seal to Water Court."

Amma closed the book halfway. "No."

Sana looked up.

Amma's eyes were hard. "Not the names. Not yet. You want to save Kade. I understand. I will not expose dead and living families because a court is finally curious."

Ro answered carefully. "We can redact."

"You can summarize."

"A summary is weaker."

"Names are not fuel."

The sentence landed cleanly.

Sana looked back at the page and felt shame because part of her had wanted the strongest evidence before thinking about the people inside it.

"Amma is right," she said.

Ro nodded after a moment. "We file an existence declaration. Ledger held by Hunger Council. Contains contemporaneous records of denied official status, emergency hunger release, courier exception, and preserved witness note. Names withheld pending consent or protective order."

Amma held her gaze. "Protective order first."

"Yes."

Tali said, "Transport route update. They are not taking Kade straight to North Rain Vault."

Everyone turned.

She enlarged the location seal. Vos's custody packet tracked the transport east, then north, then stopping at a transfer station labeled VALE TECHNICAL REVIEW ANNEX.

Ro went very still.

Sana said, "Mirek."

Kade had not been taken to the vault.

He had been taken to the man who had asked for him.

For one breath, no one moved.

Then Amma closed the debt ledger with a sound like a door deciding to become a wall.

"Now," she said, "you may hurry."


Chapter 23: The Mercy of Illegal Seeds

Hurrying with evidence was slower than panic wanted.

Ro would not let them leave Mouth-of-Ash until the existence declaration was sealed, duplicated, and lodged in four places. Amma would not let the declaration leave until every phrase stopped leaning toward exposure. Tali would not let the custody mirror run unattended because someone was still trying to throttle it, and every failed throttle attempt gave her another scrap of route information. Sana wanted the transport moving east so badly that she had to put both hands flat on Amma's ledger table to keep from grabbing keys and becoming less useful than her anger.

Kade's location seal sat in the corner of every slate.

VALE TECHNICAL REVIEW ANNEX.

Stationary.

Visible custody.

Medical check pending.

Counsel access requested.

No private interrogation logged.

The words looked like protections until Sana imagined the room around him.

Mirek Vale did not need a dark cell. He needed a clean table, a model display, and a sequence of questions shaped to make mercy look like contamination. He needed Kade tired. He needed Kade alone enough to answer as if confession and explanation were the same legal creature.

"Again," Amma said.

Ro read the declaration aloud for the third time. "The Mouth-of-Ash Hunger Council attests that it holds a contemporaneous ledger section documenting an emergency winter seed release involving courier witness Kade Morrow and archive dissenter Mara Venn. The ledger records that official relief request was denied due to absence of recognized settlement status; that restricted rootstock was released under hunger necessity; that the receiving cooperative consisted of families later incorporated into Compact population records; and that names are withheld pending protective order and consent review."

"Not 'receiving cooperative,'" Amma said.

Ro looked up. "Why?"

"It makes them sound like an entity recognized enough to receive. They were people gathered around hunger."

Ro edited. "The people who received the release..."

Amma nodded. "Better."

Sana said, "We need the effect. It has to say what the seed prevented."

Amma's face closed.

"Without names," Sana added.

Amma held her gaze, measuring whether shame had taught enough.

"There is an outcomes page," Amma said. "Not names. Counts."

She opened the debt ledger again, not at the name pages but twenty sheets later, where the handwriting changed to tight columns. WINTER RELEASE OUTCOMES. Sana leaned in.

The page tracked weeks after the illegal transfer: ration stabilization, fever reduction, seed multiplication, travel avoided, neighboring settlements fed, funerals prevented by estimate, requests for later official incorporation, and one column labeled CLUSTER STOPPED.

Ro saw it. "Cluster?"

Amma touched the column. "Hunger does not stay where officials put it. When one unlisted settlement collapses, people move. They carry fever, debt, desperation, sometimes seed disease, sometimes violence because there is no other currency left. Kade's rootstock did not just feed two hundred and forty people. It kept them from scattering into six settlements already close to ration failure."

Sana read the numbers.

Projected displacement: 173.

Actual displacement after release: 19.

Neighbor emergency requests avoided: 4.

Fever deaths reduced against prior winter: 31.

Seed multiplication achieved by week fourteen: enough for three satellite plots.

Cluster spread: stopped.

The room seemed to tilt around the last word.

"Who made the projection?" Ro asked.

"Mara Venn," Amma said. "And a basin healer named Sol Iren who official reports later called anecdotal."

Tali looked over from the uplink station. "I am developing an allergy to that word."

Ro said, "This turns the warrant. They are claiming Kade's old illegal transfer may relate to present contamination. The ledger shows the transfer prevented a famine cluster and created documented line stability."

"It still violated law," Amma said.

"Yes."

"Do not make it clean."

Sana looked at the page. "It was not clean. It was necessary."

Amma closed the ledger halfway again. "Those are different only to people who have eaten."

The custody seal blinked.

COUNSEL ACCESS: DELAYED / TECHNICAL REVIEW IN PROGRESS.

Ro's posture changed.

"No," she said.

Tali was already on it. "They are using technical review to hold before counsel."

"Can they?"

"They are doing it, which is not the same thing."

Ro opened a Water Court emergency channel. "File the declaration now."

Amma stopped her hand. "Add the outcome counts."

"Protective order?"

"Counts are not names. And if they are questioning him with the old crime, the old mercy gets to enter the room."

Ro added the outcome language, each number carefully limited, no identifying detail beyond the existence of the ledger and the sealed custody. She sent it to the Water Court, Orchard Ledger docket, Captain Vos, Iona Saye, and the public mirrors as a notice of protected evidence. Tali routed the public notice through Amma's uplink with a grin sharp enough to cut wire.

"Posted," Tali said. "Third mirror throttle failed. Fourth mirror replicated. Someone in Puget is now learning that rural bandwidth is vengeful."

"Good," Amma said.

Ro triggered a live counsel access demand. "Mediator Tamsin to Vale Technical Review Annex. Kade Morrow is a represented material witness. Delay of counsel access is contested. Confirm no private interrogation."

The channel spun.

No response.

Sana picked up her field kit. "We go."

This time no one stopped her.

The road to the Vale annex cut through a reclaimed industrial strip north of the basin, where old mineral yards had been converted into model farms and technical review stations. Everything there had fences, but none admitted they were fences. They were windbreaks, security orchards, contamination buffers, research perimeters. The annex itself stood behind three rows of sterile willow, a low white building under solar glass, clean enough to make dust look like trespass.

Captain Vos met them at the outer gate.

That was either very good or very bad.

"He is inside," Vos said before Ro could ask. "Visible custody. Medical check completed. No biological sampling. Technical review began before counsel access demand reached my slate."

"Ended?" Ro asked.

Vos's jaw shifted. "Paused."

"Paused by whom?"

"By me."

Sana looked at him.

He did not look heroic. He looked like a man measuring the distance between an order and a line he did not want to cross.

"Why?" Ro asked.

Vos held up his slate. On it was the protected evidence notice: emergency release, denied status, cluster stopped.

"This arrived," he said. "If true, the warrant omitted material context."

"It is true," Amma said.

Vos looked at her. "I assumed you would say that."

"Then why did you pause?"

"Because the counts are the kind of thing liars forget to invent."

Tali murmured, "Unexpectedly poetic."

Vos ignored her. "Mirek Vale is requesting continued technical interview."

"Deny it," Ro said.

"I cannot deny. I can require counsel present."

"Then do that."

"I did."

The sterile willow gate opened.

Inside, the annex smelled of filtered air and wet plastic. They passed through a decontamination arch that misted their boots and objected to the dust on Amma's shawl. Amma stared at the arch until it stopped chiming. A young technician led them to an observation room with a glass wall.

Kade sat on the other side at a white table.

His restraints were off. His hands rested flat in front of him. Across from him sat Dr. Mirek Vale, tablet closed, face grave. Between them stood a projection of a seed chain: restricted rootstock, courier exception, unlisted settlement, later Compact incorporation, current North Rain Vault cluster. Lines moved between old and new as if similarity were proof.

Sana's chest hurt.

Kade looked up when they entered the observation room.

Not at Ro.

At Sana.

He did not smile. Good. She might have broken something if he had.

Ro opened the chamber channel. "Kade Morrow, counsel contact present. Do you confirm you requested counsel access?"

"Yes," Kade said through the speaker.

Vale turned toward the glass. "This is a technical review, not an interrogation."

Ro said, "Then it can survive witnesses."

"The subject was clarifying historical chain anomalies."

"The represented material witness was being questioned about alleged crimes under a warrant you requested."

Vale inclined his head. "That is one framing."

Amma stepped to the speaker. "Here is another. A man moved seed to people your systems had not learned to count, and those people did not die in the numbers expected. Did your review include that?"

Vale's eyes moved to her. "Amma Quill."

"I dislike how many people know my name only when they need to step around me."

Kade looked down, and Sana saw the first crack in his composure. Not fear. Recognition.

Vale said, "Historical outcomes are relevant, but they do not erase breach risk."

Ro said, "No one says they erase breach risk. They change materiality, motive, harm, and continuity."

Vale opened his tablet. "The illegal release created an untracked descendant line."

Amma said, "The legal denial created untracked people."

Silence entered the room like a third authority.

Kade lifted his head.

"Do not make her do all of it," he said.

Ro touched the controls. "Kade, you do not have to answer."

"I know."

"Then be precise."

He nodded.

"The cooperative was called Three Forks because no one could agree which fork of the wash belonged to which office. It was not a legal name. We had children without settlement codes, salvage workers who had cleared roads the Compact used and then forgot, two pregnant women, one fever tent, and enough meal for nine days if everyone lied to the children about portions. Mara Venn denied the first request because the system would not accept the settlement. Then she sent me the exception language, because she was angrier than I was and better at hiding it."

Vale said, softly, "You cut the chain."

"Yes."

"You made later verification difficult."

"Yes."

"You may have created a route by which current actors now exploit uncertainty."

Kade looked at him. "And you may have created a model that calls dead people clean because they stayed in their assigned category."

Sana heard Tali inhale.

Vale did not move.

Kade continued, voice steady now. "I cut the chain. Mara recorded the dissent. The hunger ledger recorded the people. If you want to prosecute me, do it with all three records in the room. Not just the part where I broke a rule."

Ro's eyes shone with fierce professional restraint.

Vos said from the corner, "This is now beyond technical review."

"Agreed," Ro said. "Kade Morrow should be released to Water Court material witness custody pending hearing."

Vale stood. "He remains a breach vector."

Sana stepped to the glass. "No. He is a witness to the first time your categories failed this exact way."

"Auditor Ilyen, you keep turning failures of law into failures of model."

"Because you keep using the model to launder the law."

Vale's face tightened.

There. Not enough to convict. Enough to know the sentence had landed.

Amma said, "Ask your model about Three Forks."

Vale looked at her.

"Ask whether the official denial reduced risk."

"The historical model cannot be reconstructed fully."

"Then say that."

He did not.

Ro sent another filing while they stood there: protected evidence notice, custody challenge, motion to release Kade to material witness status, request to compel inclusion of Three Forks outcome counts in any technical review of the old transfer. The Water Court docket accepted it. Pell's clerk acknowledged emergency receipt. Vos added his custody observation.

Minutes stretched.

Kade waited with his hands flat.

Finally Vos's slate chimed.

He read the order twice.

"Water Court orders temporary stay of transfer to North Rain Vault," he said. "Subject to remain in Compact visible custody at neutral station pending expedited material witness hearing. Technical interview suspended unless counsel present and docketed. No archive-only handling."

Sana exhaled so hard she had to lean on the glass.

Kade closed his eyes.

Not freedom.

Not enough.

But not the vault.

Vale shut off the seed-chain projection. "You are mistaking delay for vindication."

Amma answered before anyone else could. "We are familiar with delay. Sometimes we use it to keep people alive."

Vale gathered his tablet. At the door, he paused.

"The old release did not solve famine," he said.

Kade looked up. "No."

"It saved one cluster."

"Yes."

"And left a breach."

Kade's voice did not shake. "Then say all of that."

Vale left.

When they brought Kade out, Sana did not embrace him. She wanted to. Instead she checked his wrists for marks because that was work and work did not ask permission to keep a person from falling apart.

"Medical check?" she asked.

"Humiliatingly normal."

"Good."

"That is a cold response."

"You are not dead."

"Your standards are moving."

"They have been under pressure."

Ro was already arranging the neutral station transfer. Tali was pulling logs from the annex guest network with an innocence so theatrical that even Vos looked away. Amma stood by the exit, ledger tucked under one arm, watching Kade as if deciding where his debt belonged.

Kade met her eyes. "I am sorry the names came back."

Amma said, "Names do that when the ground is disturbed."

"I did not know where Mara sent them."

"Now you do."

"Will you use them?"

"Only if they choose to be used."

He nodded. Sana saw relief and pain move through him together.

Outside, the afternoon light had turned the sterile willows silver. The Vale annex doors sealed behind them. Kade remained in custody, but he walked between Vos and Ro without restraints, visible to every docket mirror Tali could keep alive.

Sana looked toward the distant green line of the corridor.

Illegal seeds had not made the world just.

They had only kept enough people alive to ask why justice had arrived too late to feed them.

For now, that would have to be enough to fight with.


Chapter 24: Ro Breaks Neutral

The neutral station was neutral in the way a clean bowl was neutral after someone had chosen who was allowed to eat from it.

It stood at the junction of three jurisdictions: Water Court transport, Long Orchard Archive holding, and Compact civil security. The building had no banners, no orchard marks, no city colors, no basin cloth. Its walls were pale, its floors washable, its chairs identical. Every sign used procedural language. Intake. Interview. Rest. Review. Transfer. No sign said fear. No sign said power. No sign said that choosing a place where all authorities touched was also choosing a place where no one could easily be blamed.

Ro hated it on sight.

Sana could tell because Ro became extremely polite.

"Thank you," Ro said to the station clerk who tried to put Kade in a windowless rest room.

The clerk brightened with the doomed hope of a person who did not know Ro. "Of course."

"We will use the glass room."

"The glass room is for mediated party conferences."

"Excellent. Kade Morrow is a represented material witness whose custody conditions require continuous visibility."

"The room is not classified as a holding-"

"Even better. No one asked you to hold him invisibly."

The clerk looked at Captain Vos.

Vos looked at the ceiling.

Kade leaned toward Sana. "She is going to make someone cry."

"Probably with correct forms."

"Cruel."

"Efficient."

They got the glass room.

It was a square chamber at the center of the station, visible from the public corridor on two sides and from a clerk bay on the third. Kade sat inside with tea, a medical sensor band, and no restraints. Vos posted one officer outside the door and entered the custody conditions into three dockets. Tali found a place under a wall display to sit cross-legged with her slate and an expression that discouraged conversation. Amma placed the debt ledger on the table in front of her and rested both palms on it as if calming an animal.

Sana stood by the glass, watching people pretend not to look.

The public mirrors had changed everything.

Not enough, never enough, but visibly. Kade's arrest record, the custody conditions, the Three Forks outcome counts, the temporary stay, the corridor sabotage packet, and the Orchard Ledger's narrow certification had spread faster than Puget's first accusation could fully adapt. Puget feeds millions remained true. Basin agitators target seed claim site remained useful. But now another sentence moved beside it:

OFFICIAL DENIAL PRECEDED ILLEGAL RELEASE THAT STOPPED FAMINE CLUSTER.

The words had escaped.
