They were being fought, mocked, misquoted, clipped, and challenged. Which meant they had arrived.

Ro received the succession court notice at the same time Sana saw the first media drone settle outside the station's legal perimeter.

Ro read the notice once. Then again. Her expression went still in a way that made Sana straighten.

"What?" Sana asked.

"Succession Court has issued a neutrality warning."

Tali looked up. "To whom?"

"To me."

Kade lowered his tea.

Ro set the notice on the table where everyone could see. It bore the seal of the Cascadia Stewardship Succession Court, not Water Court. Different authority. Higher ceremony. Sharper knives.

MEDIATOR RO TAMSIN IS CAUTIONED THAT PUBLIC STATEMENTS OR FILINGS APPEARING TO ADVOCATE FOR ANY PARTY MAY COMPROMISE RECOGNIZED NEUTRAL STATUS IN ONGOING SUCCESSION AND RESOURCE DISPUTE PROCEEDINGS.

Amma snorted. "Appearing."

"That is the hinge," Ro said.

Sana read the notice again. "They want you quiet."

"They want me balanced."

Tali said, "Between hunger and the people creating it?"

Ro smiled faintly. "That would be an appearance of advocacy."

Kade said, "Can they remove you?"

"They can challenge my standing. Exclude my filings. Require substitution. Treat anything I submit as partisan."

"Would that hurt us?"

"Yes."

No one spoke for a moment.

Outside the station, another media drone arrived, then a third. Their lenses angled toward the glass room. Tali's public mirror had made the room too visible to ignore.

Sana said, "Then do not give them more."

Ro looked at her.

"I am serious," Sana said. "We need your filings. We need you inside the process."

Ro's face did not change. "Inside the process doing what?"

"Keeping Kade visible. Challenging the model. Protecting the grafts."

"And when the process asks me to call a false balance neutral?"

Amma said, "There it is."

Sana turned on her. "We cannot lose Ro."

"You may lose her more thoroughly if she stays where they tell her to stand."

Ro touched the edge of the notice. "My recognition as mediator rests on the idea that I do not favor Puget, the basins, the Archive Office, or any claimant to succession."

Kade said, "Do you?"

"No."

"That was quick."

"I do not favor them as factions." Ro's eyes moved to the glass, to the drones outside, to the corridor of clerks pretending not to watch. "I favor the record including the hungry before hunger becomes unrest. I favor a model that counts prevented loss before calling a place low-yield. I favor witnesses not being disappeared into the archive whose conduct they can describe. I favor living grafts over dormant claims when no biological danger has been named."

Tali said, "That sounds like advocacy."

"It is."

Sana's stomach dropped.

Ro stood.

"Ro."

"I know."

"If you do this wrong-"

"Then help me do it accurately."

She opened her slate and began a public statement.

Not a speech at first. A filing. Ro built it the way she built every dangerous thing: jurisdiction, standing, record, facts, objection, relief requested. But the sentences had heat under their polish.

Sana read over her shoulder.

I remain neutral among claimants. I do not remain neutral between complete and incomplete records.

Ro paused. "Too much?"

Amma said, "Not enough."

Sana said, "Keep it."

Ro continued.

A proceeding that treats extracted yield as visible and prevented loss as local sentiment has chosen a side while calling the choice methodology. A court that warns a mediator against naming omitted hunger asks neutrality to become silence. I decline that form of neutrality.

Kade whispered, "Ro."

She did not look up. "Yes?"

"They will come for your recognition."

"Likely."

"Thank you."

"Do not thank me. Testify well."

"Cruel."

"Efficient," Sana said.

The statement grew.

Ro cited the Orchard Ledger certification. The omitted categories. The Three Forks protected evidence notice. The Water Court stay. The Firebreak sabotage packet. The claim notices placed after provisional planting. She did not accuse Puget of sabotage. She did not accuse Mara Vale of conspiracy. She did not call Mirek Vale corrupt. She did something harder to strike: she laid sequence beside consequence and refused to pretend that arrangement was neutral.

Tali read the final paragraph aloud.

"Neutrality is not the equal spacing of a chair between a locked vault and a hungry witness. Neutrality is fidelity to the conditions under which parties can be heard without one side's suffering being classified as noise. I will continue to mediate between claimants. I will not mediate between evidence and its omission."

The room was silent.

Even the clerk bay had stopped typing.

Sana felt fear and admiration in such close contact that she could not tell which made her colder.

"File it?" Ro asked.

No one answered quickly.

Then Kade said, "File it before you become wise."

Ro sent it.

The public mirrors took it first. Then the Water Court docket. Then the Succession Court, where it sat pending for twelve seconds before a red procedural flag appeared.

MEDIATOR STATUS REVIEW TRIGGERED.

Tali looked at the flag. "Well. They read fast when insulted structurally."

Ro sat down.

Her hands were steady. Sana wanted to take that as comfort and knew better.

Outside, the media drones lifted and rearranged. A public feed prompt appeared on the station wall before anyone approved it. The clerk made a small panicked sound. Ro's statement had already become news.

MEDIATOR CHALLENGES SUCCESSION COURT NEUTRALITY WARNING.

BASIN-ALIGNED OFFICIAL CLAIMS "OMITTED HUNGER" IN RESOURCE MODEL.

RO TAMSIN: "I DECLINE THAT FORM OF NEUTRALITY."

"Basin-aligned," Tali said. "That took under a minute."

Ro watched the headline. "Expected."

Sana said, "Does expectation make it better?"

"No."

Captain Vos entered the glass room without knocking. "Succession Court requests immediate clarification whether your statement represents withdrawal from neutral mediator role."

Ro looked up. "No."

"They request you refrain from further public comment pending status review."

"No."

Vos's eyebrows rose.

Ro said, "You are only messenger."

"I am enjoying it less than usual."

She dictated her clarification in front of him. "I do not withdraw from mediator role. I distinguish neutrality among parties from silence regarding record integrity, witness visibility, and model completeness. I will refrain from speculative accusation. I will not refrain from docketed fact."

Vos sent it.

Amma looked pleased in the way dry ground looked pleased when rain began far away.

Kade said, "They may replace you."

"They may try."

"What happens if they succeed?"

Ro looked at Sana. "Then everything I filed remains in the record, and someone else has to explain why removing me improved neutrality."

Tali grinned. "That is obnoxious."

"Thank you."

"Professional respect."

The station doors opened and admitted a woman in a dark blue court coat, flanked by two clerks. She had the smooth step of someone used to rooms becoming quieter before she entered. The Succession Court seal glowed on her lapel.

"Mediator Tamsin," she said. "I am Adjudicator Lale Oric, status review officer."

Ro stood. "Adjudicator."

Oric looked into the glass room: Kade at the table, Amma with the ledger, Sana mud-streaked, Tali dusty and grinning, Vos tired, public drones outside like patient insects.

"You have created a difficulty," Oric said.

Ro answered, "It was already there."

"You made it legible."

"Yes."

Oric studied her for a long moment. "Do you understand that public confidence in succession procedure depends on recognized neutrals appearing neutral?"

"Public confidence in procedure also depends on procedure not starving people in neutral language."

A clerk inhaled sharply.

Oric's gaze moved to the clerk. The clerk became still.

"Mediator Tamsin," Oric said, "do you allege the Succession Court intends hunger harm?"

"No. I allege the court has been warned that its current model omits categories material to hunger harm, and that warning mediators not to say so compounds the omission."

"Do you allege partiality?"

"I allege structure."

Oric's mouth almost moved. Almost a smile. Almost irritation. "That is more annoying."

"Yes."

Sana breathed for the first time in several seconds.

Oric turned toward Kade. "This witness remains under Water Court custody?"

Vos answered. "Visible Compact custody pending material witness hearing."

"And the Succession Court has not yet accepted model scope challenge?"

Ro said, "It has received notice."

"It has received many notices today."

Amma said, "Some days are educational."

Oric looked at her. "Amma Quill."

"Apparently."

"Your ledgers are causing trouble."

"They were quiet for decades. Trouble found them."

Oric looked back to Ro. "Interim decision. Your mediator recognition is not suspended pending full review. You are admonished to label advocacy statements as record-integrity objections rather than party arguments. Further public rhetoric may alter that decision."

Ro said, "I will label accurately."

"You always do, I suspect. That is part of the difficulty."

Oric turned to leave, then paused at the door. "For what it is worth, the phrase about the chair was excessive."

Tali opened her mouth.

Sana kicked her ankle.

Oric left.

The glass room exhaled.

Ro sat down hard enough that the chair complained.

Kade lifted his tea. "To excessive chairs."

"Do not toast my disciplinary hearing," Ro said.

He lowered the cup.

Sana leaned against the glass. Outside, the media drones had plenty to feed on. Inside, the record had changed. Ro remained recognized, but not untouched. The court had named the danger without admitting it feared the truth: public rhetoric. Legibility. Chairs too close to hunger.

Amma opened her ledger, not the debt ledger but HUNGER DAYS, and wrote one line.

Sana looked over.

Date. Neutrality warning challenged. Witness remained visible. No meals restored yet.

"No meals restored yet," Sana read.

Amma closed the book. "That is how we know what victory is not."

Ro looked at the closed ledger for a long time.

Then her slate chimed.

Water Court material witness hearing set: six hours.

Succession model scope review advanced: tomorrow dawn.

North Rain Vault emergency release status: unchanged.

Sana looked at the last line.

Unchanged.

The word had become an accusation.

Ro stood again, slower this time. "Then we prepare."

"For which hearing?" Kade asked.

Ro looked at the glass, the drones, the ledgers, the people reflected faintly over one another.

"All of them," she said.


Chapter 25: Spore Trial

The first mill alarm arrived as a smell.

Not a siren. Not a flashing court notice. Not a governor's statement arranged around children and green towers. Just a sourness in the air outside the neutral station, faint at first, then unmistakable when the wind turned: warm protein going wrong.

Tali noticed before the station sensors did.

She lifted her head from the slate where she was rebuilding the pollinator drone route stub. "That is not lunch."

Kade, still inside the glass room, looked toward the vents. "No."

Amma closed HUNGER DAYS. "Mill."

Ro's slate chimed a second later.

FUNGAL PROTEIN MILL 7B / CASCADIA INTERIOR SUPPLY NODE / FERMENTATION DRIFT OUTSIDE SAFE BAND / OUTPUT HOLD.

Then another.

MILL 7B REQUESTS EMERGENCY CULTURE RELEASE.

Then the line that made Sana move before she finished reading:

STARTER CULTURE BACKING UNAVAILABLE. RELEASE AUTHORITY CONFLICT.

Mill 7B sat four kilometers from the neutral station, close enough that its waste heat fed two greenhouse blocks and its protein output fed six jurisdictions that all pretended not to depend on one another. It did not look like a farm or a factory. It looked like a hill someone had taught to breathe through silver ribs. Fermentation stacks rose under turf and moss. Condensers glittered along the north face. Intake ponds lay flat and black beside the service road, each pond covered with mesh to keep birds from becoming part of the food system in unhelpful ways.

By the time they arrived, workers were already outside in masks.

That was how Sana knew it was bad.

Mills did not evacuate for inconvenience. Fungal protein systems drifted, corrected, sulked, foamed, and occasionally made technicians invent new profanity, but they were designed to fail inward. When workers left the building, it meant the culture had stopped being food and started becoming evidence.

Ro had argued Kade's custody all the way there. Vos relented only after the mill request copied the Water Court docket and listed Kade as material witness in related emergency food proceedings. So Kade came in the second transport, visible, guarded, not free. He stepped out with a mask in one hand and irritation in both eyes.

"If I am a breach vector," he said, "this seems like the wrong place to bring me."

Vos said, "Do not make me regret procedural flexibility."

"I prefer making people regret rigidity."

"Noted."

The mill supervisor met them at the outer gate. Her name tag read EMIN SAL, CULTURE CHIEF. She had the gaunt focus of someone whose disaster had not yet selected a final shape.

"Who has authority?" she demanded.

Ro, Sana, and Vos all said, "Depends."

Emin stared.

Tali said, "Welcome to modern governance."

Sana stepped forward. "Field Auditor Ilyen. What drift?"

"Protein Stack Three. Spore balance skewed, bitter peptide spike, heat bloom in lower trays. We isolated before toxin threshold. Output held. If we do not reseed inside six hours, the whole stack goes down and we lose three days of regional protein."

"Cause?"

"Starter culture mismatch."

"Bad batch?"

"Missing batch."

Ro looked up from her slate. "Missing?"

Emin turned and walked fast. They followed through decontamination, mask seals, and a corridor lined with windows into the fermentation hall. Inside, stacked trays stretched into humid distance, each layer a pale living mat under amber work light. Fans moved breath-warm air through the room. The sour smell thickened. Workers in white suits cut sample plugs from trays and dropped them into sealed tubes.

Emin pointed to a darkened stack. "Three should have been reseeded last night with culture B-17 ash-myc line. Instead the system substituted B-19 emergency analog."

Tali said, "Substituted automatically?"

"Under release authority."

Ro stopped walking. "Whose release authority?"

Emin's jaw tightened. "That is why I asked who has authority."

They entered the culture vault.

Sana had expected cold racks and labeled vials. She got both, but the room also held something more like a chapel: glass cabinets of fungal starters descended from pre-Famine lines, drought-adapted basin cultures, coastal protein mats, firebreak nutrient bridges, floodproof mycelial scaffolds, each one a quiet ancestor of meals no one thanked. The vault lights were blue. Every breath felt borrowed.

Emin opened the release log.

B-17 ASH-MYC STARTER / HELD FOR MILL 7B STACK THREE / STATUS: REROUTED.

REROUTE AUTHORITY: EMERGENCY SUCCESSION FOOD STABILITY PROTOCOL.

DESTINATION: SEALED.

REQUESTING ENTITY: CONTINUITY MODEL TRIAGE OFFICE.

TECHNICAL RESPONDENT: DR. MIREK VALE.

No one spoke.

Then Amma said, "He took food from a mill to model food stability."

Emin's eyes widened. "Is that what this means?"

Ro's voice had gone flat. "It means the culture backing your stack was rerouted under emergency authority."

"Without notifying the mill?"

"Apparently."

Emin looked at the log as if it had insulted her personally. "B-19 is not equivalent. It can bridge for feedstock transitions, not ash-myc lower trays. Anyone with a current culture table knows that."

Tali leaned over the log. "Continuity Model Triage Office should have a current table."

"Then they ignored it or used an old one."

Sana said, "What happens if Stack Three fails?"

"Three days lost protein, maybe four if decontamination takes longer. Puget can cover itself. Firebreak cannot. Mouth-of-Ash cannot. Two hospital kitchens cannot. School ration supplement drops by eighteen percent in the affected belt."

Amma's face had emptied.

No meals restored yet, Sana thought.

Now meals removed.

Ro copied the log with a court seal. "We need the destination."

Emin gave a humorless laugh. "I need my culture."

"Can you reseed with anything else?"

"Maybe. Not cleanly. We can use B-21 ember bridge if someone authorizes breaking reserve."

"Who can?"

Emin pointed at the release screen. "Today? Apparently anyone except the people standing in the mill."

Kade moved closer to the cabinets. The guard shifted, but Vos stopped him with a look.

Kade read the labels. "B-17 ash-myc. Is that related to Firebreak ash pear support?"

Sana followed his gaze.

The starter line was not seed. Not rootstock. But the network had never been just seeds. Fungal cultures supported protein, soil, graft healing, and emergency nutrient bridges. Ash-myc lower trays were often paired with firebreak orchards because the same smoke-tolerant nutrient exchange that helped trees after burns could stabilize high-protein mats under heat stress.

"Yes," Sana said. "Same ecological family."

Tali said, "Which means the reroute may not be random."

Emin looked from one to another. "I am trying to stop a mill from failing. If you have a conspiracy, label it later."

"Fair," Sana said. "Show me B-21."

They worked in the vault for forty minutes.

It was different from night grafting but not as different as Sana wanted. Living systems under stress, human authority arriving late, labels pretending to explain more than they did. Emin knew her cultures the way Sana knew grafts: by behavior, not just tag. Together they built a reseed plan that would not save the stack cleanly but might keep enough protein online to prevent ration collapse. B-21 ember bridge for lower trays. B-12 river mat in microdose to buffer bitterness. Increased oxygen pulse. Lower heat by two degrees, no more or the culture would stall.

Tali checked the control system for sabotage and found none.

"This part is just bad authority," she said. "I almost miss wires."

Ro stood at the release terminal, fighting for reserve authorization. Every channel said pending. Every pending carried a different office seal. Succession Court. Continuity Triage. Archive compatibility. Food insurer. Water Court emergency.

"Too many hands on one throat," Amma said.

Kade, still reading labels under guard, said, "Then use the old exception."

Ro looked over. "What old exception?"

"Mill famine clause. Protein mills were given local culture authority after the Inland Rationing because central release delays killed three towns. If a stack drift threatens regional ration failure, culture chiefs can break reserve and answer after."

Emin stared at him. "That exists?"

"It did."

Tali said, "Why does the arrested courier know more useful law than the mill chief?"

Kade shrugged. "Couriers carried bad news between people who had to keep living after offices closed."

Ro searched fast. "I have no current clause."

Amma said, "Of course not."

Kade closed his eyes. "Try Emergency Fermentation Continuity Addendum, clause nine. Or Protein Commons Appendix. It used to be stamped on mill doors."

Emin said, "There is old text in the south airlock."

They ran.

The south airlock had been repainted five times, but under the chipped white seal, raised letters remained in the old composite door. Sana wiped condensation away with her sleeve.

WHERE FERMENTATION FAILURE THREATENS PUBLIC RATION, CULTURE CHIEF MAY BREAK RESERVE UNDER WITNESS, RECORD, AND RESTORATION DUTY. DELAY SHALL NOT BE MADE HOLY BY DISTANCE.

Tali read the last sentence twice. "Old law had style."

Ro photographed it, sealed it, and sent it to Water Court with a request for noninterference. Then she turned to Emin. "Break reserve."

Emin did not move.

For all her urgency, for all her anger, this was the line. Breaking reserve would save meals and risk discipline. Waiting would preserve procedure and lose food.

Sana knew the look. She had seen it in herself before opening field orders. In Ro before filing the neutrality statement. In Kade before lifting his wrists.

Amma stepped forward. "I will witness."

Ro said, "I will record."

Sana said, "I will attest biological necessity."

Tali said, "I will make the machine tell the truth afterward."

Kade said nothing.

Emin looked at him.

He smiled faintly. "I am not allowed to advise on illegal food mercy at the moment."

That broke something. Not in Emin. Around her.

She turned to the vault terminal and entered her palm seal.

CULTURE CHIEF RESERVE BREAK / CLAUSE NINE ASSERTED.

The screen hesitated. Then accepted.

For the next two hours, the mill became a trial in the older meaning of the word: a test by ordeal, not of guilt but of whether a living process could survive what authority had done to it. Workers moved with fierce relief. B-21 thawed in blue light. B-12 microdose passed through three confirmations. Stack Three's lower trays were opened in sections, sour heat rolling out in waves. Sana helped lay the starter mist, reading mat color and spore bloom while Emin called adjustments. Tali forced the control logs to preserve every override. Ro narrated the legal chain until her voice went hoarse. Amma counted workers who skipped meal break and made someone feed them anyway.

Kade watched from the clean line with a guard beside him.

Once, when Sana looked back, he lifted two fingers to his brow in a courier salute.

She nearly threw a sample tube at him.

The stack stabilized at the fifth hour.

Not recovered. Not safe. Stabilized.

Output loss projected: twenty-two percent instead of total hold.

Regional ration impact: reduced but not avoided.

Emergency supplement gap: still severe for Firebreak, Mouth-of-Ash, two clinics, and school kitchens.

Emin removed her mask and sat on the floor.

"We saved enough to be blamed for what remains," she said.

Amma handed her water. "Yes."

"That is not comfort."

"No."

Ro's slate chimed. Water Court acknowledged clause nine filing. Succession Court requested explanation. Continuity Triage Office objected to unauthorized reserve break. A food insurer filed notice of coverage concern.

Tali read over her shoulder. "They object fast when protein survives without permission."

Sana looked at the reroute log again. Destination sealed. Requesting entity: Continuity Model Triage Office. Technical respondent: Dr. Mirek Vale.

"Where did B-17 go?" she asked.

Ro said, "That is now the question."

Emin laughed once, bitter. "It was already my question."

"Now it is ours too."

Kade spoke from the clean line. "If someone rerouted ash-myc culture, they may be building a controlled demonstration."

Sana turned. "Of what?"

"That emergency release causes instability. Withhold the correct culture, let the substitute fail, then point to drift as proof that local discretion is dangerous."

Ro's face hardened. "Or use B-17 somewhere else to stabilize a favored line while this mill takes the loss."

Amma said, "Either way, someone made a meal move invisibly."

No meals restored yet.

Some meals removed.

Sana removed her gloves slowly. "We need to map every living dependency at once. Seeds, grafts, fungal cultures, pollinator routes, water, hunger ledgers, sabotage sites, claim notices, model omissions, and emergency reroutes."

Tali said, "That is not a map. That is a nervous system."

"Then we build one."

Ro looked exhausted enough to fall and awake enough to burn. "Chapter of evidence?"

"Pattern," Sana said.

Amma nodded. "The Long Orchard map."

Kade's guard cleared his throat. "We need to return the witness to neutral station."

Kade did not move immediately. He looked at the mill, at Emin on the floor, at the workers resealing Stack Three, at the culture vault where one missing starter had nearly become hunger.

"Illegal seeds," he said softly. "Illegal spores. Illegal mercy. They keep making the same mistake."

"Which is?" Sana asked.

"Thinking life cares whether permission arrived first."

Outside, the sour smell had thinned but not vanished. The mill breathed unevenly through silver ribs. Beyond it, the regions it fed would receive smaller portions and official explanations by morning.

Sana watched the workers clean the culture lines and understood that the next fight could not be won one wound at a time.

They needed the whole living pattern.

Chapter 26: The Long Orchard Map

The neutral station had never been built to hold a nervous system.

It had been built to hold arguments.

Its main analysis room was a polite architecture of denial: glass walls, equal doors on opposite sides, benches set at matching angles, projection panels mounted so no jurisdiction appeared to stand at the head of the table. Every surface insisted that power could be made harmless if no one was allowed the best chair.

By midnight, Sana had ruined the room.

She had every wall alive with maps.

Not one map. That would have been the old mistake. One map made people choose what counted. Water. Food. Law. Ownership. Disease. Weather. Transport. Labor. Debt. Seeds. Pollination. Each discipline liked its own clean layer, its own color, its own legend, its own claim that every other layer was context.

Sana wanted context to stop behaving.

She stood barefoot on the table because the floor panels had become display space. The station had not objected yet, which either meant Tali had silenced the room etiquette software or the software had decided that no one trying to overlay thirty-seven emergency records at once could be reasoned with.

"Again," Sana said.

Tali, seated inside a semicircle of borrowed slates, dragged one finger through the air. "From which disaster?"

"All of them."

"That is not a query. That is a mood."

"Start with living dependency."

The room changed.

Seed vault locations appeared first: municipal, private, cooperative, abandoned, disputed, hidden, rumored. Each one pulsed according to recent access logs where logs existed and according to transport gossip where logs did not. Graft nurseries followed in pale green. Fungal culture banks in amber. Pollinator drone roosts in blue. Wild bee refuges in softer blue, the data less complete and somehow more honest about it.

Then Amma added hunger.

She did not use the official ration layer. She brought HUNGER DAYS to the central feed and let the dead hand of the ledger write over the jurisdictional map. Kitchens. Clinic requests. School supplement drops. Unreported meal thinning. Households borrowing flour against labor. Irrigation workers paid in future protein coupons. Firebreak, Mouth-of-Ash, the lower basin towns, two mobile clinic routes, one elder house that had not appeared in any emergency model because its residents had no current utility accounts.

The map darkened.

Ro arrived carrying three sealed court packets and the expression of someone who had slept for eleven minutes in a chair and hated the chair for wasting even that.

"I have Water Court docket traces," she said. "Succession notices, insurer holds, and every legal objection that arrived faster than food."

"Put them on," Sana said.

Ro hesitated.

That hesitation mattered. Sana looked down from the table.

"What?"

"Some of this is sealed."

Tali laughed once without humor. "We are building a map of invisible hunger from stolen logs, illegal seeds, sabotage traces, and one arrested courier's memory of door law. Sealed may have become decorative."

Ro did not smile. "Sealed still has teeth."

Kade spoke from the wall bench. Vos had returned him to the station after the mill stabilization and then, under protest from every office that liked clean categories, left him in the analysis room under guard. Not free. Not removed. A human contradiction with a custody band around one wrist.

"Seals bite witnesses first," he said. "Lawyers second. Powerful people last, if at all."

Ro looked at him. "Are you advising caution?"

"No. I am advising aim."

Sana climbed down from the table. Her legs tingled from standing too long. "We do not need names yet. Put the existence of the notices on the map. Origin, time, target, effect. Keep restricted text out of the room feed."

Ro considered, then nodded. "That I can defend."

The legal layer appeared as thin white lines. Claim notices. Injunction requests. Emergency holds. Coverage concerns. Jurisdictional challenges. They did not look like law once they crossed the living layers. They looked like fences thrown from orbit.

Tali whistled softly. "That is ugly."

"Ugly can still be random," Vos said.

He stood near the door, refusing a chair, uniform immaculate except for the ash-myc stain on one sleeve from the mill. Sana had begun to suspect he kept stains when they were evidence and removed them when they were merely dirt.

"Show sabotage," she said.

Tali opened the drone incidents: pollinator route collapse, false blight signature, vandalized graft markers, the North Rain Cellar release delay, communications ghosts along dry basin routes, Mill 7B culture reroute, and three smaller anomalies Sana had not had time to absorb. Someone had spoofed greenhouse humidity readings near a cooperative nursery two weeks earlier. Someone had filed a duplicate seed ownership claim in the lower basin under the name of a woman dead for nine years. Someone had convinced an archive compatibility filter to reject three emergency graft transfers because the old orchard names contained dialect spellings.

The red marks spread.

Not evenly.

Sana stopped breathing for three counts.

"Take off jurisdiction borders," she said.

The old lines vanished.

The pattern sharpened.

The attacks did not follow law. They did not follow ownership. They did not even follow geography as governments drew it. They followed corridors of recovery: seed to graft, graft to pollination, pollination to ration, ration to labor, labor to water repair, water repair back to seed. Each strike landed where a community had more living resilience than recorded power.

Firebreak ash pears.

Dry basin bean vaults.

Mouth-of-Ash protein supplements.

Lower irrigation crews.

School kitchens on mixed-crop routes.

Pollinator roosts that serviced cooperative orchards before corporate towers.

Fungal cultures tied to burn recovery.

Claims that did not seize whole systems, only delayed the one living bridge that would let people survive without asking permission from the center.

Amma stood very still.

"They are not attacking food," she said.

Ro replied, "They are attacking independent recovery."

Kade looked at the map with the unhappy recognition of someone seeing a road from above after walking it wounded. "And making each failure look local."

Tali enlarged the mill reroute. The white legal line from Continuity Model Triage crossed amber culture dependency, then vanished into sealed destination. The red risk bloom appeared at Mill 7B. Hunger darkened downstream.

"If you do not see the culture line," Tali said, "the mill looks incompetent."

Sana pointed at the false blight event. "If you do not see the pollinator route, the orchard looks diseased."

Amma touched a cluster of school kitchens. "If you do not see the ration thinning, children look absent because parents are careless."

Ro added, "If you do not see the claim pattern, the court sees disputes, not siege."

"Who benefits?" Vos asked.

That was the question the room wanted. It was also the question that made rooms stupid if asked too early. Sana had watched councils leap at names because names were smaller than systems. Names could be arrested. Systems had to be understood.

"Not yet," she said.

Vos's eyebrows lifted.

"We have named suspects," Sana said. "Vale. The Continuity Model Triage Office. Insurer channels. Succession claimants. Puget procurement. Maybe someone inside archive compatibility. Maybe all of them, maybe some of them, maybe one hand using their habits. But the map is not ready to accuse. It is ready to predict."

Tali turned slowly toward her. "That is a dangerous sentence."

"Good."

Sana stepped back onto the table because from there she could see the whole wall at once.

"If the pattern is independent recovery, the next strike will not be at the most valuable asset. It will be at the most connecting one. Something boring. Something that lets two damaged systems help each other without central approval."

Amma's fingers moved through HUNGER DAYS, not typing yet, just counting invisible meals. "Water?"

"Maybe."

Ro added, "A legal bridge?"

"Maybe."

Kade said, "Transport."

Everyone looked at him.

He nodded toward the corridor map. "Seeds do not move themselves. Cultures do not move themselves. Hungry people walk, but not always far enough. If you want to make independent recovery fail, you let communities keep what they have and prevent exchange. Then every survivor becomes an island with a locked pantry."

Tali pulled transport layers. Official roads. Courier routes. Drone corridors. Canal barges. Footpaths recorded only because clinic workers used them. Old orchard maintenance lines. Emergency cold-chain paths for cultures.

The map changed again.

A thin route glowed between Mouth-of-Ash, Mill 7B, the lower basin graft cooperatives, and Firebreak. It had no proud name. On old transport files it was Service Link C-14. In courier notes it was called the Long Orchard run because it moved through more living food systems than towns: spores, scions, clinic supplements, school yeast, pollinator batteries, water valves, and letters written on paper because paper survived outages and embarrassment.

Kade's face had gone closed.

Sana saw it.

"You know it."

"I worked it."

"Recently?"

"Before arrest."

Ro's slate was already open. "When?"

Kade's guard shifted. Vos did not stop the question.

"Six days ago," Kade said. "I carried graft viability strips from the lower basin to Firebreak, then a sealed cold pouch from Firebreak to a cooperative kitchen outside Mouth-of-Ash."

Tali froze. "Cold pouch."

"Legal?"

Kade gave her a look. "That depends who writes history before breakfast."

Sana climbed down. "What was in it?"

"I did not open it."

Ro's expression hardened.

"Because," Kade said before she could speak, "the sender was an eighty-two-year-old graft keeper with tremors who knew exactly how to seal a culture pouch and exactly how to shame a courier into not insulting her competence."

Amma said, "Name."

"Mara Venn."

The air changed.

Sana did not know the name, but Amma did. So did Ro. Even Vos's eyes moved.

Tali searched. A new file opened: VENN, MARA. Cooperative graft keeper. Witness in three water allocation disputes. Contributor to post-Famine seed restoration. Declared unreliable by a private orchard consortium after refusing exclusive licensing. No current institutional post. Age eighty-two. Residence: lower basin, south ridge terraces.

Status: emergency contact failed.

Sana's stomach tightened. "Failed when?"

"This morning," Tali said.

Ro swore softly.

Kade stood. The guard's hand went to his baton. Kade stopped himself, but the movement had already told the room what mattered.

"She was alive six days ago," he said.

Tali kept reading. "South ridge comm relay intermittent. Clinic route delayed by claim notice on bridge access. Drone overflight denied for pollinator privacy dispute."

Ro stared at the legal layer. "That claim notice is one of mine."

"Yours?" Amma said.

"One I brought. Not one I filed. It hit the bridge eighteen hours after Mill 7B reroute."

Sana pulled the bridge onto the main wall. The Long Orchard run narrowed there between flood-scoured terraces and an old rail spine converted to foot and cart traffic. Not dramatic. Not valuable on a procurement sheet. But if the bridge closed, Mara Venn could not move cultures to Mouth-of-Ash, Mouth-of-Ash could not move clinic supplements back, Firebreak could not receive lower basin graft supports, and Mill 7B's reduced output would become more dangerous because there would be fewer side channels to compensate.

Something boring.

Something connecting.

The room went quiet enough for the display fans to sound like breath.

Vos said, "Evidence of threat?"

Tali answered before Sana could. "Bridge access claim. Relay failure. Drone denial. Emergency contact failed. Culture reroute. Hunger downstream. It is not proof of a direct threat to Venn."

"But?"

"But if the pattern is real, she is standing on the next joint."

Amma closed HUNGER DAYS gently, as if not startling the ledger mattered. "Then we go."

Ro said, "We need authority."

Tali pointed at Kade. "We have a courier who knows the run."

"He is in custody."

Kade lifted his banded wrist. "I keep hearing that as if it solved anything."

Vos looked at the map. Sana watched his face move through law, risk, career, hunger, and something older than uniform.

"Material witness field accompaniment," he said at last. "Under guard. Limited route. No communication without supervision."

Ro blinked. "You can authorize that?"

"Apparently anyone can authorize anything today if they say emergency with enough sadness."

Tali smiled despite herself.

Sana did not. She was looking at the map, at the Long Orchard run glowing like a nerve under skin.

"We also need to preserve this," she said.

Tali's smile vanished. "The map?"

"All of it. With uncertainty marked. No accusations beyond evidence. We need versions: court-readable, field-readable, and one that cannot be quietly edited by the first office that dislikes being connected."

"That last part is my love language."

Ro began sealing a court-readable extract. Amma exported hunger impact without household names. Tali made the map hostile to silent revision. Vos issued the field accompaniment order and looked annoyed when the station accepted it without a philosophical debate. Kade sat back down, but he did not relax.

Sana stayed at the wall and named the file herself.

THE LONG ORCHARD MAP / WORKING PATTERN / LIVING DEPENDENCY AND COERCION EVENTS.

The station asked for a classification.

Sana typed: FOOD.

The station rejected it as insufficient.

She typed: LAW.

Rejected.

She typed: INFRASTRUCTURE.

Rejected.

Tali leaned over. "Try evidence."

Sana shook her head. Evidence was what courts allowed after harm had been dressed properly.

She typed: SURVIVAL.

For a moment the station hesitated.

Then it accepted.

By dawn, they had a route, a legal excuse, a guarded courier, a sealed map, and a list of people who would be angry when they learned the shape of their separate emergencies had become visible.

Outside the neutral station, the sky over Cascadia had the flat pewter color that came before rain or smoke.

Sana looked once more at the glowing line to Mara Venn's terrace and understood that a map was not knowledge.

It was a promise to arrive before the next invisible cut.

Chapter 27: Vale's Honest Cruelty

Dr. Mirek Vale requested a private conversation with Sana at 05:43.

He did it correctly.

That was the first insult.

He did not send a threat through a sealed office or a charm through a subordinate. He filed a neutral station interview request under emergency evidence clarification, named no condition except privacy from recording beyond certified transcript, and copied the request to Water Court, Succession Court, Continuity Triage, and Field Audit. The form was clean. The time stamp was legal. The courtesy field contained one sentence:

Auditor Ilyen is now asking the right question.

Sana read it twice while standing in the transport bay with one boot unlaced.

Tali leaned over her shoulder. "That is either flirting for bureaucrats or a trap."

"Probably both."

Ro took the slate and read the request. Her face tightened in the way it did when law behaved beautifully around something rotten. "Do not go alone."

"It asks for private conversation."

"It asks. It does not command."

Vos, who had been inspecting the transport order with the blank patience of a man preparing to let a prisoner guide him into terrain full of legal knives, said, "Private does not mean unguarded."

Kade, seated on a bench with his custody band clipped to the rail, said, "It does if the powerful person wrote the dictionary."

Amma had not slept. She was sitting on a crate of field ration packs, rubbing one thumb across the edge of HUNGER DAYS. "Why now?"

Sana looked through the bay doors. Dawn had thinned the sky into metal. The transport assigned to Mara Venn's terrace waited with its side panels open: medical kit, culture cooler, portable relay, two legal packets, one impatient courier, and a map that had made too many offices visible.

"Because we built the map," Sana said.

Tali's levha chimed. She read, then cursed softly. "Station accepted his request. Room Nine. Certified transcript only. No live observers."

Ro said, "Absolutely not."

"How long?" Sana asked.

"Twenty minutes."

Vos said, "We leave for the south ridge in twenty-four."

The timing was another insult. Clean enough to deny, precise enough to hurt. If Sana refused, Vale could say she avoided clarification. If she accepted, he took twenty minutes from Mara Venn.

Kade watched her without the usual flash of provocation. "He wants to slow the run."

"Yes."

"Then do not give him the thing he wants."

Sana tied her boot. "He also wants me to know why."

Amma stood. "Knowing why has a cost."

"So does not knowing."

Ro stepped close, lowering her voice. "Sana. Private rooms are where people try to make you carry their logic out as if it became yours because you heard it without witnesses."

Sana looked at her. "Then I will leave it there."

"You think that is simple."

"No."

That was enough truth for both of them.

Room Nine was smaller than the main analysis chamber and designed for disputes that wanted intimacy without trust. One table. Two chairs. No windows. A certified transcript node set into the ceiling, its indicator light a steady legal blue. No projection walls. No glass. The neutrality seal on the door had been polished so often that the old scratches looked like trapped rivers.

Vale stood when she entered.

He was younger than Sana expected and older than his public images. The public images smoothed him into an abstract: sharp suit, calm eyes, hair silvered in a way that suggested wisdom without the inconvenience of age. In person he looked tired, but not softly. His fatigue had edges. A man who slept poorly and made policies for people who slept worse.

"Auditor Ilyen," he said.

"Dr. Vale."

"Thank you for coming."

"Do not thank me yet."

The corner of his mouth moved. "Fair."

They sat.

The transcript node chimed once.

Vale placed both hands on the table, empty. No slate. No notes. No visible shield except confidence.

"You named the map correctly," he said.

Sana said nothing.

"The Long Orchard. Not a facility. Not a region. Not a chain of custody. A living dependency network. That is closer than anyone in Field Audit has come."

"You requested this conversation to compliment cartography?"

"I requested it because you are about to mistake pattern for guilt."

"That sounds efficient. Usually people wait for me to accuse them."

"You have already accused me in every meaningful way except aloud."

Sana leaned back. "Did you reroute B-17 from Mill 7B?"

"Yes."

The answer arrived without a pause.

Sana had prepared for evasion. For legal grammar. For passive constructions. For a sentence in which the culture had been rerouted by procedure through necessity under regrettable constraints.

Yes was worse.

"Where did it go?"

"To a closed succession food trial."

"That is not a place."

"No."

"Whose trial?"

Vale watched her. "You know I cannot answer that inside a certified transcript."

"You asked for certified transcript."

"I asked for the limit I could survive."

Sana felt the room narrow. "How noble."

"No. Noble is a word people use when they want harm to stop smelling practical."

There it was. Not confession. Not apology. A doorway.

Sana did not step through.

"Mill 7B nearly failed," she said. "Firebreak, Mouth-of-Ash, clinics, school kitchens. You took the correct culture."

"I did."

"Knowing B-19 was not equivalent."

"Knowing it was not ideal."

"Not equivalent."

Vale's eyes sharpened. "Not equivalent."

"And you did it anyway."

"Yes."

The blue transcript light held steady.

Sana's hands wanted the table edge. She kept them flat.

"Why?"

Vale inhaled once, not theatrically. "Because the succession models are not about inheritance. They are about panic."

Sana waited.

"The public story is ownership," Vale said. "Who controls which orchard, which vault, which water share, which emergency protocol. That story is useful because it keeps ambitious people arguing over documents. But underneath, every serious office is running the same calculation: how much visible scarcity can the region absorb before cooperation fails faster than food?"

"You are describing governance as hostage mathematics."

"I am describing governance after famine."

"No. You are describing men in rooms preserving the right to be cruel first."

His mouth tightened. Not anger. Recognition withheld.

"Panic chooses worse," he said. "That is the fact no one wants in the room. Panic does not ration by need. It rations by proximity, rumor, armed confidence, old grievance, which child cries loudest, which town blocks the road first. Panic burns seed grain because it cannot eat a future it does not trust."

"And you can?"

"No. But someone must choose who eats less before panic chooses worse."

The sentence did not raise its voice. It did not need to. It sat between them, clean and obscene.

Sana thought of Amma's ledger. Of school kitchens. Of Emin sitting on the mill floor. Of Mara Venn unreachable on a terrace bridge delayed by law. Of Kade's old door text: delay shall not be made holy by distance.

"You chose Firebreak," she said.

"I chose a risk distribution that preserved a higher-confidence food line while exposing a lower-confidence line to managed loss."

"You chose Firebreak."

Vale looked at her then, fully. "Yes."

The room felt colder.

"Say it," Sana said.

"I chose Firebreak to eat less."

Sana heard the transcript node record it. She also heard what the sentence did not contain: children, elders, workers, names.

"Why not tell them?"

"Because warning changes behavior."

"Yes. It lets people defend themselves."

"It lets people run, hoard, sabotage one another, flood bridges, empty clinics, break vaults, and turn a twenty-two percent protein loss into three burned corridors."

"You do not know that."

"I know enough."

"No," Sana said. "You model enough."

Vale's expression flickered for the first time.

Good, she thought. There is still a nerve.

"Models are not fantasies," he said.

"Neither are meals."

"Do you think I do not know that?"

"I think you know meals as units."

"And you know them as faces. That is useful for finding harm and useless for distributing shortage."

Sana stood so fast the chair legs struck the floor. "You wanted an honest conversation. Do not confuse numbness with intelligence."

Vale did not stand. "Do not confuse witness with plan."

That landed because it had been aimed well.

Sana felt the anger want to become noise. She refused it.

"My plan is to keep living systems connected."

"Until who decides which connection gets the last cooler? Which bridge receives the last guard? Which culture survives decontamination? Which school gets supplement? You will face the same arithmetic."

"Then I will face it in public."

"Public arithmetic becomes theater."

"Private arithmetic becomes murder with clean hands."

Vale looked down at his empty hands.

For one beat, Sana saw the man underneath the office: exhausted, frightened, convinced that the horror he had accepted was proof of seriousness. Then the office returned.

"You think I am your antagonist," he said.

"I think you are volunteering."

"No. Your antagonist is collapse. I am one of the people willing to be hated for delaying it."

"By feeding it selected people."

"By preventing unmanaged cascade."

"You keep making hunger sound like weather."

"Because sometimes it behaves like weather."

"And sometimes it behaves like policy."

Silence.

Vale glanced at the transcript light. "You will use this."

"Yes."

"Good."

That stopped her.

He folded his hands. "Use the part where I admitted the reroute. Use the part where I said Firebreak was selected for managed loss. Force Water Court to demand the sealed destination. Force Succession Court to admit the food trials exist. Force Continuity Triage to open the model assumptions."

Sana stared at him. "You are trying to leak through me."

"I am trying to survive long enough to correct a worse actor."

"Convenient."

"True things often are."

"Who?"

He shook his head once.

"Who is worse than the man choosing which children eat less?"

For the first time, pain crossed his face without permission.

"The people who do not care whether they eat at all."

Sana did not answer.

Vale leaned forward. "The succession fight has been colonized. Not by one claimant. By an investment lattice that wins if cooperative recovery fails. They do not need to own every orchard. They need independent corridors to look unreliable until districts accept bonded food stabilization. Once that happens, hunger becomes debt, debt becomes labor, labor becomes control of water repair, and the next generation calls it order."

The Long Orchard map flashed in Sana's mind: legal fences, sabotage marks, hunger darkening downstream, the thin route to Mara Venn.

"Names," she said.

"I have fragments."

"Give them."

"Not here."

"You asked me here."

"To put enough on record that killing me becomes complicated."

There was no drama in it. That made it worse.

Sana sat slowly.

"Are you in danger?"

Vale smiled without warmth. "Auditor, I am useful, compromised, disliked, and beginning to hesitate. What do you think?"

"I think you rerouted a culture and nearly starved people."

"Yes."

"I think you are telling me truth because truth became safer than silence for you, not because you found a conscience."

"Also yes."

"I think you still believe you should be allowed to choose."

Vale's answer came softer. "I believe someone will. I have seen what happens when no one admits it."

"You have not seen what happens when the people being chosen for are allowed to build the table."

"That is not fast enough."

"It has never been allowed to be fast."

The transcript node hummed faintly. Somewhere beyond the door, the south ridge transport clock was eating minutes.

Sana stood again, this time carefully.

"Mara Venn," she said.

Vale went still.

So he knew.

"Is she the next joint?" Sana asked.

"You should already be on the road."

"Is she alive?"

"I do not know."

"Did you move against her?"

"No."

"Who did?"

"If I say a name without packet proof, they bury the transcript as instability."

"Give me direction."

Vale looked at the ceiling, at the blue light, as if measuring how much truth could fit through a certified hole.

"Bridge access claims are not the attack," he said. "They are the curtain. Look for the maintenance crew assigned after denial. Look for who certifies the temporary support if the old rail spine is declared unsafe. The money is not in closing the bridge. It is in becoming the only authorized repair."

Sana memorized every word.

"And the B-17 destination?"

"Ask why a food trial needs a burn-recovery culture on a site with no recent burn."

"Which site?"

"No recent burn," he repeated. "That is all I can put here."

The transcript clock chimed softly: three minutes.

Sana walked to the door, then stopped.

"You said someone must choose who eats less."

"Yes."

"Here is the part you never model correctly. People can survive less if they know why, if they see the same rules touch the powerful, if they are allowed to help decide, and if less does not always arrive at the same doors."

Vale looked at her.

"Panic does not begin when food runs short," Sana said. "It begins when people understand the truth has already been rationed."

She opened the door.

Ro, Tali, Amma, Vos, Kade, and one unhappy station clerk were waiting in the hall at varying distances that allowed each of them to pretend they had not been waiting.

Ro read Sana's face. "Bad?"

"Useful."

Tali handed her the field slate. "We are two minutes behind."

"Then move."

Kade stood, custody band already clipped to the guard line. "Where?"

"South ridge. Mara Venn. And the bridge."

Vos looked at the sealed transcript packet that had appeared on Sana's slate. "Do we need to wait for court clearance?"

Sana thought of Vale's sentence, clean and obscene. Someone must choose who eats less before panic chooses worse.

"No," she said. "Delay has had enough worship."

They ran for the transport.

Chapter 28: Basin March

They learned about the march before they reached the bridge.

At first it appeared on the Long Orchard map as noise: a string of low-confidence movement signals along the dry basin road, too slow for transport, too steady for scattered evacuation, too irregular for an official convoy. Clinic pings. School meal absences. Handwritten route reports relayed by two couriers and one irrigation mechanic whose slate still thought it was three months ago. A water monitor at Mile Station Twelve logged forty-seven foot crossings in one hour and then stopped logging because someone had taken the battery for a child's breathing pump.

Tali stared at the transport display from the jump seat, one hand braced against the ceiling as the vehicle bucked over cracked service pavement.

"That is not traffic," she said.

Kade leaned as far as the custody line allowed. "Where?"

"Dry basin north road. Between Silt Fork and the old pump spine."

Amma's face changed before the map finished drawing. She knew the pace of people who had decided that waiting was another word for dying.

"Families," she said.

Ro, strapped beside the culture cooler with a court packet across her knees, touched the display and expanded the route. "No permit. No relief escort. No declared evacuation."

"People often forget paperwork when hungry," Kade said.

Vos, in the front passenger seat, did not turn. "How many?"

Tali pulled every feed she could reach. "Hard count impossible. At least three hundred. Maybe eight. Movement groups merging from side roads."

"Destination?" Sana asked.

The map answered before Tali did.

North Rain Vault.

For a moment the transport felt smaller than it was.

North Rain Vault had begun as a climate archive, then become a water seed store, then a court-managed emergency release facility whose name carried more hope than its contents. It held drought reserve moisture membranes, cloud-seeding catalysts, old basin grain samples, water rights backups, and enough symbolic authority that hungry people could march toward it and believe the door itself owed them rain.

Amma closed her eyes. "They heard."

"Heard what?" Vos asked.

"That the cellar stayed locked. That Firebreak lost protein. That children are being counted by offices. That someone has food behind a door."

Ro said, "North Rain Vault cannot feed hundreds."

"Facts do not stop a march," Amma said. "Only food, trust, or force does."

No one answered quickly.

The transport climbed out of the station basin and onto the old rail service line. To the west, Cascadia's wet ridges held morning fog in their teeth. To the east, the dry basin opened in strips of beige, salt-green, and burned silver where old irrigation scars caught the light. Between them lay the south ridge terraces and the bridge to Mara Venn.

Two emergencies, Sana thought, were now moving toward each other without permission.

"If the march reaches North Rain Vault," Ro said carefully, "the court will lock down the release complex. That will freeze every pending water order attached to the vault."

Tali looked up. "Including bridge support certification?"

Ro checked, then nodded once. "Including."

Kade's jaw tightened. "So the bridge stays delayed."

"If the vault enters riot protocol, yes."

"It is not a riot," Amma said.

Vos finally turned. "It can become one."

Amma's eyes opened. "That is what everyone says before treating people like one."

The transport's interior went quiet except for the hum of the culture cooler.

Sana watched the map. The march line thickened by small increments. People joining, not fast, not dramatic. A grandmother with two children would not look like a political event to a satellite. A father pushing a cart with a water barrel would not trigger a threat model until enough fathers did it. A school kitchen closing early would become an absence statistic before anyone admitted it was a road.

"Can we split?" Tali asked.

Vos answered immediately. "No."

Ro said, "Mara Venn may be in direct danger."

"The march may put hundreds in direct danger."

"And if the bridge fails, the corridor fails," Kade said. "Then the march gets worse tomorrow."

Everyone was right. That was the cruelty of a system under pressure. It multiplied correct answers until choosing one felt like betrayal of all the others.

Sana looked at Amma. "Can you reach them?"

Amma was already opening HUNGER DAYS. "Maybe not all. I know basin kitchens. Some elder houses. Two irrigation crews. If they trust the ledger more than the vault door today."

"Tell them what?"

Amma's mouth pressed thin. "That help is coming would be a lie."

"Then do not say that."

Ro said, "We can request emergency mediation at Mile Station Twelve."

Tali snorted. "With what mediator? A packet of forms?"

"With me," Ro said.

Sana turned.

Ro looked terrified for half a second, then covered it with procedure. "A Water Court officer on the road is better than a sealed order at the vault. I can acknowledge grievance, take statements, slow the front, keep them from meeting armed lock protocol first."

Vos said, "You are not trained for crowd control."

"Good. Crowd control is not what they need."

Kade studied her. "They may hate the court."

"They should."

That surprised him into silence.

Ro held the court packet tighter. "But if they hate it in front of a person who can write it down, maybe they do not have to spend the hate on a door."

Amma nodded. "I go with her."

Sana started to object. Amma lifted one hand.

"Do not make me explain hunger to you as if it is new."

Sana stopped.

The map kept moving.

Vos looked from Ro to Amma to Sana, then to the road ahead. "We have one transport."

Tali said, "There is a maintenance spur in three kilometers. It meets the basin road near Mile Station Ten. Drop Ro and Amma there with emergency beacon, relay pack, and enough ration bars to start a conversation badly but not insultingly. We continue to Venn's bridge."

"That leaves them without extraction."

"Everything leaves someone without extraction," Kade said.

Vos gave him a look.

Kade lifted his banded wrist slightly. "I am only saying it because everyone else is being polite."

Sana enlarged the march route. If Ro and Amma intercepted at Mile Station Ten, they could meet the front before it reached the old pump spine, where the road narrowed and security drones would see compression as aggression. If the team stayed together, they might save Mara Venn and lose the march to rumor. If they diverted fully, they might slow the march and arrive at the bridge after the maintenance crew Vale warned about had already certified disaster.

Someone must choose who eats less before panic chooses worse.

Sana hated Vale for the echo. Then she used the part of the sentence that was true and rejected the rest.

"We choose in the open," she said.

Ro looked at her.

"No pretending this is clean. Amma and Ro intercept the march. They tell the front exactly what we know: Mill 7B output reduced, North Rain Vault cannot feed them, bridge delay may cut off more food, and we are going to the bridge because keeping the corridor open matters to their meals too. They take names, needs, and demands. They promise nothing we do not control."

Amma said, "And food?"

Sana opened the transport inventory. "Half the ration packs."

Vos objected. "Field team needs those."

"Field team can be hungry longer than children walking a basin road."

He did not like it. He also did not argue.

Tali said, "Half will feed maybe thirty people."

"It will not feed them. It will prove someone gave up something before speaking."

Amma's eyes softened with grief, not comfort. "That matters."

The transport reached the maintenance spur six minutes later.

It was a cracked lane veering down through scrub and old signal posts toward the dry basin road. Wind pushed dust across the pavement in thin sheets. Far below, the march was not yet visible as people. Only a faint line of lifted dirt, moving against the land's stillness.

Vos stopped the transport hard.

They moved fast. Tali packed the relay beacon, two portable chargers, court slate, water strips, and ration bars into a field bag. Ro clipped her court seal to her jacket where everyone could see it and then grimaced as if she had pinned a target to her chest. Amma tucked HUNGER DAYS under one arm and took the ration bag with the other.

Kade watched from the open side door.

"At Mile Ten," he said, "there is an old shade frame on the west side. If they are angry, stand under it."

Ro frowned. "Why?"

"People throw less well into shade. And it makes you look like you are waiting, not blocking."

Amma almost smiled. "Courier crowd theory?"

"Courier not-being-hit theory."

Vos handed Ro a distress tab. "Break this if security drones deploy below lethal threshold."

"Below lethal?" Ro repeated.

"If they deploy above lethal, I will already be moving."

The statement should have been reassuring. It was not.

Sana stepped down after Amma. Dust caught at her throat.

For one moment, the urgent pieces of the day stood around her in impossible arrangement: Ro with court law, Amma with hunger law, Kade with road law, Tali with machine law, Vos with force law, herself with living law, and somewhere ahead Mara Venn with the old law of keeping something alive because no one else had arrived.

Amma touched Sana's shoulder.

"Do not arrive late to her because you are worrying about me."

"That is not how worry works."

"Then let it work while you move."

Ro looked down the spur toward the dust line. "We will slow them."

"Listen first," Sana said.

Ro nodded. "Listen first."

Amma and Ro started down the maintenance spur. Their figures became smaller quickly, one carrying a ledger, one carrying a seal, both carrying too little food.

The transport door slid shut.

Vos drove before Sana sat fully.

The vehicle lurched back onto the rail service line and accelerated toward the south ridge bridge. Kade braced himself with one hand against the rail, custody band scraping.

"Mara's terrace is past the second washout," he said. "If the bridge claim is being used as a curtain, the maintenance crew will not wait for formal closure. They will want physical uncertainty first."

"Meaning?" Tali asked.

"Damage that can be described as discovered, not caused."

Sana looked at the road ahead.

Behind them, a march moved toward a vault that could not answer hunger.

Ahead of them, a bridge held or failed.

Between the two, the Long Orchard map no longer looked like analysis. It looked like a body under strain, and every choice they made pressed on a different bruise.

Tali's slate chimed.

"South ridge relay just came back for eight seconds," she said.

"Mara?" Sana asked.

"No voice. One automated environmental ping from her terrace."

"Status?"

Tali read the data and went still.

"Cold room temperature rising."

Kade closed his eyes.

Vos pushed the transport faster.

Sana held the culture cooler steady with one hand and the edge of the seat with the other, feeling the distance to Mara Venn shrink too slowly.

Behind them, somewhere on the dry basin road, Amma and Ro walked toward hundreds of hungry people with a ledger, a seal, and half a bag of proof.

Ahead, the bridge waited to become either passage or excuse.

Chapter 29: Orchard Monastery

The bridge was not empty.

That was the first thing wrong.

Kade saw it before the transport completed the last bend above the south ridge washout. He made a short sound in his throat, not quite a warning and not quite grief, and leaned toward the windshield until the custody line snapped tight.

"Stop before the marker stones," he said.

Vos did not ask why. He braked hard enough that the culture cooler slid against Sana's knee.

The service line ahead narrowed into the old rail spine bridge: composite beams over a flood-cut ravine, guard rails patched with orchard mesh, prayer ribbons tied to two posts where families had once thanked the bridge for holding during a winter evacuation. Beyond it, Mara Venn's terraces rose in narrow green shelves against the dry slope. The terraces should have looked asleep from this distance. Instead, one cold-room vent blinked amber.

On the near side of the bridge stood three maintenance vehicles.

No hazard lights. No visible crew.

That was the second thing wrong.

Tali lowered the transport's sensor mast. "They are broadcasting a passive safety perimeter."

"Meaning?" Sana asked.

"Meaning they want machines to slow down but people to think nothing is happening."

Kade pointed with his bound wrist. "Marker stones are load witnesses."

Vos looked at him.

"Old bridge custom," Kade said. "Set stones at stable vibration points. If one shifts after flood, you do not cross with weight until someone checks the underframe."

Sana saw the stones then: three pale blocks half-buried near the rail spine, each marked with orchard cuts and old water dates. The nearest lay on its side.

"Could flood have moved it?" Vos asked.

Kade laughed once. "In this weather? Only if the flood wore boots."

Tali was already inside the passive perimeter broadcast. "Certification packet pending. Temporary support contractor listed as..."

She stopped.

"Say it," Sana said.

"Helix Agravane Infrastructure."

Ro would have sworn. Amma would have gone very still. Sana did both internally.

Helix Agravane had appeared on the Long Orchard map as polite background: investment lattice, bonded food stabilization, post-disaster repair finance, insurers that smiled in subclauses. Not a villain's fortress. Something worse: a company that arrived after harm with forms shaped like help.

Vos unlatched his sidearm but did not draw. "We proceed on foot."

"If the bridge is unstable," Tali said, "on foot may be what they want. One injured auditor makes a cleaner closure than one suspicious vehicle."

"Then we do not cross yet," Sana said. "We get eyes under the span."

Kade pointed downslope. "Old monastery path."

"Monastery?" Tali said.

"Not religion like you mean it. Orchard monks. Archive growers. People who thought keeping scions alive counted as prayer. Path runs under the east abutment and up to Mara's lower terrace."

Sana remembered the outline on the Long Orchard map: a minor historical site below the bridge, marked inactive. She had dismissed it as context. Context had been punishing her all morning.

Vos said, "Can you guide us?"

Kade lifted the band. "Depends whether law can walk downhill."

Vos unclipped him from the transport rail and clipped the custody line to his own belt. "Law is about to try."

They left the transport behind the marker stones.

The path began as a break in scrub, then became steps cut into basalt and root. Dry grass hissed against Sana's legs. The air smelled of dust, warm metal, and the faint sweetness of overripe fruit from terraces above. Somewhere beyond the ravine a motor turned once and stopped.

Tali moved with her slate held flat, mapping signal ghosts. Vos kept one hand near his sidearm and the other on the custody line. Kade descended without looking at his feet, which told Sana he had walked the path often enough to trust it or often enough to know where distrust belonged.

Halfway down, the old orchard monastery appeared.

It was not a building so much as a set of rooms persuaded into the hillside: stone cells, grafting benches, rain jars, root cellars, a small open court shaded by a pear tree so old its trunk had folded around three supports and kept living out of stubbornness. The walls were carved with names. Not saints. Varieties. Rivers. Lost farms. Hands.

Sana slowed despite urgency.

Every surface held the language of preservation. Scion marks. Germination dates. Water debts. Famine years. Names of children born after particular trees fruited. A cracked lintel read:

KEEP WHAT CAN KEEP OTHERS.

Kade looked at it with an expression she had not seen on him before.

Respect without performance.

"Mara trained here?" Sana asked.

"Everyone useful trained here at least once," he said.

Tali looked up from her slate. "Someone is inside."

Vos pulled them behind the pear supports.

An old woman stepped from the shadowed grafting room carrying a pruning hook.

She was not Mara Venn. Taller, darker, with white hair braided close to her skull and a face arranged around permanent skepticism. She wore a patched field coat over a gray archival tunic. The pruning hook in her hand was old enough to have earned ceremony and sharp enough to end one.

"If you are Helix," she said, "I am in a mood to make poor choices."

Kade exhaled. "Iona."

The hook did not lower. Her eyes moved to him, to the custody band, to Vos, to Sana, to the culture cooler.

"Kade Arlo," she said. "You look caught."

"I am cultivating a broad legal experience."

"Good. You were becoming narrow."

Sana stepped forward slowly. "Field Auditor Sana Ilyen. We need to reach Mara Venn."

"So does everyone who comes here with urgent feet."

"Her cold room temperature is rising."

The hook lowered one inch.

"I know."

Sana's chest tightened. "Is she alive?"

"For now."

Kade moved and the custody line checked him. "For now?"

Iona Saye looked at Vos. "If you are the sort of officer who mistakes restraint for wisdom, leave now."

Vos said, "Not today."

"That is almost an answer."

Tali said, "Are you Iona Saye? Former archive grower, Compact custodian, listed inactive after refusing digitization compliance?"

Iona's eyes flicked to her. "Are you a machine wearing a girl, or a girl wearing too many machines?"

"Depends who is billing."

The old woman made a sound that might have been approval if it had lived a less suspicious life.

Sana said, "What happened on the bridge?"

"Someone moved a witness stone. Someone loosened two underframe locks. Someone parked maintenance vehicles where nervous law would see safety work instead of sabotage. Someone wants the bridge closed before Mara's cultures move."

"Can we cross under?"

"Not with the cooler. Not yet."

Tali's fingers moved fast. "Can you prove the locks were loosened?"

Iona looked toward the bridge shadow. "Proof."

The word came out like a seed with rot inside.

Sana heard Vale's voice: perfect proof.

Iona must have seen something in her face.

"Who has been teaching you bad patience?" the old woman asked.

"Too many people."

"Then sit for one minute."

Vos said, "We do not have one minute."

Iona turned the pruning hook toward the bridge. "You have less if you move stupidly."

No one sat, but everyone stopped.

That seemed to satisfy her.

"The first Long Orchard compact was not written by generous people," Iona said. "Remember that when anyone quotes it like a hymn. It was written by ashamed people."

Sana felt the chapter of history opening under their feet.

"After the Second Inland Failure," Iona continued, "archive officers held thirty-seven drought lines in sealed vaults while basin farms died. They had reason. Always reason. Ownership disputes. Disease uncertainty. Germination risk. Two lines mislabeled. Three claims from families thought extinct. One court said wait. One insurer said wait. One scientist said wait for clean proof that release would not contaminate the archive."

The old pear tree creaked in the morning wind.

"They waited," Iona said. "Perfectly. Correctly. With full records."

Kade looked down.

"Fourteen settlements lost seed grain. Three ate their planting stock. One burned its own irrigation archive for cooking heat. By the time proof was perfect, the proof was a list of dead farms."

Sana did not speak.

Iona tapped the pruning hook against the stone. "So the compact was written by the people who survived their own obedience. It said no living corridor could be held hostage to complete certainty when delay itself became harm. Witness, record, restore. Not wait, polish, mourn."

"That text is not in current law," Ro would have said.

Sana heard the answer before Iona gave it.

"Current law is often old cowardice with new margins."

Tali whispered, "I like her."

Vos gave her a warning look.

Sana said, "Mara has cultures that need moving."

"Yes."

"The bridge is being made uncertain."

"Yes."

"The dry basin is marching toward North Rain Vault."

Iona's expression changed at last. Not surprise. Pain confirming an old expectation.

"How many?"

"Hundreds."

"Then the old shame has returned wearing finance."

Kade said, "Iona, we need underframe access."

"You need more than access. You need witness strong enough that when Helix declares the bridge unsafe, the declaration looks like what it is."

"Sabotage," Sana said.

"Premature word."

Sana almost laughed. "You too?"

"No," Iona said sharply. "Not because I fear the word. Because words must arrive carrying enough hands to hold them up. One auditor says sabotage, they bury you. One custodian says sabotage, they call me relic. One courier says sabotage, they point at his band. But a bridge can testify if you ask correctly."

Tali's face lit with professional hunger. "Sensor memory?"

"Older."

Iona led them into the grafting room.

Inside, the air was cool and smelled of wax, pear wood, and paper. Shelves held clay tags, old cutting knives, handbound ledgers, sealed seed envelopes, and devices from five different eras repaired into unlikely cooperation. On the central table lay a long strip of translucent material veined like leaf and circuit.

"Root stress film," Iona said. "The monastery grew it into the bridge supports after the flood years. It records load and vibration as chemical color. No network. No remote edit. Annoying to people who prefer obedient evidence."

Tali bent over it reverently. "This is gorgeous."

"It is ugly and useful. Gorgeous makes people put things in museums."

"Can it show tampering?"

"It can show the bridge did not loosen itself."

Sana looked toward the terrace. "How long to read?"

"If your machine-wearing girl is competent, five minutes."

"Three," Tali said.

Iona almost smiled.

Vos moved to the doorway. "We may not have three. One maintenance vehicle is moving."

They all looked.

Above, through gaps in the monastery court, one of the Helix vehicles had rolled closer to the bridge mouth. A figure in a yellow-gray work shell stepped out carrying a hazard mast.

Not repair equipment. Closure equipment.

Sana's slate chimed.

A message from Ro:

MARCH FRONT VISIBLE. LISTENING FIRST. ANGRY BUT SLOWING. THEY ASK WHO HAS WATER.

Then another, from Amma:

DO NOT LET THEM CLOSE THE BRIDGE. PEOPLE CAN WAIT IF WAITING HAS A ROAD.

Sana showed Iona.

The old woman's face became a door shutting.

"Then we stop being ashamed quietly."

Tali lifted the root stress film and connected a reader clamp. Colors bloomed across the strip: old flood bands, daily cart rhythms, courier steps, maintenance tremors. Then two sharp disruptions near the underframe locks, both in the last hour, both matching tool vibration rather than structural fatigue.

"Got it," Tali said. "Timestamped chemically and locally. Beautiful ugly witness."

Iona took a clay tag from the shelf and pressed it into Sana's hand. It bore the monastery seal: a pear branch over a water line.

"Compact custody witness," she said. "Not current enough to impress fools, old enough to embarrass them."

Vos drew his sidearm now, keeping it low. "Plan."

Sana looked at the bridge, the hazard mast, the amber blink on Mara's cold room, the old compact carved into stone around them.

Waiting for perfect proof had killed farms.

Moving without witness could let Helix bury the truth.

So they would move with witness.

"Tali," Sana said, "broadcast the root film to the court packet, the field map, and Ro's relay. Mark uncertainty honestly. Mark tampering strongly."

"On it."

"Vos, you and Kade get us to the underframe path."

Vos looked at Kade.

Kade said, "Law walks downhill. It can crawl sideways too."

"Iona, can Mara stabilize the cold room from inside?"

The old woman shook her head. "She is not in the cold room."

Sana went still.

"Where is she?"

Iona pointed across the ravine, below the terrace, to a narrow service ledge under the bridge.

For the first time Sana saw the small figure there, gray coat against gray stone, one hand on a manual brace, standing where a loosened underframe lock could drop half the bridge load if the wrong vehicle crossed.

Mara Venn, eighty-two years old, had put her body under the Long Orchard.

Kade whispered something that was not a curse and not a prayer.

Above, the Helix worker raised the hazard mast.

The passive perimeter became active.

BRIDGE UNSAFE / ACCESS CLOSURE PENDING / AUTHORIZED MAINTENANCE ONLY.

Sana took the monastery seal in one hand and the culture cooler in the other.

"Then we ask the bridge correctly," she said.

And they ran into the old path under the span.

Chapter 30: The Hidden Clause

The path under the bridge had been made for hands, not dignity.

Sana learned that in the first ten meters.
