The old orchard way narrowed from stone steps into a ledge cut beneath the east abutment, wide enough for one person turned sideways, slick with mineral seep, and threaded with roots that had no respect for boots. The culture cooler's strap bit into her shoulder. The monastery seal was clenched in her left fist. Above them, the bridge hummed with the false calm of a structure still deciding whether to become passage or evidence.

Kade went first because he knew where the ledge lied.

Vos kept the custody line short but not cruel. That mattered more than he probably intended. Too much slack and Kade could fall. Too little and he could not place his feet. Law, Sana thought, had very few settings between abandonment and choke.

Tali came behind Sana, muttering to the root stress feed as if the bridge were a stubborn witness on cross-examination.

"I have the first packet sealed," she said. "Court copy, field copy, Ro relay copy. Local chemical timestamps. Tool signature probability high. Structural fatigue probability low."

"Say it in human," Vos called back.

"Someone touched what they should not have touched."

"Good."

"Good is not a legal category."

"It is today."

Above, the Helix hazard mast began broadcasting in a voice designed to sound concerned without being responsible.

BRIDGE SAFETY CLOSURE IN PROGRESS. PLEASE AVOID UNAUTHORIZED CROSSING. TEMPORARY SUPPORT REVIEW PENDING. PUBLIC WELFARE IS OUR PRIORITY.

Kade made a sound of pure disgust. "Public welfare always arrives with a speaker."

The ledge turned under the span.

Then Mara Venn came fully into view.

She stood on a maintenance bracket no wider than a table, one hip braced against a manual load lever, both hands locked around a red crank that should have been operated by two workers and a motor. Her gray coat snapped in the ravine wind. White hair had escaped its pins and streamed across her face. A safety line ran from her waist to an old ring bolt; the line was frayed, not ornamental enough to reassure anyone.

She saw Kade first.

"You are late," she shouted.

Kade shouted back, "I was arrested."

"Poor planning."

Sana almost laughed, which was absurd and therefore necessary.

Mara's gaze moved to the cooler. "Is that B-21 or medicine?"

"Neither," Sana called. "Portable culture stabilizer and cold gel. We came for you."

"Then you have misunderstood the problem."

"People keep telling me that."

Mara jerked her chin toward the underframe. "Lock two is loosened. Lock three is shimmed wrong. If the Helix truck crosses with the support mast engaged, the bridge will sag just enough for them to declare emergency replacement. If it sags while I am not holding this brace, it may drop the east load rail."

Vos assessed the distance. "Can we relieve you?"

"Can you count to six while your wrists are on fire?"

"Yes."

"Then maybe."

Kade had gone pale under the dirt.

Mara noticed. "Do not look tragic at me, boy. It makes you resemble your father."

Kade's face closed. "Unnecessary."

"Most true things are."

Tali squeezed past Sana as far as the ledge allowed and scanned the lock assembly. "She is right. The shim is deliberate. Not enough to fail now. Enough to fail under certification load."

"Can you record?" Sana asked.

"Already recording."

Above them the hazard voice repeated itself with deeper concern.

BRIDGE SAFETY CLOSURE IN PROGRESS.

Then a second voice, live this time, male and amplified: "Personnel under the bridge, withdraw immediately. This is an authorized safety operation."

Vos looked up. "Identify yourself."

"Helix Agravane Infrastructure temporary support team. This bridge is under closure review."

Iona's voice came from the path behind them, sharp as the pruning hook she still carried. "This bridge is under compact witness."

For three seconds even the wind seemed interested.

The amplified voice returned colder. "That designation is not recognized in current emergency maintenance protocol."

Iona smiled without pleasure. "There it is."

Sana looked back. "What?"

"The shape of the theft."

Mara's arms trembled on the crank. "Iona, if you have come to lecture, choose a shorter century."

"I have come to remind them they are standing on stolen law."

Tali's slate chimed. "We have incoming filings. Helix has requested immediate temporary repair trusteeship if closure is certified."

Vos said, "Repair trusteeship?"

"Authority to control access, load, emergency funding, contractor selection, and post-repair use conditions."

Kade laughed, harsh and breathless. "There is the money."

Sana felt the pieces connect: bridge closure, Helix certification, bonded stabilization, independent corridors made unreliable, then repaired into dependency. Not destruction. Capture.

"Can they get it?" she asked.

Ro would know. Ro was on the basin road with hundreds of hungry people.

Iona answered instead. "Under current protocol, yes."

"Under the compact?"

The old woman looked at her. "That depends whether anyone remembers the hidden clause."

Another filing alert hit Tali's slate. "Food insurer amicus statement. They argue compact witness has ceremonial value but no operational force."

Iona's expression became almost cheerful in the way storms are cheerful when they find roofs.

"Of course they do. Their grandfathers paid to make it ceremonial."

Mara grunted. "Iona."

"Yes, yes. Short century."

Iona pulled a small oilskin packet from inside her coat and handed it to Sana. It was sealed with wax that had cracked and been resealed more than once. The monastery pear-and-water mark pressed through every layer.

"Read the inner leaf."

Sana tucked the culture cooler against the rock, broke the outer tie with shaking fingers, and unfolded the packet. Inside lay a thin sheet of fiber-paper, pale brown, printed in old legal type and annotated in three hands. The heading read:

LONG ORCHARD COMPACT / ORIGINAL FIELD COPY / LIVING TRUSTEESHIP CLAUSE.

Tali stopped muttering.

Vos said, "Explain while moving."

Mara's wrists shook harder. "Excellent suggestion."

Sana read aloud because the bridge, the court packet, and everyone above needed to hear the words at the same time.

"Where a living corridor sustains reciprocal survival among communities, no insurer, claimant, archive, contractor, court, or emergency office may assume trusteeship by reason of disruption if that disruption is created, prolonged, or materially advantaged by its own action or financial interest."

Kade whispered, "Oh."

Iona continued from memory, voice hard and clear.

"Trusteeship remains first with living stewards: growers, carriers, culture keepers, water witnesses, ration kitchens, and affected households, until open witness proves they cannot preserve the corridor without causing greater harm."

Tali's fingers flew. "Sending to court packet. Ro relay. Field map. Public?"

Sana looked at Iona.

Iona said, "It was public before it was buried."

"Public," Sana said.

Vos's radio snapped alive. The Helix voice lost its calm. "Cease unauthorized publication of disputed archival material."

Tali smiled at her slate. "Too late has a lovely upload speed."

Above, the hazard mast stuttered. For a moment its broadcast overlapped with Tali's packet stream, public welfare dissolving into living trusteeship in the local mesh.

Then Ro's voice burst through, rough with wind and distance.

"Sana, what did you just send?"

"Original compact clause. Helix cannot claim trusteeship if they caused or benefit from the disruption. Living stewards retain first authority."

There was a roar behind Ro: people, many people, not yet riot, not quiet either.

Ro covered the receiver, spoke to someone, then returned. "Say that again in words a hungry crowd can use."

Sana looked at Mara gripping the crank, Iona with the buried law, Kade with road memory, Tali with proof, Vos with force not yet used, and the bridge trying to testify.

"They cannot break the road and then charge you rent to cross it," Sana said.

Ro repeated it.

The roar changed.

Not calmer. More focused.

Amma came on next. "They heard. They are asking who the living stewards are."

Iona shouted toward the slate, "They are."

Amma's answer was immediate. "Good."

The amplified Helix voice tried again. "Unauthorized personnel are interfering with emergency safety work. Withdraw now or security will be requested."

Vos climbed onto the bracket beside Mara. "How do I take the load?"

Mara studied him. "You look too clean."

"That can be corrected."

"Put both hands above mine. When I say six, you hold. Do not be heroic. Heroic people jerk."

Vos placed his hands on the crank.

Kade strained forward. "Let me."

Vos said, "No."

"I know the brace."

"You are in custody."

"The bridge is not."

Sana saw the old reflex in Vos: rule first. Then she saw him look at the custody line, at the crank, at Mara's shaking wrists, at the live court packet receiving everything.

He unclipped Kade from his belt.

"You run, I shoot badly near you first," Vos said.

Kade moved past him. "That is the warmest thing you have ever said to me."

"Do not ruin it."

Kade climbed onto the bracket with a courier's balance and put his hands under Mara's, not replacing her, sharing the load.

Mara hissed. "Your left hand is wrong."

"My left hand was arrested."

"Your left hand is stupid."

He corrected.

Tali said, "We have three minutes before Helix can legally escalate to security drone request. Maybe less if they lie efficiently."

Sana scanned the lock assembly. "Can we reseat the shim?"

Iona said, "Not from here."

"Where?"

The old woman pointed to a crawl space beneath the rail. "There. Someone small, stubborn, and not currently holding the bridge."

Everyone looked at Tali.

Tali looked offended. "I am not small. I am spatially efficient."

"Can you do it?"

"With a tool and two insults for courage."

Iona handed her a hooked wrench.

Tali clipped her slate to her chest and slid into the crawl space before anyone could make a safer plan. Her boots vanished under the rail. Her voice echoed from inside the bridge.

"This is disgusting. Whoever designed this hated elbows."

Mara said, "It was designed by monks."

"That explains the suffering, not the clearance."

Above, the Helix worker shouted without amplification. "Step away from the structure!"

Sana climbed back toward the near side until she could see him through the rail gaps: yellow-gray work shell, hazard mast locked open, one hand at his ear, receiving instructions.

She lifted the monastery seal.

"Field Auditor Ilyen," she shouted. "This bridge is under active compact witness. Root stress evidence shows tool interference prior to your closure action. Original living trusteeship clause is filed and public. Withdraw your closure mast."

The worker hesitated.

That told her he was not the mind of the thing. Only its hand.

"I have authorization," he shouted.

"From whom?"

He did not answer.

"Name the living steward who requested your trusteeship."

Silence.

"Name the harm you did not benefit from."

His face changed.

Tali shouted from inside the bridge, "I found the shim. It has a serial mark."

The worker reached for the mast controls.

Vos drew fully. "Do not."

Everything narrowed: the ravine wind, Mara's shaking arms, Kade's set jaw, Iona's old fury, Tali's hidden hands inside the bridge, the worker's fingers near the control, the public packet traveling outward faster than any of them could run.

Then a new sound rolled over the ridge.

Voices.

Not from the bridge.

From the basin road feed on Sana's slate.

Ro had put the crowd on open relay.

Hundreds of people repeated Amma's translation, ragged at first, then stronger:

You cannot break the road and charge us to cross.

You cannot break the road and charge us to cross.

You cannot break the road and charge us to cross.

The words came through the tinny speaker and filled the underside of the bridge like weather made of people.

The Helix worker froze.

Tali grunted. Metal clanged.

"Shim out," she said. "Realigning lock three. Someone hold the brace steady or I will invent new legal categories for pain."

Kade and Mara held.

Vos kept the worker covered.

Iona began reciting the compact under her breath, not like prayer, like measurement.

Sana watched the hazard mast.

Its light changed from red to amber.

Then to white.

TEMPORARY CLOSURE CONTESTED / COMPACT WITNESS ACTIVE / TRUSTEESHIP CLAIM DISPUTED.

It was not victory. It was delay forced to justify itself.

Sometimes that was the first honest door.

Tali slid backward out of the crawl space covered in dust and triumph. "Lock three seated. Lock two still needs a real crew, not a fake one. Light foot traffic only. No truck. No support mast."

Mara sagged.

Kade caught her before she could fall.

For a second neither of them said anything.

Then Mara looked at him. "Now you may look tragic."

He laughed once, broken.

Sana lifted the culture cooler. "Cold room?"

Mara pushed herself upright with pure irritation. "If my cultures are dead, I am haunting everyone."

Iona said, "They are not dead yet."

Above, the Helix worker stepped back from the mast, one hand still at his ear, listening to orders that had begun to fear witnesses.

Ro's voice returned, quieter. "The march has stopped at Mile Ten. Not dispersed. Stopped. They want representatives named as living stewards."

Amma added, "They also want water. Do not get poetic."

Sana looked across the bridge to Mara's terraces.

"We get the cultures stable," she said. "Then we name the stewards in public."

Kade still held Mara's elbow.

Mara looked toward her cold room and then to the bridge under her feet.

"The compact was never hidden from the people who needed it," she said. "Only from the people who were told they had to ask."

Sana thought of Vale, of insurers, of archives, of every office that made delay look like care and care look unauthorized.

"Then we stop asking wrong."

They crossed the bridge on foot, slowly, under active witness, carrying the cooler toward a cold room still blinking amber but not yet dark.

Chapter 31: Drone Ash

Mara Venn's cold room had not failed.

It had been persuaded to look like failure.

Tali discovered that while lying on her stomach beneath a condensation shelf with one arm inside a service duct and the rest of her body wedged between two crates of grafting gel labeled TOO OLD TO INSURE / TOO ALIVE TO DISCARD.

"Who labels like this?" she said.

Mara, seated on an overturned nutrient bucket with a heat wrap around both wrists, answered without looking up. "People who have met insurers."

"I respect the spite."

"Respect faster."

The cold room was built into the back of the terrace house, half cellar and half shrine to stubborn continuity. Its walls were stone, foamglass, and old ship insulation. Racks of culture pouches hung beside dormant scion bundles. Clay jars held seed lines cushioned in ash. A shelf of fungal starters breathed faintly through membrane caps. Every object had at least two labels: one official, one handwritten, one emotional if Mara had been especially annoyed that day.

Official: VENN COOPERATIVE CULTURE LOT / ASH-MYC SUPPORT FAMILY / NONCOMMERCIAL.

Handwritten: do not warm unless you want hungry ghosts.

Emotional: if this dies, haunt the procurement office first.

The amber warning light still blinked. The temperature had risen three degrees above safe band but had not crossed ruin. Sana and Iona worked at the racks, transferring the most vulnerable cultures into the portable stabilizer. Kade hauled cold bricks from an emergency pit with his cuffed wrist wrapped in cloth so the metal would not tear skin. Vos stood in the doorway where he could see the terrace, the bridge, and the Helix worker now sitting beside his deactivated hazard mast pretending to be on a very important call with people who had stopped answering quickly.

On the basin road, the march remained stopped at Mile Ten.

Stopped did not mean resolved.

Amma sent updates every few minutes.

THEY ARE LISTENING. THEY ARE ANGRY IN COMPLETE SENTENCES. THIS IS BETTER THAN CHANTING.

Then:

THREE WATER WITNESSES NAMED. TWO SCHOOL KITCHENS ASK TO BE LISTED AS LIVING STEWARDS. RO IS WRITING TOO SLOWLY.

Then:

SEND PROOF ABOUT THE DRONES. THEY STILL THINK SOMEONE IN THE BASIN DID THIS.

That last message brought Tali out of the service duct too fast. She hit her shoulder on the shelf and taught the cold room a word it probably already knew.

"Drones," she said.

Sana looked over. "Pollinator drones?"

"And security drones. And maintenance drone pings. And the fake humidity readings near the cooperative nursery. The root film gave us tool timing at the bridge. If I align that with drone ash..."

Kade paused with a cold brick in both hands. "Drone ash?"

"Residual command dust. Not literal ash. Systems shed metadata when they burn instructions through relays they do not own."

Mara said, "That sounds literal enough."

Tali slid out fully and reached for three slates at once. "People blame basin militia because militia equipment is noisy, cheap, and already hated. That makes them useful scenery. But the pollinator collapse at Firebreak, the false blight signature, and the bridge perimeter all used different surfaces with the same timing discipline. Whoever did it understands insured delay."

Vos said, "Insured delay?"

"Harm that matures into claim eligibility."

Iona, from the culture rack, gave a short approving hum. "Good phrase. Ugly thing."

Tali opened the Long Orchard map in local mode. The cold room did not have enough bandwidth for beauty, so the model rendered in hard lines: bridge, terrace, basin road, Mill 7B, Firebreak, pollinator roosts, North Rain Vault, legal filings, hunger clusters, drone pings. She added the bridge root film timestamps. Then the cold-room sensor irregularity. Then the hazard mast activation. Then the first insurer amicus filing. Then three pollinator route collapses and the false blight event.

The pattern did not appear at once.

That was how Tali knew it was real.

Fake patterns liked to impress quickly. Real ones were rude. They made you clean your own tools, distrust your first conclusion, remove the obvious line, then notice the ugly line that remained.

"Do not talk," she said.

No one did, which proved the room had learned something.

Tali stripped out basin militia signatures first: reused command shells, open-market drone keys, slang tags, angry manifest fragments, the dramatic garbage of people who wanted their enemies to know they had been touched. Some of those signatures were present. Too present. Painted on the events like ash rubbed onto a clean hand.

She removed them as probable decoration.

Underneath lay timing.

Not code style. Not slogans. Timing.

Commands sent only inside claim-response windows. Drone route alterations that created losses large enough to trigger review but small enough to avoid immediate criminal escalation. Sensor drift inserted after office hours in one jurisdiction and before emergency staffing in another. Bridge hazard mast activated seven minutes after the first living trusteeship packet, fast enough to preempt, slow enough to look reactive.

Tali felt her anger become clean.

"There you are," she whispered.

Sana came closer. "What?"

"It is not a militia."

"Who?"

"Give me one more insult."

"You are impossible."

"Useful."

"Occasionally."

"I will take it."

Tali followed the timing chain into routing contracts. Not command origin. That would be hidden under proxies, dead relays, stolen keys, devices belonging to people poor enough to be blamed. She wanted who benefited from the rhythm. Who knew when a delay became insurable. Who knew how long a bridge could be unsafe before emergency trusteeship became profitable. Who knew which drone ash to scatter.

The answer was not a person.

It was a clearinghouse.

PUQ-LINE LOGISTICS RISK POOL / PUGET REGIONAL FOOD TRANSIT INSURANCE CONSORTIUM.

Subsidiary nodes bloomed.

One linked to Helix Agravane Infrastructure.

One linked to the food insurer that had filed the amicus statement against compact witness.

One linked to Puget procurement emergency reserves.

One linked, delicately and through three layers of polite abstraction, to Continuity Model Triage data access.

Tali stopped breathing.

"Oh," she said.

Mara looked up. "That kind of oh means either discovery or fire."

"Both."

Sana read over her shoulder. "Puget-linked logistics insurer."

Kade set the cold bricks down very carefully. "Not basin militia."

"Militia signatures were planted or harvested," Tali said. "Maybe some locals were paid to make noise. Maybe their keys were stolen. But the command discipline belongs to insurance logistics."

Vos said, "Can you prove it?"

Every head turned toward him.

He lifted one hand. "I am not doubting you. I am asking what survives court."

Tali appreciated that he had learned the difference. It annoyed her less than expected.

"I can prove correlation strong enough to compel contract discovery," she said. "I can prove planted militia signatures conflict with operational timing. I can prove bridge closure and pollinator collapse share claim-window logic. I can prove the risk pool received automated notification before two public incidents were logged."

Sana's face sharpened. "Before?"

"Before."

Iona said, "That is not ash. That is smoke from the kitchen."

Tali sent the packet to Ro and Amma.

Within thirty seconds Ro called.

She sounded like she was standing inside weather made of people. "Tell me this is not another maybe."

"It is a court-grade maybe with teeth," Tali said. "Enough to demand records. Enough to tell the march they are being framed."

Ro covered the receiver. The crowd noise swelled, then dipped.

Amma took over. "Say it plainly."

Tali looked at the map. For once plain did not feel like a demotion.

"The drone sabotage was made to look like basin anger," she said. "The timing points to a Puget-linked logistics insurer that profits when independent routes fail and insured repair control begins."

Amma repeated it.

The crowd sound changed again.

This time, it thinned.

Not because anger vanished. Because anger lost one of the lies it had been handed.

Ro came back. "They are asking if Puget did this."

Sana answered before Tali could. "Say we have evidence tied to a Puget-linked insurer, not proof that Puget citizens or all Puget offices acted. Do not let them turn this into basin against city."

Ro repeated that too.

Mara said, "Good. Hungry people deserve precision."

Kade leaned against a rack, exhausted. "Powerful people rarely do."

Vos's radio clicked. "Security drone request filed by Helix support team."

Tali swore. "How long?"

"Already routed."

The cold room seemed to contract.

Sana stepped to the doorway. Outside, above the terrace, three black dots rose from the far side of the ridge.

Not large drones. Not lethal platforms. Security witnesses with enforcement hooks. Their danger was not bullets. Their danger was footage trimmed by the people who summoned them: unauthorized personnel interfering with safety closure, agitated march on basin road, known arrested courier present, old woman with tool weapon, auditor spreading disputed archival material.

Stories with propellers.

"Can you ground them?" Sana asked.

Tali looked almost offended by the optimism. "From here? With cold-room bandwidth and a slate that has been insulted by condensation?"

"Can you confuse them?"

"That is a warmer question."

Mara pointed at a shelf. "Drone veils. Third drawer."

Tali opened it and found six folded mesh strips, each threaded with reflective seed foil and old pollinator tags.

"Why do you have drone veils?"

"Because drones are rude."

Iona said, "The monastery used them to keep proprietary pollination auditors from counting private grafts."

Tali's whole face changed. "I love illegal textiles."

"They are not illegal."

"Disappointing but still useful."

Vos stepped outside with his weapon lowered. "If those drones classify us as hostile, I can disable one before the others mark escalation."

"Do not shoot the story machines unless we are already inside their story," Sana said.

"That is a sentence I hate understanding."

Tali handed veils around. "Hang these from the terrace wires and cold-room vent. They create ambiguity in object boundaries. Not invisibility. Confusion. The drones will need a closer pass to classify."

Kade took one. "Closer to the bridge?"

"Yes."

He smiled without humor. "Where the compact witness is active."

"Exactly."

They moved.

Mara refused to sit until Sana threatened to list her as a culture in need of stabilization. That worked only because Iona laughed, and Mara apparently hated giving Iona the satisfaction of being right. Kade and Vos strung two veils across the terrace line. Sana tied one to the cold-room vent, fingers numb from gel and adrenaline. Tali configured the local mesh to rebroadcast the drone ash packet on every close pass.

The security drones descended.

Their speakers opened in sequence.

UNAUTHORIZED ACTIVITY DETECTED.

ACTIVE SAFETY REVIEW IN PROGRESS.

PLEASE PRESENT AUTHORITY.

Sana stepped into the terrace court holding the monastery seal in one hand and the field slate in the other.

"Field Auditor Sana Ilyen. Active compact witness. Living trusteeship claim filed. Bridge closure contested. Evidence of tool interference, planted militia signatures, and insurer-linked timing available on public packet."

The nearest drone shifted, camera iris tightening.

PLEASE PRESENT CURRENT AUTHORITY.

Of course, Sana thought. Current authority. The phrase every buried law had to survive.

Before she could answer, the basin road relay opened again.

This time it was not Ro. Not Amma.

A young voice, amplified by distance and many cheap slates, spoke with the careful force of someone reading words a crowd had decided together:

"Mile Ten living stewards present authority. School kitchen, water witness, irrigation crew, elder house, ration line, carrier, affected households. We do not authorize closure of the Long Orchard road by parties under conflict claim."

Another voice joined.

"We request compact witness."

Another.

"We request records."

Another.

"We request water."

The drone hesitated.

Tali's mesh caught the hesitation and fed it the ash packet: timing chain, risk pool links, pre-incident notifications, planted signatures, living trusteeship clause, root stress film. Not enough to make the drone just. Enough to make simple classification expensive.

PLEASE WAIT.

Kade looked at the drone. "I have never liked those words before."

Vos said, "Do not start now."

The drones rose three meters instead of descending.

Their lights changed from enforcement red to review amber.

SECURITY CLASSIFICATION DEFERRED. MULTI-PARTY AUTHORITY CONFLICT. RECORDING CONTINUES.

"Recording continues," Tali said. "Good. Watch us."

Sana let herself breathe once.

Only once.

Because inside the cold room the amber warning finally clicked to green.

Mara heard it before anyone else. Her head turned sharply.

The stabilizer hummed. The ash-myc support pouches settled back inside safe band. The cold gel took. The most fragile cultures had not died.

Mara closed her eyes.

No one said comfort.

Some moments deserved not to be filled.

Then Amma's message came through:

THEY WANT THE INSURER NAMED.

Ro added:

SO DO I.

Tali looked at Sana. "If we name PUQ-Line publicly now, they will deny, fragment, and bury. If we do not name, the crowd thinks we are protecting Puget."

Sana looked at the drones hovering in review amber, at Helix's halted mast, at Mara's green cold-room light, at Kade's exhausted face, at Iona's hidden law now public, at the map that had become less a picture than a demand.

"We name the entity and the limits," Sana said. "Puget-linked logistics insurer. Risk pool nodes. Evidence sufficient for compulsory records. No claim against Puget people. No claim yet against all offices. We do not feed the wrong fire."

Tali sent it.

Amma read it to the march.

Ro filed it with Water Court.

The drones recorded it.

Somewhere in Puget, Sana knew, calm rooms would begin closing doors.

But on the south ridge terrace, one old woman's cultures were alive, one bridge had not been captured, and one hungry crowd had been given a truer enemy than its nearest neighbor.

That was not justice.

It was orientation.

Sometimes, before people could move, they had to know which direction harm had come from.

Chapter 32: Kade's Hearing

Kade's hearing began before anyone found a chair.

That was how Sana knew the court was frightened.

Water Court liked chairs. It liked benches, rails, marked speaking positions, names read in order, hands raised under oath, all the small furniture rituals that made consequence look less like appetite. But the emergency link opened in Mara Venn's terrace house while cold gel still smoked faintly in the stabilizer, while drone veils snapped from the wires outside, while Mara cursed at a culture pouch as if it had personally considered dying, and while Kade stood with one wrist still banded and one sleeve soaked in condensation.

The court seal filled Tali's largest slate.

EMERGENCY MATERIAL WITNESS STATUS REVIEW / KADE ARLO / WATER COURT AND SUCCESSION COURT JOINT NOTICE.

Ro appeared in one corner of the display, not in chambers but on the basin road. Dust streaked her face. Behind her, a crowd stood under the old shade frame Kade had recommended. They were not calm. They were organized enough to be more dangerous to lies.

Amma stood beside her, HUNGER DAYS tucked under one arm, speaking to someone outside the frame with the quiet authority of a person refusing to waste anger.

Another corner showed a Water Court magistrate in a room so clean it seemed fictional.

A third showed Succession Court counsel with three aides and the expression of people who had expected a courier and found a corridor.

Vos stepped closer to Kade, not quite guarding, not quite supporting.

The magistrate spoke first. "This emergency review concerns the custodial status of Kade Arlo, currently held as material witness in matters related to unauthorized seed transfer, emergency food proceedings, and related court interference."

Kade raised his banded wrist. "Still here."

"Mr. Arlo, do not interrupt."

"You began without asking whether I could hear."

The magistrate's face tightened. "Can you hear?"

"Yes."

"Good."

"Can you?"

Sana closed her eyes for half a second.

Ro's mouth twitched on the basin feed.

The magistrate said, "Mr. Arlo, in light of new evidence concerning bridge interference, culture preservation, compact witness, and potential third-party manipulation of prior events, the court is prepared to modify your custody status."

Kade went still.

That made Sana look at him more closely. He had been careless with danger all morning, insulting with precision, brave in ways that pretended to be irritating. But the word modify touched something under the performance.

"Modify how?" he asked.

Succession counsel answered before the magistrate could. "Conditional release under recognizance, pending future testimony. Possible deferred consideration of unauthorized transfer charges if cooperation continues."

Kade looked at him. "That is a long way to say leash."

"It is a significant legal concession."

"From whom?"

The counsel blinked. "The courts."

"No. Who gives up something?"

Silence gathered.

Mara stopped insulting the culture pouch.

Kade looked from face to face on the slate. "You are offering to make me grateful that you may stop holding me for carrying what people needed."

The magistrate said, "Mr. Arlo, unauthorized transfer remains a serious issue."

"So does authorized starvation."

Vos murmured, "Careful."

Kade did not look at him. "I tried careful. It required too many funerals."

Ro's face changed. Sana recognized the look: lawyer hearing not rhetoric but hinge.

The magistrate said, "The purpose of this review is your status, not broader policy."

Kade laughed softly. "That is exactly the problem."

Tali, who had been running the drone ash packet into three evidence formats at once, looked up. "He is about to make this difficult."

Iona said, "Good. Easy law is usually asleep."

The Succession counsel leaned toward his camera. "Mr. Arlo, if you decline conditional release, the prior custody order remains in effect."

"I heard you."

"Then I advise you to consider the practical implications."

Kade's smile was faint and unpleasant. "Everyone keeps advising me to respect practicality after they finish making it cruel."

Sana stepped nearer. "Kade."

He glanced at her then. Not asking permission. Measuring witness.

She understood before he spoke.

He was not refusing freedom because he wanted suffering. He was refusing the version of freedom that made his case exceptional and everyone else's hunger ordinary.

"I will accept release," Kade said, "after the saved cooperative's testimony enters the public record."

The magistrate frowned. "Which cooperative?"

Mara barked a laugh. "Listen to that."

Kade turned toward the cold room. "Mara Venn's cooperative. The lower basin graft keepers. The school kitchens and water witnesses at Mile Ten. The people whose cultures, routes, and meals were turned into legal scenery while offices decided which version of their suffering could be admitted."

Succession counsel said, "This hearing cannot become a public inquiry into every related grievance."

"Then it cannot release me."

Ro spoke from the basin road. "For the record, Water Court officer Ro Mavik requests clarification. Is the court refusing to receive testimony from affected living stewards before modifying the custody status of a material witness whose alleged conduct is inseparable from the corridor harm?"

The magistrate looked as if she had found a thorn under her tongue.

"Officer Mavik, your current field situation is unstable."

Ro looked behind her at the crowd, then back. "Yes. That is why records matter."

Amma leaned into frame. "They are listening."

"Ms. Amma, you are not counsel in this matter."

"No. I am worse. I keep count."

The crowd behind her murmured. Not loud. Enough.

Kade said, "If you pardon me quietly, I become proof the system corrected itself. If you hear them publicly, the record shows what had to be corrected."

"No pardon has been offered," Succession counsel snapped.

"Deferred consideration. Conditional release. Modification. Pick the pretty cloth you like. It still covers the same table."

Sana watched the magistrate. Fear made courts stiff, but it could also make them practical in ways justice alone did not. The march at Mile Ten, the drones in review amber, the public compact clause, the bridge evidence, the insurer timing packet, Mara's cultures alive because unauthorized people had refused delay: all of it had narrowed the court's room for clean denial.

The magistrate said, "What testimony do you demand be entered?"

Demand.

Not request.

Kade noticed too.

"First," he said, "Mara Venn's statement that the bridge closure would have prevented culture transfer and advantaged Helix-linked trusteeship."

Mara straightened. "I can say it with better nouns."

"Please do."

"Second, Iona Saye's compact custody testimony, including the living trusteeship clause and the history of why it exists."

Iona nodded once. "I will not make it short."

"Third, Tali's drone ash and timing packet tying sabotage signatures to PUQ-Line Logistics Risk Pool and related insurer nodes."

Tali lifted a hand. "I will make it understandable under protest."

"Fourth, Amma's hunger ledger excerpts showing downstream meal effects from Mill 7B, bridge delay, and North Rain Vault lock protocol."

Amma said, "Household names protected."

"Fifth, statements from the Mile Ten living stewards."

Ro added, "School kitchen, water witness, irrigation crew, elder house, ration line, carrier, affected households."

Kade nodded. "Sixth, the court records of every office that objected faster to unauthorized repair than to hunger."

The magistrate inhaled.

Sana nearly smiled.

Kade was a courier. Of course he knew how to make a list arrive.

Succession counsel said, "This is procedurally impossible within a custody review."

Ro answered, "Then convert the review."

"To what?"

"Emergency evidentiary preservation hearing under living trusteeship conflict."

The magistrate looked sharply at her.

Ro continued, "The court has already acknowledged compact witness by holding the bridge closure contested. The material witness status is intertwined with the corridor. The public record is the only instrument that prevents selective pardon from laundering systemic harm."

Tali whispered, "Ro is doing the thing."

Sana whispered back, "Yes."

"It is a good thing."

"Yes."

"I hate that I like law right now."

"It will pass."

The magistrate conferred off-screen. Succession counsel conferred with all three aides while trying not to look like he was losing control of a hearing that had begun in his category. On the basin road, the crowd waited under shade, hundreds of people learning the strange patience of being almost heard.

Kade stood very still.

Sana moved beside him. "You know they may keep the band on."

"Yes."

"You could have been free."

"No."

"Less banded, then."

That made him look at her.

She said, "It matters."

"So does why."

She could not argue.

The magistrate returned. "The court will not accept the term demand."

Kade said, "I can live without the word."

"The court will, however, acknowledge that material witness status cannot be properly reviewed without preserving testimony concerning the alleged necessity, consequences, and public-interest context of the underlying acts."

Ro closed her eyes briefly.

Succession counsel looked furious enough to become useful by accident.

"Therefore," the magistrate continued, "this status review is converted into an emergency evidentiary preservation hearing under provisional compact witness. Testimony will be received from named living stewards, relevant field officers, technical witnesses, and affected parties. Mr. Arlo's custody band remains active pending conclusion of initial testimony. Physical restraint is modified to supervised release within field evidence perimeter."

Kade looked at Vos. "Leash with scenery."

Vos said, "Take the win before someone improves it."

The magistrate's voice sharpened. "Mr. Arlo, do you accept these terms?"

Kade looked at Mara.

Mara said, "Do not look at me. I am busy being relevant."

He looked at Iona.

Iona said, "If you waste old law on personal drama, I will trip you."

He looked at Amma on the screen.

Amma said, "The people at Mile Ten want to know if they will be heard before they are hungry again."

Kade's face changed.

Performance fell away.

"Yes," he said. "I accept supervised release within field evidence perimeter, pending public testimony."

The magistrate nodded. "So ordered."

Vos unlocked the custody band from full restraint mode. It did not come off. It clicked, softened, and stopped blinking red.

Kade stared at the changed light.

"That is anticlimactic," Tali said.

"Most partial freedoms are," Iona said.

Ro began organizing testimony from the basin road. Amma called the first water witness into frame. Mara demanded five minutes to wash her hands before saying anything that would outlive her. The magistrate granted three. Mara took seven.

During those seven minutes, Sana stepped outside onto the terrace.

The security drones hovered high in review amber. The bridge lay under contested witness. Beyond the ridge, the dry basin road held a stopped march like a held breath. Puget's invisible rooms would be moving now, cutting links, renaming pools, preparing statements about isolated irregularities and commitment to public welfare.

Kade came out beside her.

"You are still banded," she said.

"You noticed."

"You are bad at hiding your wrist."

"I was never hiding the wrist."

No, Sana thought. He had been hiding what it meant to want out.

He rested his forearms on the terrace rail. "Couriers have a rule."

"Only one?"

"Many. Most contradict. This one says: never accept a cleared route if the bridge behind you remains marked dangerous for everyone else."

"That sounds less like a courier rule than a conscience pretending to be logistics."

"Logistics gets more funding."

She smiled despite herself.

Below, Tali shouted from the cold-room doorway, "If either of you is having a meaningful emotional conversation, stop. I need witnesses who can spell PUQ-Line correctly."

Kade called back, "No one can spell conspiracy without assistance."

Tali appeared in the doorway. "It is not a conspiracy. It is a risk pool wearing a hat."

"My mistake."

Sana looked once more toward the basin road.

"You did the right thing," she said.

Kade's jaw shifted. "Do not make it clean."

"I did not say clean."

"Good."

Inside, the hearing resumed.

The first water witness from Mile Ten gave her name, her route, her household count, and the number of days since the last reliable delivery. Her voice shook only once. When the magistrate asked her to repeat the count, hundreds of people behind her repeated it with her.

The public record took the number.

Not enough.

Necessary.

Sana and Kade went back inside before the second witness began, carrying with them the ugly, fragile fact that a partial freedom had forced a fuller record open.

Chapter 33: Salmon Corridor

Ro learned that a stopped march was heavier than a moving one.

When people walked, anger had somewhere to put its feet. When people stopped, anger gathered in ankles, mouths, shoulders, in the hands gripping carts and water jugs and the sleeves of children who kept asking whether the vault had bread even after everyone explained the vault was mostly water law and disappointment.

Mile Ten had become a town without walls in less than an hour.

Families stood under the old shade frame and along the cracked road. Irrigation workers had parked two handcarts sideways to keep the youngest children out of the truck lane. School kitchen volunteers divided Sana's half bag of ration bars into pieces so small Ro wanted to object on mathematical grounds and did not, because every child receiving a fragment watched the adult hand it over and learned that someone had given something up before speaking. That mattered more than the calories, and the unfairness of that fact made Ro's throat hurt.

Amma sat on a stone mileage block with HUNGER DAYS open across her knees. She had three water witnesses beside her, two school kitchen stewards, a carrier from the lower road, an elder house representative, and a boy of maybe sixteen who had been sent forward by three families because he could read court notices without spitting.

Beyond them, the salmon restoration zone began.

It looked fragile and defended at the same time: low water channels braided through gravel beds, shade cloth over hatchery side pools, willow stakes, sensor posts, oxygen monitors, and old treaty markers painted blue and red. In another season, young salmon would move through those channels toward the river, a living debt paid slowly by people who had once nearly destroyed the return. Today the channels were low. The gravel shone. Every water line in the zone was a promise that could not bear much more taking.

The North Rain Vault road crossed the restoration zone at a narrow causeway.

That was the problem.

The march could not reach the vault without crossing salmon water.

And the first armed food patrol arrived just as Ro understood that fact.

They came from the north in two electric trucks with mesh grilles and municipal seals half-covered by temporary FOOD STABILITY PATROL placards. Not army. Not police exactly. The worst category: people carrying authority assembled quickly from fear. Four stepped out with visible sidearms and long restraint poles meant for animals, crowds, or anyone an office preferred to classify as force problem before hunger problem.

The crowd changed shape.

No one charged. No one ran. But parents shifted children behind carts. Irrigation workers put down water jugs and picked up tools. A woman with a baby tied to her chest lifted a stone without seeming to decide to.

Ro stepped into the road before the patrol leader finished closing his truck door.

Amma said, "Ro."

"I know."

She did not know. Not fully. But she knew the first person to define the scene would own too much of what happened next.

Ro raised both hands, palms open, court seal visible on her jacket.

"Water Court field officer," she called. "Active compact witness. Emergency evidentiary preservation hearing in progress. No one crosses, no one disperses, no one points anything until we name the problem correctly."

The patrol leader was a square man with rain-pale eyes and a face built by too many briefings. His badge read Lieutenant Hask.

"This assembly is blocking a protected restoration corridor," he said.

The word assembly hit the crowd badly. Not march. Not families. Assembly.

Ro heard the inhale behind her.

"This is a hunger march stopped for negotiation before entering a protected corridor," she said.

Hask's eyes flicked to the crowd, then back. "They are attempting to access North Rain Vault."

"They are attempting to be heard before decisions about their food and water are made without them."

"My order is to protect the salmon channel and the vault road."

"Good," Ro said. "Mine is to prevent avoidable harm to people, water, evidence, and corridor trust. Those orders overlap if we do not behave stupidly."

One of the younger patrol officers looked uncertain.

Hask did not. "The road must clear."

Behind Ro, the sixteen-year-old reader said, too loudly, "We knew it."

The murmur spread.

Amma stood. She did not raise her voice. She did not need to. "No one moves yet."

The murmur held.

Ro breathed once. She had argued with courts, offices, and sealed packets. She had not stood between hungry people and armed patrol in a salmon corridor with the legal authority of a bridge clause older than the policies being shouted at her. Her hands wanted paper. Her mouth wanted a bench. The road gave her neither.

Her slate chimed.

Sana:

BRIDGE STABLE FOR LIGHT FOOT TRAFFIC. CULTURES ALIVE. HEARING OPEN. KEEP THEM FROM BEING MADE INTO THE WRONG STORY.

Then Tali:

PATROL BODY CAMS LIVE. THREE EXTERNAL FEEDS WATCHING. ONE TAGGED PUQ-LINE MEDIA MONITOR.

Of course.

The risk pool did not need to fire a shot. It needed the hungry to look like a threat to fish, the patrol to look like defenders of ecology, and the corridor to become impossible because every side had been handed a reason to fear the other.

Ro turned slowly so the crowd could hear.

"They want this story to be basin families against salmon water," she said.

A restoration worker near the channel fence, a woman in mud boots with a hatchery patch, shouted back, "It is if they cross the beds."

"Then come here and help me make it not true."

The woman blinked.

Ro pointed to the causeway. "You know the channel. They know the hunger. I know the court. We need a route that does not kill fish, does not put children under restraint poles, and does not hand the insurer a riot."

Hask said, "This is not a negotiation."

Ro looked at his body camera. "Lieutenant Hask states this is not a negotiation in the presence of named living stewards, Water Court field officer, restoration staff, and an active evidentiary hearing. Noted."

His jaw tightened.

The mud-booted woman came forward, angry and afraid in equal measure. "I am Nera Voss. Salmon corridor supervisor. The causeway can hold foot traffic, not carts. No one steps off stone. No one touches side pools. No dogs."

"We have no dogs," the sixteen-year-old said.

Nera looked at him. "You have three."

He looked back at the crowd. Three dogs immediately became everyone's shame.

Amma said, "Dogs stay here with cart guards."

Someone objected. Someone else said the dogs would be eaten if left. Amma turned her head very slowly, and that argument died of embarrassment.

Ro said, "What about water access?"

Nera's face hardened. "No drawing from restoration channels."

The crowd disliked that too.

Ro held up a hand. "Alternative?"

"There is a service cistern at the hatchery shed. Non-potable unless filtered."

Amma said, "We have strips."

"Not enough," Nera said.

Hask said, "This is irrelevant. The assembly must disperse."

Ro did not look at him. "Lieutenant, if your order is to protect the channel, then a supervised crossing with restoration staff protects it better than pushing hungry people until they scatter into the beds."

"My order includes preventing unauthorized approach to the vault."

"Then say that part clearly."

He stopped.

Ro heard the court in her head: say what the order is. Say who benefits. Say which harm you are preventing and which harm you are causing.

Hask's body camera watched him consider honesty and reject it.

"No one proceeds north," he said.

The crowd moved.

Not forward. Inward.

Ro felt the pressure at her back like weather.

Amma stepped beside her. "They heard no."

"I know."

"No without bread is a match."

"I know."

The young reader called, "Then why did we stop?"

The question was not hostile. That made it worse.

Ro turned to him. "Because if you had reached the vault first, the record would say you threatened water. If you cross this corridor wrong, the record will say you threatened salmon. If you strike the patrol, the record will say you were the danger. We stopped so the record has to learn your names before it learns anyone's fear."

He stared at her.

"Will names get water?" he asked.

"Not by themselves."

"Then what?"

Amma answered. "Names, witnesses, route, demand, and refusal. In order. So when they say later that no one knew what you needed, the lie has nowhere clean to sit."

Nera looked at Amma with new attention.

Hask spoke into his shoulder mic. Ro caught fragments: delay, noncompliance, court interference, restoration risk.

Tali's message arrived:

PUQ-LINE MONITOR FLAGGED "SALMON THREAT." THEY ARE WAITING FOR IMAGE OF BASIN PEOPLE IN CHANNEL.

Ro showed it to Nera.

The restoration supervisor read it twice. Something in her face altered.

"They tagged what?"

"Salmon threat."

Nera looked back at the channel she had spent years rebuilding, then at the children under the shade frame, then at Hask's patrol trucks.

"No," she said.

It was not loud, but it was a hinge.

She walked to the causeway entrance and stood in front of it.

Hask said, "Supervisor Voss, step aside."

"No."

"You are obstructing patrol action."

"I am protecting the restoration corridor."

"By allowing passage?"

"By supervising passage before your poles make people run through gravel beds."

Ro could have kissed her, but law discouraged useful impulses.

Nera pointed at the march. "Fifty at a time. Children, elders, and water carriers first. Carts unload and pass empty or stay. Dogs stay. No side pools. No shouting on the causeway. Anyone who steps off stone goes back."

The crowd began to speak all at once.

Amma raised HUNGER DAYS.

Silence came in ragged pieces.

"You want passage or performance?" Amma asked.

That worked.

Ro said, "Where do they go after the causeway?"

Nera pointed. "Hatchery shed. There is shade, cistern, and old sorting yard. It is not the vault road yet."

Hask said, "I do not authorize movement toward the vault."

Ro said, "Then authorize movement away from immediate conflict and out of the restoration beds."

"You are twisting my order."

"I am giving it a chance to survive contact with reality."

The younger patrol officer coughed.

Hask heard it.

Good, Ro thought. Let him know his own people can still recognize absurdity.

The hearing slate chimed. The magistrate's voice entered, thin but official.

"Water Court acknowledges supervised humanitarian repositioning through salmon restoration causeway under corridor protection, without prejudice to North Rain Vault access dispute."

Ro nearly sagged.

Hask looked furious. "Court order?"

"Court acknowledgment," Ro said. "If you want to oppose it, do so on record."

He did not. Not yet.

The first fifty took ten minutes to assemble.

No process involving hungry families took the shape authority wanted. Children cried because dogs could not come. Elders argued about whether they counted as elders. A man refused to leave his cart until three other people promised to carry the flour sack that was not actually flour but soil from a dead garden he intended to show the vault. The sixteen-year-old reader organized names with Ro while pretending not to be proud when she handed him a court slate.

Nera walked at the front of the first group, naming each stone of the causeway as if introducing it.

"Center. Center. Not there. That stone wobbles. Child on the left, hold the rail. You, with the blue jug, look at me, not at him."

Him was Hask.

The patrol stood aside by less than dignity required but more than fear preferred.

As the first group crossed, the salmon channels glimmered on either side. Small fish flickered in the shaded pools, nearly invisible until they moved. One child saw them and whispered, "Food?"

His mother closed her eyes.

Nera heard. Ro saw the flinch.

"Not today," Nera said, softer than she had spoken yet. "Those are a later promise."

The child said, "We need today."

No one had a good answer.

So Ro recorded the sentence.

At the hatchery shed, water strips turned cistern water from suspect to grudgingly acceptable. Not enough. Never enough. But enough to wet mouths, reduce panic, and make the second group believe the first had not vanished into a trap.

Halfway through the third crossing, Hask received a call that made his face close.

Tali sent the explanation two seconds later:

PUQ-LINE MEDIA MONITOR DROPPED SALMON THREAT TAG. NEW TAG: COURT-CONTESTED HUMANITARIAN PASSAGE.

Ro looked at Nera. "You changed the image."

Nera watched a boy carry an empty cart across the center stones. "I protected my fish."

"Yes."

"And your people."

"They are not mine."

Nera's eyes stayed on the causeway. "They are while you stand there."

Ro had no procedural answer to that, which was becoming a theme.

By late afternoon, the march had moved in ordered groups through the salmon corridor to the sorting yard. They were still far from fed. They were still angry. The vault access dispute remained. Hask's patrol remained. Puget's insurer remained unnamed in too many rooms. But no one had been beaten into the channels. No image of basin families trampling salmon beds had been delivered to the people waiting to profit from it.

Ro stood at the causeway entrance after the last group crossed and looked down into the low water.

A silver flicker moved under shade.

Amma came beside her. "You look like someone who just negotiated with fish."

"The fish were more reasonable."

"They usually are. They only ask for water."

Ro laughed, then almost cried, then did neither because the day was not finished.

Her slate chimed with a message from Sana:

KADE HEARING OPEN. TESTIMONY ENTERING RECORD. CULTURES STABLE. BRIDGE CONTESTED BUT HOLDING.

Ro sent back:

MARCH THROUGH SALMON CORRIDOR. NO CHANNEL BREACH. PATROLS CONTAINED. VAULT DISPUTE NEXT.

Then she added:

THEY STILL NEED WATER.

Amma read it over her shoulder and nodded.

That was the sentence under every sentence.

Ro looked toward the sorting yard where hundreds of people waited under hatchery shade, not dispersed, not pacified, not yet answered. The road north to the vault remained blocked by law, fear, and men with temporary placards.

But behind them the salmon corridor still lived.

For one narrow passage, at least, no one had been made into the wrong enemy.

Chapter 34: The Second Blight Flag

The second blight flag arrived while the first water witness was still spelling her grandmother's name for the record.

Tali saw it before the court did.

That was becoming a pattern she disliked because it meant the court was always downstream of harm and then surprised to find itself wet.

The alert bloomed on the Long Orchard map in the wrong color: official disease crimson, not field uncertainty amber. It appeared over a drought maize reserve outside Lower Silt, one of the seed blocks scheduled to support the dry basin kitchens if North Rain Vault remained locked. The label was blunt and expensive:

QUARANTINE RECOMMENDED / MAIZE RUST COMPLEX / SPREAD RISK HIGH / ALL TRANSFER HALTED.

For three seconds, no one in Mara Venn's cold room spoke.

Then Mara said, "No."

It was not denial. It was refusal.

Sana stepped to Tali's slate. "Which block?"

"Lower Silt Cooperative Maize Four. Drought line K-Delta Yellow, basin-adapted, low-water milling grade." Tali's fingers were already moving. "It feeds school kitchens better than it feeds markets, which explains why someone hates it."

Kade, now in supervised release with the band blinking a mild and insulting green, looked up from the testimony feed. "That block is on the old canal shelf."

Iona said, "It is also in the compact annex."

Vos moved to the terrace doorway. "Can quarantine be automatic?"

"Recommended," Sana said. "Not imposed."

Tali laughed once. "It is functionally imposed if every transport system reads the flag before any human reads the field."

On the basin road feed, Ro's face turned toward someone off-screen. The witness stopped spelling. The crowd noise altered, a ripple traveling through people who did not yet know what had happened but had learned to recognize the sound of new trouble arriving.

Amma's message came in:

WHAT NOW.

Not a question mark.

Sana looked at the map. The maize block sat south of the salmon corridor, east of the stopped march, close enough to reach before night if someone already had a route and no one placed another legal fence across it. It connected to the school kitchen stewards just named in the hearing. It connected to the empty carts. It connected to tomorrow's food, not today's water, which made it exactly the kind of thing panic would surrender first and regret after.

"Who reported it?" she asked.

Tali opened the alert chain. "Automated field image. Drone survey. Disease classifier. Archive compatibility cross-check. Insurance risk notification. Transport halt recommendation."

"Order?"

Tali smiled without warmth. "Wrong one."

She threw the timestamps onto the wall.

Insurance risk notification: 14:08:22.

Transport halt recommendation: 14:08:31.

Disease classifier output: 14:09:04.

Drone image uploaded: 14:09:16.

Archive compatibility cross-check: 14:10:41.

Kade leaned closer. "The insurer heard before the image existed."

"Again," Tali said.

Mara stood despite the heat wraps falling from her wrists. "K-Delta Yellow does not rust like that."

Sana turned to her. "You know the line?"

"I helped carry its grandmother through a flood."

No one asked if she meant that literally. By now everyone knew better.

Iona took the slate and enlarged the official image. A maize leaf filled the display, streaked with orange-red lesions. It looked convincing to anyone who had learned plants from warning posters. Sana had learned plants from fields, failures, and elders who did not trust posters that arrived before boots.

"Too centered," she said.

Mara nodded. "Too pretty."

Iona added, "Too hungry for attention."

Vos looked between them. "Explain."

Sana pointed to the lesion pattern. "Rust travels with breath, water, contact, stress. It does not arrange itself for a diagnostic brochure on one leaf while the surrounding canopy remains visually polite."

"Unless early?"

"Unless planted," Mara said.

Tali overlaid the first false blight event. "Same visual grammar. Different crop. Same theatrical disease."

Ro's voice came through the feed. "Say that in hearing language."

Sana did not realize the court had opened the cold room audio until the magistrate's seal appeared beside Ro's face.

The magistrate said, "Field Auditor Ilyen, are you challenging the disease flag?"

"Yes."

"On what basis?"

Sana answered before caution could domesticate the sentence. "The alert chain is temporally impossible, the lesion image is biologically suspicious, and the target crop is a living corridor asset just named by the march's school kitchen stewards."

Succession counsel said, "A disease risk cannot be ignored because it is inconvenient."

Mara snapped, "A crop cannot be killed because a computer coughed orange."

The magistrate said, "Ms. Venn."

"I am relevant now. You did that to yourself."

Tali opened another pane. "Drone ash present. Survey drone used a sanitation relay owned by a contractor tied to the same PUQ-Line risk pool. It shed command dust in the same claim-window rhythm as the bridge event."

The room seemed to tilt around that.

Ro said, "Can you get field verification?"

"Yes," Sana said.

Vos looked at her. "With whom?"

That was the real question. The team was spread thin: Ro and Amma at Mile Ten, Sana at Mara's terrace, Tali tied to data, Kade supervised, Vos balancing force and restraint, Iona guarding old law, Mara guarding living culture. Every corridor had begun asking for a body.

Kade said, "I can get there."

Vos said, "No."

Kade lifted his green-blinking band. "Supervised release within field evidence perimeter."

"Lower Silt is outside the current perimeter."

Ro spoke from the road. "Then expand the perimeter."

Succession counsel objected immediately. "Absolutely not. Mr. Arlo remains implicated in unauthorized transfer."

Kade looked at the screen. "That is why I know where the maize is."

Amma's face appeared beside Ro's. "We have two school kitchen stewards from Lower Silt here. They can identify the block and name who has authority to enter."

Nera Voss, the salmon corridor supervisor, stepped into Ro's frame. "I can send restoration field staff with sterile sampling kit. We handle waterborne pathogen protocols."

Hask's voice from off-screen: "My patrol will not escort an unauthorized evidence run."

Ro said, "Then do not. Stand there and be recorded declining."

Silence.

Sana almost admired how quickly Ro had learned to make a road behave like a courtroom.

The magistrate conferred off-screen again. When she returned, she looked less clean than before, as if reality had finally left fingerprints on her room.

"The court authorizes limited field verification of the maize flag by named living stewards, restoration staff, and Field Audit under remote supervision. Mr. Arlo may serve as route guide if accompanied by Officer Vos or a designated security officer."

Vos said, "I am at south ridge."

Nera said, "I have Rell."

A young restoration worker lifted a hand in Ro's frame, startled to find himself promoted by geography.

Nera continued, "Rell knows sterile kit, not guns. That is better."

Hask said, "No."

The magistrate said, "Lieutenant Hask, the court requires noninterference unless actual violence occurs."

Hask's face was not visible, but Ro's smile was, and it was sharp enough to count.

Kade turned to Sana. "You need a courier."

"You need not to get recaptured by every office with a grudge."

"That list is too long to plan around."

Mara pointed a wrapped wrist at him. "If you step on my maize, I will request your recapture."

"Noted."

Iona handed him a clay tag. "Show this to Lower Silt. It says you are carrying compact witness."

Kade took it carefully. "Does it also say I am not an idiot?"

"No. I will not commit fraud."

For the first time all day, the laugh that moved through the cold room did not break anything.

It ended quickly.

The field verification team assembled through the hearing feed: Kade guided remotely from the south ridge until a basin courier could meet him at the salmon corridor; Rell carried sterile kit; two school kitchen stewards, Mala and Jor, carried authority from Lower Silt households; Amma remained at Mile Ten to keep the march from outrunning the evidence; Ro kept the record open; Tali tracked drone ash; Sana prepared biological challenge criteria; Mara provided crop-specific markers; Iona recited the compact annex so no one could pretend the maize was merely inventory.

The first argument was over whether the maize could be approached before quarantine lifted.

Mara solved it by saying, "If the quarantine is false, delay kills food. If quarantine is true, sloppy entry spreads disease. Either way, you enter like you love what you might lose."

That became the field protocol.

Enter like you love what you might lose.

Tali typed it into the packet title before anyone could stop her.

The second argument was over images.

"No single-leaf conclusions," Sana said. "Canopy sweep, underside samples, soil moisture, neighboring rows, wind direction, irrigation history, spore trap if they have one."

Mala, one of the school kitchen stewards, said, "We have children waiting."

"Then we do it fast and correctly."

"Correctly is what offices say when they mean late."

Sana took that because it was earned. "Then you watch me do it differently."

The verification feed began nineteen minutes later.

It came through shaky and dust-colored from Rell's field cam. Lower Silt Cooperative Maize Four stretched under a pale afternoon sky, not lush, not picturesque, but alive in the practical way of crops bred to endure insult. Rows of knee-high maize held their leaves upright despite low water. The soil was cracked between drip lines. Wind moved the field in small, dry shivers.

No one spoke for the first ten seconds.

Then Mara said, "Canopy is wrong for rust."

Sana nodded. "Too uniform in health."

Rell walked the first row. The official flagged plant stood near a marker stake, its damaged leaf tied with red thread.

Kade's voice came through another channel; he had reached the old canal overlook and was guiding from memory. "That marker stake was not there last month."

Mala said, "It was not there yesterday."

Tali whispered, "Hello, lie."

Rell knelt. "Sampling."

"Underside first," Sana said.

The camera dipped. The underside of the leaf showed orange streaks, but no powdering, no spread, no neighboring lesions. The lesions sat on the surface like paint pretending to be illness.

Mara said, "Wipe a corner with sterile damp."

Rell did.

Orange lifted onto the cloth.

Mala made a sound that hurt to hear.

"Not rust," Sana said for the record. "Surface application. Possible pigment or spore mimic. No visible infection structure."

Tali ran spectral read from the camera. "Contains iron oxide, plant binder, and synthetic volatile marker designed to trigger cheap rust classifiers."

Ro said, "So someone painted sickness."

"Yes," Sana said. "On food needed by hungry people."

The field went very quiet.

Jor, the second steward, stepped into view. He was older, with hands that looked built by mills. He did not touch the leaf.

"My granddaughter's school was told this maize might make them sick," he said.

No one had an answer large enough.

The court transcript took the sentence.

Then Tali cursed.

"What?" Sana asked.

"The quarantine recommendation just escalated to mandatory. They did it after the field wipe."

"Who?"

"Archive compatibility emergency overlay. Same insurer notification attached. They are trying to outrun the correction."

The magistrate said, "The court has live contradictory evidence."

Tali said, "The transport networks do not care unless the court pushes faster than the overlay."

Ro leaned toward her camera. "Your Honor."

The magistrate was already moving. "Temporary stay of maize quarantine pending field verification. Transport halt suspended for Lower Silt Cooperative Maize Four under compact witness. Any office maintaining contrary flag must show live biological evidence, not classifier output."

Tali sent it. Ro sent it. Amma read it. Nera relayed it through the salmon corridor. Kade, standing at the canal overlook, repeated it to a basin courier who ran it down the road because paper still moved through signal fights.

The mandatory flag turned amber.

Not cleared.

Contested.

Contested was becoming a kind of oxygen.

Mala looked at the field camera, then beyond it to people watching from a road, a terrace, a cold room, a court, a salmon corridor.

"Can we harvest if needed?" she asked.

Mara answered. "Not yet. But you can keep it alive."

Sana added, "And now they have to fight you in public to stop you."

Mala nodded. "That is better than private."

Not good.

Better.

The second blight flag remained on the map, no longer crimson but bruised amber, tied now to drone ash, insurer timing, painted disease, and a live court stay.

The Long Orchard map changed again.

Not because harm had stopped.

Because one more invisible cut had failed to stay invisible.

Chapter 35: Proof in Soil

Soil did not lie cleanly.

That was why Sana trusted it more than images.

Images arrived framed. Classifiers arrived confident. Court packets arrived with margins wide enough for cowardice. Soil arrived under nails, in cracked sample cups, clinging to roots and boots and the underside of leaves, carrying too much history to make a simple accusation without being forced.

The first Lower Silt soil sample reached Mara Venn's terrace at dusk in the pocket of a basin courier who had run the old canal shelf so hard that he vomited behind the cold room before handing it over.

"Sorry," he said, wiping his mouth with the back of his hand.

Mara took the sample cup. "For running or for vomiting?"

"Both."

"One was useful."

He looked uncertain.

"The running," she said. "Sit before you fall into my cultures."

His name was Pell. He could not have been older than nineteen, with dust caked into the folds of his eyelids and a strip of blue cloth tied around one wrist to show he had crossed the salmon corridor under Nera's supervision. He carried three sealed cups: soil from the flagged maize plant, soil from two rows away, and soil from the old canal bank where Kade said the marker stake had likely been stored before someone planted it.

Sana set them on Mara's worktable under a lamp that had been repaired with wire, tape, and what looked like spite.

The emergency evidentiary hearing remained open on Tali's slate. Ro had moved the Mile Ten testimony to the hatchery sorting yard. Amma was rationing witness time the way she rationed food: enough for truth to survive, not enough for anyone to waste. The magistrate had stopped trying to make the proceeding resemble a chamber and now looked like a woman riding a flood on a desk.

Tali projected the maize data beside the soil cups.

"Current status," she said. "Quarantine contested. Transport halt suspended but fragile. PUQ-Line nodes deleting old promotional pages at an adorable speed. Archive compatibility office claims its escalation was automatic. Helix has discovered sorrow about being misunderstood. The painted leaf sample is on its way by courier two."

Kade leaned against the wall, band green, face gray with fatigue. "Pell beat courier two?"

Pell looked offended. "Courier two took the road."

"Poor soul."

Vos, stationed near the terrace door, said, "Focus."

Sana opened the first soil cup.

The smell came up dry and mineral, with a faint organic sharpness from stressed maize roots. She closed her eyes. Lower Silt soil had its own grammar: old flood silt, canal salt, ash amendments, trace fungal support from cooperative compost, little nitrogen, stubborn life. The flagged plant's soil carried all of that.

And something else.

"This is wrong," she said.

Mara leaned in. "How wrong?"

"Old wrong."

Iona, who had been annotating the compact annex in a hand so severe it looked like it could cut paper by itself, looked up. "Old as in archive?"

"Old as in not from this field's living surface."

Tali stopped moving.

Sana took a sterile glass rod, lifted a crumb of soil from the flagged sample, and smeared it across a slide. She added moisture, not enough to dissolve, enough to wake. Under the portable scope the field opened into grains, fibers, spores, fragments, mineral flecks. She adjusted light. The image appeared on the wall.

Mara made a soft, ugly sound.

"What?" Vos asked.

"Collapse-era storage dust," Mara said.

Sana nodded. "Maybe."

Kade pushed off the wall. "Explain."

Sana pointed at the slide. "See these spore shells? Too empty. Too uniform in breakage. They are dead and old, not field-senescent. This mineral coating is vault desiccant residue. Lower Silt uses ash and canal silt. It does not produce that kind of storage scar."

Pell, sitting on an overturned crate, whispered, "Someone put dead sickness in the dirt?"

"Someone put the memory of sickness in the dirt," Iona said.

That was precise enough to make the room colder.

Tali pulled up old datasets. "Collapse-era samples. Maize Rust Complex. Basin failures. Archive reference images."

"Not just images," Sana said. "Physical reference material."

Mara's face hardened. "Who still has physical rust complex samples?"

No one answered because everyone knew the shape of the answer.

Archives. Insurers. Research vaults. Continuity offices. The same rooms that called living seeds risky and dead evidence safe.

Sana opened the second sample, two rows away. Living field soil, stressed but coherent. No storage dust. No dead uniform spore shells. She opened the third, from the canal bank.

There it was again.

Old wrong.

"The marker stake carried contaminated reference dust," she said. "Or staged reference dust. It was planted near the flagged maize, then the leaf was painted. The soil was salted with old sample material to make a physical test look plausible if anyone checked only for presence, not vitality."

Tali's eyes narrowed. "So the system was designed to survive shallow verification."

"Yes."

"A cheap field kit would say rust markers present."

"Yes."

"A rushed court would uphold quarantine."

"Yes."

"A hungry crowd would lose tomorrow's maize."

Sana looked at Pell. He had both hands clasped around his knees, gripping hard.

"Yes," she said.

The hearing slate chimed. The magistrate had heard enough to sit straighter.

"Field Auditor Ilyen," she said, "can you state your finding for the record?"

Sana looked at the slide on the wall: dead spores, storage scars, old dust used to impersonate living threat.

"Preliminary finding," she said. "The Lower Silt maize flag is not supported by active field infection evidence. Soil from the flagged plant and adjacent marker storage site contains dead collapse-era or archive-stored rust complex material inconsistent with living field spread. Visual lesion evidence appears surface-applied. The pattern indicates simulation using old disease reference material, not a natural outbreak."

Ro, from the hatchery feed, closed her eyes.

Amma said, "Again, shorter."

Sana's mouth tightened. "Someone used old dead disease to frame living food."

Pell flinched as if the sentence had struck him.

Amma repeated it to the sorting yard.

The sound that came back was not a roar. It was worse: hundreds of people understanding quietly.

Tali began linking datasets. "I have collapse-era reference access logs. Most are sealed. But metadata shows three recent compatibility pulls using a Continuity Model Triage credential and two insurer research mirrors."

"Vale?" Sana asked.

Tali checked. "One credential class could include Vale. Not enough to name him. The insurer mirror is more interesting."

"PUQ-Line?"

"Adjacent. Registered through Puget Regional Food Transit Insurance Consortium research indemnity program."

Kade laughed without humor. "Every path leads to a polite name."

Mara took the slide from the scope and placed it in a seal tray. "Polite names rot too."

Vos said, "If archive material was removed, that is criminal evidence."

Iona's expression went flat. "If archive material was removed, it was probably done under a form created to ensure nothing criminal could be seen from the hallway."

The magistrate said, "The court is issuing preservation orders for all collapse-era maize rust reference access logs, physical sample inventories, insurer research mirrors, and Continuity Model Triage credential use related to this event."

Succession counsel objected. "Overbroad."

Mara turned toward the slate. "A disease flag was just painted onto food."

"The scope of the order must remain proportionate."

Iona said, "Fourteen settlements once died proportionately."

That silenced him for one blessed second.

Tali used the second to push the soil proof packet into every live record channel. She titled it:

PROOF IN SOIL / DEAD DISEASE USED AGAINST LIVING CORRIDOR.

Sana did not correct the drama.

Sometimes drama was just accuracy finally getting tired.

The painted leaf sample arrived twenty minutes later with courier two, who had indeed taken the road and looked ashamed of it. The leaf confirmed what the soil had already said. The orange lesion material contained synthetic volatile markers, iron oxide, and fragments of dead rust spores from an old preserved strain. No active hyphae. No viable spore bodies. No field infection.

Mara watched Sana seal the leaf and said, "They are getting better."

Pell looked up, horrified. "Better?"

"At lying to shallow tests."

"How do we stop that?"

Mara looked at Sana.

So did Iona.

So did Tali.

Sana understood the question was bigger than maize. It was every hidden cut, every legal delay, every model omission, every fake disease, every authority that arrived before evidence and called itself caution.

"We change what counts as a test," she said.

Tali leaned back. "That is either the most obvious thing anyone has said today or a revolution with bad user documentation."

"Both."

Sana turned to the hearing slate.
