"Contingencies do not know which political sentence to finish unless someone writes them that way."

Ansel's face closed. "Auditor Ilyen, unless you can extinguish the corridor from here, accusation is not response."

"Neither is surrender."

Orren said, "North Rain security recommends immediate seal lockdown and transfer of outer perimeter command to Compact emergency force."

Vos said, "No."

Orren's head turned. "You still have no command."

"I have eyes. Your recommendation points security inward at the crowd while the fire is outward on the corridor. That is how you lose both."

"We cannot permit panic movement toward the vault."

"Then do not cause panic movement."

Ro cut in. "All armed perimeter holds outward orientation. No vault approach escalation. Water distribution continues. Threshold table remains open."

Ansel stared at her. "This is governance theater while a corridor burns."

Amma Quill had reached the terrace by then, breath sharp, paper ledger pressed under one arm. "No. This is the part where you try to use a burning corridor as permission to take the door."

Ansel's control thinned. "Mrs. Quill, with respect--"

"Do not spend respect as a delay fee."

The marchers closest to the terrace heard her. The sentence moved.

Tali's slate chirped again.

She swore once, softly, then pushed a second pane into the docket. "Central emergency recommendation includes draft order naming Regional Food Stability as temporary unified release authority, Compact emergency force as distribution security, and North Rain Vault as compulsory supply node."

Kade said, "There is the real flame."

Sana read the draft order. The language was smooth and ready. Too ready. It cited corridor collapse risk, pollinator dependency, public disorder probability, seal integrity, and continuity of food stabilization. It did not cite second witness provenance. It did not cite founder threshold. It did not cite Tovin Pell except as "recent mortality event increasing unrest." It converted a child's name back into risk weather.

Her throat tightened.

"Ro," she said.

"I see it."

"They are using the fire to make the living trusteeship impossible."

"Yes."

Mara's head came up. "Not only impossible. Irrelevant. If the corridor burns, the pollinator witness is gone. Soil match shifts. Release routes change. They can say the table assembled too late to reflect conditions."

Iona opened her eyes. "They are burning the witness because the door listened."

No one spoke for a breath.

Then Oren Lusk said, "Can we save it?"

Not the case. Not the rule. Not the threshold.

It.

The corridor.

The living thing.

That pulled Sana back into her body faster than accusation had. She looked at the map not as evidence, but as plants, insects, moisture, workers, shelters, children who would never know the names of bees and would still eat because they moved.

"Yes," she said, before she knew whether it was true.

Mara looked at her with anger and gratitude both. "Do not say yes like a prayer."

"Then give me facts."

Mara jabbed at the map. "Hand-pollinator houses have manual mist systems. If they still have pressure, they can wet the nesting walls and slow upper flame. Wild refuges need smoke diversion, not foam. Foam kills what fire misses. Sugar slurry shed will flash if heat reaches it. Mycelial mat must be cut ahead of the smolder line. If the mat carries heat under soil, we lose root bridges for the drought lines."

Tali was already building a response grid. "Drone suppression unreliable due spoof. Manual crews on road delayed. Worker distress at north spur. We have local stewards?"

Amma flipped pages. "Firebreak Seven corridor crew lead: Jalen Odet. Pollinator keeper: Sivi Marr. School garden witness: Pel An. Maintenance spur worker: Lio Gant, if the distress beacon ID is current. Oren?"

Oren blinked at hearing his name used as if he belonged to the map. "My sister's children volunteer there on wet weeks."

"Names."

He gave them.

Amma wrote.

Ro began issuing orders into three channels. "Water Court emergency preservation order: pollinator corridor evidence and life support priority. No foam without pollinator keeper authorization. Drone suppression remains grounded unless authenticated by local corridor witness and court tech. Road access cleared for Firebreak crews. Media traffic off route. Any central order attempting to reclassify local suppression as unauthorized is stayed pending live witness."

Ansel said, "You cannot stay an order that has not issued."

"Then tell your office not to issue it."

"I do not control every emergency office."

"That was not denial."

Tali looked up. "I can authenticate local hand controllers if someone at the corridor reads old hardware keys over open audio."

Kade raised his unburned hand. "Courier route to Firebreak Seven runs through Moss Corridor and an old pump house. There is a maintenance bypass, too narrow for vehicles, passable on foot. If clean shoes have not burned it."

Vos said, "I can send unarmed runners."

"No," Sana said. "Send people the corridor knows."

Vos's eyes moved to her, then to the march. He understood. Outsider uniforms on a burning pollinator corridor would turn rescue into occupation.

Amma called down the road. "Firebreak volunteers who know South Seven corridor, step to blue cord. No weapons. No cameras. Names first."

People moved.

Not many. Enough.

A woman with a scarred cheek. A boy no older than seventeen with smoke training beads on his sleeve. Two school garden stewards. A courier with a limp. Oren's niece, who could not have been more than fourteen and glared at anyone who noticed. Nera tried to stop her, then stopped herself when the girl said, "I know the house valves."

Sana caught Ro's eye. "I need field authority."

Iona said, "No."

Sana turned.

The old woman's refusal had not softened for fire.

"I am not asking to open the vault."

"You are asking to become necessary in a way that can be captured later."

"The corridor is burning."

"Then go as part of the chain."

Sana swallowed impatience that tasted like smoke. "How?"

Iona pointed from the threshold table to the volunteers forming at the blue cord. "Field auditor, culture witness, hunger witness, corridor custodian, court witness. Not one."

Mara stepped beside Sana. "I am going."

"Your leg--"

"Can hurt while I work."

Ro said, "I will assign a court clerk with portable witness."

"No," Sana said. "You."

Ro's brows lifted.

"The central draft order will move the second you are out of sight," Tali warned.

"Then keep it in sight," Ro said. "Clone everything. Put my channel on public relay. If they issue, I answer from the fire."

Ansel said, "Mediator, abandoning the threshold table now would be reckless."

Ro looked at her. "So is staying dry enough to call burning witnesses disorder."

They were not dry. None of them were. Still, the sentence worked.

Tali held up her slate. "I can stay here and fight the paperwork with Amma and Iona. I am better at hating documents than breathing smoke."

Kade said, "I know the bypass."

Sana looked at his bandaged palm.

"I have another hand."

"You have a talent for being where crimes become visible."

"That is almost affection."

"It is logistics."

Vos said, "Take two outward perimeter medics. Unarmed."

Iona nodded once. "Medical witness."

The chain formed because Iona had refused to let urgency turn back into a single hand.

That was the maddening thing. The refusal slowed them for seconds and saved them from a mistake that would have cost hours in court later. Each person named why they were going. Sana: field audit and line safety. Mara: culture and pollinator harm. Kade: route and second witness. Ro: court witness. Oren's niece, whose name was Sella: house valves. Jalen Odet by remote: corridor command. Two medics: medical witness. A school garden steward named Mir: distribution and child food dependency.

Tali recorded the chain and threw it onto the public docket before Ansel's aides could call it irregular.

"Irregular," one aide said anyway.

"Visible," Tali corrected.

The maintenance bypass began behind a drainage wall below the spillway terrace. Kade found the old marker under moss by touch, a courier habit older than signage. Vos helped lever the panel aside. Cold air breathed out, smelling of stone, roots, and old pump metal.

Sana looked back once.

North Rain Vault remained closed above the threshold table. Iona stood before it like a hinge made of bone. Amma had taken Sana's place at the wet arc, ledger open. Ansel stood across from her, silver mantle dimmed by rain. Orren watched the bypass with the expression of a man filing future objections inside his skull. The crowd watched because the map had become people moving toward fire.

Sana ducked into the bypass.

The passage was narrow enough to scrape shoulders. Water ran along one side in a concrete lip. Roots had entered through cracks and thickened around pipe brackets. Old service lights blinked in patches, some dead, some steady, some pulsing as if the mountain had a failing heartbeat. Kade led, one hand on the wall. Sella followed close behind him, jaw set, hair braided tight with blue string. Sana came after, then Mara, then Ro, Mir, and the medics.

Above them, the public channel played through Ro's shoulder speaker.

Ansel: "Regional office cannot certify this ad hoc chain."

Iona: "You are not asked to certify its existence. You are watching it."

Amma: "Names entered."

Tali: "Central emergency office is typing. I can feel them typing."

Ro said, "Tali."

"Fine. I can see draft activity increasing in the filing pane."

Kade smiled despite the dark. "Typing was better."

Smoke touched the bypass before light did.

It seeped through a ventilation seam in a thin bitter ribbon. Mara stopped, lifted her head, and breathed through her scarf.

"Reed shelter," she said. "And sugar. The slurry shed is heating."

Sella shoved past Kade. "Then we hurry."

"Careful," Ro said.

The girl did not slow. "Careful is why adults arrive after things are gone."

No one had an answer good enough to spend breath on.

They emerged through a pump house half-hidden under sword ferns at the edge of South Seven corridor.

The world outside was orange and wet and wrong.

Rain fell through smoke. Fire ran low along the bramble windbreak, not towering yet, but fast, licking through dry training lattice under living green. Pollinator houses stood on short stilts beyond the windbreak, their walls painted with old child murals: blue bees, red flowers, hands the size of suns. One house already smoked from the roof seam. Workers moved in silhouettes, some carrying hose packs, some beating at sparks with wet canvas, some standing because fear had taken their instructions away.

The corridor was not wilderness. It was labor made green. Trellises, nesting walls, wild strips, water beads, fungus mats, hand gates, plantings arranged so insects could survive weather no single crop could survive alone. It had taken decades to build a place where hunger could be answered by wings.

Someone had tried to reduce it to emergency footage.

Sana stepped into the rain.

"Sella," she said. "Valves."

The girl ran.

Kade followed because the route around the slurry shed mattered more than his hand. Mara limped toward the nearest pollinator house with a medic beside her. Ro climbed onto the pump house step and opened her channel to maximum pickup.

"Water Court live witness at South Seven corridor," she said. "Local suppression chain present. No militarized release authority accepted by implication. Repeat: emergency fire response is not consent to consolidated seed authority."

Sana almost laughed.

Ro could make a hose line sound like jurisprudence.

Then the wind shifted.

Smoke dropped to the ground.

The burning corridor disappeared around them.

And from somewhere inside the orange-gray, a worker screamed for the north spur.

Chapter 42: Smoke and Pollen

Smoke made distance dishonest.

The north spur could have been twenty meters away or a kilometer. The scream came again, higher this time, then broke into coughing. Sana dropped to one knee because her body remembered what her mind was still sorting: air improved near the ground until fire taught the ground to lie too. Rain tapped the back of her neck. Ash dissolved on her gloves. The corridor around her had become fragments: orange under bramble, black silhouettes, white steam where mist struck heat, the wet painted wall of a pollinator house flashing and vanishing behind smoke.

"North spur," she said into Ro's open channel. "Worker distress vocal. Visibility poor. Sella at valves. Kade with her. Mara at House One."

Ro's voice came back through the public relay, raw with smoke pickup. "Recorded. Local suppression chain remains active. Tali, map."

Tali was still at North Rain, but her attention arrived faster than a body could. A corridor schematic snapped onto Sana's wrist slate, rendered in hard lines because beauty was useless in smoke. "North spur is thirty-two meters upslope from your position. You have a drainage ditch left, bramble wall right, collapsed shade trellis ahead. Worker beacon is under or behind the trellis. Heat bloom moving toward sugar slurry shed. If that shed flashes, please do not be there."

"That was already my preference."

"I support your preferences when they are not stupid."

Mara coughed over the channel. "House One mist is failing. Pressure drops after three seconds."

Sella shouted from somewhere ahead, "Because the main split is half closed."

Kade said, "Which one is the main split?"

"The one with the old handwheel."

"There are four old handwheels."

"The angry one."

"That is not a technical description."

"It is if you know it."

Sana could hear the girl moving by sound: boots on wet grate, a metal clang, a curse too old for fourteen. Then pressure hit the corridor. A sheet of mist opened along the nearest pollinator house, catching firelight in a million brief beads. The smoke thinned by inches.

"Sella found the angry wheel," Kade reported.

"Sella is busy," Sella snapped.

Sana crawled toward the north spur, Mir close behind with a wet canvas roll. One medic followed, the other staying with Mara. The ground changed under Sana's palm from gravel to root mat. Warm. Too warm. The mycelial moisture layer was carrying heat below the visible flame.

"Mara," Sana said. "Mat is heating under north spur."

"Cut it before the fork," Mara answered. "Do not pull. Pulling tears the live bridge into the safe side. Slice, wet, lift with soil under it."

"With what?"

Mir slid a curved pruning blade into Sana's hand. "School gardens do not survive children without tools."

"That is bleak."

"Children are thorough."

They reached the collapsed trellis. A worker's arm protruded through the lattice, sleeve smoking. Sana grabbed the wrist, felt a pulse, and then the worker screamed again because pain had found someone listening.

"Alive," Sana said. "Pinned under shade frame. Mir, canvas. Medic, brace."

Ro's voice stayed level. "Worker alive, pinned north spur. Rescue in progress."

Ansel's voice cut into the public channel from North Rain. "Regional command can deploy aerial suppression if local crews clear the zone."

Tali answered before Ro could. "Aerial suppression is still spoof-contested and foam composition unknown."

"The corridor is burning."

"Yes, Chair, and the corridor contains pollinators, soil cultures, people, water control hardware, and evidence. Dropping generic foam is a great way to save a headline and kill the thing under it."

"Technical objections should be routed through emergency command."

"I am emergency technical objection with legs."

Sana almost smiled. Then the trellis shifted.

The worker under it gasped. Sana saw a face through wet leaves and ash: Lio Gant, probably, if Amma's ledger was right. Young. Eyes wide. A cheek burned red. A broken trellis spar pressed across his hip, and beyond him, through the lattice, a low line of fire ran along the reed shelter toward House Two.

"We need lift," the medic said.

"No," Sana said. "If we lift, it shifts onto his pelvis. Cut cross spar first."

Mir was already sawing.

The blade stuck.

Heat crawled closer.

Sana looked at the root mat, the spar, the worker, the approaching flame. There were too many needs. That was always how disaster made its argument: not by making one demand impossible, but by making five demands simultaneous and then calling the failed four negligence.

"Kade," she said. "Where are you?"

"By the slurry shed. Bad news: it is warm. Worse news: clean-shoe relay tag on the service latch."

"Proof?"

"Holding it up to Ro's lens."

Ro said, "Captured."

Kade continued, "Sella says if we open the bypass drain, slurry runoff goes into containment ditch, not houses."

Sella yelled, "If the ditch grate isn't blocked."

"If the ditch grate isn't blocked."

Tali said, "Ditch grate is on maintenance map. Three meters behind slurry shed, under blackberry overgrowth. Kade, you are about to dislike plants."

"Already a theme."

Mara broke in. "Do it. If the shed flashes, Houses One and Two bake from the side. Bees cannot survive heat through wall."

The word bees changed the channel.

Not pollinator assets. Not corridor support. Bees.

Sana braced the trellis while Mir sawed. The medic slid an inflatable lift under the spar, hand pump working in short, disciplined bursts. Lio Gant cried without shame. Good, Sana thought. Crying meant breath. Breath meant time.

From House One came Mara's voice, close and fierce. "Open the lower brood shutters. Not all the way. Two fingers. Smoke out, heat out, bees stay. Pel, not that latch. The brass latch. Do you want to cook them?"

Pel An's voice answered, strained. "No."

"Then listen faster."

At North Rain, the argument sharpened.

Ansel: "The draft order exists because local response cannot scale."

Amma: "Local response is currently saving the thing your draft order names as reason to take power."

Orren: "Security notes unauthorized personnel inside active burn zone."

Iona: "Security also notes they are the people who know where the valves are."

Tali: "Adding that to record because it was excellent."

Ro coughed once. "Less commentary, more map."

"Fine. Sana, flame front twelve meters from House Two. Kade, slurry shed heat climbing. Sella, valve pressure stable but main line will drop if House Two opens full mist."

Sella answered, breathless. "Then House Two gets pulse mist. Count me."

Tali did not hesitate. "Three seconds on, seven off. Repeat. Mara, can House Two tolerate pulse?"

"Better than fire."

"Not a yes."

"It is the yes we have."

The cross spar gave way with a wet crack.

"Lift," Sana said.

The medic pumped. Mir pulled the cut spar sideways. Sana reached under the trellis, got both hands under Lio's shoulders, and pulled when the medic said pull. Lio screamed, then slid free by half a meter. His boot caught. The flame reached the reed shelter behind him.

Mir crawled past Sana and cut the boot lace.

"Now," the medic said.

They dragged Lio into the drainage ditch as the reed shelter caught.

Fire climbed faster than Sana had believed possible. It ran through the dry reeds with a hungry whisper, then roared when it found stored air. The heat struck her cheek. The medic threw wet canvas over Lio. Mir shoved Sana down as sparks crossed the ditch.

House Two's mist came on.

Three seconds.

The world became white steam.

Seven seconds.

Orange returned.

Three seconds.

White.

Seven seconds.

Orange, but lower.

"Pulse is working," Tali said. "Barely, which is still a word."

Kade's voice came from the slurry shed, tight. "Ditch grate blocked."

Sana closed her eyes for a fraction of a second. "Of course."

"With blackberry, mud, and a metal mesh somebody wired shut. Not old wire. Recent."

Ro said, "Evidence."

"I am evidence-adjacent and annoyed."

Sella shouted, "Move!"

There was a metal shriek, then Kade swore so hard the public channel clipped.

Tali said, "I am marking that as force applied to obstruction."

Kade came back breathless. "Grate open. Sella has bypass drain. Slurry level falling."

Mara said, "House One brood shutters holding. We have live movement."

For the first time since the alert, something like relief touched the channel.

Then a council aide from North Rain said, "Regional Food Stability requests confirmation whether local responders accept regional command support."

Ro answered, "Local responders accept material support under local corridor command and court witness. They do not accept authority transfer."

"That distinction may delay resources."

Amma's voice went cold. "Then the delay has a name."

The aide went silent.

Sana stayed in the ditch beside Lio while the medic sealed burn gel over his cheek and cut trouser fabric away from his hip. He clutched Sana's sleeve. His hand was shaking.

"I didn't set it," he said.

"I know."

"I saw someone by the shed. White clean boot covers. Thought they were inspection."

"Did you see a face?"

He shook his head, then winced. "No. Badge on sleeve. Three rings. Gray center."

Sana looked toward Ro.

Ro had heard.

Tali had too. "Private continuity observer badges use three-ring seal variants."

From North Rain came sudden overlapping voices.

Ansel: "That is not verified."

Orren: "Witness is injured and under stress."

Iona: "Injured witnesses remain witnesses."

Amma: "Entered."

Kade said, "Also I have a relay tag from the shed latch with the same short-hop signature as the drone."

Tali's voice sharpened. "Send."

"Sending."

The data packet hit the docket while Sana watched House Two disappear and reappear in steam. Tali overlaid the relay tag, drone spoof, ditch grate wire composition, and emergency draft order timestamps. Lines drew themselves not as proof complete, but as proof with a direction.

The central emergency draft had begun before public confirmation.

The drone spoof and shed latch shared a routing mask.

The ditch grate had been wired recently.

The fire had not only been set. It had been staged to fail slowly enough for cameras and fast enough for authority.

Mara came over the channel, voice raw. "House Two reports live bees on inner wall. Repeat, live bees."

The corridor heard it.

Workers who had been silent shouted to one another. Sella whooped once, then pretended she had not. Mir laughed and coughed at the same time. The medic beside Sana bent over Lio and said, "You hear that? Your corridor is rude enough to live."

Lio closed his eyes. Tears cut clean lines through ash.

Sana stood, legs shaking. Fire still burned along the bramble. The wild refuges were not safe. The mycelial mat ahead of the fork still had to be cut. House Three might yet smoke. They had saved nothing final.

But they had kept the corridor from becoming only footage.

Ro raised her voice for every channel. "Water Court live witness confirms local corridor chain has rescued injured worker, opened bypass drain, prevented slurry flash, preserved House One brood, stabilized House Two under pulse mist, and identified evidence of sabotage. Emergency response continues under living chain authority."

Ansel said, very carefully, "Regional Food Stability continues to assess."

Tali answered, "Assess faster or help without stealing."

No one at North Rain corrected her.

Sana looked through steam toward the north where the fire still crawled. Pollen, ash, and rain mixed on her tongue. It tasted bitter, green, and alive.

"Mara," she said. "Next."

Mara's answer came at once.

"Cut the mat before the fork."

Sana took Mir's blade and stepped toward the smoking ground.

Chapter 43: Vale Takes the Stage

The mat did not want to die cleanly.

It had been built to hold moisture through heat, to carry fungal memory through dry weeks, to keep roots talking when soil cracked and rain chose other places. Sana knelt in smoke and cut into it with Mir's blade, not straight down, because Mara had shouted no, angle it, angle it, take soil under the braid, do not strip the bridge naked. The blade met living fiber, wet mineral, root hair, ash, and heat. Steam rose around her wrists.

Mir held the wet canvas open. The medic dragged Lio farther down the ditch. Sella pulsed House Two's mist on Tali's count. Kade fought blackberry around the slurry bypass with one good hand and a mood that could have stripped paint. Ro kept talking the corridor into the public record.

For two minutes, the world was only work.

Cut. Wet. Lift. Breathe through cloth. Do not cough while slicing. Do not drop the mat. Do not think about how many small living things a hand could destroy while trying to save them.

Then every public channel chimed at once.

Not alert.

Authority tone.

The sound was older than any one office and carefully designed to make bodies remember classrooms, ration lines, storm shelters, court hearings, and the places where adults stopped arguing because someone had begun deciding.

Tali said, "Oh no."

Ro said, "Identify."

The answer came before Tali spoke.

Dr. Mirek Vale appeared across the public docket.

Not on a side pane. Not as a participant. As a forced central address carried through Regional Food Stability, emergency succession relay, Compact continuity channel, and North Rain's visible threshold feed. His image rose over the burning corridor map and the wet stone of the vault alike, crisp, dry, and lit as if weather were a failure of lesser planning.

Sana kept the blade in the mat because if she looked too long she would cut wrong.

Vale's voice was calm.

That was the insult.

"Citizens of the Cascadia Watershed Compact, affected households, archive officers, court representatives, and emergency responders," he said. "The South Seven pollinator corridor fire has confirmed an escalating cascade risk to regional food continuity. Fragmented response, contested authority, and procedural improvisation now threaten to convert a localized attack into systemic crop failure."

Ro's channel clicked. "Water Court objects to characterization."

Vale did not pause. Either he could not hear her or had arranged not to.

"Under emergency succession authority delegated by the provisional stability quorum and continuity article nine, I am accepting temporary command responsibility for drought line release stabilization."

Ansel's face appeared in a smaller pane from North Rain, expression locked between relief and calculation.

Iona stood below the projection with rain running down her face.

Amma said, very softly, "There it is."

Vale continued. "North Rain Vault is ordered to prepare controlled release of drought line cluster N-RV-14, N-RV-16, and N-RV-18 under centralized distribution security. Initial deployment will prioritize basin survival acreage, school kitchen continuity, and pollinator loss compensation models."

Mara's voice cracked through the channel. "He named the lines."

Sana heard the fear under it. Naming lines was not just logistics. It was custody. Once a line entered a central release order, every later argument had to prove it was not already moving under the authority that named it first.

Kade said, "He waited for the bees to be alive enough to matter and endangered enough to use."

Tali's typing turned vicious over the open channel. "Draft order timestamp confirms prefire preparation. Updating public packet."

Vale's image shifted. Behind him appeared a model: clean maps, blue arrows, crop icons, neat corridors that did not show smoke, burned hands, Lio's face, Sella at the valves, or dead soil sitting beside living culture at a wet threshold table.

"This action does not erase the need for investigation," Vale said. "Sabotage claims will be reviewed. The second witness ledger will be evaluated. Community testimony will be preserved. But food systems cannot wait for every historical dispute to be reconciled while crops fail and children hunger."

The sentence was almost impossible to oppose.

That was how Sana knew it was built as a weapon.

She finished the first cut and lifted a smoking strip of mycelial mat with soil under it. Mir slid canvas beneath. The heat had browned the lower braid but not killed it. Not yet.

"Mara," Sana said. "First strip alive."

Mara answered through a cough. "Wet the underside. Set it safe. Do not let ash coat the cut face."

Vale kept speaking.

"I recognize the pain and anger of Dry Basin families. I recognize the moral urgency of those who have raised concerns about delay. I recognize Keeper Saye's commitment to archive integrity. Recognition, however, is not governance. Governance is the assumption of responsibility when no consensus can arrive in time."

Iona said, "Responsibility without consent is capture."

Vale finally turned his eyes toward her pane. "Keeper Saye, with respect, the vault cannot be allowed to become a shrine to process while living systems burn."

"And it cannot become a trophy for the person who says burn loudest."

The public feed caught that. It moved fast. Captions, repeats, side streams. Tali added it to the docket with a tag: KEEPER OBJECTION.

Vale did not show irritation. Sana hated him for that too.

"No single person is taking the vault," he said.

Ro said, "You just named yourself command responsibility."

"Mediator Tamsin, I am accepting burden, not ownership."

"Burden that orders doors."

"Burden that feeds people."

Amma's voice cut through both of them. "Which people, by whose count, with whose soil, under whose correction?"

Vale's expression softened in the way public figures softened when they wanted grief to look unreasonable beside them. "Mrs. Quill, the proposed release prioritizes precisely the communities you represent."

"Then why did your order not ask us what survives in our ground?"

"The model incorporates Dry Basin need."

"Need is not knowledge."

Sana cut the second strip. Heat surged under it. She jerked her hand back too late. Pain flashed across her knuckles. Mir slapped wet cloth over the cut line.

"Sana?" Ro said.

"Still working."

Tali said, "Central order has moved from draft to issued."

Ro's voice hardened. "Water Court enters immediate procedural objection and stay request."

"Stay request queued," Tali said. "Regional emergency channel rejecting automatic stay because succession authority invoked."

"Of course it is."

Vale raised his hand slightly, and the clean model behind him highlighted drought line N-RV-16 in gold.

"Line N-RV-16 is authorized for immediate preparation," he said. "North Rain Vault security will secure access. Distribution will be escorted by Compact emergency force. Field auditors may observe at designated review points."

Sana heard the trap even through smoke.

Observe.

Not validate.

Not object.

Not stop.

Iona's refusal had made the door answer to a chain. Vale had answered by giving the chain a spectator rail.

"No," Sana said.

She had not meant to broadcast it.

Ro's shoulder channel carried it anyway.

Vale turned toward her pane now. The feed showed Sana kneeling in smoke, face streaked with ash, wet blade in hand, a half-cut living mat steaming beside her. Not a podium face. Not a clean expert. A field auditor in the dirt.

"Auditor Ilyen," Vale said. "Your work has been valuable. I am ordering all field teams to continue preservation efforts while release preparation begins."

"No."

Mara said, "Sana."

But Sana had already stood. The mat smoked at her feet. Fire snapped behind the bramble. House Two mist pulsed white, then clear. Lio groaned in the ditch. Sella shouted at someone to keep pressure under half. All of it entered the channel with her breathing.

"You do not get to use the corridor while we are saving it," Sana said.

Vale's face remained calm. "I am using the corridor's risk to prevent regional failure."

"You are using the corridor's injury to seize the seed line."

"The seed line must move."

"Maybe. Not like this."

"Then offer a faster lawful alternative."

There it was. The old machine. Demand an alternative while holding the clock. Treat every refusal as delay. Treat every complex answer as proof that command should simplify the world.

Sana looked down at the living mat. At the cut she had almost ruined by rushing. At the difference between cutting and tearing.

"Living threshold emergency release," she said.

Ro went still on the channel.

Iona lifted her head.

Vale blinked once.

Sana continued before anyone else could fill the space. "Not full vault opening. Not central command. Emergency preparation of any drought line requires live threshold sign-off: field audit, culture witness, hunger witness, soil witness, water witness, pollinator witness, archive keeper, court witness, distribution steward, and affected household witness. If the corridor condition changes the release model, the corridor witness is required, not bypassed."

Vale said, "That is not an active mechanism."

"It is now on the public docket under founder threshold, second witness provenance, and court death-prevention authority."

Ro said, "Water Court recognizes motion."

Ansel's pane flickered. "Mediator--"

Ro spoke over her. "Recognizes motion. Does not grant. Does not deny. Enters for immediate argument."

Tali said, "Filed. Mirrored. Public. I am beautiful."

Kade coughed. "Debatable but useful."

Vale's eyes sharpened. "You are asking to improvise governance during an active fire."

Sana knelt again and cut the third strip because her hands had to keep earning the words her mouth was spending. "No. I am asking you to stop calling the older governance improvisation because it is inconvenient."

Mara said, "Third strip should be cooler. Angle left."

Sana angled left.

Vale said, "If release is delayed by this motion and basin planting fails, responsibility will be recorded."

Amma answered, "Good. Record all of it."

Oren Lusk's voice joined from the threshold table, rough and unexpected. "Record that my dead soil is sitting there because people with models recorded us wrong for ten years."

Sella shouted from the corridor, "Record that House Two is alive because I knew the valve, not because his model did."

Someone at North Rain tried to mute the crowd pane.

Tali did something. The pane stayed open.

Ro said, "Tampering with public witness feed will be entered as obstruction."

The third mat strip came free. Cool enough. Alive enough. Sana laid it on Mir's canvas and pressed wet soil along the exposed edge.

Mara's voice softened for one breath. "Good."

Then House Three alarmed.

Not fire. Heat stress in the brood wall.

Mara swore. "Sella, pressure?"

"Dropping."

Tali said, "Main line cannot hold House Two pulse and House Three prewet."

Kade said, "Can we divert from slurry bypass now that level is falling?"

Sella: "Maybe if someone opens the old orchard feed."

Mara: "That feed is sealed."

Kade: "Sealed like lawful sealed, or sealed like hit with a wrench?"

Sella: "Wrench."

Ro said, "Water Court authorizes non-destructive emergency access to old orchard feed."

Kade said, "I heard destructive."

"You did not."

"Signal bad."

Sana grabbed the canvas with Mir. "Move the strips. Then House Three."

Vale's voice came again, quieter now, because the feed had refused to become only his stage. "Auditor Ilyen, if your threshold cannot produce a release schedule within the hour, centralized order proceeds."

"If your order proceeds without threshold," Sana said, "field audit objects to chain integrity."

"Noted."

"No," she said. "Noted is what people say before ignoring. Enter it as active objection."

Ro said, "Entered."

Tali said, "Mirrored."

Iona said, "Carried."

Amma said, "Named."

Kade said from somewhere near the old feed, "And hit with a wrench."

Metal rang across the corridor.

Water surged.

House Three's prewet line opened in a ragged fan, ugly and uneven and enough. Steam burst from the brood wall. Workers cheered. Mara laughed once, a broken sound.

On the public docket, Vale's clean model still glowed gold around N-RV-16.

Beside it, Tali overlaid the live corridor chain: Sella at valves, Kade at old feed, Mara at House One, Sana and Mir at mycelial cut, Ro in court witness, Amma at threshold, Iona at the door, Oren with dead soil, Lio injured and testifying, House Two alive, House Three prewetted, sabotage evidence accumulating.

Two maps stood side by side.

One said authority could save life by simplifying it.

The other showed life refusing to be simplified while being saved.

For the first time, Vale's silence lasted too long.

Sana picked up the blade again.

"Next," she said.

Chapter 44: Sana Says No

The next cut was not in the mat.

It was in the story Vale had tried to make of the day.

Sana did not understand that immediately. At first she understood only the work: House Three's brood wall cooling by degrees, Mir carrying the living mat strips toward a wet soil cradle, Sella cursing at the pressure gauge as if anger could become water, Kade hammering the old orchard feed with a tool he insisted was not a hammer because Ro had said non-destructive and he preferred plausible vocabulary. Fire still crawled in the bramble. Smoke still made ghosts of workers ten steps away. Lio Gant still shook in the ditch while the medic argued with him about staying conscious.

Then Vale's gold model moved.

N-RV-16 brightened from preparation status to validation request.

The request did not arrive as a question. It arrived as a procedural pane on Sana's wrist slate, neat and bloodless:

FIELD AUDIT OBSERVER ACKNOWLEDGMENT REQUIRED.

Line safety: provisional.

Chain integrity: provisional.

Emergency release stabilization: proceed under central authority.

Field auditor: Sana Ilyen.

Tap to acknowledge observation.

Sana stared at it while smoke blew sideways across the screen.

Observation.

Not agreement.

Not validation.

Not responsibility, except later, when the record needed a field auditor's name near the release.

She felt anger arrive slowly because her body was busy and could not afford fire inside as well as out. Vale did not need her to approve. He needed her name adjacent to movement. Later, when drought line N-RV-16 failed in a soil it did not fit, or displaced a corridor dependency no model had named, or became collateral in a ownership claim already waiting, the record would say field audit observed. The difference between observing and validating would be litigated by people dry enough to enjoy grammar.

"Sana?" Ro said.

"I have a pane."

Tali's voice sharpened. "Do not tap anything."

"I was not planning to."

"Good. I am emotionally fragile about buttons."

Vale spoke before Tali could pull the request apart. "Auditor Ilyen, the acknowledgment does not ask you to assume release authority. It records that field conditions have been incorporated into the central model."

Sana looked at the corridor around her.

House Two pulsed mist in three-second breaths. House Three steamed. Mara knelt beside a brood wall with one hand pressed to paint where children had drawn blue bees. Kade emerged from blackberry with scratches across his cheek and a grin that looked like pain using humor as a coat. Sella stood on a pipe brace, both hands on a valve wheel too large for her shoulders. Mir held living mat strips like fragile bread. Ro stood in smoke with court witness open. Beyond them, somewhere through rain and signal, Iona stood at the closed vault and Amma stood at the wet table beside dead soil.

This was not data incorporated into a model.

This was the model's missing world.

Sana wiped ash from the slate with her sleeve and opened the response field.

Ro said, "Auditor Ilyen, state for the record before entering."

Sana swallowed. Her throat tasted of pollen, smoke, and metal.

"Field audit refuses acknowledgment."

Vale's face did not change. "On what basis?"

The pane offered checkboxes. Insufficient field data. Contested authority. Hazard conditions. Pending review.

Sana ignored them and dictated into the free field.

"Basis: governance chain corrupted."

For the second time that day, silence behaved like weather.

Tali whispered, "That is not one of the boxes."

"Add it."

"With pleasure."

Vale said, "Auditor, be precise."

"I am."

"The seed line's biological safety is not in dispute."

"I did not say biological safety."

"Then identify the corruption."

Sana's fingers were shaking. Not from fear. From fatigue. From heat. From the knowledge that every word now had edges.

"Central release authority invoked before public confirmation of the sabotage event. Emergency draft order prepared before live corridor condition was verified. Drone spoof, slurry shed relay, and ditch obstruction share directional evidence with actors adjacent to centralization interests. North Rain Vault founder threshold and second witness provenance remain active public claims. Affected soil, hunger, water, culture, pollinator, distribution, and household witnesses have not signed. Field audit has not validated soil match, corridor dependency, or chain integrity for N-RV-16 under altered fire conditions. Therefore any release under central command would move through a corrupted governance chain."

Ro repeated, slower, for the record.

Tali mirrored it.

Amma said from North Rain, "Entered in ledger."

Iona said, "Received at door."

Ansel's voice arrived tight. "Auditor Ilyen, that is an extraordinary allegation during an emergency."

Sana looked at the fire. "Emergencies do not make ordinary lies honest."

Kade made a sound that might have been admiration and might have been smoke.

Vale's calm thinned for the first time. Not broke. Thinned. Sana saw the person under the model look out and calculate whether to crush, absorb, or ignore.

"If you refuse acknowledgment," he said, "you may delay seed preparation for communities you claim to defend."

"No. Your office may choose to delay rather than accept threshold review."

"That is rhetoric."

"It is chain integrity."

"Children cannot eat chain integrity."

Amma's answer cut in before Sana could speak. "They cannot eat your gold map either."

Oren Lusk said, "My mother tried."

No one laughed.

That made it land harder.

Ro stepped closer to Sana, though the court officer was still several meters away through smoke. "Water Court asks field auditor to distinguish refusal from blanket obstruction."

Sana nodded because Ro was not helping Vale. Ro was making a record that could survive appeal.

"Field audit does not object to emergency preparation of drought line candidates," Sana said. "Field audit objects to preparation under exclusive central authority without living threshold sign-off. Field audit proposes immediate threshold validation for N-RV-16 and alternatives, including soil match, water burden, pollinator dependency after fire, distribution witness, and affected household review. Field audit can begin within minutes if North Rain provides line data to the open threshold table."

Tali said, "Formatting as counter-protocol."

Ansel said, "There is no counter-protocol."

Iona said, "There is now a request at the door."

Orren said, "North Rain security cannot release line data to an open table during active threat."

Iona answered, "North Rain keeper can release non-germplasm line suitability data under witness without opening stock custody."

"Keeper--"

"Do not keeper me as if my title is a leash."

Mara's laugh came over the channel, sharp and surprised, then turned into a cough.

Sana looked toward her. "House Three?"

"Prewet holding. Brood heat down two degrees. House Two alive. House One angry. Wild refuge still ugly."

"Mat?"

"If you cut the next strip wrong, I will haunt your audit license."

"Useful metric."

Vale said, "This conversation is consuming the hour you asked for."

Sana crouched again and set the blade to the mat. "Then stop spending it."

She cut while the argument moved above her.

At North Rain, the threshold table reconfigured. Tali dragged Vale's gold release model into a side-by-side pane with Sana's counter-protocol. Amma read the witness categories aloud. Oren Lusk placed his dead soil sack in the soil witness field. Nera Voss entered school kitchen distribution burden. A water steward from the march, Leu Mar, came forward with cistern measurements written on her sleeve because her slate had died in rain. Iona opened North Rain line suitability data in redacted form: drought tolerance, cold storage age, germination risk, water draw, pollinator dependency, soil caution, known historical release failures.

Not seed custody.

Knowledge.

Vale had asked for speed.

The living chain became fast because everyone could see what it was doing.

Tali narrated as she worked. "N-RV-16: high drought tolerance, moderate water establishment, pollinator dependency medium-high, soil alkalinity caution, prior basin trial incomplete, ownership claim contested by second witness ledger. N-RV-14: lower yield, lower pollinator dependency, better school garden compatibility, poorer deep basin performance. N-RV-18: fungal partner required, high failure risk if mycelial corridor damaged."

Mara snapped, "If South Seven mat damage persists, N-RV-18 is stupid."

Tali added: N-RV-18 STUPID UNDER CURRENT MAT DAMAGE.

Ro said, "Use formal phrasing."

"Suboptimal to the point of cruelty."

"Better."

Vale watched the threshold table move faster than his model had predicted. That was the first visible wound Sana saw in him.

He had expected delay.

He had not expected distributed competence.

Sana cut the next mat strip right. Mir took it. The ground beneath smoked but did not glow. The fork might hold.

Sella shouted, "Pressure stable if nobody gets ambitious."

Kade said, "A life principle."

The public docket updated:

FIELD AUDIT ACKNOWLEDGMENT: REFUSED.

ACTIVE OBJECTION: GOVERNANCE CHAIN CORRUPTED.

COUNTER-PROTOCOL: LIVING THRESHOLD EMERGENCY VALIDATION OPEN.

Vale said, "Regional command reserves authority to proceed if threshold validation fails to produce actionable release."

Ro said, "Reserved authority noted. Active objection remains."

"If crops fail--"

Sana interrupted him.

She had never interrupted a regional command address before. A part of her noticed that. Another part of her had no patience left for ceremony.

"If crops fail, the record will show why. If release succeeds, the record will show how. If you override the chain, the record will show who."

Rain hissed on hot ground.

Vale looked at her through the feed. "You are willing to own that?"

Sana thought of her mother's hands sorting seed. Of Steward Voss dead in a tower. Of Tovin Pell's name locked to a node because delay had finally been forced to show a body. Of Iona refusing to become the door. Of Ro turning grief into procedure without letting procedure eat it. Of Sella too young to have to know valves and old enough that no one could afford to ignore her.

"No," Sana said. "That is the point. I am not willing to own it. No one person should. Put it on the chain."

For a moment the corridor seemed to listen with more than microphones.

Then House Three's alarm cleared.

One clean tone.

Mara exhaled so hard it sounded like something breaking. "Brood wall stable."

Workers answered in scattered shouts. Sella slid down from the pipe brace and sat hard in the mud, exhausted beyond pride. Kade reached to help her, remembered his burned hand, used the other. Mir laid the last mat strip in the wet cradle. The fire still burned beyond the bramble, but the corridor had stopped losing faster than people could name.

At North Rain, Tali's threshold pane flashed its first result.

N-RV-16: HOLD PENDING SOIL AND POLLINATOR MATCH.

N-RV-14: LIMITED PREPARATION FOR SCHOOL KITCHEN AND NEAR-BASIN TRIAL REVIEW.

N-RV-18: HOLD PENDING MYCELIAL CORRIDOR ASSESSMENT.

Amma read it aloud.

Oren said, "That sounds like less food."

Iona answered, "It sounds like food that might live."

Vale did not concede.

Men like Vale rarely conceded in public. They adjusted vocabulary and called it continuity.

"Regional command will review the threshold output," he said. "Central order remains in effect pending integration."

Sana looked at the docket. "Field audit objection remains in effect pending consent."

Tali added a bright line under that.

Vale's pane faded smaller, not gone, no longer central.

The map of the corridor expanded again.

Smoke still rose. Fire still moved. The work was not done.

But the gold line around N-RV-16 had stopped pulsing.

Sana sank back onto her heels, blade across her knees.

Ro's voice came softly over the channel. "Auditor Ilyen?"

"Yes."

"You understand they will come for that refusal."

Sana looked at the living mat strips, at the water beading over them, at Sella in the mud, at Mara's hand still pressed to a painted bee.

"They were already coming," she said. "Now they have to say why."

Chapter 45: Bread Riot Weather

The phrase reached Puget before the smoke did.

FIELD AUDIT REFUSES RELEASE.

It crossed the food channels stripped of every word that had made it true. It left behind no corrupted governance chain, no living threshold, no N-RV-14 limited preparation, no House Two alive because a fourteen-year-old knew a valve, no dead soil on a wet table, no second witness ledger, no sabotage evidence, no line between observation and consent. By the time it hit the Puget tower feeds, it had become a sentence with teeth:

FIELD AUDITOR BLOCKS DROUGHT SEED RELEASE AS CORRIDOR BURNS.

Then:

BASIN DEMANDS DELAY PUIGET FOOD SECURITY.

Then, on a channel whose logo wore wheat like a halo:

WHO GETS BREAD WHEN AUDITORS SAY NO?

Tali read the channel mutations aloud because hiding them would not make them less real. Her voice came through the public relay at North Rain and through Ro's shoulder speaker in the smoking corridor, each version edged with contempt.

"Feed Seven is calling you unelected obstruction," she told Sana. "Puget Civic Grain says Dry Basin representatives are exploiting Tovin Pell's death. Three continuity channels are pushing the word hoard. The insurer-friendly channel has a poll asking whether vault stock should be protected from local interference. Never read polls while democracy is ill."

Sana sat on a wet crate beside House Three while Mara checked the brood wall for the fourth time. Her knuckles throbbed where heat had found them. The fire had been pushed back from the houses, not extinguished. Wild refuge crews were still cutting smoke lines under rain that could not decide whether it wanted to help. Every breath tasted like burned sugar and green things refusing to die.

"What about Dry Basin channels?" Ro asked.

Tali was quiet for half a second.

That was worse than her swearing.

"They are showing Puget feeds."

Amma's voice entered from the threshold table. "Of course they are."

"And the central order pane," Tali said. "And clipped video of Vale saying children cannot eat chain integrity."

Oren Lusk said something too far from the pickup to hear. Amma repeated it for the record, because that was how she fought erasure.

"So they think the cities are taking the seed."

Tali said, "Some of them do. Some of them think you refused the seed. Some think North Rain is hiding seed. Some think Puget burned the corridor. Some think the court is staging process to starve them politely."

Ro closed her eyes. "Crowd positions?"

Tali threw a civic map into the public docket. Puget towers glowed in stacked blue. Dry Basin marchers at North Rain glowed amber. School kitchen queues glowed pale yellow. Road closures pulsed red. Feed heat clustered around words: bread, hoard, audit, vault, riot, children, traitor, release, theft.

"Puget lower food decks have two large gatherings," Tali said. "One at Dock Grain Exchange. One outside Tower Twelve ration market. Both started as demand lines, now shouting at basin solidarity feeds on public walls. Dry Basin lower road at North Rain is still held by school kitchen stewards, but anger is climbing. A smaller group has broken from the water line toward the council capsules. Firebreak volunteers are staying with corridor crews, for now. Private continuity channels are pushing rumor that basin marchers plan to storm the vault at dusk."

Vos's voice came from North Rain perimeter. "False."

"Yes," Tali said. "But useful false."

Kade sat in mud beside Sella, one hand bandaged, the other holding a cup of water someone had forced on him. "Bread riot weather."

Sana looked at him.

He shrugged without humor. "Old courier term. Not a riot yet. Air that can become one if enough people profit from thunder."

Mara did not look up from the brood wall. "Do not make that poetic."

"It predates me."

"So does mold."

Ro stood, grimacing as smoke pulled at her lungs. "We need direct address to both crowds."

Ansel's voice arrived immediately. She had been waiting for a phrase she could use. "Regional Food Stability can issue a calm advisory."

Amma said, "Your calm advisories sound like cupboards locking."

"Mrs. Quill, if inaccurate information spreads--"

"It is already spreading from rooms with your seals on them."

Vale's pane remained small but present. "Public communications must avoid inflaming conflict."

Sana almost laughed. "Then stop saying food like a hostage note."

No one spoke for a beat.

Tali said, "I am adding 'food like a hostage note' to the informal index."

"Do not."

"Too late."

Ro cut through them. "We need correction that people believe. Not institutional tone. Not model tone. Names. Actual status. Who is eating tonight. Who is not. What line is held and why. What line is being prepared and for whom. What burned. What was saved. What is still contested."

"That will expose scarcity," Ansel said.

Amma answered, "Scarcity is already exposed. You are only deciding whether it arrives wearing rumors."

At Puget Dock Grain Exchange, the first window broke.

They heard it through a city witness feed: a sharp crystalline sound followed by a human gasp large enough to become a crowd. The feed was from a school kitchen volunteer who had gone to collect tower flour allocation and had stayed when the line began arguing with a wall screen. The screen had shown the clipped headline about Sana's refusal. Someone had thrown a metal ration cup. The window behind the screen had not been the target. It broke anyway.

People became aware of themselves as a crowd.

That was the dangerous moment.

The volunteer's feed swung wildly. Puget residents in clean work coats, dock loaders, kitchen staff, tower parents with children on hip carriers, elderly people with grain cards sealed in plastic sleeves. Not villains. Hungry enough to fear being next, comfortable enough to be shocked by their own fear, angry enough to look for someone less protected to blame.

A man shouted, "Why do basins get veto?"

A woman shouted back, "They get graves!"

Someone else shouted, "My kid's school bread is down to two days."

"So is ours."

"Then open the vault."

"To who?"

The feed cut to static as someone grabbed the volunteer's arm.

Ro said, "Puget court office."

A clerk answered, panicked. "We are aware."

"Send visible non-armed food mediators, not patrol."

"Patrol already dispatched."

Ro's face tightened. "Recall them to outer streets. Put school kitchen staff at front."

"We do not command city patrol."

Vale said, "Regional command can coordinate."

Ro snapped, "If uniformed command enters that line, every rumor becomes uniform-shaped."

Sana pushed herself to her feet. "Put the live threshold results on the walls."

Tali said, "Which walls?"

"All of them."

Ansel said, "Incomplete threshold analysis could increase confusion."

"Good," Sana said. "Confusion is better than a clean lie."

Ro nodded once. "Do it."

Tali pushed the public pane wider: N-RV-16 hold pending soil and pollinator match; N-RV-14 limited preparation for school kitchen and near-basin trial; N-RV-18 hold pending mycelial assessment; emergency water continuing; corridor houses alive; sabotage evidence under live review; no party has exclusive release authority; field audit refusal applies to central-command validation, not to emergency food preparation under living threshold.

She added plain text because Tali understood that truth could still fail if it wore the wrong clothes:

SANA DID NOT REFUSE FOOD.

FIELD AUDIT REFUSED A CORRUPTED COMMAND CHAIN.

LIMITED SEED PREPARATION IS OPEN UNDER WITNESS.

Amma said, "Add: Dry Basin families are not taking Puget bread."

Tali added it.

Nera Voss said, "Add: Puget school kitchens are named in the limited preparation review."

Added.

Mara said, "Add: if pollinator corridors fail, everybody's bread gets stupider."

Ro looked at the sky.

Tali translated: POLLINATOR CORRIDORS SUPPORT BOTH BASIN AND CITY FOOD.

Mara said, "Coward."

The corrected pane hit Puget public walls twelve seconds after the first window broke.

It did not calm the crowd.

Truth rarely arrived with that much magic.

But it changed the argument. People stopped shouting only at the basin feed and began shouting at the wall. That mattered. A wall could take more blame than a body.

At North Rain, the lower road surged toward the council capsules.

Sana saw it on the threshold feed. A line of Dry Basin marchers moved as one, not charging, but no longer waiting. School kitchen stewards tried to slow them. Amma stood in front of the wet table and shouted names into the sound. Oren Lusk lifted his dead soil sack over his head as if it were a flag and then seemed horrified by what the gesture invited.

"They think Puget is calling them hoarders," Tali said.

"Puget is calling them hoarders," Kade said.

"Some Puget feeds are."

"When a feed screams, people do not ask whether the whole city opened its mouth."

Vos moved at North Rain, putting his body between the marchers and the nearest patrol line again. "Perimeter holds. Shields down."

Orren said, "If they advance--"

"Shields down."

Iona's voice cut through rain. "Open the table sound to the lower road."

Ansel objected. "That will amplify disorder."

Iona said, "They are the table."

Tali opened it.

Amma did not give a speech. Speeches would have been too smooth.

She read.

"N-RV-16 held because soil and pollinator match not proven. N-RV-14 limited preparation because it may feed school kitchens sooner and fail less cruelly. N-RV-18 held because the corridor that would carry its fungal partner is burned and being saved by people whose names I will read now."

Then she read names.

Sella Oren-niece, though Oren shouted her full name and Amma corrected it. Lio Gant, injured worker. Mara Venn. Sana Ilyen. Ro Tamsin. Kade Morrow. Mir Pel. Jalen Odet. Sivi Marr. Leu Mar. Nera Voss. Oren Lusk. House One workers. House Two workers. House Three workers. People who would not have been in a central model until they died or delayed it.

The lower road slowed.

Not stopped. Slowed.

At Puget, another wall feed picked up Amma reading names. For a moment, the Dock Grain crowd watched a woman in rain read names beside a vault. The man who had shouted about veto lowered his arm. The woman who had shouted about graves covered her mouth. A child asked whether Sella was a grown-up. No one answered quickly enough.

Then one of the private continuity channels pushed a new banner:

UNVERIFIED BASIN LISTS USED TO DELAY CITY FOOD.

The Dock Grain crowd surged again.

Tali made a strangled sound. "I am going to commit an interface crime."

Ro said, "Legal crime only."

"No promises."

Sana looked at the map. Two crowds. Two hungers. Two sets of fear being aimed at each other while the people who had lit the corridor waited for the word riot to mature enough to harvest.

"We need them to hear each other without feeds between," she said.

Ro was silent.

Sana knew why. Direct crowd-to-crowd audio could heal nothing or detonate everything. Puget fear in Dry Basin ears. Dry Basin grief in Puget ears. No institutional smoothing. No captions making it neat.

"Not open shouting," Sana said. "Witness pairs. School kitchen to school kitchen. Parent to parent. Distribution steward to distribution steward."

Amma said, "Names first."

Nera said, "I can find Puget school kitchen stewards."

Ansel said, "Regional communications cannot endorse unmoderated cross-crowd contact during unrest."

Ro said, "Then we moderate it under Water Court witness."

Vale said, "Mediator Tamsin, that is beyond normal court practice."

Ro looked at his pane. "So is everything that works today."

Tali was already building the bridge. "Pairing channels. Privacy masks for children. Audio delay three seconds for direct incitement only. No feed captions. No channel logos. Names, location, role."

Kade stood slowly. "Courier houses can carry if signal gets throttled."

"With that hand?"

"Mouth still works."

Mara said, "Regrettably confirmed."

The first pair connected: Nera Voss at North Rain and a Puget school kitchen quartermaster named Halem Rui, standing outside Tower Twelve with flour dust on his sleeves and fear doing its best to become anger.

Nera said, "My kitchen has water tonight and not enough grain."

Halem said, "My kitchen has grain for two days if the tower children do not take seconds."

Nera said, "Our children stopped asking for seconds last season."

Halem looked away.

Not victory.

Contact.

The second pair: Oren Lusk and a Puget dock loader whose brother worked in a vertical bean farm.

The third: Amma and an old tower grandmother who remembered ration chalk from childhood and hated herself for fearing basin carts.

The fourth: Sella, against everyone's better judgment, and a Puget girl who had asked whether Sella was grown-up.

"No," Sella said. "I am fourteen."

"I am twelve," the Puget girl said.

"Do you know valves?"

"No."

"Lucky."

The Puget girl considered that. "Do you have bread?"

"No."

"We have some."

"Good."

"People here say you are taking it."

"People there are stupid."

Ro leaned toward the pickup. "Sella."

"Fine. People there are being lied to."

The Puget girl said, "People here are scared."

Sella's face changed. "People here too."

That one moved through both crowds faster than any correction pane.

People here too.

For five minutes, bread riot weather held at thunder.

Not clear sky.

Not peace.

But thunder that had not yet found ground.

Then Tali's private channel flashed red.

She did not put it public immediately.

Sana saw the hesitation and felt cold under the smoke heat.

"What?" Sana asked.

Tali answered on the chain channel only. "Regional command is filing emergency objection against Ro."

Ro's face did not move. "Grounds?"

"Exceeding mediation authority. Unauthorized public communications. Obstruction of succession release. Creating cross-crowd instability."

Vale's pane remained calm. Too calm.

Ansel would not look at the court feed.

Amma said, "They are going to remove you."

Ro watched the paired channels. Nera and Halem were arguing now about yeast. Oren was crying without turning off his mic. The Puget grandmother was teaching Amma the old chalk marks for hidden flour. Sella and the twelve-year-old had stopped insulting each other and were comparing how adults lied when they were ashamed.

"Not yet," Ro said.

Sana heard the decision in her voice.

Ro was not going to defend herself first.

She was going to make the table bigger.

Chapter 46: Ro's Open Table

Ro did not ask permission to convene the open table.

That was the first argument against it and the reason it worked.

Permission had become a corridor full of locked doors, each office waiting for the one before it to accept enough blame to let the next one move. Regional command had objected to Ro's authority. North Rain security had objected to the threshold. Food Stability had objected to disorder. Private continuity had objected without admitting it had hands. Puget feeds objected to basin grief. Basin road anger objected to Puget fear. Everyone objected to being the first to say what they wanted in the same room as the people who would pay for it.

So Ro made the room.

She stood at the edge of South Seven corridor with smoke behind her, a court coat streaked with ash, and a portable witness light blinking red on her collar because regional command had filed to suspend her authority. The objection pane hovered in the docket beside her face.

EXCEEDING MEDIATION AUTHORITY.

UNAUTHORIZED PUBLIC COMMUNICATIONS.

OBSTRUCTION OF SUCCESSION RELEASE.

CREATING CROSS-CROWD INSTABILITY.

Ro looked at the list long enough for every feed to read it.

Then she said, "Water Court acknowledges emergency objection against Mediator Ro Tamsin."

Vale's smaller pane sharpened.

Ansel straightened at North Rain.

Orren's security officers shifted, as if words could become handcuffs if they stood ready.

Ro continued. "In response, I am convening an open emergency table under death-prevention authority, live witness preservation, and active multi-basin food-risk conditions."

Tali whispered, "That is either brilliant or career-ending."

Kade said, "Often adjacent."

Ro heard them and ignored them.

"All claimants will be visible. All authority claims will be named. All objections will be recorded with the hunger, soil, water, pollinator, archive, court, distribution, and affected household witnesses they would override. Any party refusing visibility while asserting authority will be entered as attempting to govern off-record."

Ansel said, "Mediator Tamsin, you cannot convert a mediation into a constitutional assembly."

Ro looked at her pane. "Watch me convert a crisis into a table."

Amma made a sound that might have been approval if approval had been a knife.

The public docket changed.

Not beautifully. Quickly.

Tali built it like a field rig: crude panes, hard labels, no soothing background. At the center sat a wet map of the Long Orchard: North Rain Vault, South Seven corridor, Dry Basin hunger clusters, Puget food decks, Mill 7B, salmon corridor, Lower Silt maize, school kitchens, water convoy routes, Tovin Pell's node, the second witness ledger, Vale's central order, Sana's active objection, Ro's emergency objection, sabotage evidence, and threshold results. Around the map, claimant windows opened one by one.

Regional Food Stability: Chair Ansel.

Continuity Command: Dr. Mirek Vale.

North Rain Keeper: Iona Saye.

North Rain Security: Orren.

Water Court: Ro Tamsin, challenged mediator.

Field Audit: Sana Ilyen.

Culture Witness: Mara Venn.

Hunger Ledger: Amma Quill.

Dry Basin Soil Witness: Oren Lusk.

School Kitchen Distribution: Nera Voss and Halem Rui.

Water Witness: Leu Mar.

Pollinator Corridor: Jalen Odet, Sivi Marr, Sella Aru, Lio Gant.

Courier Second Witness: Kade Morrow.

Puget Civic Food Decks: three stewards, two parents, one dock loader, names visible.

Firebreak Towns: Mayor Odet and crew witnesses.

Private Continuity Interests: empty.

Tali highlighted the empty pane in unpleasant yellow.

"I do not see private continuity," Ro said.

No answer.

Tali made the pane larger.

"That is petty," Vale said.

"No," Tali said. "Petty would be adding shoe icons."

Ro said, "Technical clerk."

"Fine."

The pane remained yellow.

Ro's voice lowered. "Private continuity interests have been named in relation to relay tags, drone spoof routes, feed amplification, and obstruction materials. If no representative enters, their absence will be recorded beside their evidence."

After seven seconds, a face appeared. Not one of the clean-shoe observers. A counsel face, middle-aged, smooth, lit from an office where rain was a graphic on glass.

"Meridian Continuity Mutual appears under protest," he said.

Kade murmured, "There you are."

Ro said, "Name."

"Counsel Erian Holt."

"Authority claim."

"We have no authority claim. We are risk advisors."

Tali dragged three evidence lines beside his pane: relay tag, drone spoof mask, ditch grate wire purchase chain. "Risk appears active today."

Holt's mouth tightened. "Those are unverified allegations."

Lio Gant lifted his head from a medic sling in the corridor pane. His cheek was sealed under gel, one eye swollen nearly shut. "I verified a boot cover."

Holt looked pained by the inconvenience of injured people.

Ro said, "Recorded. Counsel Holt remains visible."

The table widened again.

What Ro built was not a meeting. Meetings had agendas. This had exposure.

She began with claims, not speeches.

"Regional Food Stability," she said. "State what you want."

Ansel hesitated because public institutions preferred to state what conditions required, not what they wanted.

Ro waited.

Ansel said, "We want emergency seed release to prevent regional food instability."

"Under whose control?"

"Coordinated regional authority."

"Say central."

Ansel's jaw worked. "Central authority."

Tali typed it in plain text.

"Continuity Command," Ro said. "State what you want."

Vale did not hesitate. "Temporary command responsibility for drought line stabilization, with field observations integrated and subsequent review."

"Translation," Amma said.

Ro pointed at Tali without looking.

Tali typed: CENTRAL RELEASE FIRST, REVIEW LATER.

Vale's expression cooled. "That is not my phrasing."

"Plain-language field," Ro said. "You may object."

"I object."

Tali added: VALE OBJECTS TO PLAIN-LANGUAGE SUMMARY.

Sana, despite smoke and exhaustion, nearly smiled.

"North Rain Keeper," Ro said.

Iona stood in rain before the closed vault door. "I want no common survival stock released to single authority while hunger, succession, capture, and sabotage conditions are present. I want living threshold review for any emergency release, with affected witnesses able to object before custody moves."

Tali typed without translating.

"North Rain Security."

Orren said, "I want vault integrity preserved and unauthorized access prevented."

"Does that include preventing keeper release of non-germplasm suitability data?"

"If threat conditions require."

Iona looked at him with old disappointment. "Threat to whom?"

Ro said, "Answer."

Orren did not.

Tali typed: SECURITY CLAIM UNCLEAR; MAY INCLUDE CONTROL OF INFORMATION.

"Field Audit."

Sana wiped smoke from her mouth with the back of her wrist. "I want no release validated through a corrupted chain. I want immediate living threshold validation for any drought line candidate. I want field conditions, sabotage evidence, pollinator survival, soil match, and distribution burden visible before custody moves. I do not object to feeding people. I object to disguising capture as feeding people."

The words landed across both crowds.

At Puget Dock Grain, someone shouted, "Then feed us."

Halem Rui turned toward the crowd pickup. "She is trying to keep the seed from failing in the wrong ground."

Another voice shouted, "My child cannot eat correct ground."

Nera Voss answered from North Rain. "Mine cannot eat dead seed."

Ro let both stand.

That was the table's cruelty and its usefulness.

"Hunger Ledger," Ro said.

Amma opened her paper ledger. Rain darkened the oilcloth edges. "I want delay counted as harm before death makes it respectable. I want every release measured by who eats, who waits, who loses soil, who loses water, who carries the correction when models are wrong, and whose dead are used as arguments after being ignored as people."

No one asked Tali to summarize.

"Puget Civic Food Decks," Ro said.

A parent named Soren Valez, no relation to Mirek Vale and visibly tired of saying so, stepped into the Puget pane. "I want tower children fed. I want to know whether school bread is being cut because seed is going to basins, because insurers are holding grain, because vault law is stuck, or because everyone is lying. I want someone to stop telling us to be calm while shelves get lighter."

"Dry Basin Soil Witness."

Oren Lusk gripped the sack in his lap. "I want seed that can live where we are, not seed that lets a city say it sent help. I want my mother's dead garden counted as warning, not failure."

"Pollinator Corridor."

Jalen Odet's pane shook because he was walking along a smoke line. "I want the fire fully suppressed, no foam that kills brood, water priority for houses still alive, and no release model using corridor collapse until we finish proving whether collapse was caused."

Sivi Marr added, "And I want whoever says pollinator loss compensation to spend one hour carrying bees out of smoke."

Sella leaned into the frame. "And new valve handles. These are stupid."

Ro blinked.

Tali typed: VALVE INFRASTRUCTURE FAILURE AFFECTS EMERGENCY RESPONSE.

"Thank you," Sella said.

"Courier Second Witness."

Kade looked as if he would rather be under the pump house again. "I want the route record to stop being treated as either crime or romance. Couriers carried hidden provenance because official custody could be captured. That does not make us pure. It makes us evidence. I want every emergency movement tracked in a way that a hungry child, a court, and a future thief can all understand."

"Private Continuity," Ro said.

Counsel Holt clasped his hands. "We want stabilization, liability clarity, and protection against unauthorized release that could create broader scarcity."

Tali hovered over the keyboard.

Ro said, "Do not."

Tali removed her hands with visible regret.

Amma said, "I will. They want to decide which hunger is insurable."

Holt said, "That is inflammatory."

Amma looked at him. "So is hunger. We are still naming it."

The table had now done what closed rooms avoided.

It had made wanting visible.

Wanting did not solve anything. But hidden wanting had been steering the region like a hand under water.

Vale leaned forward. "The open table has value as consultation. It cannot command emergency logistics."

Ro answered, "It does not command logistics. It constrains legitimacy."

"Legitimacy does not move grain."

Halem Rui said from Puget, "Neither does illegitimacy. It just makes people break windows before grain gets there."

That came from a tower quartermaster, not a basin marcher, so Puget heard it differently.

The Dock Grain crowd shifted.

At North Rain, the lower road settled by a few inches of posture. Not calm. Listening again.

Ansel saw it. To her credit or her calculation, she adapted.

"Regional Food Stability will participate in living threshold validation for N-RV-14 limited preparation," she said. "But we need a timeline."

Tali immediately started a clock. "Excellent. We love measurable concessions."

Ro said, "Timeline proposal. Thirty minutes for preliminary suitability confirmation of N-RV-14. Parallel review of N-RV-16 hold conditions. N-RV-18 held pending corridor assessment. Emergency water and school kitchen grain transparency updates every fifteen minutes. Puget and Dry Basin paired witness channels remain open. Private continuity evidence preservation order issued now."

Holt objected.

Everyone expected that.

Ro said, "Grounds?"

"Proprietary risk systems."

Sana said, "A child died under your proprietary weather."

The table went still.

Ro did not soften it. "Objection recorded. Evidence preservation order stands pending review."

Vale said, "Mediator, your authority remains under challenge."

Ro nodded. "Yes."

"Any order you issue may be void."

"Then everyone can see who obeyed only when they thought care was validly notarized."

The sentence moved through the table like a current.

Tali typed it, then stopped. "Sorry. That one was not an order."

"Leave it," Amma said.

Ro took a breath. Sana saw the cost then. Not fear. Cost. Ro was spending institutional capital faster than anyone could replenish it, and she knew exactly how overdraft worked.

"Open table ruling," Ro said. "Pending review of the objection against my authority, the emergency table remains live under public witness. Any party may challenge my procedural authority. No party may use that challenge to move seed custody, suppress witness channels, alter public records, interrupt emergency water, deploy armed crowd control into food lines, or remove evidence from South Seven corridor. If I am removed, this table and all records transfer to the next visible Water Court officer with no private recess."

Vale looked away for a fraction of a second.

Sana caught it.

So did Tali.

"He wanted recess," Tali said softly.

Ro did not answer.

At Puget, the Dock Grain crowd began chanting.

Not a slogan from the feeds.

The words came unevenly at first, then steadied:

"Show the table. Show the table."

At North Rain, the lower road heard it and did not know whether to trust it.

Amma lifted her ledger. "We hear you."

Halem Rui raised both flour-dusted hands in the Puget pane. "We hear you."

Sella muttered, "This is weird."

The twelve-year-old Puget girl, still paired with her, said, "Better than stupid."

"Barely."

"Barely is still better."

Sella considered that and nodded as if it were a valve setting.

The open table did not end the emergency.

It made the emergency harder to steal.

For the first time since the fire began, Sana saw the next shape of the fight. Not one door. Not one model. Not one refusal. A table big enough that power had to show its hands before reaching.

Then Tali's clock hit thirty minutes and the first N-RV-14 suitability packet arrived from North Rain.

Iona looked at it.

Mara looked at the corridor data.

Amma looked at the hunger ledger.

Sana looked at all of them and understood that the table had only opened the harder question.

Now they had to decide what could move without becoming theft.

Chapter 47: The Provenance Flood

The N-RV-14 packet looked harmless.

That was how dangerous records survived.

It arrived from North Rain as a neat suitability bundle: germination confidence, cold age, drought tolerance, school kitchen compatibility, pollinator burden, water establishment curve, storage custody, prior trial notes, chain restrictions. A clean sheet for a seed line that might move without killing itself in the wrong ground.
